# 道德经注释（清黄元吉）

**《Dao De Jing Commentary》 by Huang Yuanyi (Qing Dynasty)**

**Table of Contents**
*Explanatory Preface to *The Essence of the Dao De Jing* (abridged)*

---

The Way of the Three Teachings is but the Way of the Sage.
The Confucian says *sincerity*, the Daoist says *golden elixir*, the Buddhist says *true emptiness*—yet all speak of the same boundless *qi*, permeating heaven, earth, and all beings.

Only the Sage delves to its source, attaining its utmost, harmonizing with the boundless void of heaven and earth without distinction—thus becoming the Sage.
Capable of both firmness and yielding, adaptable to round or square, without form to imitate, without sound or stench to constrain—its divine transformations are boundless, beyond comprehension.

If all is natural and effortless, originally so, neither augmented by the Sage nor diminished by the common—even descending to birds, beasts, grasses, and trees, all share this same *qi*, this same principle.

Observe the vast ocean—water most difficult to fathom. Yet a single sea contains all its waters, and a single dipper holds but water. The *qi* of the boundless void is likewise uniform.

Though heaven, earth, sages, and beings differ in myriad ways, at their formation, they all receive this same *qi*. Beyond this, there is no vital breath, no generative principle—only varying dispositions.

Mencius said, *"The Sage and the commoner share the same nature,"* and *"The difference between humans and beasts is slight."* Truly, a precise observation.

Alas, the world’s customs decline daily, and the people grow ever more lax. Though the Dao is inherently plain and ordinary, many regard it as deceptive, strange, and bizarre. Thus, Confucianism becomes ever less Confucian, Buddhism ever less Buddhist, and Daoism ever less Daoist.

If we do not expose the root causes or pierce through the subtle mysteries, the Dao will grow ever more obscured and unmanifest, while human hearts will decay and become ever harder to govern—until, inevitably, we descend to the level of birds and beasts, and sages are no better than the ignorant masses. The Dao will then hang in peril like a fragile egg suspended between heaven and earth, with none to bear the responsibility of preserving civilization.

Fortune favors this age of peace, and the land enjoys tranquility. At this opportune moment, I encountered a Daoist friend among the sacred mountains, discussing profound truths and subtle mysteries. I listened in secret, and their words were no mere empty talk for idle ears—I gained true insight from their teachings.

The assembled revered me as a master, so day and night I lectured on the *Dao De Jing*, using it as proof for cultivating virtue and establishing moral power. Unwittingly, I wrote endlessly, page upon page. Though my flaws are glaring and unworthy of scrutiny by the wise, there are moments where my words align with the subtle intent of the *Tao Te Ching*, offering some small benefit to the world.

My friends urged me to publish this work for the benefit of future generations. Though I feel deep shame and dare not deceive myself or others, their earnestness compelled me to consent. Now, as this printing begins, I preface these annotations with a few words to explain their origin.

Winter of the tenth month, Guangxu decade
Recorded by **Gǔ Dàn**, **Yuán Jí**, and **Huáng Zhāng** in self-preface.

**Chapter One: The Gateway to All Mysteries**

The Dao/Way that can be spoken is not the constant Dao/Way;
The Name that can be named is not the constant Name.
Nameless, it is the beginning of Heaven and Earth;
Named, it is the mother of all things.
Thus, without desire, one observes its subtlety,
With desire, one perceives its openings.
These two, though born from the same source and bearing different names,
Are both rooted in the Mysterious.
The Mysterious again and again—
This is the gateway to all mysteries.

Master Zhu [Zi] said: *"The Dao/Way is like a path, which all people share."*
In truth, it is the principle that gives birth to Heaven, Earth, humans, and all things,
Hence it is called the Dao/Way.
Before Heaven and Earth were divided,
This Dao/Way hung suspended in the boundless void;
After Heaven and Earth were opened,
This Dao/Way was entrusted to the heavens and the earth.
What Dao/Way is this?
Before Heaven and Earth, it endured eternally;
After Heaven and Earth, it does not decay.
Born before Heaven and Earth,
Mingled within the formless,
Neither visible nor audible.
Thus, the Sage of Highest Virtue said:
*"To speak of the Dao/Way is to exhaust and obscure it—
In truth, there is nothing to speak of;
What can be spoken of are but the Dao/Way’s manifestations,
Not the constant Dao/Way itself.*
*To name the Name is to empty and void it—
In truth, there is nothing to name;
What can be named are but the Dao/Way’s dross,
Not the constant Name itself.*

If people do not know the Dao/Way,
How can they read the *Book of Poetry* and say:
*"The Heavenly carriage moves without sound or scent"*—
The Dao/Way cannot be confined by words!
And again:
*"The mandate of Heaven, serene and unceasing"*—
The Dao/Way cannot be without designation.

One must know that within the utmost void,
The utmost reality resides;
Within the utmost emptiness,
The utmost substance dwells.
The Dao/Way’s truth cannot be measured by what is spoken of.

The Sage of Highest Virtue, in compassion and mercy,
Broadly expounded the teachings for the world:
*"Before the primordial chaos had yet taken form,
It was but a boundless, undifferentiated unity—
Though called Nameless, all things under Heaven and Earth, humans and nature,
Are nurtured within it.
This is why it is called the beginning of Heaven and Earth.

When stillness endured long,
The movement of *qi* arose,
Then came the Named,
A vast and harmonious *qi* of the Great Unity,
Flowing through the cosmos,
Nourishing all life.
This is why it is called the mother of all things.

The beginning is the undivided *qi* before Heaven and Earth opened;
The mother is the transformation giving birth to all things after their division.
For the student at the outset,
There is no other method—
Only to sit upright with a single mind,
Discard all thoughts,
And observe with a veiled gaze.

Below the heart, above the kidneys,
There seems a void, a hollow space.
Spirit meets spirit, breath returns to its source,
Following its ebb and flow,
But the focus must be on the convergence of spirit and *qi* in the central palace.
In no time, spirit and *qi* merge into one.

Then, one listens to its boundless chaos,
Without stirring a single conscious thought.
After long stillness, a faint, indistinct awareness arises,
Entering the realm of the Unknowable.
At this moment,
One does not know whether spirit enters *qi* or *qi* returns to spirit—
All is indistinct, neither self nor other, neither here nor there,
Yet not dull or lifeless—
If it were dull and lifeless, it would be but a withered tree, dead ashes.
The cultivator, reaching this stage,
Should extinguish the stirring mind,
Yet preserve the observing mind.
Only then does one attain wisdom as if foolish,
And insight without application.

In the midst of ignorance and unconsciousness,
A sudden awakening stirs—
This is the foundation of the *Tai Ji*.
One must know that this awakening,
Arising naturally and not through external influence,
Is the true awareness of my original nature.

The Daoists call this the Mysterious Gateway,
Found within the space of a single breath.
When inhaling, it is yin, stillness, void;
When exhaling, it is yang, movement, presence.
Even this fleeting breath contains mystery.

If one seeks to cultivate true awakening,
At the moment of this awakening—
A genuine, unshakable, thoughtless, and pure original person emerges.
Thus it is said:
Heaven and Earth awaken, giving birth to myriad things;
Humans awaken, refining the Golden Elixir.

But this awakening is like a flash of lightning or a spark of stone fire—
Before one’s eyes, it is present;
In an instant, it vanishes—
The difference is but a hair’s breadth.

The scholar must discern it clearly in ordinary times,
Only then can they grasp it when the moment arrives.
Even the ancient Great Awakened Ones,
Did not attain otherwise than through this awakening’s accumulation.

If the cultivator begins their work
Without first mastering the stages of no-desire and desire,
Of observing subtlety and perceiving openings,
From where can they establish their foundation?

Though no-desire and desire,
Subtlety and openings,
Are but yin-stillness and yang-movement—
One *qi* divided into two,
Two *qi*s still returning to one.

When stillness endures and movement arises,
The void generates presence—
This is called *One Yang Emerges, the Child is Born*.
When movement reaches its peak and stillness returns,
Presence returns to void—
This is called *Restoring the Command, Returning to the Root*.
Both are but the yin and yang of the *Tai Ji*.

Though they bear different names,
They spring from the same source—
The Sage of Highest Virtue calls this *One Mysterious*.
The Mysterious means profound and distant.

The scholar who seeks the Mysterious Dao/Way,
Must still the mind again and again,
Achieve steadiness once more—
Within, all is boundless and free,
This is observing subtlety without desire.

This is the first Mysterious.
When the *qi* stirs,
Though one knows,
They do not produce the perception of knowing;
Though one moves,
They do not cling to the thought of movement.
With one mind,
Without dual thoughts—
This is perceiving openings with desire.
This is the second Mysterious.

As for the Mysterious again and again,
It is truly the realm of returning to the root—
What else could it be but the gateway to all mysteries?

What is regrettable is that ordinary people,
Though possessing this mystery,
Do not nurture it directly—
Thus it opens and closes in cycles,
Until it is exhausted without end.

The True Person, when the Mysterious Gateway opens,
Fixes it with a single glance,
Seizes it with a single hand,
Cultivates and nurtures it—
Never allowing it to stray for an instant,
Thus directly attaining the supreme primordial source,
And achieving the Great Awakening as a Golden Immortal.

The initial practice lies within the single Mysterious Gateway.
The Supreme Chapter first distinguishes the Nameless as Named,
And perceiving subtlety without desire, with desire.
This clearly shows the essence of cultivation—
Beyond this single Mysterious Gateway,
There is nothing else to progress toward.

Thus the opening four lines speak of the Great Dao’s root,
Which is truly formless and boundless,
Unthinkable and inexhaustible.
Only at the dawn of Heaven and Earth,
When all was indistinct and chaotic,
Without any trace or clue,
Is it akin to when a person steadies themselves in stillness.

At the moment of Heaven and Earth’s sudden manifestation,
When stillness reaches its extreme and motion begins,
Awareness dawns and clarity awakens—
This is when a person detects *qi* in movement,
The very foundation for refining the elixir.

Within this mere blink of an eye,
Without the wisdom-pearl or insight-sword,
One cannot grasp it.
Essentially, the moment thoughts arise,
The Mysterious *yin* (womb) emerges—
The very beginning of Heaven’s opening and Earth’s splitting.

Since ancient times, all divine immortals
Have been born from this moment of awakening motion.
It is also said:
Perceiving subtlety without desire,
Perceiving openings with desire—
These two, one still and one moving,
Mutually forming their roots,
Thus differing in name though arising from the same source.

All things with form and appearance
Can be pondered, measured, and divined.
But this subtle opening—
It is without yet present,
Present yet without—
Cannot be named or described by any fixed standard.

Even if the tongue flows like a suspended river,
It cannot utter a single word of it—
Thus it is called the *Dao/Way* of *Dao/Way*.

Learners also do not regard it as distant and obscure,
Nor do they exhaustively seek its concrete reality.
If one truly finds this definite place,
Where others see it as vague and elusive,
In me, it is solidly grasped.
With prolonged practice, vitality springs forth—
Every moment is the Dao itself.

**Chapter Two: Achieving Merit Yet Not Claiming It**
All under Heaven know beauty as beauty—
Then ugliness already exists;
All know goodness as goodness—
Then wickedness already arises.

Thus the presence and absence of forms generate,
Difficulty and ease complete each other,
Length and shortness define one another,
Height and low incline toward one another,
Sounds harmonize with one another,
Front and back follow one another.

Therefore, the Sage acts in affairs of non-doing,
Practices teachings of no-speech,
All things arise yet they do not take credit,
Give birth yet do not possess,
Act yet do not rely—
Achieving merit yet not claiming it;
Only by not claiming it,
Does it not depart.

The ancients said:
*"I urge you to exhaustively seek the origin of your vital body,
Returning to the root and returning to the source is the Elixir King."*
And again:
*"Exhaustively seek where your vital body receives its mandate,
Do not blame Heaven’s *qi* for being fully revealed."*

From this perspective, it is clear that at the very beginning of receiving one’s mandate,
The natural principles of Heaven are complete,
Without any flaw.
Those who speak of beauty and ugliness,
Goodness and deformity,
Are all harming the Dao.

What is the Great Dao’s nature?
In Confucianism, it is called *"hidden and subtle"*,
Within which lies the essence of unseen and unheard.
In Buddhism, it is called *"that"* (which is),
Within which lies the truth of neither good nor evil.
In Daoism, it is called *"Mysterious Gateway"*,
Within which lies the profound mystery of no-thought and no-contemplation.

The Great Dao’s root originates from here.
Once imagined or theorized,
It falls into the trap of duality;
Once discussed or deliberated,
It stumbles into the snare of words.
Though immortals of old praised the Dao’s marvels,
Saying it is beautiful and good,
They were all but dimly grasping its form—
Not truly seeing its essence.

For when they say it is good,
Evil already opposes it;
When they say it is beautiful,
Deformity already opposes it.
Moreover, beauty exists here,
Yet ugliness also exists here;
Goodness exists here,
Yet deformity also exists here.
This is doubtless the post-Heaven duality of *yin* and *yang*,
With their interplay of victory and defeat—
Not the pre-Heaven unity of *qi*.

Thus the Supreme Chapter says:
*"All under Heaven know beauty as beauty—
Then ugliness already exists;
All know goodness as goodness—
Then wickedness already arises."*

It is known that if one does not seek the emptiness of *qi* beyond extremes,
But merely speaks of beauty as beauty and goodness as goodness,
This is abandoning the root for the branches.

The Supreme Chapter specifically instructs beginners:
At the start of seated practice,
One must cast aside all entanglements,
Hold nothing in mind—
Only then, lowering the curtain and sealing the *fu* (mouth),
Observing the elixir field in emptiness,
Concentrating the spirit and regulating the breath,
Regulating the breath and concentrating the spirit—
Thus, with prolonged effort,
Spirit and *qi* merge into one cluster,
Instantly entering the distant and obscure realm—
This is non-doing.

When emptiness reaches its utmost,
Suddenly, a single awareness stirs—
This is doing.
From this single thought arising within the rule,
Continuous yet indistinct,
Unceasing yet surging—
Spirit relies on *qi* to stand,
*Qi* relies on spirit to move,
Without knowing yet knowing,
Without awareness yet aware—
The Gateway of the Mysterious *yin* (womb) is already established.

From this point, one must diligently uphold the rule,
Concentrating the spirit beyond form.
With each inhalation and exhalation,
Each advance and retreat,
One must ensure *qi* returns to the Mysterious opening.
Breathing naturally as Heaven dictates—
This is the root of Heaven, Earth, and all beings,
The foundation of sages, immortals, and Buddhas—
The most profound secret of our Daoist school,
Not to be carelessly revealed.

For cultivators’ practice,
Rather than seeking the boundless and unattainable,
Why not seek the *yin* and *yang* with tangible proof?

The generation of presence and absence,
Is but movement and stillness,
Stillness and movement,
Emerging from the Mysterious and entering the *yin* (womb),
Harmonizing the principles of *yin* and *yang*.

Difficulty and ease completing each other,
Is but firmness and yielding,
Yielding and firmness,
The cauldron, furnace, lute, and sword—
One simmers, one warms.

Length and shortness defining one another,
Is the inhalation and exhalation,
The advance and retreat of the *Task* (governor) and *Du* (conceptor) vessels—
The forward movement is short,
The backward movement is long.

Height and low inclining toward one another,
Is fire above yet making it descend,
Water below yet making it ascend—
The wondrous reversal of above and below,
The transformation of *kan* and *li* (water and fire).

The harmony of sounds and voices,
Is the merging of spirit and the flow of *qi*,
With the hundred channels coursing freely—
Not unlike the cry of a phoenix summoning its flock,
Or the resonance of voices in unison,
Coming without being called.

The following and preceding in succession,
Is the *Du* vessel descending at the rear,
The *Task* vessel ascending at the front—
*Kan* and *Li* (water and fire) in union,
A harmonious whole, clinging yet never parting.

These are all the return of *yin* and *yang* from the post-heaven realm
To the pre-heaven realm of *wuji* (non-extreme).

The sage understands the origin of the Dao/Way,
Vague and boundless, vast and traceable—
In their conduct, they esteem *wuwei* (non-action),
And follow the manner of *gongji* (humble self-restraint) and *chuangshang* (hanging sleeves),
Whereby they are revered without boast.

In their teaching, they uphold *buyan* (non-speech) as their principle,
And illustrate the wondrous method of *guohua cunshen* (transcending transformation to preserve the spirit).

The sage acts, yet all things behold them—
What difficulty could there be?

Even in the tilling of fields and the digging of wells,
They are born to life yet forget their own practice;
In regulating the source and conserving the flow,
They diligently guide transformation yet conceal their own traces.
Though their merit fills the heavens and earth,
And their fame echoes across the land,
The sage regards it as shame—
For hollow titles are never matched with true accomplishment.

How could this be compared to the *Book of Documents* saying:
*"You alone do not boast, do not claim—*
*None under heaven can contend with you in ability,*
*None can contend with you in merit."*
Yet even this still harbors the thought of *miren zhangjing* (dispelling strife and competition)?

This is truly the realm of *shenhua* (spiritual transformation),
The *danding* (tranquil and settled) heaven,
Where one simply finds contentment in their own nature,
And never forgets the true essence of self-realization.

The ancients spoke of viewing wealth and nobility as floating clouds,
And discarding fame as worn-out footwear—
Was this not their meaning?

Though so, when the Dao is complete,
Virtue/power establishes itself,
And true attainment naturally brings renown.

The sage, though not dwelling on merit,
Is praised by the world and posterity—
Their merit shines like the sun and moon,
Their fame weighs heavy in ancient and modern times.
It is only because they do not claim,
That it is never lost.

Students must cultivate from the utmost emptiness and profound stillness,
Nurturing forth the true essence of neither beauty nor virtue/power,
Only then can their cultivation be said to have genuine roots.

What is this Dao/Way?
The *xuan guan* (mysterious aperture)!

Abandon this, and there is no life.
For those who cultivate the Dao/Way,
Abandoning this mysterious aperture,
There is no other Dao/Way.

If one takes beauty and virtue/power as the Dao/Way,
It belongs to the dust of post-heaven concerns.
The *Taoist Canon* warns of this,
Hoping people will trace the flow back to its source.
Otherwise, the praise of beauty and virtue/power
Is also for the gentlemen of the Three Dynasties—
How could one blame them too harshly?

The *I Ching* says:
*"One *yin* and one *yang*, this is called the Dao/Way."*
Thus *yang* is not the Dao/Way,
And *yin* is also not the Dao/Way—
The Dao/Way lies between *yin* and *yang*!

Moreover, the Dao/Way is principle,
While *yin* and *yang* are *qi*.
Principle without *qi* cannot manifest,
And *qi* without principle cannot move.
To speak of the Dao/Way alone is formless and elusive—
Only when observing where *yin* and *yang* emerge,
Can one truly grasp its foundation.

This chapter speaks of the true essence of neither beauty nor virtue/power,
Directly piercing the root of the Great Dao/Way,
Urging people to establish their foundation and extreme,
As the basis for cultivating the self and governing the world.

The phrases on presence and absence, ease and difficulty,
Teach people to return from the duality of *yin* and *yang*
To the true unity of *qi*.

Within this, it also teaches people
To seek the mysterious aperture from the interplay of presence and absence,
As the fundamental root of alchemical refinement.

As for guarding the middle and nurturing the elixir,
Revitalizing *yang* within the *Du* vessel,
Operating the *heche* (river car),
All are progressively unraveled.

Only the sage points directly to its source,
Thus *gongji* (humble self-restraint) and *wuwei* (non-action),
Silent yet trusted—
Though there be action and creation,
In themselves, there is no trace of virtue/power.

When their work is complete and they retire,
They regard wealth and nobility as of no consequence—
Who but the sage could attain this learning?

Students who ponder and gain insight,
Not only have a basis for their practice,
But also a path to transcendence.

Other commentaries say:
*"All under heaven know why beauty and virtue/power are beautiful and virtuous—*
*Then they themselves will not be evil or unvirtuous."*
This explanation is also correct.

But the *Taoist Canon* often speaks from the source,
Without falling into the realm of the Two Vehicles.

**Chapter Three** does not show what is desirable,
Does not exalt the worthy—
So the people do not contend;
Does not prize difficult-to-obtain goods,
So the people do not become thieves;
Does not show what is desirable,
So the heart does not become disordered.

Thus the sage’s governance,
Empties the heart, fills the belly,
Weakens the will, strengthens the bones,
Ever keeping the people in ignorance and without desire,
So that even the wise dare not act.

To do *wuwei* (non-action),
Is to have nothing left ungoverned.
The sage’s governance of the world,
Rather than having action,
Is to prefer non-action—
Even more, to not knowingly act while non-acting.

In transforming the people and establishing customs,
Rather than being able to influence,
It is better to be able to transform—
Even more, to let them rest in harmony with the natural state of no affairs.

Thus Yao and Shun, with *gongji* (humble self-restraint) and *chuangshang* (hanging sleeves),
Brought the four quarters to reverence and moved them by their example—
What profound method of transformation is this?

It is merely:
The ruler’s own emptiness brings the people stillness,
The ruler’s own lack of partiality brings the people rectitude,
The ruler’s own lack of desire brings the people stability.

Otherwise, in their bustling and meddling,
They believe they are bringing benefit and removing harm to the people,
Yet they do not realize that their disturbance of the people is indeed great.

Thus it is said:
The people originally have no contention,
Yet the ruler contends and seizes;
The people originally have no greed,
Yet the ruler is greedy and covetous;
The people originally have no thoughts or desires,
Yet the ruler leads them with strange arts, seductive cunning,
Gay attire, and delicious food.

Could one then make the people free from contention, greed,
And all cravings and partialities?

If the ruler can cultivate their own self,
Rectify their own heart,
And remain serene and detached,
With no involvement in any affairs,
And does not exalt the worthy and able,
Then the people naturally settle and are at peace, without contention;
If the ruler does not prize difficult-to-obtain goods,
Then the people value modesty and shame,
And do not become thieves.

Moreover, the fields of sound, color, wealth, and profit are varied—
If the eyes are without sight and without desire,
And the ruler and the people each find contentment in their own natural state,
Then the spirit, which is *xu ling huo po* (empty, responsive, fluid, and lively),
Naturally remains ever responsive and ever still, without disorder.

「Is this matter one that only the extraordinary can achieve? Only the Sage can close off the ears and eyes, sever all crooked and private inclinations, and embrace *One* to empty their heart. When the heart is empty, the foundation for refining the elixir is established. Thus, they gather the *Daoist* **Lingyang** (精陽, the refined yang-qi of heaven and earth) to transform the **Yinjing** (陰精, primordial yin-essence), day by day, month by month. Naturally, the yin-essence diminishes and the yang-qi flourishes, filling the belly so that the form is preserved—this is what is called "concentrating the *Dao* to solidify the body, employing techniques to prolong life," the very method of transcending birth and escaping death. Furthermore, through focused *qi* to achieve softness, one is like an infant whose strength is weak and cannot grasp objects. Though supremely soft, when moved, it becomes firm. Observing its vast and profound nature, its towering and surging essence, it truly encompasses heaven and earth with abundance. All the senses of knowledge and the inclinations of craving—where do they retreat and dissolve? The Sage cultivates themselves with this.

To govern the world, they must first be without knowledge or desire—
Not only do the foolish wander in muddled confusion,
But even the clever and learned *Rujia* (儒, Confucian scholars),
Who daily boast of their abilities and rely on their wisdom,
Fearful lest they fail to assert themselves,
At this point, having become serene and detached, their private thoughts of knowledge and cleverness
All merge into simplicity and purity.
This is the power to act yet not act—
Not because they cannot, but because they dare not.
Nevertheless, humans cannot escape the *void* while alone transcending the material world;
They must follow the *Dao* of human relations and daily practice.
Yet how can they avoid action entirely?
But if they follow nature and act without contrivance,
Even if they act, it remains as *non-action*—
Just as Heaven speaks not yet transforms all things,
And the seasons proclaim their teachings without words.
The Emperor governs without action, and the ministers administer the affairs.
The trace of action is in the *form* (迹), while the essence of non-action is in the *spirit* (神).
Thus, seated in state with hands clasped,
The whole realm rejoices in peace and harmony—
Ah, how magnificent!

The *Dao* is inherently plain and ordinary,
Not seeking to display novelty or oddity,
Nor to exalt the strange and unusual.
When a state honors the worthy and able,
It is indeed a good thing,
But if they boast and vie over this,
The truly worthy remain worthy,
Yet the unworthy will also feign virtue.
Even worse, the worthy may condemn the unworthy as unworthy,
While the unworthy condemn the worthy as unworthy—
Thus, contention arises.
Each side mutually flaunts their merits,
Taking turns to support factions,
And from this, the world becomes embroiled in affairs.
When a state manages finances,
It is also a common practice,
But if they prize rare goods and treasure distant commodities,
The people will climb mountains and cross seas,
Risking peril and danger,
Undeterred by the hardships of travel or the fear for their lives,
To gather and present them to the court.
When they fail to obtain these,
They resort to every scheme imaginable—
Even thieves and brigands will not hesitate to seize them.
As for clothing and food,
These are also daily necessities,
Yet if one insists on delicacies and rare dishes,
And seeks only fine silks and embroidered garments,
Seeing what is desirable and desiring it—
When will this extravagant trend be corrected?

Thus, the Sage values the inner over the outer,
Necessarily nurturing the *heart* (神) with emptiness,
And filling the belly to nourish the *qi* (氣),
So that the *heart* and *qi* merge into one,
Flowing harmoniously throughout the body—
Smooth and integrated, soft and joyful,
And the will becomes weak.
Moreover, when the *heart* is still as a mountain,
And the *qi* flows like a spring,
The bones grow strong.
Ever embracing *One* (抱一),
And guarding the *Middle* (守中),
Not only does one remain without desire or thought,
But even the clever and learned,
Feeling the influence, are transformed within,
Daring not to act arbitrarily.
Some say, "If one acts, it will lead to chaos and criticism;
If one does not act, it will invite ridicule for its purity."
Between action and non-action, the distinction is indeed subtle.
Yet to act in accordance with nature—
This is not reckless action;
Action and non-action are equal in essence.
Thus, Confucius praised Shun (舜), saying:
*"Is it not Shun who governs without action?"*
**Chapter Four: Harmonizing with the Common**

The *Dao* is boundless yet applied—
Sometimes it does not fill,
Profound, like the source of all things.
It dulls the sharp, resolves the tangled,
Harmonizes its brilliance,
Merges with the dust.
Deep and still, as if it existed—
I know not whose child it is,
Resembling the Emperor before all beginnings.
The Emperor (帝) is the **Tian Di** (天帝, Heavenly Sovereign).
The *beginning* (先) is the beginningless beginning.
What is the *Dao*?
The **Taihe** (太和, Great Harmony) of *One Qi* (一氣),
Filling and permeating the heavens and earth,
Its extent encompassing the universe,
Its essence permeating past and present,
Its *virtue/power* (德) reaching all nine regions and myriad lands.
From the *taihuan* (胎卵, womb and egg) to the *shihua* (湿化, damp transformation),
From the *feizhan* (飛潛, flying and burrowing) to the *dongzhi* (動植, moving and growing),
No place exists where it is not present.
How vast is the *Dao*?
Yet its substance is empty, void, and boundless—
Without form, yet overflowing.
When it manifests and takes action,
It is vast and harmonious in its embrace,
Applied here and there, yet always fitting.
The *Classic of Poetry* says:
*"Left and right, left and right—
There is nothing it does not suit;
Left and right, left and right—
There is nothing it does not possess."*
Truly like rivers converging at the sea,
Yet the sea does not appear to be full—
Is it not indeed the fundamental principle of all things?

Confucius said:
*"The virtue/power (德) of ghosts and spirits
Nourishes all things without remainder."*
And again:
*"Speak of small matters without breaking them,
Speak of great matters without exhausting them."*
Its vast and profound nature is truly inexhaustible.

The *Dao* is so difficult to describe—
How then shall later generations cultivate it?
The **Taishang** (太上, Supreme Sage) out of compassion for ordinary people,
Points to its essence, saying:
*"The reason ordinary people cannot enter the *Dao*
Is that those with cleverness and wisdom,
Relying on their own abilities,
Act arbitrarily and haphazardly,
Thus, their minds become obstructed,
And the empty, responsive essence is not seen.
Now, to cultivate the *Dao*,
One must know that cleverness and wisdom
Are all demons that obstruct the *Dao*.
From this point onward,
One must abandon cleverness and lose wisdom,
Set aside the private inclinations of the ears and eyes,
And return all to the undifferentiated chaos—
All the sharpness of boasting in ability,
Relying on wisdom,
Arrogance toward things,
And domineering over people,
Must be completely blunted and extinguished,
So that the human heart dies and the *Dao* heart is born,
Knowledge vanishes and *wisdom* (慧) shines forth.»

The sages of old said: *"Those with cleverness and wisdom are not to be feared,
But those who sink deeply into the *Dao*, purify the heart, and observe its principles—these are truly to be feared.
Is this not the truth?*

The practitioner must take as primary
Settling the spirit, refining thoughts,
Reducing desires, and clearing the heart.
Yet how can one know?
The spirit of perception, thought, and deliberation—
The turbulent, false, and impure thoughts—
Swirl and disturb in ceaseless flux:
This thought not yet stilled, another arises;
This reflection not yet extinguished, another follows.
I must exhort and encourage myself,
Grant myself patience and release—
As tangled silk, I must trace its threads and unravel it;
As overgrown vines, I must uproot them and clear the ground.
Thus, all confusion dissolves,
And the *Heavenly Sovereign* remains ever serene.

Yet this is but the practice of solitary stillness,
Still not reaching the clamor of the world.
If one can be still but not move with the world,
It remains a rootless study.
One must examine oneself in stillness,
But when entering bustling scenes,
One must be all the more vigilant and cautious!
In all things, yield precedence to others,
Place yourself behind them,
Hold others above you,
And willingly submit below them.
Thus, there will be no conflict with the world,
No strife with others.

Moreover, men delight in the like and reject the unlike—
This is the common nature of the world.
I must only follow the currents, conform to their kind,
And harmonize with them.
Though I possess a luminous and upright heart,
I will not display its sharp edges.
I must only silently recognize their ways,
Subtly uphold their form,
Favor those who align with me,
And tolerate those who do not.
Thus, the people of Lu hunted hares,
Yet Confucius also hunted hares.
In ancient times, when the *Dao* was not yet illuminated,
The sages all mixed with the vulgar thus.

Again, observe the Sixth Patriarch:
Though his *Dao*-fruit was complete after receiving the robe and bowl,
Its subtlety was not yet exhausted.
Thus, he remained in the world, dwelling in Four Assemblies Mountain,
Living among hunters, unperturbed by their ways,
And so escaped harm.
Had he not blended with the dust and followed the light,
How could he have long preserved his life?

Through such dynamic and static cultivation,
Ever adapting with skill,
The originally pure, still, and luminous essence
Naturally remains embraced—
Yet it is not a fixed attainment:
If it seems to be somewhere, to be stored,
It is not truly stored.
A single radiant light flickers beneath the golden court.
What *Dao* is this?

It arises before Heaven and Earth,
Mingles within the void.
I do not know from whence it comes,
Nor to where it goes—
Whose child is it, truly?
The *Classic* says: *"There is a thing, mixed and complete,
Born before Heaven and Earth."*
Is this not the profound mystery of the *Dao*?

What form did the *Emperor* have before?
It was nothing but the undifferentiated chaos,
The indistinct *Hun-Meng*,
A single breath of empty clarity.
When the One Principle gave rise to myriad transformations,
And all things returned to spring,
The *Dao* dispersed through Heaven, Earth, and all beings—
Yet it remains beyond measure.
The cultivator who seeks to illuminate the *Dao*-body
Must observe the moment when Heaven and Earth are on the verge of opening,
When the unopened suddenly opens,
And thus discern the source of the *Dao*—
Then the foundation for practice will not lack.

The *Most High* outlines the *Dao*-body for all to see,
And teaches those who cultivate the Great *Dao*
To first recognize the *Dao*-source.
To seek the *Dao*-source,
One must first, within one’s own heart-nature,
Eliminate deceit and preserve sincerity,
And through gradual study,
Eventually reach the realm of spiritual transformation,
Only then knowing that within one’s heart-nature
Lies the essence of the *Dao*,
Ever present, never leaving one’s embrace.

One must seek its beginnings in stillness,
Using cultivation, reflection, and preservation
To safeguard the original purity,
Not overwhelming others with arrogance,
Nor confusing the mind with excess.
This is what Zhangzi called *"liberation from private desires."*

Parting the clouds to reveal the blue sky,
Severing the vines to expose the true face—
When one moves among people,
One must not conceal one’s light while displaying it,
Nor blend with the dust while remaining in it.
Some may flaunt their cleverness and wisdom,
Others their abilities,
Or perhaps assume the guise of a recluse in mist and stone,
Displaying an air of lofty detachment.
Observing history, many have invited disaster and calamity
Through such harm to the *Dao*.

Like the Han dynasty’s imprisonment of Confucius,
Or the *Qingliu* disasters of the Jin—
Though rooted in the treachery of petty men,
It was also because they did not know the *Dao* of preserving life through wisdom.

If one blends with the world, follows the light,
And mingles with the dust like the *lingzhi* among common grasses,
Or the phoenix flying with ordinary birds—
Unheard yet fragrant, unseen yet exalted—
Thus hiding one’s talents,
Thus nurturing oneself directly,
Then the still and ever-present *Dao*
Will hover between the eyebrows,
Existing yet not existing,
Having form yet none.

As the *Zhongyong* says:
*"The carriage of Heaven moves without sound or scent."*
What else could be before the *Emperor*?

**Chapter Five: Not Knowing to Guard the Center**

Heaven and Earth are not kind;
They treat all things as sacrificial dogs.
The sage is not kind;
He treats the people as sacrificial dogs.
Between Heaven and Earth,
Is it not like a bellows?
Empty yet not collapsing,
Moving yet ever producing more.

Much speech leads to exhaustion;
Better to guard the center.

Between Heaven and Earth,
The ceaseless generation and transformation,
The unrestrained motion—
All depend on this original *qi*,
Sustaining all between them.
It props up Heaven above,
Supports Earth below,
And flows through all beings,
Excluding none.

When this *qi* is undifferentiated and boundless,
It governs all things in orderly harmony—this is *principle*.
When it is vast and pervasive,
Encompassing all things,
Flowing and turning freely—this is *qi*.
The unity of *principle* and *qi* is *virtue*.

Thus, the sages of old said:
*"The virtuous one, with all human desires exhausted,
Allows Heaven’s *principle* to flow freely,
Without a trace of artificiality."*

「”Further, it says: *“Generating and transforming is called *Dao/Way* (仁).”* In essence, *Dao/Way* (仁) is like the *Dao/Way* (仁) inherent in wood: its generative *principle* (理) and generative *qi* (气) are fully complete without exception.

Between Heaven and Earth, all things are born; the sages nourish all people—none of this is without the *principle* (理) and *qi* (气) permeating through it. How could it be so trifling as to be seen merely in outward actions?

Thus, the *Taoist Master* (太上) spoke to clarify the *Dao/Way* (道):
*"If Heaven and Earth lacked this vital generative *qi* (生气), relying only on the teachings of the four seasons and the alternations of cold, heat, warmth, and coolness—
merely passing through cycles of movement—
then all things would lack any foundation to draw upon.
How would *qi* be received?
How would form be established?
In their view, all things would be as light as straw dogs (刍狗),
utterly unworthy of regard,
and would only see their gradual decay.*

*"If the sages lacked this true primordial essence (真元心体), depending solely on ministers, officials, and the promulgation of decrees, laws, and regulations
to instruct the common people,
then the rural masses would have no way to observe or be moved,
and how would people be transformed?
How would households prosper?
It would be as if the sages regarded these people as lowly as straw dogs (刍狗),
completely indifferent to their well-being,
and would only see their ruin and destruction.*

*"Yet why does Heaven and Earth, though lacking conscious intent (无心),
encompass all things—wind, cloud, rain, and dew—
without a single being excluded?
And why do the sages, though silent in speech (忘言),
assist and perfect all beings,
so that none fail to thrive within the world?
This clearly shows that Heaven, Earth, and the sages
all rely on this original generative *qi* (一元真气),
permeating and nourishing beings and things—
though there may be deprivation,
there is also creation;
though there may be coercive power (刑威),
there is also virtuous transformation (德化).
This so-called ‘lack of *Dao/Way* (仁)’ is precisely the ultimate expression of Heaven, Earth, and the sages’ *Dao/Way* (仁).*

*"If people do not know the *Dao/Way* (圣),
let them observe Heaven and Earth:
The upper *qi* (上浮) solidifies as Heaven,
the lower *qi* (下凝) condenses as Earth.
Between them, it is hollow yet luminous (空洞了明),
boundless and without obstruction (浑浩流转),
as vast as the unfathomable belly of a gourd (橐), yet freely communicating (龠之相通).
When it is empty and contains nothing (虚而无物),
it naturally follows the *qi*'s rise and fall (随气机之升沉),
unbent and unyielding (不挠不屈).
When it moves and becomes sound (动而为声),
it also listens to the transformations of beings and things (听人物之变化),
ever producing more that is strange and marvelous (愈出愈奇).
Observing Heaven and Earth,
it is no different from the gourd and its hollow (橐龠).
The sages are surely no different either.*

*"The scholar who guards the center (守中) and embraces the One (抱一),
boundless and without trace (空空无迹),
vast and without end (浩浩无垠),
the deeper the concealment (藏之愈深),
the broader the manifestation (发之愈溥).
Compared to those who fill halls with words (言堂满堂) or rooms with speech (言室满室),
the difference is as vast as Heaven and Earth (相隔不啻天渊).
Those who rely on words to set teachings (以言设教)
and exhaust their teachings through words (以教有尽),
how can they compare to those who treasure their vital essence (宝吾之精),
nourish their generative *qi* (裕吾之气),
and wander spiritually beyond form (神游象外),
while their *qi* permeates the rules of the world (气注规中),
ensuring nothing is left untransformed?
Truly great is the study of guarding the center (守中之学),
the essential path for cultivating the self (修身之要道).*

*"This is the original generative *qi* (一元真气).
Cultivating the self lies here,
governing the world also lies here.
Beyond this, so-called institutions and laws (制度法则)
are like traps and snares (筌蹄) for catching fish and rabbits (取鱼兔).
Fish and rabbits must rely on traps and snares to be obtained,
yet saying that one cannot catch fish and rabbits without them is wrong,
and saying that traps and snares *are* the fish and rabbits is also wrong.*

*"The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir (金丹大道) is like capturing Yang to supplement Yin (采阳补阴):
the forward path is short,
the backward path is long;
the small return of the Jade Nectar (玉液小还),
the great return of the Golden Nectar (金液大还)—
all are methods for catching fish and rabbits (取鱼兔之筌蹄).
If one regards them as the source of the Dao (道源),
one errs by a hair’s breadth and strays a thousand miles.*

*"Only this original *qi* (元气) is without sound or scent (无声无臭),
without form or shape (无象无形),
the shared generative *qi* of Heaven, Earth, and all beings.
The scholar who cultivates and refines must seek this single essence (一件丹头),
otherwise they will only burn and refine in vain (空烧空炼).
Otherwise, refining essence (炼精),
refining *qi* (炼气),
refining spirit (炼神),
refining emptiness (炼虚)—
all belong to studies without foundation (无本之学).
Practiced from childhood,
they will still yield no results by old age (到老犹无成).*

*"The *Taoist Master* (太上) teaches people to focus on guarding the center (守中用功),
while the key lies in the gourd’s hollow (消息在橐仑).
The learner must explore this themselves!"*

*"The phrase ‘not *Dao/Way* (仁)’ in the chapter is merely rhetorical.
**Chapter Six: The Immortal *Spirit* (谷神不死)**
The immortal *Spirit* (谷神) does not die—
this is called the Mysterious Mother (玄牝).
The Mysterious Mother’s gate—
this is called the root of Heaven and Earth (天地根).
Endlessly present (绵绵若存),
yet never exhausted in its use (用之不勤).*

*"The matter of cultivation and refinement (修炼一事)
arises only because humans have a body (自有身后),
while their *qi* and nature are bound to the past (气质拘于前),
and desires for things obstruct the future (物欲蔽于后)—
just as gold and jade, though pure and flawless (精金良玉),
once plunged into filth (陷于污泥),
lose their brilliance (金之精者不精),
and their excellence (玉之良者不良).
Thus, to restore their original form (复原形),
one must not rely solely on washing away impurities (淘汰之力),
but also on the work of polishing and refining (琢磨之功),
to return to their initial essence (还乎初质).*

*"The *Taoist Master* (太上) shows the starting point for practice:
“‘The immortal *Spirit* (谷神) does not die.’*
What is meant by *Spirit* (谷神)?
Mountain caves are called *gǔ* (谷), speaking of their emptiness (虚);
Variation and unrestraint are called *shén* (神), speaking of their responsiveness (灵).
Not dying (不死) means the unceasing clarity of awareness (惺惺不昧).
If one can nourish the body of emptiness and responsiveness (虚灵不昧之体)
as the foundation for the elixir (丹头),
then cultivation and refinement will be effortless.*

*"Yet this is formless and shadowless (无形无影),
ungraspable and elusive (不可捉摸).
One must seek its traces in things that have sound and color (有声有色者).
The ancients said:
“‘To make the *Spirit* (谷神) of the *gǔ* (谷) endure without dying,
one must establish the foundation in the Mysterious Mother (玄牝).’*
Why is it called ‘Mysterious’ (玄)?
Mysterious (玄) is Heaven (天).
Why is it called ‘Mother’ (牝)?
Mother (牝) is Earth (地).
When Heaven and Earth unite (合),
the Mysterious Mother emerges (玄牝出),
and from the Mysterious Mother,
the opening and closing of all things is formed (阖辟成).
Between them, one ascends and one descends (一上一下),
one goes forth and one returns (一往一来),
cycling endlessly within the hollow of emptiness (旋循于虚无窟子)—
this is the gate of the Mysterious Mother (玄牝之门).*

*"Confucius said, ‘The *Gāi* (乾) and *Kūn* (坤) are the gate of the *Yì* (易),’
is this not true?
Through this gate (此门),
the path of Yin and Yang’s coming and going (阴阳往来之路) is traversed,
the realm of Heaven and Earth’s transformations (天地造化之乡) is realized,
and the birthplace of beings and things (人物发生之地) is found.
Obtaining it means life;
losing it means death.*
*If common people follow it conventionally (顺用之),
they become the path of death (死户);
If the sages invert and reverse it (颠倒之),
they become the gate of life (生门).*」

Alchemists who seek to refine elixirs for eternal life and enduring vision must pass through this **Mysterious Mother’s Gate**—there is no other path.

What else could be the root of Heaven and Earth?

When the cultivator draws the curtain to observe and reflect, in the state of undifferentiated ignorance, the common heart dies.
Suddenly awakened and stirred, the Dao-heart is born.
This is called: stillness as **Original Spirit**, movement as **True Intention**.

Within this, when the prenatal breath stirs—do not rigidly cling to the **dantian**;
rather, in the space neither inside nor outside, observe its rising and falling, its coming and going—
effortless, lively, and unrestrained—
and thus the true prenatal breath is attained.

The ancients said, *“Exit through the Mysterious, enter through the Mother”*—
this exit is not one’s original face,
this entry is not one’s original face either.
Only in this space of exit and entry lies the subtle truth,
which is **emptiness and responsiveness**.

This is the **True Yin** and **True Yang**,
manifesting as the **True One Qi**.

Is there anything beyond this as the root of Heaven and Earth?

Know that the **Valley Spirit** is the principle of the **Supreme Ultimate**;
the **Mysterious Mother** is the **Yin-Yang qi**.

In the **pre-heaven**, principle and qi were originally one;
in the **post-heaven**, principle and qi cannot be spoken of together.

Those who cultivate the Dao must seek this subtle orifice—
they must not be hasty in their intent,
nor negligent in their awareness.
Only by relying on the empty **Mysterious Mother**,
nurturing the **Valley Spirit** of emptiness and responsiveness,
neither clinging nor rejecting,
forgetting yet not assisting,
can it be attained.

Thus it is said:
*“Continuous as if it exists,
yet used without strain.”*

The **Great Dao** is formless, giving birth to Heaven and Earth.
The **Great Dao** is nameless, nourishing all things.
The Sage, though having substance, appears formless;
though real, appears empty—
revealing this most hidden, most subtle essence as the **Valley Spirit**.

The **Valley Spirit** is the spirit of the empty valley—
when questioned, it responds as if answering;
when called, it echoes as if replying—
this is immortality.

In the human body, it is the unified **emptiness and responsiveness** of true clarity.
Since humans lost their innate virtue, the **Valley Spirit** has long been submerged!

Later cultivators, seeking the **Valley Spirit** to remain eternal,
the **emptiness and responsiveness** to be unobscured,
as the foundation of the Golden Elixir and the root of the Dao,
must revolve in the void without grasping—
only by observing the **subtle orifice** through **non-desire and desire**,
when the **having and non-having** align,
when the **subtle orifice** fully opens,
does the **Mysterious Mother** stand revealed.

Thus it is said:
*“This orifice is no ordinary orifice,
Heaven and Earth together form it.
Called the **Divine Qi Hole**,
within it lies the **Kān-Lǐ essence**.”*

Only when **essence, qi, and spirit** are fused as one
does it become the **orifice of having and non-having**,
the **gate of life and death**;
otherwise, it remains an ordinary orifice,
with no **True One Qi** dwelling within—
if empty, it falls into coarse vacuity;
if solid, it is bound by form—
neither is the body of **emptiness and responsiveness**.

Only this **Mysterious Mother’s Gate**—
neither empty nor solid,
both empty and solid—
holds an ineffable truth:
still, it is formless;
moving, it takes shape.
Stillness is not the root of Heaven and Earth,
movement is not the origin of beings—
only at the juncture of stillness and movement
lies the **Kān-Lǐ inversion**,
the realm where **sun and moon intersect**—
truly the **Heavenly Root and Earthly Cavern**.

When the student attains the **True Mysterious** and **True Mother**,
each ascent and descent—
the qi within this space,
condensed as **nature**,
emanating as **emotion**—
from the **extreme emptiness and profound stillness**,
the **Law-forms** arise.

Knowing this orifice,
the Dao of immortality is exhausted here.

When it says *“Continuous as if it exists”*,
it means nourishment must be sustained long,
before the prenatal breath can stir.
When it says *“Used without strain”*,
it means extraction and supplementation must be timed,
so the **sympathetic fire** is neither recklessly added nor diminished.

If one follows the **natural Dao of Heaven and Earth**,
the Golden Elixir will be obtained.

---
**Chapter Seven: Heaven Endures, Earth Persists**

Heaven endures, Earth persists.
The reason Heaven and Earth can endure and persist
is that they do not **self-generate**—
thus they attain eternal life.

Therefore, the Sage places his body last,
yet his body is first;
sets his body aside,
yet his body is preserved.
Is this not because he is without selfishness?
Only because he is without selfishness
can he fulfill his selfishness.

The **qi** of Heaven and Earth flows boundlessly,
enduring countless eons without exhaustion,
all because the **True One Sovereign** silently governs within—
this is why Heaven and Earth endure without limit.

Even as they give birth and nourish all things,
endlessly sustaining life,
Heaven and Earth are never depleted—
this is why the **mechanism of qi** remains unbroken through the ages.

The **Supreme Sage** said, *“Heaven endures, Earth persists”*—
is this not true?

Yet why can Heaven and Earth endure and persist?
Because they do not **self-generate**.
If they had a **self-generating** intent,
Heaven and Earth would have emotions,
and Heaven too would age.

Only by not possessing their own life,
and instead taking all beings as their life,
does the unceasing generation of all beings
become the unceasing generation of Heaven and Earth—
thus, **eternal life**.

Most people are ignorant of this principle of generation:
they do not seek life but seek death,
do not seek eternal life but seek swift death.
They drown in wealth, honor, fame, and power;
they sink in sounds, colors, goods, and profits.
They constantly calculate, endlessly scheme—
in just a few years, their essence is exhausted,
their qi weakened,
their spirit and vitality scattered—
they toil through countless hardships,
yet barely enjoy one lifetime.
Pursuing life only to court death—
how can this be lamented enough!

Thus the Sage follows Heaven and models Earth:
he does not chase after the world’s gains,
nor does he greedily seek techniques for prolonging life.
Only the **Great Dao** comes first—
he clears his mind’s field,
refines his **command root**,
and in all matters of nourishing the body,
he never has time to scheme—
as if he places his body last,
yet his body is preserved.

In the end, his **virtue** is established,
and none surpass him among his kind;
his **name** is fulfilled,
and later generations still revere his model.

The Sage does not place his body last yet preserves it first,
nor does he neglect his body yet sustains it—
how far above the world’s selfish who destroy their own lives!
Yet the Sage is not one who feigns emotion or acts out of eccentricity;
from ancient times, he has loved simplicity and clarity,
maintaining purity of heart, never floating with the vulgar,
nor relying on form to establish himself—
unbounded and impartial, like Heaven and Earth’s boundless generosity.

Mortals are deeply self-absorbed, their hearts burdened with cares,
while the Sage holds no private attachments—
his luminous essence transcends all things.
A single expanse of empty clarity, a hollow yet radiant spirit,
constantly shining yet never dimmed.

If one were to obsess over this mortal coil—
this fleshly vessel, this stinking sack—
constantly fretting day and night,
how could one ever be first yet not last,
or endure yet perish?

Only through selflessness does one align with Heaven and Earth,
harmonize with the sun and moon,
and thus fulfill one’s true purpose.

Later cultivators who seek an immortal body and unbroken spirit,
though they refine with relentless austerity,
shaking off filth and enduring prolonged tempering,
naturally attain a realm beyond the mundane—
a world apart from the world’s rise and fall—
what greater joy is there?

Heaven and Earth speak not, yet their primal *qi* circulates endlessly,
so life’s cycle renews without ceasing.
Thus Heaven and Earth endure eternally, ever fresh through the ages.

The Sage harmonizes with Heaven and Earth,
nurturing the *qi* of Great Unity,
returning to the primal undifferentiated truth.

At rest, his virtue stands firm—
in action, his conduct is royal.
What of those who speak of self-cultivation yet only seek secluded mountains,
or of rulers who exhaust themselves in schemes?
These cling to extremes, each serving their own narrow ends—
some empty and detached, others frenzied and grasping.

The Sage in adversity remains pure and unstained,
his true form as enduring as mountains and rivers;
in prosperity, he benefits all beings,
his transient body and noble rank alike light as feathers.

When his name echoes through the cosmos,
his body transcends time—
his deeds cover the world,
his presence lingers beyond his age—
is this not the power of selflessness?

The scholar who discards all attachments,
empties all forms,
and forever severs worldly roots—
if his virtue accumulates, he abides in the mortal realm;
if he ascends, he endures in the celestial expanse.
To forsake self-interest yet attain longevity—
how vast the difference from those bound by worldly calculations!

Can one truly sever external temptations?

---
**Chapter Eight: The Supreme Virtue is Like Water**

The supreme virtue is like water.
Water benefits all things yet does not contend—
it dwells where others disdain,
and thus approaches the Dao.

It rests in the lowest places,
its heart is deep,
its words are trustworthy,
its governance is effective,
its actions are skillful,
its movements are timely.

Only because it does not contend
does it incur no blame.

The Great Dao has no other mystery—
only the unification of spirit and *qi*,
returning to the boundless *Tai Ji*,
the single point of primal *qi* from which parents sprang.

If one does not attend to the Dao,
spirit and *qi* separate—
lead scatters, mercury flees—
thus water and fire become divided.

Mencius said: *"Without water and fire, people cannot live."*
This saying, shallowly interpreted, refers to daily needs;
deeply understood, it is the key to cultivation.

Sometimes fire warms water and true *yang* manifests;
sometimes water nourishes fire and sweet dew arises.
The marvels of water and fire—
truly beyond words.

Yet water and fire share the same essence;
speak of water, and fire is known.

Water’s nature is yielding—
the Dao values humility.

Thus the Sage of supreme virtue,
heart serene and *qi* balanced,
with gentle intent,
allows all things to flourish naturally—
none escape his beneficence.

Ultimately, his deeds cover the world yet he knows no merit,
his actions fill the cosmos yet he claims no credit.
Only by following Heaven and Earth’s natural order,
fulfilling all things’ true nature,
does he remain unopposed—
truly like water, benefiting all without contention.

Not only this—
all things prefer clarity to turbidity,
elevation to lowliness.
Yet water cleanses others while dwelling in filth,
elevates others while humbling itself.
Oh water, water—
how perfectly you embody the Dao!

The Sage’s nature aligns with water’s—
gentle, not rigid,
humble in self-cultivation.
What others cannot endure,
the Sage accepts as natural;
what others most abhor,
the Sage treats as ordinary.
Thus he harbors no ill will toward himself,
nor contends with others.
Only the Sage of the Dao can do this.
Hence it is said:
*"Dwelling where others disdain—
this approaches the Dao."*

The Dao’s blessings upon oneself
are received in one’s proper place—
nowhere is it not advantageous.
Even in poverty or wealth,
gain or loss,
calamity or death—
what mortals cannot bear,
the Sage regards with equanimity.

The gentleman discusses principles, not *qi*;
speaks of nature, not fate—
only reflecting inward to cultivate virtue.

Yet virtue arises from a single source,
though its paths vary—
how could it drift without foundation,
boundless yet without end?
There must be a place of ultimate goodness,
where one rests without straying,
only then can one grow silently,
nurturing true nature daily,
while worldly desires fade.

The *Yijing* says:
*"His back is firm yet he does not grasp his body,
he walks in the courtyard yet sees no one"—
this is the Sage’s dwelling in the lowest place.

When dwelling is secure,
its resources are profound.
Gazing inward at the heart—
empty, containing nothing,
deep as the depths—
oh, how profound!

The Sage examines outward encounters,
choosing worthy companions—
truly, how profound his virtue!

When it comes to words,
a thousand gold pieces cannot match a single promise,
for *"speech must be true to virtue"* (i.e., *"faithful and good"*).

In governance,
great benevolence is as nothing compared to great virtue—
*"virtue governs well"* (i.e., *"governance is true to virtue"*).

Extending this to all things—
objects, deeds, actions, or stillness—
every aspect aligns with the Dao.

Assigning tasks to others,
he only hopes they do not fail in their abilities.

Considering actions with care,
he seeks only that they occur at the proper time.

Thus does the Sage cultivate himself and govern the world.

This arises from *"resting in ultimate goodness,"*
finding his place of security,
and then all his actions are measured and harmonious.

For in every moment and place,
there is no flaw, no deficiency—
what need is there for strife?

Only when one does not contend,
does humanity respond with gratitude and reverence,
engraved upon the bones and etched in the heart—
then one attains *"loyalty without ambition"* (i.e., *"true devotion without ulterior motives"*),
and what grievance could there be?
What blame could there be?

Even if there are wicked men,
they too are transformed into goodness.

When one reaches this pinnacle,
effortless and natural,
all are at ease in the *"unaware and unknown"* (i.e., *"the boundless, primordial state"*),
nearly forgetting the *"water-like goodness"* (i.e., *"the natural, unassuming virtue"*),
soft yet resolute, beneficent without fail.

Pointing to the heart of ultimate goodness—
ever ordinary, without preference or aversion,
boundless and without obstruction,
perfectly harmonious and yielding—

Thus, in high station he is not arrogant,
in lowly place he does not falter;
toward himself he finds no fault,
toward others he bears no grudge.

Yet how does one attain this state?
It is not through the learning of the vulgar world,
nor by the hollow studies of those without foundation.

Hence, the highest Sage dwells
behind what the crowd abhors,
then reveals a place of goodness.

What is this place?
It is the *"inner depths of the heart"*—
an inch of sincerity is an inch of ground.

To find this place is to have a foundation for life and vitality;
to lose it is to be without master of spirit and *qi*.

Without mastery, there is confusion—
how could one harmonize all things,
uniting inner and outer,
acting in self and others without strife?

Yet if one says this place *exists*,
one risks falling into rigid attachment;
if one says it *does not exist*,
one stumbles into dull emptiness.

This is the *"neither existence nor nonexistence"* (i.e., *"the transcendent, ungraspable state"*),
unbound by action or stillness.

To cultivate the ultimate Dao,
one must carefully ponder its essence—
accumulate abundant *yin* merit,
nurture wide-ranging virtue,
and thus ascend toward divine harmony,
either through a teacher’s guidance,
or through sudden enlightenment,
to attain this place,
lest one stray into false paths and left-handed doctrines,
and fulfill one’s lifelong aspirations.

This place understood,
the Dao is half attained.

What follows—words of *"heart"* and *"speech"*—
illuminates that in every moment and place,
one must examine and align with ultimate goodness,
lest one dwell without first securing it.

All that is said thereafter,
nothing can surpass goodness—
this is the true essence of learning,
the foundation of practice.
If one neglects this,
holding oneself and engaging with others,
all matters will crumble.

I have briefly revealed this,
hoping those in the world with resolve
will not rely on their talents and intellect,
arrogantly guessing and presuming,
while failing to cultivate virtue and turn back the heavens;
only with a humble heart may one seek the Dao.

**Chapter Nine: Accomplishment and Withdrawal**

*"Filled and overflowing, it is not as good as stopping in time."*
*"Sharpened and kept keen, it cannot be long preserved."*
*"Gold and jade fill the halls, yet none can guard them."*
*"Rich and noble yet proud, one invites calamity upon oneself."*

When accomplishment is achieved and name secured,
one must withdraw—
the Dao of Heaven.

The ancients said:
*"To cross the river, use a raft;
having reached the shore, no need for the boat."*

Again they said:
*"Before attaining merit, one must study the Way;
when merit is attained, one must forget the Way."*

These few words are indeed the most vital for cultivating the Dao.

If one practices the Dao and fulfills its work,
having already entered the realm of profound and vast attainment,
yet still clings and boasts—
*"My learning is refined, my cultivation pure"*—
one cannot avoid the rise of arrogance and agitation,
either falling into withdrawal or contradiction.

If such is the case,
how could the Dao endure?
How could virtue exist?

Thus the **Tàishàng** says:
*"Hold and fill it to the brim—
it is better to stop before that.
Sharpen it to a point—
it cannot be long preserved."*

When the practitioner’s refinement is yet insufficient,
one must undergo countless purifications and selections,
washing out a single point of utmost purity
to serve as the foundation for longevity.

If one obtains **True Yang**,
boiling it at dawn and refining it at dusk,
the **preheaven essence** fills the body inside and out—
then the body stands firm as a thousand-foot wall,
the mind serene as autumn moon over still water.

When the **external kidney** shrinks like that of a child,
the state of **leak-proof, all-pervasive attainment** is achieved.

At this time, when essence is full within the body,
one must not further apply the **fire talisman**—
instead, one should cease all fire-working,
and in **non-knowing, non-awareness**,
become **muddle-headed and indistinct**,
completely forgetting the state of fullness as attainment.

If one persists in holding to fullness,
one risks the peril of **elixir collapse and cauldron overturn**—
better to stop early than to press on.

When **qi** is yet insufficient,
one must undergo countless firings and refinings,
stirring the **civilized and martial divine fires**,
to forge the **preheaven one-element true qi** as the basis for longevity.
When all **profane qi** is exhausted,
it transforms into a single piece of **pure yang**—
most vast, most potent—
penetrating the body’s sinews and bones,
nimble as a dragon, fierce as a tiger.
What sharper refinement could there be?

I must **concentrate qi to attain softness**,
as a babe in its **innocent, unknowing** state—
only then can one long preserve the qi,
reaching **perfection of form and spirit**, merging with the Dao and attaining **authentic truth**.

If one persists in sharpening without cease,
one risks the danger of **wildfire consuming all**—
how could such a state endure?

This is like **gold and jade filling a hall, yet none able to guard them**—
or a wealthy noble family,
indulging in extravagance, trampling morality,
proud and domineering—
like the **Luan clan’s extinction** or the **Fan family’s ruin**,
all because they failed to curb their excess,
acting lawlessly until they invited their own ruin.

Thus, observing human affairs,
the **Heavenly Way** becomes clear.

See how in spring it is warm,
yet by summer the **hot sun rules supreme**—
no longer temperate.
By autumn it grows cool,
and in winter the **cold dominates**—
no trace of the western wind remains.

When things are nurtured to completion,
their season passes—
Heaven and Earth withdraw and conceal,
preserving the **harmonious virtue of yang**.
If winter were both cold and warm,
or summer both hot and cool,
this would be **Heaven’s Way in reverse**—
seasons out of order,
completion without withdrawal—
just the **perverse qi** that brings great harm to humankind.

Thus it is said:
*"When accomplishment is achieved and name secured,
one must withdraw—
the Dao of Heaven."*

If Heaven itself is thus,
how much more so for humankind?

In history, wise ministers of great achievement—
their deeds resplendent, names renowned—
yet failing to retreat into the mountains,
like **Wenzhong of Yue** or **Han Xin of Han**,
met with disaster—
many perishing, their families ruined.

Thus, those who study the Dao,
when their essence is refined and qi is full,
must **forget the method, forget the form**,
lest they ruin their own Way.

But those who have not yet reached this state—
how could they abandon method or form?

This teaching instructs those who cultivate the Great Dao:
**drop one link, advance one link**—
never self-satisfied, lest **slothful and arrogant hearts arise**,
and disaster strike before one turns.

Even without the peril of **leakage**,
one risks **halfway abandonment**,
unable to cross to the other shore
and enter the realm of **spirit-transformation**.

The *Awakening to Truth* says:
*"Before refining the elixir, refine swiftly;
after refining, know when to stop.
If one clings to fullness without end,
one risks sudden ruin and disgrace."*

It is clear that the Dao has no end,
and achievement no limit—
what use is **pretended fullness**?

Many ancient cultivators met with death or ruin,
all because they failed to **conceal their light and nurture obscurity**,
to **blend with the vulgar and follow the dust**.

The **Alchemical Canon** states:
*"Cultivate while blending with the vulgar,
harmonize while keeping light hidden.
Round when round, square when square—
hidden or revealed, following others unrecognized,
teaching people how to see **retreat and concealment**."*

Thus, the **true adept** must hide deeply,
adjusting with the times—
only then can one **not differ, not oppose**,
**neither good nor evil**,
long preserving one’s life.

Otherwise, **virtue cultivated invites slander**,
**high Dao invites calumny**—
though this may stem from others’ malice,
it is also self-inflicted by **excessive display**.

The *Book of Changes* says:
*"Hoard lazily and invite theft;
adorn oneself and invite licentiousness."*
Truly, self-inflicted.

Why should those who are self-satisfied expect otherwise?

I wish later learners:
Before advancing, follow the method with discipline;
when deeply attained, cease all fire-working—
only then may one avoid the peril of **self-immolation**.

---
**Chapter Ten: Concentrating Qi to Attain Softness**

*"Carrying the vital essence and embracing unity—
can one avoid separation?
Concentrating qi to attain softness—
can one resemble an infant?
Purifying and illuminating the mysterious—
can one avoid blemish?
Loving the state, governing the people—
can one act without action?
Heaven’s Gate opening and closing—
can one avoid passivity?
Bright and all-pervading—
can one avoid knowing?
Nurturing and preserving—
nurturing without possession,
acting without reliance,
nourishing without domination—
this is called **mysterious virtue**."*

This chapter begins by speaking of the **method of refining essence into qi**.
Having obtained essence and qi within the body,
one must **unify heart and virtue**,
preventing even momentary separation—
for separation scatters the **Three Treasures** of essence, qi, and spirit,
preventing their reunion at the **ultimate extreme**,
which serves as the foundation for elixir-refining.

Thus the **Tàishàng** says:
*"Carrying the vital essence and embracing unity—
can one avoid separation?"*

**Yíng** is blood.
Blood is generated by the heart, **pò** stored by the heart—
its light must illuminate the **elixir field**,
the heart unmoving,
so that the **soul** may settle into the **spirit**,
and the **moon** returns as **pure yin**.
This occurs when **heart-yang enters kidney-yin**,
the **divine fire** illuminating the **blood-water**—
though water is cold and metal frigid,
the **divine fire** boils and refines it,
and it rises spontaneously,
uncontrollably effervescent.

「The **Perfect Adept** understands this **mysterious mother** as the root of heaven and earth. Thus, within each breath—one inhalation, one exhalation—when the subtle **yang** stirs, he fixes his gaze with one eye, grasps it with one hand, and directs a drop of his own **mercury** to meet it. With left rotation and right withdrawal, he draws it back to the **middle elixir field**, congealing it so that it does not scatter. Then, he carries the **soul** and returns, embraces **unity** and dwells within it, without pause. In this way, the **true yang** flourishes, and the **true qi** moves powerfully.

From this, he operates the **River Vehicle**, igniting fire from the **empty perilous acupoint** and drawing it to the **tail end**. He strikes the **Ninefold Iron Drum**, mobilizing the **Three-Legged Golden Toad**, ascending it to the crown—all must be done with **one heart** focused, unerring and unceasing—until it reaches the **mud pellet**, where the **Muni’s treasure pearl** is obtained. If he does not then **nurture and temper** it for a moment, the **mud pellet’s yin essence** will not transform, and how could **lead and mercury** fuse harmoniously? How could it become **sweet dew divine water** to moisten the hundred **vessels** of the body?

Once the **mud pellet** is nurtured, he further guides it downward through the **lower ninefold palace** and into the **crimson palace**, which is the **retreat of yin at noon**. Yet when advancing the fire, he must take its **rigidity**, not the **virtue/power** of **pure health**—otherwise, the **true gold** cannot ascend. At the time of **yin’s retreat**, he must take its **gentleness**, not the **virtue/power** of compliance—otherwise, the **yang lead** remains scattered, unable to subdue the **mercury** into **elixir**. Hence it is said:
*"Can one focus **qi** to achieve **gentleness** like a newborn infant?"*

Its meaning instructs that after **yin’s birth**, one must maintain a **one-hearted** clarity, allowing the **vital mechanisms** to descend naturally, as they are, as they are, without adding a single intention or exerting a single force. This is the **gentleness** of the **Kūn hexagram**, the **Way** of the **Noble Person**’s cultivation. When descending to the **palace** for nurturing, returning to the **earthen cauldron**, and sealing it firmly, one must only dwell in **serenity and tranquility**, blending and calming it. In a mere instant, the **qi** breathes as if nonexistent, and the **spirit’s mechanisms** seem extinguished—this is the attainment of **gentleness**.

After nurturing for a while, the **spirit** and **qi** return to their roots, like a **fire seed** in the furnace, slowly congealing and refining. If one does not let it wander recklessly, the **true qi** will naturally flow and circulate throughout the body. With **one heart** at peace, the limbs become supple, as if the body of an infant—soft as cotton, delicate as silk—exhibiting a **gentleness** that cannot bear the weight of things. This is sufficient evidence that the **elixir** is congealing.

From this point, **lead and mercury** merge, and **water and fire** are harmonized. One must then **purify the heart** and **cleanse the mind**, cultivating the **true lead** in solitude. If the mind wanders even slightly, the **elixir** will scatter wildly—it cannot be refined, nor will the **lead** from the **water realm** belong to you. The *Scripture of Purity* states:
*"The heart without its heart, things without their things.
The void lacks nothing to void, the none lacks nothing to none—
serene and ever still,
naturally tranquil."*

And what flaw could there be?

Hence it is said:
*"Can one purify and perceive the **mysterious** without flaw?"*

Even if the **external elixir** is obtained, if the **internal illumination** is not strict, then **human desires** remain uncleansed and **heavenly principle** impure. How could one attain a single **grain of barley**—**void** yet forming the **embryo**?

When the **elixir** is present in the body, one must still **preserve essence** and **abundantly nourish qi**, to form the **sage’s womb**. Yet even so, **preserving essence** must follow **nature’s spontaneity**, and **nourishing qi** must align with **effortless ease**. This **non-preservation** surpasses preservation, and this **non-nourishment** surpasses nourishment. Otherwise, if one clings to **treasure** and **value**, harm is inevitable.

The **elixir** is the **primordial essence** of the **first heaven**, formless and without trace—why must it be made? If one seeks it through **manifestation**, one risks **stirring fire** in the **later heaven**, and the **Great Dao** of the **first heaven** will be lost. Hence it is said:
*"Can one govern the people and rule the land without action?"*
The people are likened to **essence**, the land to **qi**. The key to governing lies in **love** for the people. To establish laws and rule the land is but a minor relief for the **tyrant**—far from the **sage’s** effortless governance.

The **auspicious and harmonious** reigns, the **vital mechanisms** in contention—all lies between **action** and **non-action**. The **Way** of cultivating the body is to **stabilize essence** as the people’s security and **abundant qi** as the land’s prosperity. Refining oneself **stabilizes essence**, while **direct nourishment** fills **qi**. At its utmost, the **vast and mighty** **yang** overflows the two realms—yet even then, it is seen as an ordinary possession, a mundane practice. One does not boast of **capabilities**, nor does one flaunt **wisdom**. In **primordial unity**, one forgets to forget what is **full and complete**—**non-action** yet **great action** emerges.

When the **student** reaches this stage—**essence overflowing**, **qi abundant**—if he nurtures it long enough, the **crown will naturally split open**, and he may ride the clouds, roam the islands, far surpassing those who formerly **strictly adhered** to **circular breathing** and **focused qi to achieve gentleness**. Hence it is said:
*"Can one open and close the **heavenly gate** without **femininity**?"*
This refers to the former days of **adjusting spirit** and **nurturing the womb**—one could not avoid **femininity**. But now, with the **yang spirit** fully robust, **detached from the mundane body**, it bursts through the **heavenly gate**, ascending toward the **celestial expanse**—truly a joy!

How could **qi** be so robust? At this point, the **heart** is like a **clear mirror**, the **nature** as **still water**—**clear as the sky**, **lively as the earth**. All **perceptions and cognitions** of the **spirit of knowledge** dissolve into the **void**, transforming into the **primordial spirit**. Formerly, one knew what came before and was aware of what followed; now, **illuminated and transparent**, one is **omniscient yet unobstructed**.

Yet **ever-serene** and **ever-illuminated**, there is no **heart of serenity or illumination**; **ever-clear** and **ever-aware**, there is no **thought of clarity or perception**. It is as if all things come and are **reflected**—like a **high-suspended mirror**, nothing can hide. And all this is rooted in **non-action**, with **action** as its application.

When **yang** has not yet arisen, one **accumulates essence** and **nurtures qi** to generate it. Once **yang** has arisen, one **treasures essence** and **abundantly nourishes qi** to store it. When later, one **remains in form** and **transforms the world**, having **accumulated merit** and **amassed virtue**, even in birth, one does not boast of **nurturing**—even in action, one does not rely on **clinging to gentleness**—even in longevity, one does not depend on **forced subjugation**.»

「The **heart** of one **kalpa**—the **heart** of ten thousand **kalpas**—truly may be called the **Celestial Sovereign**, wielding the authority to govern **creation and transformation**. Hence, it is termed **"Mysterious Virtue/Power."**

Here, the nine stages of **foundation-building to obtain the elixir**, **refining the self to return the elixir**, and **shedding the mortal coil to attain the Pearl** are expounded—yet all boil down to **ultimate emptiness and profound stillness**, **embracing the Three and holding the One**, **vague and indistinct, dim and obscure** as the essence.
