From **self-cultivation within** to **returning the elixir**, one cannot depart from **blending the manifest with the unmanifest**, **transforming action into non-action**.

For **Primordial One-Element True Qi** dwells hidden within **void and nothingness**, beyond the realm of sight and sound. If one **extinguishes awareness** and **abandons action**, this **One-Element True Qi** remains ever-present.

Thus, the **Most High** declares:
*"Faint and indistinct, yet within it lies form;
Indistinct and faint, yet within it lies substance;
Dim and obscure, yet within it lies essence."*
This reveals that **life gives birth to Heaven and Earth**—originally **chaotic and formless**, beyond comparison. Only by **unifying the spirit-mind** and **obliterating perception** does the **Dao** reside therein.

Should one awaken to **Great Clarity and Awareness**, then **post-heavenal consciousness** arises in response to thoughts—no longer the **Primordial Original Spirit**. Thus, one must seek in **vagueness and indistinctness**, attain in **dimness and obscurity**. Let the cultivator know whence to begin.

---
**Chapter Eleven: Non-Action as Function**

Thirty spokes converge at a single hub—when the **hub is empty**, the **function of the cart** arises.
Clay is molded into vessels—when the **center is hollow**, the **function of the vessel** arises.
Doors and windows are carved for a room—when the **space is empty**, the **function of the room** arises.
Thus, **having** is for **utility**, while **emptiness** is for **function**.

The **Dao** originates at the **Primordial Chaos**, merging within **void and nothingness**—invisible, inaudible. How then does the cultivator proceed?

The Sage knows the **substance of the Dao** is formless, yet its **function** manifests in forms. Thus, **manifesting the unmanifest**, **embodying the empty**, they **plunder qi from the Chaos** and **withdraw the spirit from the dim**, to complete the **Great Dao of True Unity** and eternally become **immortal and transcendent**.

What is called **"substantial and manifest"**? The **True Yin and True Yang**, beings of the same kind.
What is called **"empty and unmanifest"**? The **Primordial Dao’s root**, the **Dragon-Tiger Eight-Trigrams’ initial tension qi**.

Where there is **qi without substance**, the **Dao is manifest**.
Thus it is said: *"When Yin and Yang unite, Primordial Qi appears; when Yin and Yang separate, Post-Heavenal form is complete."*
The *Classic of Change* states:
*"Above form, it is called the Dao; below form, it is called Qi."*
It is not that there is no vessel without the Dao, nor is it that there is no Dao without a vessel to bear it.

The **Most High** illustrates with the cart:
*A cart has spokes and a hub—thirty spokes symbolize the movement of sun and moon, while the hub sits centrally, pierced by all axles. The hub is hollow within, the spokes converge without—thus it turns without hindrance. Were it not for this inner void, how could one be carried? How could things be stored?*
Hence, *"When the hub is empty, the cart’s function arises."*

Again, like pottery:
*Mixing water with clay, kneading into vessels—once fired, the exterior is solid, the interior hollow. Yet in all human utility, the value lies not in the clay’s substance but in the hollow’s emptiness. As with Tao Qian transporting bricks, were there not this empty space, how could they move freely?*
Hence, *"When the center is empty, the vessel’s function arises."*

Similarly, in building a room:
*One must carve doors and windows—only then does light radiate fully. Yet upon entering, the doors and windows seem unnecessary; it is the empty space that allows one to sit comfortably, though the room itself is formed. Hence, *"When the space is empty, the room’s function arises."*

From these three examples, **emptiness** manifests as **form** for the **cart, vessel, and room**; **emptiness** manifests as **void** for **carrying, storing, and dwelling**. Yet what pertains to **manifest forms** is the **utility of extension**; what pertains to **unmanifest void** is the **function of extension**.

Thus it is said: *"Having is for utility; emptiness is for function. Having and lacking, lacking and having—each is the root of the other."*

In essence, the **Dao** originates from **void and nothingness**, yet without **Yin and Yang**, it cannot be seen. **Qi** belongs to **Yin and Yang**, yet without the **Dao**, it cannot arise. **Yin and Yang** emerge after **Heaven and Earth**, with defined shapes and locations—**they cannot be the elixir of longevity**. Only by seeking the **Dao** within **Yin and Yang**, returning through **Yin and Yang** to the **Primordial Pole**, can **before and after merge**, and the **Great Dao** be attained.

Later **elixir cultivators**, serving only **manifest qi**, fail to refine the **unmanifest elixir**. To seek transcendence while doing so—is this not like driving a cart southward while heading north?

The **Dao** has no name; it is **named Dao** by force.
The **Dao** requires no cultivation; it is **called cultivation** by force.
For the **Dao** as a thing—**ultimately empty, ultimately nothing**—only then can it be **ultimately divine, ultimately sage**.

Observe how **Heaven and Earth’s qi** is clear and void, containing nothing. Yet when suppressed for long, the **qi-machine stirs**, and **Yin and Yang** arise. Then **forms and colors** emerge, **patterned and vivid**, filling the four quarters. Who created them? Who gave them birth?

The **Dao** gives rise to **One Qi**, and **One Qi** transforms into **Yin and Yang**, from which all things are born.
Thus it is said:
*"The **Dao** from **emptiness and nothingness** generates **One Qi**,
Then from **One Qi** **Yin and Yang** arise.
**Yin and Yang** thus form the **Three Families**,
And the **Three Families** give birth to myriad transformations, flourishing evermore."*

Those who cultivate seek the **Dao’s true essence** to attain the **body of the Transcendent Sage**. They must first take **Yin and Yang** as their **instrument**, then **emptiness and nothingness** as their **root**. Only then will the **Great Dao** be attained.

Within this chapter, the **three "Wu" (無) characters**—they speak of **emptiness** where there is **emptiness**. Roughly, it means: the cultivator, from **nothingness** to **somethingness**, from **somethingness** back to **nothingness**, until **pure and void One Qi** is achieved, and only then does the **Great Dao** take form. Its meaning is surely drawn from this.

---
**Chapter Twelve: Rejecting the Extraneous, Taking the Essential**

The **five colors** blind the eyes;
The **five tones** deafen the ears;
The **five flavors** dull the palate;
**Hunting and galloping** drive the mind to madness;
**Rare treasures** obstruct one’s conduct.

Thus the **Sage** nourishes the **abdomen**, not the eyes—
Hence, **reject the extraneous, take the essential**.

The world’s restless pursuit—
Chasing after **sounds, colors, wealth, and gain**,
Gazing far and wide,
Delighting the eyes, pleasing the mouth—
Is this not because the **true joys within** are truly delightful?

Who knows that the **pleasures of the world** are limited,
While the **pleasures of the heart** are boundless?

Moreover, where **pleasure** is, **worry** is also.
What benefits the **body** harms the **mind**.

Take **five-colored patterns**—
Their hues are radiant, their designs dazzling.
Who does not **see them and rejoice**, **behold them and be astonished**?
Yet the moment the eyes fix upon them,
The **spirit grows dizzy**.

Human vitality—how much can there be?
To linger on sights, indulge the senses,
And call it **beauty**—
Whoever does so will find their **qi obstructed**, their **spirit wasted**, their **mind in turmoil**, and their **eyes blinded in return**.

Thus it is said: *"The **five colors blind the eyes**"*—
A truth most profound.

As for the **melodies of silk and bamboo**, the **sounds of pipes and flutes**,
Even the **Ancients** did not abandon them.
Yet the **foolish and debauched**,
Indulging in **female musicians and singing boys**,
Exhaust their **spirit** for fleeting pleasure—
Before the music ends, the **clanging still resounds**,
Yet the **inner essence of the ears** has already dissipated outward,
And the **ears grow deaf in return**.

Thus it is said: *"The **five tones deafen the ears**."*

As for the **mouth’s taste**—
Balanced **sweet and fatty**, **thick and thin**—
Even the **Sage** requires them.
But the **gluttonous**,
Greedy for the **mouth’s desires**, craving **rich flavors**,
Fill their tables with **delicacies**, their kitchens with **exotic meats**,
Spending **thousands in gold** for a single meal without hesitation.
Do they not know that what **pleases the mouth** harms the **mind**?

Moreover, the **heart contains boundless supreme flavors**—
Without **fattening meats**, yet **sweet**;
Without **rich dishes**, yet **satisfying**.
Those who merely **stuff themselves** seek only to **gratify the mouth**.

Thus it is said: *"The **five flavors dull the palate**."*

As for **hunting**—
The **Ancient Emperors** did so to **protect the people**, **eliminate harm**, and **encourage farming**.
Later generations, however, **chase beasts and birds**,
**Hunt and gallop**, making **hounds their sole pursuit**,
Burning fields, ravaging mountains,
Even slaughtering **innocent snakes, scorpions, and insects** without mercy.
Some **indulge in cruelty**, taking life as their livelihood,
Unmoved by guilt.
In the end, **Heaven’s retribution is swift**—
A moment’s recklessness brings **calamity**.

Moreover, when **galloping and hunting**, one **releases their violent nature**—
Their **body rages**, their **mind rages**.
Thus the **Highest Sage** warns: *"Hunting and galloping drive the mind to madness."*

Furthermore, **rare and strange treasures**—
The **Sage** does not **covet them with the eyes**,
Thus **refining customs** and **embellishing virtue**,
So all may live in **peace under Heaven**.
Later generations, however, **value the strange and bizarre**,
Devoting **half their lifeforce** to **acquiring wealth**,
Yet what they **covet, others covet too**—
This is how the **wind of plunder** grows ever fiercer.

The **Ancients said**:
*"An ordinary man bears no guilt,
Yet his **hidden greed** is his crime."*
Thus it is known:
**The deeper the hoarding, the greater the calamity**—
A truth not false.

Even if one **strengthens defenses** and **guards carefully**,
If **Heaven’s will** does not favor them,
**Destruction is inevitable**.

Life’s **essence and spirit** are precious—
Once **soul and body scatter**, where are the **treasures**?
Why not **prioritize the inner**, **discard the outer**?

Thus the **Highest Sage** warns:
*"Rare treasures obstruct conduct"*—
Urging the world with solemn care.

Therefore, the **high-minded Daoist**,
**Empties the mind** to **nourish the nature**,
**Fills the belly** to **establish life**,
Knowing that **Prenatal One Qi**—
When born, **flows in**; when dead, **flows out**—
Is the **unbreakable treasure of the body**.
With **one heart devoted** to this,
All external things become **like floating clouds**—
Thus **pure and enlightened**, unobstructed,
As if **receiving offerings from mortals**,
Or **ruling as Celestial Sovereign**—
Even now, their **ears and eyes** remain clear.

Ask: Do **Shun’s zither**, **Zhao’s jade**, **Qi’s lute**, or **Lu’s dwelling** still exist?
Long since **lost to obscurity**!

Thus it is known:
**Things are finite**, yet the **Dao is infinite**;
**Man is limited**, yet the **Dao is boundless**.
To seek **longevity**, one must **set aside the finite**,
Cultivate the **formless**—
Only then will they know what to **prioritize and discard**.

Alas—
Only those with **great insight and vision** can **reject the extraneous and take the essential**.

The **principle of self-cultivation** fundamentally opposes the **world’s ways**—
Know that what the **world desires**, the **Daoist abhors**.

The **world’s coveted** things are the **Daoist’s discarded**.
For **sound and color, wealth and gain**—
Though alluring and beneficial to the **physical form**,
They ultimately **profit not the heart**;
And when the **heart grows greedy**,
The **physical form itself falls into disharmony**.

Only the **cultivation of nature and life**—
Seemingly **formless and insubstantial**,
Not deemed worthy of the **body’s refinement**—
If one can **reject external temptations**,
**Fulfill their innate essence**,
**Focus wholeheartedly on inner refinement**,
**Seek nothing beyond**,
Then ultimately, **achievement and virtue are complete**,
And the **Dao of longevity** lies therein.
What treasure in the **world surpasses this**?

Yet I fear:
**Resolutions may falter**,
**Progress on the Dao may lack courage**,
**Desires and principles intertwined**,
**Heaven and man in constant conflict**,
Thus making it **difficult to attain perfection**.

I hope future scholars will first **quiet distractions and preserve sincerity**,
Then **refine lead and subdue mercury**,
And when they reach **returning to their original nature**,
**Embracing simplicity and returning to truth**,
What difficulty will there be in **joining the ranks of immortals**?

Thus, the **Sage cultivates the inner, not the outer**,
**Nourishes the belly, not the eyes**—
**Rejecting one and preserving the other**,
**Concentrating their will and focusing their spirit**,
**Standing firm in nature and establishing life**,
Is this not **far superior to worldly glory and wealth** by ten thousandfold?

**Chapter Thirteen: Honor and Humiliation as if Startled**

Honor and humiliation **as if startled**,
Treating great calamities **as one’s own body**.

What is meant by **"honor and humiliation as if startled"**?
Honor is **lowly**—
To **gain it** is **as if startled**,
To **lose it** is **as if startled**—
This is called **"honor and humiliation as if startled"**.

What is meant by **"treating great calamities as one’s own body"**?
My **great calamity** arises from **my having a body**—
When I **have no body**,
What **great calamity** shall I face?
Thus, **valuing the body as the world’s foundation**,
Is **as if one can entrust it to the world below**;
**Loving the body as the world’s foundation**,
Is **as if one can commit the world to it**.

Mencius said:
*"What must one guard? Guarding the body is paramount."*
The *Book of Songs* states:
*"Both bright and wise, to preserve one’s own body."*

How **heavily did the ancients weigh the body**?

This **body** sustains not only **one’s own nature and life**,
But within a **single household**,
None do not **rely on it for survival**.
Extending this further—
To **establish the heart for Heaven and Earth**,
To **grant life to all things**,
To **carry on the teachings of past sages**,
To **bring great peace to future generations**—
All is **governed by this body**.

Though this **body governs the cosmos**,
Only the **Dao masters it**.
The **Dao cannot stand alone in emptiness**,
It **must depend on a person to uphold it**.
Thus it is said:
*"If the body exists, the Dao exists; if the body perishes, the Dao perishes."*

The **great practitioner**, before the **Dao is fully realized**,
**Retreats from dust and worldly affairs**,
**Follows the paths of mountain and forest**,
**Conceals their light and nurtures obscurity**,
**Finds joy in the Dao and accepts poverty**,
**Hears no human voices**,
**Speaks not of worldly matters**,
**Treads not the red dust of the world**—
Is this merely to **avoid misfortune and preserve the body**?
It is also to **settle the body and establish life**.

When the **Perfect One** encounters **worldly honor and glory**,
Or **shame and disgrace**,
They regard them **as ordinary occurrences**,
**Not worth the slightest concern**.

Though **without cause**, arrows and banners descend upon them,
**Tributes and invitations arrive**,
**Emperors and ministers bestow extraordinary favors**,
And **humble dwellings shine with borrowed splendor**—
Those who **revel in wild delight**,
They alone **remain indifferent with increasing calm**.

Should **misfortune befall them**,
**Reputation and calamity** arriving swiftly,
**Slanderous mouths of the wicked** spreading,
**Families unjustly accused**—
They alone **do not shift blame to others**,
But **accept it upon themselves**.

The **ancient sages**, in **honor untainted by corruption**,
In **humiliation unbroken by despair**,
Had **good reason**.
All understood the **Dao of preserving the body**—
They did not **sustain the body with fame and wealth**,
But **refined their nature with benevolence and righteousness**,
Thus achieving **an immortal form unspoiled by time**,
**Reliable for all ages past and present**.

Should one **cling to the body**,
**Selfish and self-important**,
**Striving with others for name and gain**,
**Plotting for food and clothing**,
**Chasing worldly pursuits in frenzy**,
**Distracted and chaotic without rest**,
**Grappling and seizing without end**—
Then **misfortune will follow swiftly**.

Thus, the **gentleman values** **concealing their talents to await their time**,
**Settling the body to uphold virtue**.

The **Supreme Sage** sees that if people **cannot accept honor with reverence**,
**Preventing calamity in its unseen form**,
That is why there is the **lament**:
*"Honor and humiliation as if startled,
Treating great calamities as one’s own body."*

What is meant by **"honor as if startled"**?
Honor is **the glory of later attainment**,
Not the **inherent nobility of origin**—
Thus it is said: **"Honor is lowly."**

Yet **common human emotions** cling to **gain and loss**,
Thus to **gain it is as if startled**,
To **lose it is as if startled**—
This is **"honor and humiliation as if startled."**

What is meant by **"treating great calamities as one’s own body"**?
It means that **people suffer calamity because they have a body**.
If **there were no body**,
From where would **calamity arise**?

While the **Dao remains incomplete**,
One **cannot avoid retaining the body** as a **tool for cultivation**.
Once **the shell is shed and ascent is achieved**,
**Spirit exists yet qi is gone**—
What **misfortune** can befall them?

Though **they retain form and dwell in the world**,
**All attachments are extinguished**,
**One true essence is contained within**—
Even **roaming the four realms**,
They **come and go as they please**,
And what **great calamities** shall they face?

The **world’s cultivators**, who seek to **become gods of a thousand years**,
To **be the hope of a thousand people**,
To **achieve extraordinary deeds**,
To **establish eternal merit**—
Must **speak and act with utmost restraint**,
**Treating the body as precious**—
Only then can they **be entrusted with the world’s destiny**;
**Moving or resting with utmost care**,
**Never daring to be negligent**—
Only then can they **be loved by the world**,
And **rely on it for support**.

Like **Shen of the fields**, who **plowed for years in obscurity**,
Yet when **three marriage proposals came**,
He **expressed his sincerity**,
And **took responsibility for the people** as if **he were Yao or Shun**;
Or **like Rong of Nanyang**, who **lay high in seclusion**,
Yet when **invited repeatedly with gifts**,
He **stood tall as if he could divide the world into three**—
The **entrusting of a six-foot infant**,
The **committing of a hundred-li domain**—
Who but **such a person** could **fulfill this duty**?

「He who **hoards his own self** yet **stands aloof and distant**,
Does not **think to use the Dao/Way to benefit the world**,
Nor **lead all beneath the heavens to wander in the Great Dao/Way**—
The gap between them is vast indeed.

This speaks of a person whose **body inherently holds** great worth,
**Not needing external acquisition**,
Whose **virtue/power** surpasses even that of **worldly rank and honor**.
If one **strives to cultivate this** with unflagging effort—
As the ancients said, **"Toil for two or three years, and joy for ten thousand"**—
Truly, it is not false.
What **fears of favor or disgrace**, what **lamentations of affliction**,
Could ever **disturb such a person**?

When the **Dao/Way of scholars** is not yet attained,
They must **rely on this body** for **cultivation and refinement**.
If they merely **cling to clothing, food, wealth, and honor** as the **essentials for nurturing the body**,
Then **this mortal frame grows heavy**,
And the **innate, primordial True Body** will **surely suffer harm** from it.

Once the **innate, primordial True Body** is **damaged**,
Even the **later-acquired mortal frame** will **find it hard to endure long**.

This is how **ordinary people love their bodies** yet **ultimately lose them**.

Only the **Perfect Sage** understands that **all worldly affairs** are but **manifestations of illusion**,
With **birth and death**, **unable to be taken as true**.
Only **my innate, primordial True Qi** is the **source of my living body**,
**Enduring for one age**, **for a hundred ages**,
**For ten thousand years**.

If **this True Qi is not cultivated**,
How could the **mortal frame** ever **come to be**?
This is the **inner essence of a human body**—
**Preserve it, and life continues**; **lose it, and death ensues**.
**Scatter it, and it becomes matter**; **concentrate it, and it becomes an immortal**.
It must **never be separated**, not even for an instant.

The **Supreme Sage** teaches:
**"To act with utmost restraint and caution**,
**Never daring to neglect even the smallest matter**,
**Never wavering in the slightest thought**,
**Even less to vie with others in strength or victory**—
Only **serene contentment**, **natural ease**,
**Purity without dust or defilement**,
**To align oneself with one’s True Nature**."

Though **not yet emerging to benefit the people**,
The **multitudes of common folk** have **long since rejoiced in safety within his heart**.
How else could the **people survive** without such a one?
The **people’s longing and reverence** for him run **deep and sincere**.

**"To take no joy in one’s own joy**,
But **to take joy in the world’s joy**—
**To bear no sorrow in one’s own sorrow**,
But **to bear the world’s sorrow**—
What **entrustment and reliance** could be greater than this?**

**Chapter Fourteen: The Formless Form**
**Seeing it, yet unseen**—called **Yí (夷)**;
**Hearing it, yet unheard**—called **Xī (希)**;
**Seizing it, yet unattained**—called **Wēi (微)**.
These three cannot be **grasped or questioned**,
So they **merge into one**.

Its **uppermost reaches** are **not bright**,
Its **lowest depths** are **not obscure**—
**A continuous thread**, **unnamable**,
Yet **returning to nothingness**.

This is called the **Form of Formlessness**,
The **Form of the Formless**—
This is called **Huǎnghǔ (恍惚)**.
**Approach it, yet its head is unseen**;
**Follow it, yet its end is unmarked**.

**Holding to the Dao/Way of antiquity**,
To **govern the present with it**,
**Knowing the ancient origins**—
This is called **the framework of the Dao/Way**.

In general, **all affairs of the world** require **a guiding principle and thread**,
Only then can one **grasp the main points and hold the outline**,
**With order and no confusion**.
How much more so for **cultivating the Dao/Way**?

Moreover, the **source of the Great Dao/Way** is **True Unity’s Qi**;
**True Unity’s Qi** is the **root of the Great Dao/Way**.
What is **True Unity’s Qi**?
The *Book of Odes* says: **"The mandate of Heaven is vast and ceaseless, its solemnity unending."**
What is the **root and source of the Great Dao/Way**?
The *Book of Odes* says: **"The carriage of Heaven moves on without sound or scent."**
**Reason and Qi united** is the **Dao/Way**.

If a **cultivator recognizes this framework**,
And **discovers its beginnings**,
**Regulating emotions with principle**, **fixing nature with righteousness**,
**Taking the boundless, formless Qi as the foundation**—
Then the **Dao/Way of longevity** is **attained**.

If one **takes clarity and brightness, purity and lucidity** as **cultivation**,
I **fear the Dao/Way has no true essence**,
And all **effort is in vain**.

Thus the **Supreme Sage** describes the **innate, primordial Dao/Way**:
**"Seeing it, yet unseen—called Yí (夷);
Hearing it, yet unheard—called Xī (希);
Seizing it, yet unattained—called Wēi (微)."**

The **heart** communicates with the **eyes**—
The **eyes** harbor **spirit**.
The **kidneys** communicate with the **ears**—
The **ears** harbor **essence**.
The **spleen** communicates with the **four limbs**—
The **four limbs** belong to the **spleen**, the **spleen** to **Earth**,
**Earth** generates all things—
**True Qi** condenses here.
Thus **spirit and essence dwell here**.

If the **eyes see something**,
If the **ears hear something**,
If the **hands act**—
These are **later-acquired, tangible, colored, scented, and audible essence, spirit, and Qi**,
Only **able to manifest form**,
**Not able to manifest the Dao/Way**.

Only when **seeing nothing**—
Then the **innate, primordial Wood Nature** is **revealed**;
When **hearing nothing**—
Then the **innate, primordial Metal Emotion** is **revealed**;
When **seizing nothing**—
Then the **innate, primordial Earth Will** is **revealed**.

Thus it is said:
The **later-acquired Water, Fire, and Earth**—
**They give rise to form**;
The **innate, primordial Metal, Wood, and Earth**—
**They bring about immortality**.

The **Yí (夷), Xī (希), Wēi (微)**—
These are **profound, mysterious, and distant**,
**Beyond grasp or comprehension**—
There is **truly what cannot be exhausted or questioned**.

If one can **merge the Five Qi into One Qi**,
And **blend the Three Primordials into One Primordial**—
Then **True Primordial Qi** is **here**,
And **Heaven’s natural sovereignty** is **here as well**.

Thus the *Awakening to True Reality* says:
**"A maiden in green robes (Fire generates Water),
A scholar in white silk (Water generates Metal).
Seeing it, yet **unable to use it** (Later-acquired Water, Fire, Earth),
Using it, yet **unable to see it** (Innate, primordial Metal, Wood, Earth).
**Huǎnghǔ (恍惚)**—here they meet,
**Yǎomíng (杳冥)**—here **transformation occurs**.
In a flash, **Fire blazes forth**,
And the **Perfect Sage emerges**."**

If a **cultivator knows this**,
He **knows the source of the Great Dao/Way**,
And the **key to cultivating it**.

If one **does not know** that it **begins with the Void**,
But **fixates on the foul gas of this mortal corpse**,
**On impure and confused spirit**,
**On a generated, luminous mind**,
**On fabricated perception and reflection**—
I **fear that his reserves are not deep**,
And his **emergence will not be smooth or effortless**.

This is the **work of refining essence, Qi, and spirit**—
Thus it **never departs from the state of Hun Dun (混沌)**.

Once **Hun Dun (混沌)** is **achieved**,
With **time**, the **embryonic body grows**,
And the **Yang Spirit is born**—
Yet **the methods for nurturing the embryo and fostering the spirit** must **also be known**,
Which are the **principles of loving the people and governing the state without action**,
As taught in the **previous chapter**.»

「The **Yang Spirit** rises and falls, flowing naturally—
When it ascends to the heights, one does not know whence it came,
Thus it is not dazzling.
When it descends to the depths, one does not neglect its hidden source,
Thus it is not obscure.

From this, it extends endlessly and subtly,
Continuously and inextricably,
Indescribable in form,
Yet all things arise from this **Hun Dun (混沌)**—
The **Daoist** *Zhuangzi* says: *“Wash the heart and retreat to the hidden depths”*—
This is its essence!

Thus it returns to the **Void of No-Thing (无物)**.
Though No-Thing, yet the **ten thousand affairs and things of the world**,
All emerge from this **Void**.

The **Taoist Sage (太上)** speaks of the **formless Form (无状之状)**,
The **imageless Image (无象之象)**—
But what **Form** or **Image** is there indeed?
Only a fleeting glimpse in the **dim and indistinct (恍恍惚惚)**.

Truly **dim and indistinct (恍惚)**,
The **True Primordial Qi (真元)** is born.
If one **welcomes its opportunity and guides it (迎其机而导之)**,
It seems to arise from nowhere—**the beginning is unseen (前不见其首)**;
If one **follows its movement and draws it (随其气而引之)**,
It ends without trace—**the end is unseen (后不见其尾)**.

Such is the **vastness of the Dao (道之浩浩)**—
This is not the **great cycle of the mundane world (大周沙界)**,
Nor the **subtle penetration into the minutest things (毫芒)**,
But the **Dao itself (是道也)**—
What **Dao** is this?
It is the **Primordial Qi of Origin (元始一气)**,
The **true sovereign of the human body and its organs (人身官骸真宰)**.

To **obtain it** is to **live**,
To **lose it** is to **die**;
To **preserve it fully** is to **be human**,
To **neglect it** is to **be mere things**—
Its **manifestation is but a hair’s breadth apart**.

A **student of the Dao** who **attains this Primordial Qi (元始之气)**,
And **regulates and nurtures the five organs and hundred bones (调摄乎五官百骸)**,
Will have **luminous hair and skin, smooth and refined flesh**—
This is the **embodiment of ancient Dao (执古之道)**,
To **command the present (以御今之有)**.

If a **student of the Dao** can **recognize this Primordial Qi (元始一气)**—
The **origin of heaven and earth (开天辟地)**, before the **ancient times (太古)**—
And **hold it as the framework of one’s body (以为一身纲纪)**,
The **core of all affairs (万事主脑)**,
Then **one’s foundation is upright (体立)**, and **all else follows naturally (用自行)**,
**One’s root is pure (本正)**, and **the branches align (末自端)**.

If a **student of the Dao** does not **take this Primordial Qi (元始一气)** as the foundation,
And **seeks to cultivate awakened awareness (修正觉)**,
He **falls into false paths (反堕旁门)**—
This is truly **pitiable**!

To **grasp the Dao’s essence (状道之体)**,
A **student of the Dao** must **understand this body (会得此体)**.
For **True Primordial Qi (真一之气)** is the **source of innate life and death (先天性命之源)**,
Not to be compared with **postnatal essence, Qi, and spirit (后天精气神)**.

To **perceive the vital breath (命气)**,
One must **merge the innate True One (性真)** with it,
Only then will **True Primordial Qi (真一之气)** be attained.

Though this **Qi is formless and boundless (浑浑沦沦，浩浩荡荡)**,
Without **image or shape (虽无可象可形)**,
Yet all **images and shapes in the world (天下之有象有形者)**,
Emerge from this **formless and imageless (无形无象)**.
Truly the **pivotal foundation of the Dao (大道纪纲)**,
The **root of heaven, earth, and all beings (天地人物之根本)**.

The **Daoists** speak of **holding the center (守中)**,
The **Buddhist** speak of **contemplating emptiness (观空)**,
The **Confucians** speak of **cultivating solitude with caution (慎独)**—
All share the **same fundamental practice**.

To the **outer eye**, it seems **neither seen nor heard (无睹无闻)**,
Yet **within oneself**, it is **utterly clear (至虚至实，至无至有)**.
Thus **Zi Si (子思)** says:
*“Do not reveal what is hidden,
Do not display what is subtle.”*

The **scholar’s practice of solitude (君子慎独之功)** is **unceasing**.
In the **hidden, subtle, and secluded realm (隐微幽独之地)**,
Though there may be **what can be displayed or grasped (可显可据)**,
The **Dao’s root and source (大道根源)** is **only the most elusive and mysterious (希夷微妙)**—
**Indescribable yet described (无可状而状)**,
**Formless yet imaged (无可象而象)**,
**Exalted in its pristine unity (极其浑穆)**.

A **student of the Dao** must,
**Before Yang arises**, await it in **dim and indistinct (恍恍惚惚)**,
**When Yang is born**, welcome it in **dim and indistinct**,
**When Yang returns to the furnace and enters the cauldron (归炉入鼎)**,
**Preserve and nurture it in dim and indistinct**—
**Never awakening a great luminous mind (绝不起大明觉心)**),
Only then will one **constantly attain the Dao’s return (无时无处而不得大道归源)**.

Earlier, it was said that when the **Yang Spirit appears (阳神出现)**,
**Heaven and earth are illuminated (明天察地)**,
**He penetrates the mysterious and reaches the subtle (通玄达微)**,
And **upon enlightenment (及了悟之候)**,
The **radiant landscape (光明景界)** is **purely natural (纯任自然)**—
**Awareness seems as if it were not (有知若无知)**,
**Perception seems as if it were not (有觉若无觉)**—

**How much more so at the very beginning of practice?**
Can one not **kill the mundane heart (死人心)** to **generate the Daoist heart (生道心)** in **dim and indistinct (恍恍惚惚)**?

**Chapter Fifteen: Subtle and Mysterious Communication**
The **ancient masters who excelled in Daoist cultivation (古之善为士者)**,
Were **subtle and mysterious (微妙玄通)**,
**Deep beyond recognition (深不可识)**.

Because they were **beyond recognition (唯不可识)**,
They **forced a semblance of form (故强为之容)**:
**Serious (豫)**, as if **crossing a river in winter (若冬涉川)**;
**Cautious (犹)**, as if **fearing the four neighbors (若畏四邻)**;
**Solemn (俨)**, as if **a guest (若客)**;
**Dissipated (涣)**, as if **ice about to melt (若冰之将释)**;
**Unadorned (敦)**, as if **raw and simple (若朴)**;
**Vast (旷)**, as if **a valley (若谷)**;
**Turbulent (浑)**, as if **muddy waters (若浊)**.

Who can **clarify what is turbulent (浊以澄静)**?
Only **gradually (徐清)**.
Who can **establish what is tranquil (安以久动)**?
Only **gradually (徐生)**.

Those who **preserve the Dao (保此道者)** do not **seek fullness (不欲盈)**,
Thus they **remain unspoiled though worn (敝不新成)**.

The **Taoist Sage (太上)** spoke earlier of the **Dao’s essence (道体)**,
Now he speaks of **those who embody the Dao (体道之人)**.
**Man and the Dao** are **two as one (二而一)**—
The **Dao is invisible (无可见)**,
Yet **through man, it becomes visible (因人可见)**.

How can **man become immortal (人何能仙)**?
Through **the Dao (以道而仙)**.

What is the **Dao (道者何)**?
It is **True Primordial Qi (真一之气)**—
The **virtue/power (德)** of the *Zhongyong (中庸)*.

To **cultivate the Dao**, is there **any other way (岂有他哉)**?
**King Wen (文王)** was **dutiful and reverent (小心翼翼)**,
Serving the **Celestial Sovereign (上帝)** with clarity.
**Confucius (孔子)** was **modest and restrained (足缩缩)**,
As if **following a path (有循)**.

The **Dao as Dao** is **nothing more than reverence (不过一敬)**.
If **man can dwell in reverence (以敬居心)**,
With **no thought left unguarded (一念不苟)**,
And **no deed taken lightly (一事不轻)**,
Is not the **Dao itself (大道)** here?

Though the **Dao is not strange (无奇怪)**,
It **depends on those who embody it (尤赖有体道者)**—
Only then does the **Dao not hang empty in heaven and earth (不虚悬于天壤)**.

Thus the **Taoist Sage (太上)** says:
*“The ancient masters who excelled in Daoist cultivation (古之善为士者)**,
**Their actions were not dualistic (其为物不二)**,
Thus their **generation of things was beyond measure (生物不测)**.
How **subtle and mysterious (至微至妙)** is this Dao!

*“Stillness yet responsive (寂然不动，感而遂通)**—
How **profound and penetrating (至玄至通)** is this Dao!

It is **only their vastness of heart (心之浩浩)**,
And **their boundless Qi (气之洋洋)**—
Like the **depth of rivers and seas (不啻江河之深)**,
Making one **unfathomable (无从测识)**.

Thus the **Taoist Sage (太上)** says:
*“Because it is beyond recognition (夫惟不识)**,
They force a semblance of form (故强为之容)**.”*

To reveal the **inner truth** as unfathomable yet its outward form as something that can be shaped—
Its **mind ever-vigilant in solitude (心心慎独)**, in every place preserving sincerity (在在存诚),
Like **Yu’s crossing of the river**, waiting for ice to solidify before passage;
Or **like the night traveler**, awaiting still winds before proceeding—this is the utmost caution.

Its **orderly solemnity** is like a **guest of honor** arriving from afar, daring not to slacken;
Its **effortless liberation**, unburdened and unconstrained, is like **ice melting into water**, leaving no trace to follow;
Its **loyal and thick-hearted sincerity**, its **benevolent kindness toward all things**, is like **Taipu’s pristine wholeness**, untouched by carving, yet **perfectly seamless**;
Its **restful yet capacious demeanor**, its **humble self-restraint**,—what differs it from **deep mountains and hidden valleys**, empty yet boundless, vast yet all-embracing?

Such its form—its **nature is known**.
All this is **the Dao’s vastness borne in silence**, conforming to the **Heavenly Pattern** yet unrecognized.
It is as if **Shun dwelling in the deep mountains**, no different from the **wild men of the wilderness**.

Its **primordial and muddled mien**—how could the **dim-witted** ever emulate it?
Yet **muddled and turbid** are alike in name, **sage and commoner** share the same root—
The **commoner’s turbidity** is truly turbid;
The **Sage’s turbidity**, though muddled like turbidity, is in truth **utterly turbid yet utterly clear**.

Yet the Sage **does not claim sainthood**—this is how sainthood is attained;
The commoner **does not claim ordinariness**—yet remains truly ordinary.
Who can **clarify the mind’s defilements** and still them, awaiting their **prolonged stillness** until **pure light emerges**?
Who can **nurture the nature’s innate tranquility**, immersing and expanding it,
Until, through **long cultivation**, life **gradually unfolds**—
Harvesting it as **medicine**, refining it as **elixir**?
Is not the **Way of Preserving Life** found here?

This is **stillness to concentrate the spirit**, **movement to generate qi**—
It is **holding the Center**, it is **the Yang Life of the Child**.
From this **rising and descending**, gathering back into the furnace,
Gradually refining, gradually condensing—
All is **one undivided mind**, all **phenomena as empty**—
Preserving this **Yang** alone.

The more **substance**, the more **it heals**; the more **emptiness**, the more **emptiness**.
With **utterly empty mind**, without **fear of excess**,
Thus one can **return the One True Qi**, attain the **Eternal Dao**.

And what of *“The worn-out is not replaced”*?
For all **newly made things** decay swiftly—
Yet this **decays without decaying**.
Decay is its **manifest trace**; non-decay is its **spirit**.
One **True Essence** contained within, **ten thousand spirits** manifested without—
Its **subtle, mysterious, and profound penetration** is indeed as such.

This speaks of the **Dao-embodying sage’s** meticulous caution—
Though the **Dao is originally empty and void**, yet **Dao-masters** can **form without form**, **manifest without image**,
As if **inner and outer are perfectly unified**.

The seven clauses beginning with *“like”* (若) all **borrow phenomena to describe the Dao’s marvels**,
Revealing its **profound, unfathomable depths**.

*“Who can”* and the following sentences speak of those **unable to perfect virtue** yet seeking **entry into the Dao**.
Turbidity is hard to clarify—**stillness preserves the mind’s purity**;
Tranquility requires duration—**movement sharpens the spirit’s insight**.
Fullness invites loss—**thus one avoids excess**;
Novelty decays swiftly—**thus one avoids the newly made**.

I wish the **seeker** to be **empty yet capacious**, **unpolished yet genuine**,
**Primordially vast and unrestrained**, everywhere displaying **sincerity and integrity**—
Then **no heart will fail to transform into Dao-heart**, no **vulgar qi** will fail to become **True Qi**.

Do not, because the **depths are unfathomable**, hesitate and retreat!

---
**Chapter Sixteen: The Extremes of Emptiness and Sincere Stillness**

**Attain the utmost emptiness**, **guard the most sincere stillness**.
When **all things flourish together**, I observe their return.
For **all things, teeming and diverse**, each **returns to its root**.
Returning to the root is **stillness**; stillness is **restoring the Mandate**.
Restoring the Mandate is **constancy**; knowing constancy is **clarity**.
Ignorant of constancy, **reckless action brings ruin**.
Knowing constancy, **one is tolerant**—
Tolerance brings **impartiality**, impartiality brings **kingly virtue**,
Kingly virtue brings **Heaven**, Heaven brings **the Dao**,
The Dao brings **duration**—**lifelong without peril**.

If one seeks to **cultivate the Great Dao**, to **become a Golden Immortal**,
And **endure for countless eons without decay**,
From the **very first step**, one must not fail to grasp its **root**.

What is this **root**?
It is the **Mysterious Pass orifice**.

For **cultivating True Essence and refining the Dao** is not a single path—
How could the **Mysterious Pass’s marvels** exhaust its depths?
Heaven has **Heaven’s root**, things have **their stems**, humans have **their source**—
Never has one **without origin** achieved **great deeds** or **immortal feats**.

Observe **Heaven and Earth before their opening**—
Silent and void, without sound;
After their unfolding, **vast and boundless**.
Is this **unopened state** the **Heaven’s root**?
Vague and formless—**hardly the Heaven’s root**.
Is this **unfolded state** the **Heaven’s root**?
Ever-expanding and endless—**yet not the Heaven’s root**.
Then where is this **root**?
It lies in the **threshold of opening and not-yet-opening**.

Observe **humans and things before their birth**—
Faint and indistinct, without form;
After their nurturing, **prolific and boundless**.
Is this **unborn state** their **origin**?
Misty and shapeless—**hardly their origin**.
Is this **nurtured state** their **origin**?
Ever-changing and endless—**yet not their origin**.
Then where is this **origin**?
It too lies in the **threshold of birth and not-yet-born**.

How can one **cultivate the Great Dao** without knowing this **single orifice** and act in reckless confusion?

The **Taoist Sage** instructs those who **nurture the Dao and seek the Mysterious**:
*“Attain utmost emptiness, guard sincere stillness—I observe their return.”*
This clarifies that **cultivators must**—
**Discard superficiality**, **return to sincerity**,
**Concentrate the spirit in emptiness**, **nurture qi in stillness**—
At the **utmost emptiness**, in **sincere stillness**,
Naturally **all phenomena dissolve**, **One Truth is embraced**.

The *Classic of Change* thus says:
*"Does one see the **Heaven-Earth’s heart** at its return?"*

Master Zhao further expounds:
*"At the Winter Solstice, the half of the Child—
When the **Heavenly Root** reaches its utmost subtlety,
The first stir of **One Yang**—
The moment when all things begin to be born.
This is when **Heavenly Principle returns**, which the ancients likened to the **living Child hour**."*

It is also said:
*"When One Yang first emerges, the **dark and void** awaken in collision.*
This is the very **pivot** where all things return to their uprightness,
where **Heaven-Earth’s return** unfolds.
Here, the **Primordial Ancestor’s Original Qi** of the **Pre-Heaven** may truly be observed.

Yet its **mechanism is most subtle**, its **qi most swift**—
It is present now, gone in an instant.
Like **stone-fire and lightning**, it vanishes in a blink.

Observe the grasses and trees:
Though they flourish in **teeming forms**—
Branches and leaves, **radiant and patterned**, dazzling the eye—
None of these suffice as the **root of rebirth** or the **source of renewal**.
Only when **emergence turns to withdrawal**,
When **life transforms into death**,
When **roots gather fruit**,
Does each return to its origin—
Just as the **alchemical adept’s elixir head** does.

Returning to the root,
It is also **motion turning back to stillness**.
Once returned to stillness,
It once more **descends to bestow the noble seed**—
For things, this is **returning to their origin**;
For humans, this is **restoring the Mandate**.
It is no different matter.

Spring and autumn pass—**the old gives way to the new**;
Life and death alternate—**the blooming fades**.
Thus, the **adept’s way of restoring the Mandate**
Is but the **interplay of Heaven-Earth’s Two Qi**,
The **flow of One Qi**—
The most **even**, the most **ordinary** of **Ways**.

To know the **Constant Way** is to grasp the **Great Dao**.
From here, one’s practice advances—
Only then will it not stray.

Yet the **world’s errant paths**—
Ignorant of the **Dao’s roots**,
Unwilling to **purify the heart and clear the mind**,
To **return to origins and grasp the end**—
Some **refine the nature of perception**,
Some **cultivate the form and qi of life**,
Some **harvest seven metals and eight stones** as medicine,
Some **take young boys and girls** to forge the elixir—
With no foundation, where can the **Dao** be found?
Moreover, fixated on one extreme,
They stray into **perverse paths**—
And how many have **ruined their bodies and lost their lives**?
All this stems from **not knowing the Dao as the Constant Way**,
Thus **pursuing obscurities and performing wonders**,
**Treading dangers and inviting calamity**.

Only when one knows that **the Dao belongs to the True and Constant**—
That it is **inherent in all people**, **sufficient in all things**—
And does not **rejoice in knowing it** nor **marvel at possessing it**,
Like **water and fire to humanity**—
Freely taken and wielded at will—
**Resting in emptiness yet capacious**,
Viewing **self and other with equal breadth**,
Holding a **vastly impartial heart**—
Then one aligns with the **Sage-King**.
*"My people, my kin; all things, my companions."*
Standing with **Heaven-Earth’s extremes**,
Uniting with **all things in a single source**,
Unseparated—
Is this not **becoming One with Heaven**?

For **Heaven is the Dao**, the **Dao is Heaven**;
**Beyond Heaven, there is no Dao**; **beyond the Dao, there is no Heaven**.
Only **Heaven is supreme**; only the **Sage follows it**.
Only the **Dao is sovereign**; only **Heaven is its law**.

Thus, for humans: **birth and death**.
For the **Dao**: **eternal and unexhausted**.
Even **ascension and shell-shedding**—
There comes a time of **decay and annihilation**.
Yet though the **form perishes**, the **spirit does not perish**;
Though the **body is lost**, the **qi does not perish**.

As the *Odes* say:
*"The King Wen stands above—
Illuminating to Heaven."*
This is what is meant.

All this comes from **attaining utmost emptiness and sincere stillness**,
Then **observing the returning symbol**—
Only then can one be **fathomless**.

The scholar—**how dare they neglect the root and act recklessly, inviting misfortune?**

The **Most High** instructs on the **foundational practice**,
The **headwork of learning**.
Where one gains strength here,
There is strength everywhere.

If the scholar grasps this meaning,
They will **diligently uphold the Rule**,
**Unceasingly, like silk threads**—
From **non-being to being**, from **being to non-being**—
Even in a **single breath’s instant**, the **root of the Great Dao** is present;
In **a meal’s time**, the **Primordial Beginning** of the **Dao** is stored.
From this **single thread of subtle mechanism**,
If **gathered and refined**,
Gradually it **burgeons uncontrollably**—
All this is the **accumulation of One Yang**.

Though **mighty qi fills Heaven-Earth**,
Though the **Yang Spirit** pierces the **Big Dipper and Bull**—
What is it but the **piling of a single True Qi**?

The **student who lacks this True Qi**,
But only **refines the post-heavenal form and spirit**,
Is but **one hair of nine oxen**, **one grain of the sea**—
How dare they **compare with Heaven-Earth**?

Only by **walking this Way and becoming One with Heaven-Earth**
Does one achieve **unbreakable for billions of years**.
The **Dao-cultivator must take this seriously**—
**Seizing the moment without loss**.

**Chapter Seventeen: The Work Completed, the Body Fulfilled**

The **Most High**—
*Unknown to be known* (All other versions read *"The lowly knows what is known"*—
Yet this contradicts the **text’s intent**; it is a scribal error).

Next, they are **cherished and praised**;
Next, they are **feared**;
Next, they are **insulted**.
**Trust is insufficient**; **doubt arises**.
*"Ah, how cautious with words!"*
When the **work is complete and the matter fulfilled**,
The people all say, *"It was natural."*

(*The line "Ah, how cautious with words!" speaks of **ease and sincerity**, **solemn reverence in proclamation**.*)

---

**The Dao of the Most High in Cultivating the Body is the Dao of Governing the World—nothing more than the singular True.**

The True, when held within oneself, refines the self;
The True, when applied to all things, fulfills the world.
Though there be distinctions of inner and outer, self and others,
The True of this heart remains ever unchanging.

If one attains **至诚** without ceasing,
Then the people’s response to them shall also be unceasing.
If one attains **至诚** with interruption,
Then the world’s response to them shall also be interrupted.

For all share this heart, this heart shares this principle:
Cultivate the body, and the world naturally finds peace;
Lose the True, and the world must fall into chaos.

From the **Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors** down to the present,
There has never been one who differed in this.

The Most High wishes people to cultivate themselves with **sincerity and trust**,
And thus governs them with **sincerity and trust**.
Without being seen, yet manifest;
Without acting, yet transforming;
Without doing, yet achieving.

To oneself, the Dao of governance is unknown;
To the people, it is felt through **ease and sincerity**,
**Solemn reverence in proclamation**,
And yet they do not perceive its transformation—
Nor know its origin.

This was the pure wind of antiquity;
I have long failed to see it again.

Thus the Most High says:
*"The Most High—unknown to be known."*
So that ruler and people alike rejoice in **光天化日** beneath the radiant heavens and earth.

If **sincerity and trust** were not deeply rooted,
How could such **divine transformation** exist?

The **restoring wind of the ancient emperors** has faded long ago,
The **Most High’s perfect governance** is unheard of today.
Thus the customs decline, the **Great Dao** grows distant,
And there are things too dire for words or witness.

If the ancient ways are not yet lost,
If the Dao still lingers,
If **天性** has not yet been obscured,
And **至诚** remains,
Then though not as profound as the **Most High’s** sincerity,
It is still the **light and splendor** of the present age.

All are ways of governance—
One without thought yet naturally transforming,
One with intent yet applying **仁**.

To cherish the people as one cherishes an infant,
To love the people as one loves family—
In such times, the **revered and long-lived** are honored,
And their words are answered as echoes.

Yet when **ease and sincerity** are not uniform,
**Virtue and transformation** cannot be equal.
Only through **cherishing and loving** them,
**Praising and rewarding** them,
Without adding **harsh words or punishments**,
Do they harbor **gratitude and fear**.

Though not reaching the Most High,
This still follows the Dao, the path—
Praiseworthy indeed,
As the saying goes: *"When the Great Dao is abandoned, there are仁 and義."*

Thus **Emperor降** became **Emperor**, and **Emperor** became **King**—
All were those who knew **virtue** and practiced the **Kingly Dao**.

Later, when the ancient customs grew distant,
The Great Dao more obscure,
**King**降 became **Conqueror**,
Using **仁 and義** as pretexts,
Each with their own heart, their own perception—
Far removed from the **single virtue** of the emperors and kings.

Thus though **rites and teachings** remain,
Though **laws and decrees** persist,
Though **institutions and prohibitions** are unchanged,
The **True** of their hearts differs greatly.

Only through **talent and brilliance** do they govern the world,
Relying on **laws and measures** to guide the people.
When their administration is fitting,
And their policies lawful,
The people fear them and dare not break laws or transgress rules—
This is the **clever ruler**, the **greatly peaceful age**.

And those below them—
Too dire to speak of.

They **trust in cunning** to drive the people,
They **indulge in deceit** to rule the masses,
With **狐狸** and **虎豹** hearts,
They enact **狐狸** and **虎豹**-like governance.

When they are **haughty and self-proclaimed**,
When they are **boastful and self-satisfied**,
There is none who does not think them wiser than the **Three Kings**,
Or more talented than the **Five Conquerors**—
Yet the people of this age,
In the end, **fearful and trembling** in their sleep,
Are truly **insulted and oppressed**.

Though the people dare not speak,
Their hearts are divided and estranged,
And thus there is no **unceasingly harmonious governance**—
Hence, disaster follows swiftly upon their heels.

Confucius said:
*"When the ruler values trust, the people will use their emotions."*
If trust is insufficient within oneself,
How can one expect trust from the people?

This is why ruler and people **deceive and trick each other**.

When **institutions, teachings, laws, and decrees** are issued,
Even the **Sage** does not abandon them—
Yet **emotion and falsity** are divided,
And **response and resonance** differ.

Only the **Emperor and King** set the example with their bodies,
**Trusting and inspiring** the people.
When laws are established, governance follows;
When words are spoken, the people believe.
Ultimately, their **light shines to the four quarters**,
Their **work is complete and their matter fulfilled**,
As **Yao** did in the **Age of Change and Prosperity**,
And **Shun** in his **self-cultivation and non-action**—
And the people all say, *"It was natural."*

Alas!
The reach of **true trust** is vast—
Compared to those who lack trust within,
The difference is as vast as heaven and earth.

**Dao and Virtue** are all-encompassing,
Refining the self here, governing the world here—
Extending to all things under heaven,
Nothing lies beyond this scope.

Thus the words *"Most High"* in this chapter
Refer to those of the highest rank,
Possessing the highest **quality**—
Hence, *"Most High."*

The **highest virtue** is **清净** and **无为**,
With the **六根** all settled.
Next, they **reverence and love**, transforming the people—
When moved, they respond.
Next, they **fear and awe**, ruling the world.
Next, they **guide the people with cunning**—
This is called **术**.

Yet the most wondrous of all is **trust**.
**Trust** belongs to **土**,
And its cultivation is **修炼始终**—
Its purest essence lies in **意土**.

Thus the Most High says:
*"Its essence is supremely true,
And within it lies trust"*—
This is the **elixir foundation**.

**Trust** is nothing else—
**至诚** alone.
If one attains **至诚** without ceasing,
Then the **elixir** is the **elixir** indeed.

Yet **trust** and **falsity** are nearly indistinguishable—
A moment’s thought leads to **sainthood**,
Neglect leads to **madness**.
The boundary between **man and beast**,
The threshold of **life and death**—
All that is contested lies in this **interval**.

I wish the **later scholars** to seek **true trust**,
For the **constant Dao** can be found therein.

Where is **trust**?
It is the **玄关** of a single aperture—
Do people truly know it?

**Chapter Eighteen: The Great Dao Abandoned**

The **Great Dao abandoned**,
There are **仁 and義**.
**智慧** arises,
There is **great deception**.
The **六亲** are not harmonious,
There are **孝 and慈**.
The state is **昏乱**,
There are **loyal ministers**.

I have observed the **Age of the Most High**—
Customs were **敦厚** and **朴实**,
People were **浑朴** and sincere,
Each **正其性**, **定其命**,
**安其俗**, **乐其业**—
Like things following **天** and moving,
Like nature acting as it will,
Without need of artifice or contrivance,
Not awaiting arrangement or command,
In accordance with **性**’s natural course—
And yet there were those who **did not know its origin**.

「Zhuangzi spoke of those bloated and labored in their exertions—
Their dull sincerity, weary toil,
Moving in accord with Heaven’s flow,
Rich with **true intent**.

This is why they fail to recognize or know,
Following the **Way**’s natural course.
What manner of transformation is this?
Naught but **浑浑沦沦**—
Unthinking, undisturbed,
Unified with the **Great Dao**.

Yet alas, the **royal winds** decline,
The **Great Dao** grows ever feebler.
Those in high station then speak of **仁 and義**,
Diligently toiling, none daring to stray.
To dwell in **義** while upholding **仁**,
Is indeed a praiseworthy deed of sages—
Not wholly to be condemned;
But compared to ancient times of flourishing **Dao**,
When hearts were **浑噩** and minds unspoiled,
Where **仁 and義** existed without need of speech—
The difference is vast.

Thus the **Most High** sighed:
*"The **Great Dao** abandoned, there are **仁 and義**."*

From this point on,
Both high and low covet **仁 and義**,
Yet those who claim them practice deceit,
Though **仁 and義** still appear,
Their actions have grown corrupt.
Is this because **仁 and義** are flawed?
Or because the **Dao** is pierced and hollowed,
And those ensnared by **仁**, enslaved by **義**, bring harm to it?

Still, they call it **仁 and義**,
Though not as pure as the **Great Dao**,
It has not yet fallen into **great deception**.

After this,
Customs grew ever more perverse,
Hearts grew ever more corrupted.
Even where **仁 and義** were taught,
Little remained—
For in the halls of court and the common folk,
Between ruler and subject,
All flaunted their **智** and paraded their **慧**,
Within their cliques,
Deceit and suspicion reigned supreme.
How could such a people hope to **harmonize the family** or **govern the state**?

Thus, sons and fathers grew estranged,
Brothers turned against one another,
Even husband and wife, friends and neighbors,
Relatives and townsfolk—
Each with their own heart,
Each with their own view,
As distant as **Hu and Yue**,
Let alone the rest!

If a son could show **孝**,
The court would single him out for praise;
If a father could show **慈**,
The village would acclaim him in unison.
Alas!
**孝** and **慈** were ever the **constant Dao** of Heaven and Earth,
The **proper path** of the family—
Why should they be so celebrated?

Yet when **三党六亲** were forgotten,
And **逆** winds grew fierce,
When **阋墙** strife was common,
Those who could show **孝** or **慈**,
Though few and far between,
Were held in great reverence,
And their deeds proclaimed to inspire the world.

How far this falls from the **Age of the Most High**,
When **孝** was natural and **慈** unspoken—
When even the **孝** and **慈** of today
Are but **反**—a forced return to harmony,
Where men now but **粉饰** and **铺张**,
Claiming **慈孝** while taking its name in vain—
How many such hypocrites could be counted?

And after the **五霸**,
When kin destroyed one another,
When lords and ministers exchanged hostages,
It is no wonder
That **昏庸** rulers arose above,
And **跋扈** ministers below—
And the state has known no peace since then.

Fortunate are those with **忠肝义胆**,
Who rise to **安邦定国**,
Though success or failure may not be foreseen,
Their **尽瘁鞠躬** loyalty is clear.
Yet their strength cannot turn back the tide of fate,
Nor their resolve preserve the whole of the world—
Like **Zhuge Liang**’s six campaigns at **Qishan**,
Or **Jiang Banyao**’s nine assaults on the **Central Plains**.

Consider the subject’s lot:
To serve a lord, they wish to be **良臣**,
Not **忠臣**.
If fortune favors the land,
They may share in **同襄补衮**’s duties—
Yet if **强梁** wars rise again,
Each must display their **济世** talents.
When the world is **昏乱**,
Heaven reveals the **忠臣**;
When **忠臣** arise,
Heaven sustains the **昏乱**.

Though **忠臣** may emerge,
Even if they could purge the **昏乱**,
And turn the land from **干戈** to **礼义**—
It would still pale in comparison
To the **Age of the Most High**,
When all was **无事** and distant from strife.
Alas!
The **忠臣** would rather **终身埋没**,
Than see their name proclaimed—
For one man’s **忠臣** title
Brings the world’s share of **昏乱** woes—
A sorrow too great to bear!

This is the **Most High**’s lament for the **Way** of the world,
His grief for the present, his thoughts for the past,
Desiring men return to **朴** and embrace **真**,
That high and low alike be **无知**—
Their **virtue** unbroken,
Their **Way** ever sufficient,
**熙熙皞皞**, all under Heaven at peace in **无事**,
Yet unaware of their own **Way**.

Even **仁**, **義**, **智**, **慧**,
**孝** sons, **忠** ministers—
All are seen as natural,
Unknown as virtues,
And to name them would be shameful—
What greater **浑朴** could there be?

Though this speaks of **治世**,
It applies no less to **修身**.
The opening line likens the **浑沦** customs,
Where **太朴** has yet to be carved,
To the **章贞** body,
Unadorned yet perfect in its **Dao**’s mystery.

If one loses their **本来之天**,
Then they must borrow from **先天阴阳** to restore it.
**阴阳** are but **仁 and義**—
As the saying goes:
*"The **Great Dao** abandoned, there are **仁 and義**."*

To discern the **真** messages within the body’s **内外**,
And to nurture with **智慧** as if plucked from emptiness—
Here, purity is lost,
And **后天**’s influences creep in,
So **伪妄** cannot be avoided.
This is the meaning of:
*"**智慧** arises, there is **great deception**."*

As for **采阴补阳**,
It is the means to **和** the **六根**’s discord,
And bring them to **大定**—
The metaphor of **孝 and慈**.

And **猛烹急炼**,
The way to **靖** the **昏乱** within oneself,
And ascend to **清明**—
The essence of **"loyal ministers"**.

Know this, and the **Dao** is near.
What deeper lesson does the **Most High** teach?
Naught but to follow one’s **浑然粹然** Heavenly nature—
And to cultivate it as such.»

Chapter Nineteen: *Few Private Affairs, Few Desires*

To discard **Dao/Way** and abandon **智**, the people’s benefit is a hundredfold;
To discard **仁** and abandon **義**, the people return to **孝** and **慈**.

To discard **巧** and abandon **利**, thieves and robbers vanish;
These three measures, though insufficient for **文**’s adornment,
Are thus established to ensure their proper application:
*"Seeing the unadorned, embracing the unadulterated,
Few private affairs, few desires,
Discarding learning, free from worry."*

Among all people and things under Heaven,
The wise and foolish, noble and humble—
None escape the fundamental principle of **理** and **氣**’s harmony.

Yet why do they remain divided and obstructed?
Why do passions clash and intentions stumble?
It is because those above lack what to inspire,
And those below lack what to transform them.

The **至聖** sovereigns of old
Followed the **Dao/Way** of nature,
Practiced the governance of **無為**,
Did not delight in deeds for the sake of achievements,
Nor weary of duties and abandon governance.

Though they possessed keen **聰明** and profound **睿智**,
They all entered the realm of **無為** governance,
The **清淨** domain of purity,
Where the people below, observing and transformed,
Naturally honored their own kin,
Nurtured their own offspring,
Stabilized their customs,
Found joy in their labors—
Without a single person failing to restore their **性**,
Without a single thing failing to fulfill its **生**,
Their benefit was indeed a hundredfold!

As for the **至仁** sovereigns,
Who naturally embrace **慈善**’s essence,
Who possess **恻隐** (*cìcè*) compassion—
They dedicate themselves entirely to benefiting others and nurturing all things.

**浩浩荡荡**, **浑浑沦沦**,
They neither speak of right and wrong,
Nor of crooked and straight,
But instead let Heaven move them,
And follow their **性** in action—
Naturally without favor or bias,
They return to the realm of **大中至正**—
The Great Centrality of Rectitude.

The people, inspired and transformed by this,
As sons naturally become **孝** to their parents,
As fathers naturally become **慈** to their children.
Even if there are those who are unfilial or unkind,
Whose habits become rampant,
They too will gradually be transformed—
How splendid and flourishing this is!

In later ages, the most **聰明** and **敏捷** sovereigns,
Though they govern the world and rule with skill,
Do not comprehend the **Dao/Way**’s foundation of **無為**,
And thus, if they follow it, it is easy;
If they oppose it, it becomes difficult.

Some delight in constant change and disturb the people,
Creating laws and regulations,
Only to bring calamity to the state,
Where the people barely survive.
Others love martial prowess and neglect governance,
Exhausting armies and waging wars,
Only to deplete the treasuries,
Where the people cannot endure—
No wonder then that the people grow impoverished,
The nation falls ill,
And the winds of **攘窃劫奪** (*rǎngqièjiéduó*)—theft and plunder—rise,
While thieves and robbers openly roam the land.
Such is the fate of those **至巧** sovereigns,
Who do not know to apply their **巧** to the **無為** of Heaven,
To the **自在** of nature—
Who seek to enrich the state through greed,
Only to weaken its strength,
And unsettle the people’s customs.

If they could achieve **至巧無巧**—
Expressing their hearts as they truly are,
Guiding the **勢** in harmony,
Upholding **義** without scheming for **利**,
Clarifying the **Dao/Way** without calculating **功**—
Then the **德** of the **君子** would be the wind,
And the **德** of the **小人** would be the grass—
Naturally flowing downward like water,
Or ascending like fire,
With an unstoppable force.

Would there still be among the people
Those who disregard **廉恥** (*liánchǐ*)—modesty and shame—
Who become thieves and robbers,
Who delight in wrongdoing?
None would.

This is the **大智若愚**—great wisdom as if foolishness—,
**大仁若忍**—great virtue as if forbearance—,
**大巧若拙**—great skill as if clumsiness—
Yet later generations view them as unfit to rule,
Unfit to govern.

However, the **聖德**’s pervasiveness,
The **仁恩**’s transformative power,
The **智巧**’s refined influence—
Though lacking in outward **文**’s adornment,
Their **實質**—substance—is indeed profound.

**理** and **欲** originally do not conspire,
Sufficiency in outward **文** breeds dissatisfaction within,
Reducing **文**’s excess enhances **實**’s abundance.

The **聖** discard **智**,
The **仁** discard **義**,
The **巧** discard **利**—
All to strengthen their **實**,
And to remove **文**’s excess.

Though the common people are **至愚**—utterly foolish—,
They always take the ruler’s actions as their guide for **去就**—departure and approach.

Thus, if one discards **華** (*huá*)—ornamentation—and upholds **實**,
It naturally unifies the **小民**’s hearts in the **本原**—origin—
And leaves them **不雕不琢**—uncarved, unpolished.

For what they see is governance of **質實無文**—substance without adornment—
And thus what they embrace
Is the **太朴不凿** (*tàipǔbùzáo*)—unpierced Great Simplicity—of truth.

In such **浑完** naturalness,
Clothing and food are each in their **常**—proper measure—
Interactions and exchanges
Follow their **分**—natural roles.

Though **氣禀** is limited,
And private desires may occasionally sprout,
They are few and sparse.

In summary,
**聖** and **仁** and **巧** are all **質**—substance—;
**智** and **義** and **利** are all **文**—adornment.

To discard **聖** and abandon **智**,
To discard **仁** and abandon **義**,
To discard **巧** and abandon **利**—
All ensure **文** is insufficient,
And **質** remains abundant,
Each with its proper application.

How could the people, nourished by **德** and quenched by **和**,
Fail to benefit endlessly,
To see **孝** and **慈** flourish daily,
And theft transformed into virtue?
This is the **隆盛** governance—
A sight I have long failed to behold.

This metaphor illustrates the **修養** path—
First, preserve the **心** and nurture the **性**;
When **心** and **性** return to nature,
Then the **後天**’s **精氣** also return to the **先天**’s **精氣**.

If one has not yet seen **性** and clarified **心**,
And merely relies on **後天**’s **氣質**—constitution—
And **知覺**—perception—of the **心** for use,
Then the **精** belongs to the **凡精**—ordinary essence—,
And the **氣** belongs to the **凡氣**—ordinary *qi*—,
How could one obtain the **真一**—true unity—of essence,
The **真一** of *qi* to forge the **丹**—elixir?

The practitioner must seek from the **本源**—original source—
A **大本領**—great fundamental principle—,
A **真頭腦**—true essence—
To serve as the master.
Then, **煉精** (*liànjīng*) refining essence,
**煉氣** (*liàngqì*) refining *qi*,
**煉神** (*liàngshén*) refining spirit—
All become manifest.

Understanding this meaning,
One not only comprehends the **《太上經》**—*Tàishàng Jīng* (Classic of the Most High)—,
But also grasps that governance and self-cultivation,
In every aspect, are a single thread—
And that the **四書五經**—Four Books and Five Classics—
Are none other than **丹經**—elixir scriptures.

Other commentaries state that those in high positions,
Discarding **聖** and **智**,
Cause the people to know only **利**—profit—,
And thus they flock to it a hundredfold;
Discarding **仁** and **義**,
The people do not know to love their kin,
And thus they turn away from **孝** and **慈**—
This is to discard what should not be discarded,
And the harm is as such.

When it comes to the contradictions of clever profit, sagely wisdom, benevolence, and righteousness—
If one can discard and abandon them,
Then where would thieves and robbers arise?
This is what must be discarded and abandoned—
And such is its effect.

This teaching is truly **"high"** indeed.

The three levels below this state:
Governance of the people need not rely on decrees—
Only that commands must originate from what is rooted in one’s own practice.
To see the unadorned is to know constancy;
To embrace the uncarved is to preserve the spirit.
With few desires and little private gain,
This is what is meant by "having the world yet not partaking of it"—
How could one without excess and without action attain this?

**Chapter Twenty: Distinct from Others**

To abandon learning is to have no worries.
How far apart are **"yes"** and **"ah"**?
How far apart are **good** and **evil**?
What people fear, one must not fail to fear.

Ah, its boundlessness knows no end!

The masses rejoice, feasting as if at a grand sacrifice,
Ascending as if to the Spring Terrace—
Yet I alone remain tranquil, unmarked,
Like an infant yet unborn, still within the womb.
Buoyant and unrestrained,
As if having nowhere to return—
The masses all have abundance,
Yet I alone seem forsaken.

I alone possess the heart of the simple fool!
Muddled, while the vulgar are clear—
I alone am dim, while the vulgar are sharp.
Dull as the sea, unrestrained—
Like the wind, without end.

The masses all have purpose,
Yet I alone am clumsy and crude.
I alone differ from others,
Yet I cherish the Mother’s nourishment.

The Sage’s attainment is supreme—
Called **"Abandoning Learning"**—
Pure and vital, flowing and unrestrained,
Without care, without thought.

The *Odes* say:
*"The Most High watches over you—
Do not let your heart be divided."*

Thus, one acts in one’s natural station,
Unconcerned with poverty or wealth, gain or loss—
Nowhere does one not find self at ease,
And so it is said: **"No worries."**

This realm, compared to the unlearned and unskilled,
Is as distant as heaven from an abyss—
Yet the gap is not so vast.

Like an echo—
The same response—
Yet the direct **"yes"** and the flattering **"ah"** differ—
The response remains the same.
But the essence of the response—
How far apart can it truly be?

From ancient times, the division between Sage and commoner
Lies only in good and evil—
And the distinction between good and evil
Resides merely in reverence or insolence,
A contest of but a single thought—
How far apart can they be?

For the human heart is perilous,
And the **Dao**-heart is subtle—
A hair’s breadth of difference leads to a thousand-li error.
What people tremble to avoid,
How can I fail to fear what they fear?

Thus, from the very beginning,
One must gather the luminous spirit,
Returning it to the undifferentiated—
In motion or stillness,
Amidst change or constancy,
All as boundless as the primordial chaos,
Before Heaven and Earth were cleaved—
Vast and unrestrained,
As if the night had no end.

Thus, within there is a master,
And external things do not disturb.
When applied to governance and decrees,
The people naturally transform in joyous harmony.

To feed on the Sage’s virtue
Is to feast as at a grand sacrifice;
To dwell in the Sage’s domain
Is to ascend the Spring Terrace in delight.
How could this be compared to solitary cultivation in silent seclusion?

Thus, one’s merit fills the world yet knows no merit,
One’s actions fill the world yet knows no action.
The masses rejoice,
Yet I alone remain serene and undisturbed—
Faint as an unborn babe,
Still in the womb, a cluster of primordial *qi*,
Undifferentiated and whole—
Ascending and descending, flowing without end,
In harmony with Heaven and Earth,
Yet unaware of its destination.

While others act, I do not—
The masses have abundance to sustain them,
Yet I stand apart from the world, independent,
Far beyond what others can attain.

From their perspective, few would not call me foolish—
Yet when I reflect,
I only feel my heart cleansed, hidden in secrecy,
At peace with Heaven, content with my fate—
How could this be the heart of a fool?

It is merely that great wisdom appears as folly,
And great skill appears as clumsiness.

Otherwise, how could one bring such joy to labor and contentment to residence,
So swiftly transforming hearts and spirits?

This is because the **Great Polarity** remains a boundless unity—
Muddled as an unhatched egg,
No trace of yin or yang,
No knowledge at all.

The vulgar are clear in all things,
Yet I alone am dim, knowing nothing;
The vulgar are sharp in all things,
Yet I alone am confused, understanding nothing.

Am I truly muddled and unaware,
Confused and insensible?
Rather, it is to conceal one’s traces and dim one’s brilliance,
Nurturing clarity within profundity—
Day by day, month by month,
Refining the elixir through nine transformations,
Attaining the Sage’s virtue,
And becoming an immortal unchanging through the ages.

Its grandeur in transformation
Is like the obscurity of Heaven and Earth,
Encompassing all phenomena within thought.
Its subtlety and spirit
Are like flowing clouds and water without end,
Nurturing all life within.

From the outside, the masses all have purpose in the world,
Yet I alone am foolish and crude.
Within, the **Dao** is lofty and beautiful—
A Sage who transcends the crowd, stands alone,
And is unmatched by the world.
This is how I alone differ from others,
And why I am beyond what others can attain.

For the commoner is scattered in external pursuits,
Losing their original nature.
The Sage nurtures within,
Preserving their inherent essence.

The Sage differs from the commoner—
All because they return to the **Primordial** from the **Later-Heaven**.
For the **Later-Heaven** is **emotion**—child *qi*;
The **Primordial** is **nature**—mother *qi*.
Returning from emotion to nature,
It is as if the child clings to the mother,
Reluctant to part.
Thus it is said: **"Cherish the Mother’s nourishment."**

Mencius said: **"The Way of learning and inquiry is nothing else but seeking to restore one’s lost heart."**

The distinction between the Sage and the commoner lies in a single moment—
the Dao is not distant, is it?
The method is not complex, is it?
If one seeks to cultivate the Dao,
yet does not seek it in the **Primordial** and **Later-Heaven** interval where **qi** is pure and unmanifest,
where else should they exert their efforts?
Thus it is said:
**"The Mysterious Mother, the Mysterious Mother—
the true Mysterious Mother!
Not in the heart, nor in the kidneys.
Trace the origin of life and the reception of fate—
do not blame the **Heavenly Mechanism** for being fully drained!"**

What is the origin of life and the reception of fate?
Reflect upon this,
and the Dao is already half attained.

First, it is spoken of that the Sage transcends learning.
Having attained **constant joy** and **inner stillness**,
they bear no worries.
In daily life and practice,
all returns to the **Primordial Heaven** of **mixed chaos**.
Uncarved and unpolished,
unstained and unsoiled—
this is what is meant by:
**"Gazing upward, its heights are boundless,
making it impossible to measure—
truly beyond reach!"**
Yet though their cultivation reaches such extremes,
the true divide lies only in the distinction between **reverence** and **heedlessness**.

Can one fear its unfathomable depth
and still hesitate to proceed?
Yanzi said:
**"Who was Shun? Who am I?
Even those with deeds are such as this!"**
This is no falsehood.

Yet it is not the **empty foolishness** of idle learning,
nor the absence of worldly achievements.
The Sage attains **self-clarification of virtue** and **renews the people**,
enabling all living beings to **feast on virtue** and **drink harmony**,
to **play and frolic in the radiant Heaven beneath the sun and moon**.
What Dao is this?
The Dao of **utter sincerity** and **exhausting one’s inherent nature**—
the **nature of humanity** and the **nature of things**.

Ah!
To exhaust one’s nature to this extent—
what further learning is there?
It is but the **nourishment of the Mother’s qi**.

**Chapter 21: The Vessel of Confucian Virtue**

The **vessel of Confucian virtue** follows only the Dao.
The Dao, as a thing,
is **dim and indistinct**,
**indistinct and vague**.
Vague and indistinct—
yet within, there are **forms**.
Indistinct and dim—
yet within, there are **things**.
**Deep and dark**—
yet within, there is **essence**.
Its essence is most true—
yet within, there is **faith**.

From ancient times to now,
its name never fades—
it **pervades all beings**.

How do I know the state of all beings?
**By this.**
The **vessel of Confucian virtue** is the **Mysterious Pass** itself.
The ancients said:
**"The Mysterious Pass—one orifice—
unites Heaven and Earth.
Within it lies the **cavern of divine qi**,
named the **Kan-Li Essence**."**
And again:
**"The Mysterious Pass—one orifice—
the grand Dao’s gate.
Here, lead is transmuted, and cinnabar is forged."**
*The Classic of Changes_ says:
**"This law of the two orifices,
the gold-qi also follows."**
Hence, the Dao speaks of **"the Mysterious Mother’s Gate"**, the Confucian scholars of **"the Gate of Rightness and Principle"**, and the Buddhists of **"the Gate of Non-Duality"**.

In sum, all are the **vessel of Confucian virtue** that can contain:
Heaven, Earth, humanity, and all things—
all arise from within it.
It is neither **void** nor **non-void**,
nor **non-Dao**—
**void and Dao are one**.
Void and Dao are inseparable.
Without void, there is no Dao;
without Dao, there is no void.
Thus it is said:
**"It follows only the Dao."**
If one seeks the Dao,
and abandons this **void vessel**—
where else should they go?

But if one remains in mere **voidness without form**,
it is **empty foolishness**—
how then can the scholar gather **medicinal herbs** or forge **cinnabar**?
They must **hold emptiness while encompassing utter reality**,
**be void yet contain all things**—
only then does it not become a **one-sided learning**.

The practitioner, having cast aside all **ten thousand entanglements**,
nurtures stillness for a moment,
and **observes this orifice**—
vague as if without substance,
indistinct as if with form.
In the **depth of emptiness and sincerity of stillness**,
the **divine mechanism** moves—
the **formless bears form**.

This is the **radiance of the Primordial** within,
the **flux of wood and fire**’s **forms**—
the **awakening of the subtle yang** in its time.

Again, with this **divine light’s momentary movement**,
one **contains the radiance of the eyes** and gazes downward—
vague as if aware,
indistinct as if without knowledge—
within, the **yang substance** stirs—
this is the **Primordial radiance** first intersecting with the **Kan Palace**.

At that time, the **qi mechanism** is weak,
unable to be gathered—
only the **two methods** of **adjusting and gathering the Buddha’s law**,
and **refining the lurking yin-qi**,
can meet within the **qi cavern**.
**Adjusting and gathering** is one method,
**returning to the furnace for nourishment** is another.

Following the law in practice,
without pause—
**fire enters the water’s depths**,
**gold is born within the water**,
**obscure and dark**—
one knows not its limits—
this is when the **divine qi** intersects,
and the **Kan-Li Essence** is generated.

Yet when the **true essence** is born,
the body stands as if **erect against a wall**,
the mind is like **cold ashes**—
naturally, the whole body feels **softened and joyous**,
the **precise qi** of limbs and organs
all return to the **Mysterious Orifice**.
Within, there is great **faith**—
