To save the world and rescue the endangered—
What cause is there for pride?

For the proud love to kill.
Even if boldness leads to chaos,
Plunging the people into fire and water,
Yet if the heart harbors threefold deceit,
Though no killing occurs, the seed of slaughter lies within—
This is not the **Dao/Way**.

Know this: war is a last resort for the sage,
Even if no soldiers are lost, no generals fallen,
No civilians harmed, no creatures slain,
It is still not the path of lasting peace.
How much less so for those unworthy of the sage’s approval?
Thus, if war must be waged, let it be without excess.

The *Odes* say:
*"Exhort the ruler not to seek the path of feudal lords,
For every general’s triumph rests on ten thousand bones."*

Reflect on this: war is perilous, battle is a dire omen,
Not the **Dao/Way** of nurturing life.

What of prolonged hostilities, where guest and host clash,
Dragging on endlessly, exhausting troops and treasury,
Alienating subjects and inciting grievance?
If it can be stopped but is not, how can it benefit the state?

Or when armies face entrenched defenses,
Years pass in supply lines,
A single moment of negligence—
The enemy seizes the pass,
Cuts off provisions—
Soldiers flee at the first rumor,
Troops scatter at the first sign,
Once mighty forces now reduced to ashes—
What wonder is there in this?
For *"when things reach their peak, they grow old,"*
Such is the inevitable course.

War is used to punish the **Dao/Way**-less with **Dao/Way**.
Yet if one delights in war and strife,
Claiming to pacify the people only to harm them,
To suppress rebellion only to breed it—
This is the ultimate **non-Dao/Way**.

To punish the **Dao/Way**-less while acting without **Dao/Way**—
How is this better than cultivating fields to repel bandits,
Resting arms and fostering goodwill among neighbors?
Early prevention is far superior.

As when **Shun** subdued the **Three Miao**—
The Miao people resisted stubbornly,
Yet Shun did not rely on arms but on **virtue/power**,
Dancing the *Ganyu* at the hall’s steps,
And in seventy days, the Miao submitted in harmony.
This was **威** without coercion, **武** without force—
Far surpassing mere coercion and arms.
Do those in high station know this?

This speaks of the **Dao/Way** of **fire-refinement**,
Of **symbolic talismans**, **gathering**, and **alchemical transformation**—
All deliberate action.
Compared to waging war and defeating the enemy,
This is a far graver matter, not to be taken lightly.

As when **Zengzi** tempered himself in battle,
Or **Zisi** warned of strict discipline in war—
Only thus can one transform **qi** and **spirit**,
Restoring the original, pristine state.
Like the unbroken purity of youth,
Unbroken, unharmed,
One may act in accordance with the times,
Follow the **Dao/Way** without partiality,
Free from peril or danger,
Ascending to the realm of **spiritual transformation**.

But for those already broken or aged,
When **post-heaven** lead and mercury are exhausted,
Where does life depend?
Then must one resort to **enchanting the tortoise** or **summoning the phoenix**—
Striking the bamboo flute, plucking the zither—
Only then may **immortal herbs** and **elixir plants** be gathered,
**Swords of jade** and **alchemical medicines** refined,
Transforming the mortal body into nothingness,
Nurturing the **sacred embryo** at the **spiritual pass**.

Yet the **fire-alchemy** requires the utmost secrecy—
Without a true master’s oral transmission,
One cannot penetrate its deepest mysteries.
Even if the **heavenly secrets** are obtained,
If inner **virtue/power** and outer cultivation are lacking,
Still, the **spirits and **Heaven** will not favor them.

Only with a **empty heart** seeking the **Dao/Way**,
Accumulating **virtue/power** and refining **function**,
In all things without guilt,
In all places embracing **ren**,
With **humility** and **gentleness**,
With **patience** and **yielding**—
**Spirit** abides in **qi**,
**Qi** clings to **spirit**,
Unbroken, ever-flowing,
Rooted in the deepest **primordial foundation**.

Never to be forgotten in haste,
Never to be abandoned in distraction—
At times remembered with urgency,
Never to cease.

Thus it is said: *"Those who aid the sovereign with the Dao/Way do not rely on arms to subdue the world."* Even if one attains perfect refinement, yet after the fire-alchemical tempering, when all coarse *qi* has been purged and true *qi* has yet to accumulate, the result will be indeterminate—neither fully present nor fully absent, subtle and difficult to discern. Moreover, there are those who, without cultivation, scatter their *qi*, and the more they refine, the more their *qi* disperses—all due to the mind’s restless fluctuations, like creeping vines that resist eradication. Hence it is said: *"Where the master treads, brambles spring up."* How much more so when the divine fire is applied, for yin *qi* resists retention, and long-standing ailments or childhood infirmities are driven forth—some exiting through sweat, turbid urine, or waste, while others manifest as external sores or toxins, varying in form. The cultivator must not mistake these for illness; only a tranquil mind can transmute *qi*.

Thus, the Dao of self-cultivation must transform the common body into a jade-like one, the turbid form into a golden one. Never be alarmed—for alarm stirs the posterior-heaven’s vulgar fire and gravely injures the primordial *qi*. Hence it is said: *"After a great campaign, there will surely be years of calamity."* Those skilled in warfare value decisiveness; those skilled in fire-alchemy value swiftness and subtlety. Hence it is said: *"Decisive and complete."* When the cultivator reaches the stage of pure *yang* transformation, they must not rely on their attainment, supposing themselves unfettered by hunger or thirst, able to move like the wind, requiring no stove in winter or fan in summer, displaying marvelous feats beyond ordinary men, and thus deeming themselves strong. To boast of such strength is to again agitate the posterior-heaven’s vulgar fire; if not met with external censure, the internal elixir will surely falter. Worse still, if one harbors a self-aggrandizing spirit—even if they complete ten months of gestation and three years of nurturing, fulfilling every task with precision, yet still pride themselves and boast, arrogantly overbearing others—they are far from the vessel of the Dao. Even if successful in their endeavors, they will surely meet ruin in their debts. The peril of collapse is beyond reckoning!

What then of those who, relying on their strength, fail to cultivate humility and sincerity? Though they may practice virtue and concentrate on the Dao, they remain like rigid trees—strong yet lifeless—while the supple and fragile retain vitality. Day by day, year by year, as time passes, their vital essence wanes. How could they long enjoy the joys of mortal life? Hence it is said: *"When things are at their peak, decay begins."* By this reasoning, those who exalt themselves only bring themselves low; those who boast invite peril. How un-Dao!

Better to discard the rigid heart, maintaining simplicity and steadiness, adhering to principle while letting go of contrivance, yielding to Heaven’s arrangement—thus achieving strength without force, the Dao without effort. Here lies the peril of the initial fire-alchemical stages; let the scholar beware.

---
**Chapter Thirty-One: Tranquility and Simplicity as Supreme**

Warfare is an inauspicious instrument; men detest it, and thus those who follow the Dao avoid it. The noble man, at home, values the left; in war, the right. Warfare is an inauspicious instrument, not the tool of the noble. Used only when unavoidable, tranquility and simplicity are paramount. To triumph yet take no pleasure in it—those who do are but lovers of killing. He who delights in killing cannot prevail under Heaven.

Thus, auspicious matters are honored on the left; inauspicious on the right. Hence the left general is stationed left, the right general right, and funeral rites are observed. Many lives lost are mourned with tears; victory is treated with funeral solemnity. The sage governs Heaven and Earth with virtue and morality as supreme, law and teaching secondary. Only when forced to raise armies, wield arms, and send eldest sons to war—where younger sons bear the dead—does the *Yijing* warn: *"This is ominous, this is perilous."*

How much worse for those who revel in the tiger’s gaze, the eagle’s fierceness, who display arms on the battlefield and wield might in the barracks, who see conquest as gain and warfare as skill! Such warfare is not merely chaotic—it is the finest of arms. For the finest arms are inauspicious instruments. The ancients called halting the blade *wu* (martial virtue); these men call exhausting warfare skill—hardly the tool of the sage.

The noble’s true instruments are the Dao, virtue, and the love of life while abhorring death. To speak of arms is to speak of slaughter. When the sounds of killing resound and the land trembles, even chickens and dogs are unsettled—what then of the horror inflicted? Even earthworms cling to life, ants fear death; the most insignificant creatures recoil. How much more so for men? Thus, those who follow the Dao avoid such places.

All things value *yang* over *yin*—the left is *yang*, the breath of life; the right is *yin*, the seed of slaughter. Hence the noble, in ordinary times, honors the left. Only in war does he abandon the left for the right, revealing how little he esteems arms.

Even if one eliminates tyrants, punishes the wicked, and raises righteous banners in orderly ranks, warfare remains an inauspicious instrument, one the sage avoids. In times of peace, a state needs no martial preparations. When borders cry out in alarm and rebellions flare, and only then must arms be raised, the discipline must be strict, rewards and punishments unyielding. Where the army passes, the people offer wine and grain; where the soldiers march, the fields remain undisturbed. Thus does one conquer the rigid with flexibility, the fierce with virtue—evident in such conduct.

Let there be no display of martial might, no boasting of strategy. Only tranquility and non-action, ease and naturalness, suffice.

Though stationed amid the tumult of war, yet maintaining the orderly and tranquil demeanor of disciplined ranks.
With this approach, what fortress cannot be taken?
With this approach, what enemy cannot be crushed?
Its victory is inevitable.

Yet even so, such victory is but the common fate of arms—how dare one carve triumphs upon bronze and stone, or record exploits upon banners and scrolls, and thus claim eternal renown for posterity?
If such renown is sought, then one must endure the suffering of countless lives to achieve a single man’s glory.
To lack the virtue of nurturing life while harboring the intent to take it—how can this be justified?

He who delights in killing is cruel of heart, his laws harsh and unyielding.
He cannot embrace breadth to contain others, ever petty and self-righteous.
Such a man cannot prevail in the world.
If he were to prevail, the common folk would be utterly destroyed.

In ancient times, auspicious matters were observed on the left, inauspicious on the right.
Yet the *piān jiāngjūn* (偏将军), a subordinate commander, is placed on the left—
while the *shàng jiāngjūn* (上将军), the supreme commander, is stationed on the right.
For the supreme commander wields the authority of slaughter, while subordinate generals must obey his orders and cannot flaunt their martial might—
this is but the military equivalent of funeral rites.

How much more refined than opposing armies with national decrees,
commanding soldiers to sing dirges as if escorting the dead!
How meticulous and reverent!

And what of the saying, *"When many are slain, weep with sorrow"*?
It is clear that warfare wounds the natural compassion for life,
and arises only from dire necessity.
Thus, those who mourn ancient battlefields see corpses filling the moats,
blood swelling the ravines—
the heavens and earth seem to weep,
the grasses and trees to sigh,
and what of mortal men?
Even if war is won, the chieftains submit,
the people find peace—
yet reflecting inwardly,
one sees beneath the heavens countless children
scattered by blade and arrow,
half exiled, half dead—
and the heart is filled with grief!
How dare one return in triumph,
singing praises of valor,
and boast of one’s prowess?

To ponder this and yet show no remorse,
but instead wear a mournful countenance,
treating it as a funeral rite—
this is the way.

Mencius said:
*"If I excel in arraying troops and waging war, this is a great sin.
Only when the sovereign loves virtue does the world have no rival."*
And again:
*"One does not subdue the world through the advantage of arms."*

This proves that the martial and mighty do not kill,
and the virtuous are unconquerable—
truly the ideal for an age of peace,
while warfare is not the constant path of the sage,
nor the ambition of the king.

This is like using fire at the furnace—
an act of necessity for the weak and infirm,
to revive decay and mend what is broken.
How dare one boast of cleverness or novelty,
and claim such deeds as merit?

Those who follow false paths—
retreat and advance with talismans,
gather herbs and refine elixirs,
all contrived methods of action—
mistakenly believe them the Dao of immortals.
They err greatly.

Yet for the vigorous and young,
no need for such alchemical labor—
one may attain the elixir naturally.
But for the aged and debilitated,
whose vital essence and desires have long been corrupted,
without fierce refining and urgent transformation,
the essence remains unrefined,
desires unchecked.
With a polluted body,
how can one practice the Way of effortless spontaneity?

It is like welcoming honored guests in a filthy house—
impossible.
One must first purge the defilements within,
until form and perception dissolve,
the dust of attachment is uprooted,
and the original true nature manifests freely.

Even if one strives in cultivation,
one must remain at ease and unconstrained,
lest artificial fire harm the innate.
Thus, the highest sage values tranquility above all,
and victory without vanity.

Otherwise, there is only the artificial without the natural,
mere common *qi* without true *qi*.
A single flame of passion—
how long can it endure?
Thus it is said:
*"To glorify such things is to delight in killing."*

To delight in killing is to harbor slaughter in one’s heart—
how can such a man govern the world
and receive its blessings?
His failure to prevail is certain.

Thus, those who cultivate must,
though they act,
nurture a heart of compassion and pity,
a mind of humility and gentleness—
only then does the artificial contain the natural.

The ancients adjusted their methods without rigid formulas,
measured their medicines without precise weights,
acting in harmony with Heaven,
following their true nature.
Though they acted,
they did no harm.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Two: Knowing When to Stop Brings No Peril**

The Dao is ever nameless.
Though crude simplicity is small,
the world dares not submit to it.
If lords and kings could guard it,
all things would come of their own accord.

Heaven and Earth unite,
and sweet dew descends—
no one commands it,
yet all is evenly nourished.

When names first arose,
and names were established,
then one must know when to stop—
knowing when to stop brings no peril.

Compare the Dao in the world
to rivers and valleys flowing into the sea.
The Dao is originally boundless and formless,
yet all phenomena arise within it—
present in all beings,
inherent in all things.
Before humans existed,
this was their origin.
After humans emerged,
this remains their root.
Though subtle and hidden,
it cannot for an instant be separated—
for if it were, all things would shatter.

Thus it is said:
*"The Dao is ever nameless."*

For those who seek the Way,
names must be given.
A grain of millet, three inches of yang spirit—
dwelling in the mysterious palace,
pervading the Dharma realm—
just as the Original Simplicity is whole.
Though small in form,
its function is vast.
All things in the world rely on it as sovereign—
who would dare to be its subject?

Even as lords and kings hold the power of life and death,
and rule over the people,
who would dare not obey their commands?

The person who attains this Original Simplicity,
with unwavering reverence,
holds fast and never loses it—
though lands and realms may differ,
none would fail to cross seas and scale mountains to come,
let alone those nearby.

Thus it is seen:
All under the heavens submit in homage,
all due to the power of this Original Simplicity to preserve.

Yet once humans are born,
their **qi** and nature constrain them,
desires cloud them—
few retain the Dao in its entirety.

If one seeks to embrace Original Simplicity and remain whole and pure,
only by imitating the heavens and earth can this be done.

The **qi** of heaven descends,
the **qi** of earth ascends—
just as in humans, the **Kan** and **Li** energies intermingle,
water and fire in harmony.

When heaven and earth unite,
sweet dew descends like pearls,
naturally falling into the **Zhong Gong**—
this is the **Yin-Yang** balancing,
the **sun and moon** sharing the same palace—
who commands it?
Who directs it?

All arises from the Dao as sovereign.
Yet what name does the Dao truly have?

Some say *"True Lead"* (真鉛),
others say *"Golden Elixir"* (金丹).
The ancients coined these names
only for the sake of later cultivators.

Once cultivators know its name,
they must then seek its reality.

「From the very moment the **Yin-Yang** energies intermingle,
a single point settles within the **Yellow Court** (黃庭).
Then one must cease its movement and remain steadfast,
settle its dwelling and remain unshaken—
thus the **Great Dao** endures perpetually.

Having known where to halt,
there is an inherent master that does not change,
so why would it suffer the humiliation of birth and death?

Thus, it is seen that the Dao disperses outward,
vast and boundless, unfathomable and indistinct.
When gathered inward,
it becomes indistinct and chaotic,
retiring and concealing itself in profound mystery.

If scholars do not grasp the **root principle** (統宗),
they lack a foundation to return to,
and thus scatter without measure.
Even establishing oneself proves impossible,
how then could one reach others?

Hence it is said:
*"The Dao in the world is like rivers and valleys to the ocean."*

Only by attaining the **root principle** does one achieve greatness.

Zi Si (子思) spoke of the **Way** of the **Junzi** (君子) as costly yet hidden—
this is the **one source** giving rise to myriad transformations,
where myriad transformations return to the **one source** of the **Way**.

In this chapter, the **sweet dew** (甘露) is the **True Lead** (**Kan**) and **True Mercury** (**Li**) merging before descending.
**"Knowing the halt"** (知止) is the **divine qi** gathering in the **Yellow Court** (中宫).

The **Taoist Canon** (太上) speaks of **perfect unity** (俱浑),
but I shall elaborate further:
Those who wish to cultivate **life and death** (性命),
must first clarify **True Lead** and **True Mercury**.

The ancients said:
*"Mercury is the inherent substance of my family,
Lead is the elixir of immortality from another."*

If one speaks only of **heart-nature** (心性),
there is no way to grasp it,
so the ancient **true immortals** (真仙) and **true ones** (真人) borrowed the name **Mercury**.

This **Mercury** is not the other—
it is the **heart’s essence**, the **soul’s elixir**—
borne from **nasal mucus, saliva, seminal fluid, vital essences, blood, and fluids**,
from the **postnatal turbid residues** (後天阴滓物),
illuminated by **divine fire** (神火) descending for a long time,
refined into the most **sublime essence**.

This **sublime essence** is the **host of original nature** (元性所寄).
For **original nature** is inherently pure and clear,
untainted by the slightest dust,
equal to the **Great Void**.

It is not the **postnatal essence** (後天之精) of the physical body,
nor the **vital breath** (氣) produced by **civilized and martial fires** (文武火),
nor the **divine light** (神光) that illuminates it—
otherwise, the **sublime essence** would not refine,
and the **sublime nature** would lack its foundation.

Thus, those who forge the **elixir** (丹),
must first **refine essence into breath** (炼精化气),
as said:
*"This essence is not the common man’s essence,
but the **Jade Emperor’s** (玉皇) saliva from within his mouth."*

The **Jade Emperor** is likened to the **heart**,
and this **sublime essence** is the **saliva**.

Once **essence is refined into Mercury** (精生汞化),
the **sublime essence** descends into the **Kan palace** (坎宫),
and the **True Yang** (真陽) ascends as well,
uniting within the **Yellow Court’s** (黃庭) **inner cauldron** (內釜).
I condense my **divine qi** (神气) here,
and over time, **True Lead** emerges, flourishing and manifesting—
this is what is called **"obtaining the medicine"** (得藥).

Yet the **sublime essence** is **True Water** (真水),
and the **True Yang** is **True Qi** (真氣),
**True Qi** being **True Lead** (鉛).

**Mercury** is **essence**,
**Lead** is **common**—
both are **postnatal substances** (後天) with form and appearance,
capable only of **nurturing vitality** (順而生男育女),
not of **eternal life’s** great medicine.

Only when **Mercury** descends and **Lead** ascends,
uniting in the **Yellow Court**,
condensing **divine qi** and regulating **breath**,
does one feel, in an instant,
a **boiling, surging** sensation—
like mist and smoke,
like tides and waves—
then does it become **True Lead**,
worthy as the foundation for **elixir forging** (炼丹之本),
as said:
*"Kan and Li unite to obtain the medicine."*

Then, **Yang fire** (陽火) and **Yin elixir** (陰符) are stirred,
ascending from the **tail vein** (尾闾) to the **muddy pellet** (泥丸).
The **muddy pellet**, long storing **Yin essence** (陰精),
combines with this **True Lead qi** (真鉛之氣),
forming a single entity—
this is what is called:
*"**Qian and Kun** unite to forge the **elixir** (結丹)."*

When **Yang qi** ascends to the **muddy pellet**,
what manifestation does it show?
A **refreshing clarity** in the head and eyes,
unlike the usual **dullness and dizziness**,
as if the wind disperses clouds and the sky clears—
a distinct transformation,
this is **True Mercury**.

The **Mercury** of before was still **common Mercury**,
unfit to nourish the **embryo of immortality** (仙胎).

When **Lead** and **Mercury** meet in the **muddy pellet**,
the **postnatal essence** and **postnatal qi** (凡精凡氣)
merge and transform,
no longer seen as **Lead** or **Mercury**,
but as a **pure, cool, and tranquil** taste—
refined into **sweet dew divine water** (甘露神水),
fragrant and sweet to the palate,
unlike the usual **coarse essence and turbid qi**,
this is what the ancients called:
*"**Amrita** (醍醐) anointing the **crown"** (灌顶).*

Swallowed from the **upper palate** (上腭),
it enters the **Yellow Court** to be **nurtured in warmth** (溫養),
and is then **sealed and secured** (封固).

This **True Essence**, once born,
softens the entire body like silk,
neither sleep nor wakefulness is desired;
while the usual **agitated body and mind** (動蕩之身心)
now rest in **perfect stillness** (浑然湛然),
unmoved and unshaken—
naturally attaining **stillness** (安所止) and **halt** (得所止),
what peril could there be?

This realm requires **Great Stillness and Great Clarity** (大静大定).
If one **harvests** (采取) with **single-minded focus** (一意凝注),
not scattering in the slightest,
the ancients called this:
*"**Non-harvesting harvesting** (不采之采) surpasses harvesting."*

Cultivators advance one step and see one step’s effects.
If there is no **true foundation** (真实处),
the practice remains incomplete.

When **heavenly mechanisms** (天机) are fully revealed,
one may **attain verification** (取证) by oneself.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Three: Knowing Others is Wisdom**
Wisdom in knowing others is wisdom,
self-awareness is clarity.

Overcoming others requires strength,
self-mastery is power.

Contentment brings wealth,
unyielding resolve brings purpose.

Losing nothing ensures longevity,
dying yet not perishing is true life.

The **Way** of cultivating oneself
lies only in **life and death** (性命).
If one seeks to **fulfill nature and establish fate** (尽性立命),
one must first **preserve the heart and nurture nature** (存心养性),
**safeguard life and maintain form** (保命全形).

I **cultivate** and **forge** it,
**accumulate** and **pile** it—
then **original nature** (本性) endures and **heavenly fate** (天命) is mine.

Yet to **fulfill the heart** (尽心),
one must first **know nature** (知性),
to perceive **the foundation of human life** (人生之本),
purely **heavenly principle** (天理),
unmixed with **human desires** (人欲)—
this is called **insightful wisdom** (睿智).

From this, **restraining desire and preserving principle** (遏欲存理),
constantly **self-examining** (省察),
ever **vigilant** (防闲),
ensuring **private desires are utterly purified** (私欲尽净),
**heavenly principle flows freely** (天理流行),
**piercing the original face** (洞见本来面目),
**lucid and unobstructed** (惺惺不昧),
**ever-clear** (了了常明)—
this is **perfect clarity and wondrous awakening** (圆明妙觉).

This is not **external speculation**,
but **one’s own true knowing**—
no other can comprehend it.

Hence it is said:
*"Knowing others is wisdom,
self-awareness is clarity."*

To **establish the vital mandate** (立命),
one must first **refine oneself** (炼己).
Refining oneself has two aspects:
One is **material desires** (物欲)—
if **material desires are not eliminated** (物欲不除),
**heavenly purity will not manifest** (天真难现).
To abandon these and seek **elixir and alchemical immortality** (药结丹) instead
is like a fine grain of millet mixed with weeds and tares—
without first uprooting the weeds (芟荑),
the good and the bad will inevitably grow together (苗莠并植).
Only by first **conquering human desires** (胜人欲),
constantly **maintaining and preserving** (常操常存),
can one achieve **steadfastness in practice** (有定守),
though not necessarily **steadfastness in power** (有定力).
Hence it is said:
*"To conquer others is to have power."*

The second is **constitutional qi** (气质)—
if **constitutional qi is not transformed** (气质不化),
how can the body **become firm** (身何由固)?
Thus, one **peels away the flesh to preserve the fluids** (剥肤存液),
**peels away the fluids to preserve the spirit** (剥液存神),
**peels away the spirit to return to the void** (剥神还虚),
layer by layer, stripping away until
one can **align with the Dao in truth** (与道合真).
If not **qi is robust and spirit is vigorous** (精固气壮),
how could one **vanquish all pathogenic forces** (战胜群阴),
**sweep away the six external evils** (扫除六贼),
so that **one’s inner and outer self becomes luminous as jade** (一身内外，精莹如玉),
**transmutes the mortal form** (变化凡躯),
and **refines into an immortal body** (炼成仙体)?
Hence it is said:
*"To conquer oneself is to grow strong."*

When refining oneself is thus complete (炼己了矣),
the vital mandate is **established** (命已立矣).
Does the **work end here** (功不于此尽乎)?
Does the **Dao reach fulfillment here** (道不于此成乎)?
Though it may seem so,
**igniting the furnace has its time** (起火有时),
**extinguishing the furnace has its moment** (止火有候).
If one does not **practice extinguishing the furnace** (止火之功) when the time is right (火足之时),
the **essence will move with the stirring of qi** (精必随气之动而动),
thus one knows that **stopping to nourish the elixir** (止养丹)
is like a poor man **accumulating wealth until he becomes rich** (积财而富),
**ever-awakened with abundance** (常觉有余).

Having known how to **extinguish the furnace** (止火),
one must also **advance the furnace to nourish the elixir** (进火以养丹),
**retreat the furnace to warm the elixir** (退火以温丹).
Only those with **resolute determination** (有志之士)
can **persistently and meticulously** (绵绵密密)
**inhale and exhale without interruption** (不二之息).
The *Classic of Changes* (《易》) says:
*"Heaven moves with vigor,
the virtuous person **strengthens himself without ceasing** (自强不息)."*

The **resolute determination** spoken of is indeed the resolve of the **willful** (有志之士).
After this **nourishment and tempering** (温养),
merely **calm the mind and rest** (安神息),
letting nature take its course (一任天然),
without ever losing one’s grounding (无一时一刻之失所).
As **Master Zisi’s son** said:
*"The utmost sincerity knows no cessation;
without cessation, it endures."* (至诚无息，不息则久)
This is the very meaning.

As for the **transformation of the mortal form** (凡身脱化) and the **ascension of the true spirit** (真灵飞升),
it is much like the death of an ordinary man.
But the death of an ordinary man means the **dispersion of the spirit** (神散);
while the death of the **sage** (圣人) means the spirit remains whole (神完).
Though the form dies, the spirit lives on—
how could it not share in the longevity of Heaven and Earth? (与天地同寿耶)
This speaks of **mastering human nature and conquering desires** (知人道、胜人欲),
still rooted in the pursuit of **exhausting principle and perfecting nature** (穷理尽性).

Only when **nature is revealed and the mind illuminated** (性见心明),
when one **penetrates the original source** (洞彻本原),
when **spirit is strong and qi robust** (神强气壮),
and when **all yin impurities are purged** (煅尽阴滓),
can one **fulfill nature and establish fate** (了性立命).
For **nature and fate are not two separate paths** (性命不分二途),
but return to the **primordial chaos before differentiation** (混沌未开之天),
where **yin-spirit is extinguished and yang-spirit perfected** (阴神尽灭，阳神完成).

Even in the stages of **refining essence into qi** (炼精化气)
and **refining qi into spirit** (炼气化神),
there remains the **practice of extinguishing the furnace to nourish the elixir** (止火养丹).
*The Awakening to Reality* (《悟真》) says:
*"If one clings to fullness without restraint,
one risks sudden ruin and humiliation."* (若也持盈未已心，不免一朝遭殆辱)
This is precisely the warning.

**Refining essence into qi** marks the **beginning of gestation** (入胎之始);
**refining qi into spirit** marks the **completion of gestation** (成胎之终).
If one does not **know how to extinguish the furnace** (不知止火),
then **qi will not enter the womb** (气不入于胎).
Though essence is refined into qi,
it may yet **revert to essence through the movement of qi** (因气之动而复化为精).
Moreover, if one does not **know how to extinguish the furnace**,
then **spirit will not congeal in emptiness** (神不凝于虚空).
Though qi is refined into spirit,
it may yet **revert to qi through the movement of spirit** (因神之动而复化为气).
Thus it is said:
*"He who knows sufficiency is always sufficient,
and thus never meets disgrace."* (知足常足，终身不辱)
The words of the **Supreme Sage** (太上) are no deception.

When **spirit attains Great Stillness** (神归大定),
**qi follows into Great Stillness** (气亦因之大定).
A hundred years pass like a single day.
A wandering thought risks **losing the elixir** (走丹).
Such a burden is long and arduous—
only the **resolute and willful** (强行有志者)
can **steadfastly remain grounded** (常止其所),
enduring without decay over time (历久而不敝).

When the **threefold alchemical fire is perfected** (三昧火化),
one **ascends to the celestial heights** (立上凌霄),
living even in death—
their spirit suffices to share in the longevity of Heaven and Earth. (精神足与天地同寿)
The **beginning and end of the Golden Elixir** (金丹始终) are fulfilled here.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Four: The Sage Never Claims Greatness**

The **Dao** flows vast and boundless (大道汜兮),
yet cannot be confined to left or right.
All things rely on it for life,
yet it does not demand recognition for its deeds.
It accomplishes without claiming ownership.
It **clothes all things yet does not rule them** (衣被万物而不为主).
**Constantly without desire** (常无欲),
it may be called **small**.
All things return to it,
yet it does not rule them—
thus it may be called **great** (可名于大).
Therefore, the **sage never claims greatness** (终不自为大),
and thus **achieves true greatness** (能成其大).

The **Dao** is profound and boundless,
its expanse without end.
As the *Odes* say:
*"Left, left—what the lord approves;
Right, right—what the gentleman possesses."* (左之左之，君之宜之；右之右之，君子有之)
From this, one sees how the **Dao adapts to the times** (随时取用),
fulfilling all without fail,
nourishing all without measure.
What **Dao** is this?
It is the **origin of all things’ generation** (万物生生之本).

The **Dao** dwells in Heaven and Earth,
and all things draw from it for life,
yet it does not shun their chaos.
Because the **Dao lacks nothing** (道无不足),
all things thrive without obstruction (其生无不畅).
Though the **path of fulfillment** (生之遂之之道) is sufficient,
and things rely on it to complete themselves,
it is as if they **generate and fulfill themselves** (若物之自生自遂),
while the **Dao does not appear to act** (道不见为有).
What then is its accomplishment?
Though unnamed, it **permeates Heaven and Earth** (天地之大),
spanning the **four seas** (四海之遥),
covering all without exclusion (无人不被其涵濡),
encompassing all without remainder (无物不荷其帡幪).
Let things **generate and nurture themselves** (自生自育),
while the **Dao seems unaware of birth or nurturing**.
It **universally protects all** (普护一切),
**embracing all existence** (包涵万有).
Truly, it **"clothes all things yet does not claim life"** (衣被万物而不以为生).

When the **Dao’s work is complete** (道之功成),
its vastness is beyond naming (浩浩乎无可名也).
**Constantly without desire** (常无欲),
it is **always pure and clear** (常清常净)—
the **true and eternal Dao** (真常之道).
When viewed as **small** (就其小而名之),
even the **humblest blade of grass or tree** (一草一木之微)
is not excluded.
It **encompasses all existence** (弥纶万有),
leaving no gap unfilled (无隙可寻),
**undivided and complete** (浑然一团),
transmuting even the finest dust (纤尘悉化).
This is the meaning of **"nothing can break it"** (小莫能破之义).
Thus it is said:
*"Constantly without desire, it may be called small."* (常无欲可名于小)

When viewed as **great** (就其大而名之),
it **spreads across Heaven and Earth** (铺天匝地),
**nurturing all life** (统育群生);
it **endures from antiquity to the present** (亘古及今),
**embracing all things** (包含万夤),
yet **nothing is excluded, no one left out** (无一物之不归，无一夫之或外).
This is the meaning of **"nothing can contain it"** (大莫能载之旨).
Thus it is said:
*"All things return to it, yet it does not rule them—
thus it may be called great."* (万物归焉而不为主，可名于大)

How **profound and hidden** is the **sage’s Dao** (何其费而隐哉)!
The **sage aligns with the True Dao** (与道合真).
At rest, they **guard the Center and embrace Oneness** (守中抱一),
merging with the **serene Heaven** (浑同于穆之天).
In action, they **follow things and respond to conditions** (因物随缘),
embodying the **symbol of timely conduct** (俨寓时行之象).
Only Heaven is great;
only the **sage follows its example** (惟圣则之).
The **sage’s greatness is as Heaven’s** (圣实与天同其大).
Yet the **sage never claims greatness** (然圣终不以为大).
Only by **not claiming greatness** can one **achieve true greatness** (能成其大)—
this is what makes the **great sage** (大圣人) so.

This speaks of the **Dao’s vastness** (道之浩浩),
**generating all things with abundance** (生万物而有余),
**clothing all things with sufficiency** (被万物而至足).
Whether small or great,
it **encompasses all within**.
The **sage achieves greatness** (能成其大)
through **rooted cultivation** (修造有本).

Now, let us **delineate the initial practice**:
When **sitting in meditation** (打坐之时),
first **concentrate the spirit** (凝神),
then **regulate the breath** (调息).
Once the **spirit is concentrated** (神已凝了)—
there is no need for **overwhelming righteous qi** (浩然正气),
nor for **utter grandeur and rigidity** (至大至刚),
filling Heaven and Earth.
Only that the **mind is free of agitation** (心无烦恼),
the **intent is unburdened** (意无牵挂),
feeling the **heart like an empty vessel** (心如空器),
utterly devoid of attachment (一点不有).
The **mind melts like ice** (意若冰融),
with **not a single thought arising** (片念不生).
The **body stands erect** (此身耸立),
as if a **mountain steadfast and unmoving** (恍如山岳镇静，不动不摇)—
thus the **spirit-light descends** (神光下照),
illuminating the **qi-caverns** (于气穴之中),
**silently observing** (默视)
as the **yin-ascending qi** (吾阴蹻之气)
and the **palace-of-the-cinnabar-field qi** (绛宫之气)
**converge within the alchemical cauldron** (两相会于丹鼎之中).

「I then use the gentle, warm **spirit-fire** (温温神火) to carefully refine and cook,
with the subtle, yielding **wind** (巽风) softly blowing and exhaling—
naturally, the **essence melts** (精融) and the **qi transforms** (气化).

How do I know this is **refining essence into qi** (炼精化气)?
Before, I did not draw in **external qi** (外来之气),
but relied solely on my **inner heart-spirit** (吾心内之神),
the two harmonizing as one, blending into a unified whole.
Then, the **yin-ascending qi** (坎离) and the **kidney essence** (阴精)
were each scattered, utterly independent,
and even my breathing was not in accord.

When I begin to **retrieve external qi** (收回外气)
to regulate my **internal kidney essence** (制内里阴精),
upon its arrival, the **yin-ascending qi** (阴精) naturally transforms.
The **qi of the heart and kidneys** (上下心肾之气)
then merges into one, naturally settling the **palace-of-the-cinnabar-field** (绛宫) in tranquility,
and the **kidney storehouse** (肾府) in ease.

The **external breath** (外之呼吸)
and the **internal true breath** (内之真息)
merge as one **qi** (合为一气),
like a husband and wife in perfect union—
gathered yet never dispersing.
Day fills, month overflows,
and the **true yang** (真阳) begins to manifest its forms.

This is the clear sign of **transforming qi** (化气).

Once **qi is transformed** (既已化气),
I proceed to **ascend upward** (向上之事).
What is **ascending upward** (向上之事)?
At this time, when **breathing unites** (呼吸合)
and **spirit and qi exchange** (神气交),
it condenses in the **alchemical field** (丹田),
swirling and rising, like a living dragon swimming—
endless transformations occur in a single day.

I merely **concentrate the spirit within** (凝神于中),
**direct the breath outward** (注息于外),
and **listen to its natural course** (听其天然).
Naturally, it reaches **ultimate stillness** (静极)
and then **spontaneous motion** (动),
then **ultimate motion** (动极)
and then **spontaneous stillness** (静)—
this is **refining qi into spirit** (炼气化神).

When **stillness is firmly established** (静定久久),
my **qi becomes more regulated** (我气益调),
and the **swirling essence** (宛转流行于丹田者)
now refined to perfection, feels **both present and absent** (似有似无),
**both moving and still** (若动若静).
The **essence is imperceptible** (精看不觉),
but the **subtle convergence is known** (细会始知).

At this juncture, I must **extinguish all awareness** (务将知觉之心),
**dissolve all thoughts** (百想无存),
**dispel all illusions** (万虚全消).
The **spirit and qi of the alchemical field** (丹田交会之神气)
are then left to **self-stir and self-regulate** (自鼓自调),
**self-warm and self-fortify** (自温自煅).
I merely **maintain emptiness and stillness** (致虚守寂),
**entirely yielding to nature** (纯任自然).
The **spirit enters the qi** (神入气中) yet is unaware,
the **qi surrounds the spirit** (气周神外) yet is unnoticed.

After such a **refinement** (烹炼),
a **fragrant breeze** (香风) naturally arises,
rising to **penetrate the hundred meridians** (上冲百脉),
pervading the body in **scented transformation** (遍体熏蒸).
This is called **spirit giving birth to qi** (神生气).

Again, I sense that **spirit and wisdom grow daily** (精神日长, 智慧日开).
Within the **single mind** (一心),
only a **single breath** (一息) arises from the **alchemical field** (规中),
pure and subtle, refined like jade.

This is called **qi giving birth to spirit** (气生神).

Thus, **spirit and qi nourish and generate each other** (神气交养, 两两相生).
At this stage, it is most fitting to **release all grasping and return to emptiness** (撒手成空),
**neither clinging nor detaching** (不粘不脱),
**as if the spirit exists yet the mind does not** (若有心, 若无意).
This is the **practical essence of refining spirit back to emptiness** (炼神还虚之实际).

These **three practices** (此三件功夫),
though requiring time, can be **attained simultaneously** (一时可行可到).
The **student must follow the Dao** (遵道而行),
not merely reaching **coarse convergence of spirit and qi** (神气粗交)
before abandoning the seat—
nor **leaving upon achieving great union** (神气大交),
when the two are indistinct, **before attaining the realm of effortless, pure, and free stillness** (虚无清净自在之境).

One must **persist in this cultivation** (照此行持),
beginning with **refining essence** (炼精),
until over time the **qi grows strong** (气长) and the **spirit flourishes** (神旺),
transforming into **pure, natural ease** (清净自然).
Then, applying the **refining furnace techniques** (归炉工法),
one aligns with the **Heavenly cycle of filling and emptying** (天地盈虚消息),
and the **four seasons’ qi manifestations** (一年春夏秋冬气象).
Only then is the **great cycle fully complete** (始完全一周).

By **diligently cultivating thus** (工夫照此修持),
my **qi becomes ever more refined** (我气益调),
my **spirit ever more still** (我神益静).
Within, there are **endless transformations** (无穷变化)
and **inexhaustible vitality** (不尽生机).
Through **unceasing, day-and-night practice** (日夜行工),
**continuous and meticulous** (绵绵密密),
**stillness and insight merge** (寂照同归),
and **true qi** (真气) naturally arises,
**ascending to the brain’s marrow** (上朝泥丸),
**descending to the cinnabar storehouse** (下流丹府),
**penetrating the hundred meridians** (透百脉) and **piercing the flesh** (贯肌肤),
**bursting forth with an unstoppable force** (勃然有不可遏之状)—
this is the **path of the River Vehicle** (河车之路),
**naturally open** (自然而通).

I merely **follow its course** (顺其所通)
and **slightly guide it** (略微引起足矣).
Unlike **errant external methods** (旁门左道),
which **forcefully manipulate** (以自家私意空空去运)
or **rigidly control** (死死去行),
I do not **artificially stir or halt** (为之起止)
but **observe its own spontaneous motion and stillness** (观他自动自静).

Over time, the **cinnabar is perfected** (丹成) and the **Dao is established** (道立),
**walking through mists and flying through emptiness** (走雾飞空),
**becoming a companion of Heaven** (与天为徒).
The **sage’s attainment of greatness** (圣人之成其大)
is truly not a **light or easy matter** (轻易也已).

---
**Chapter Thirty-Fifth: To Go Without Harm**

Hold the **Great Symbol** (大象),
and the world will **turn toward it** (天下往).
To go **without harm** (往而无害),
is to **rest in ease and tranquility** (安平泰).
**Delights and baits** (乐与饵)
cause the **passing traveler to stop** (过客止).
The **Dao’s outward expression** (道之出口)
is **plain and without taste** (淡乎其无味).
To **look upon it, it cannot be seen** (视之不可见),
to **listen to it, it cannot be heard** (听之不可闻),
to **use it, it cannot be exhausted** (用之不可既).

What is the **Great Symbol** (大象)?
It is the **Dao that gives birth to Heaven, Earth, humans, and things** (生天生地生人生物之大道).
Because it **encompasses all** (无所不包),
it is called the **Great Symbol**.

What **Symbol** (象) is there to hold?
It is **the Boundless** (无极).
Yet **without a symbol is the symbol** (无象为象),
and what **holding** (执) is there to grasp?
**Without grasping is grasping** (亦无执为执).
Thus, one does not **contradict the Dao** (斯于道不悖).

If one can **always maintain and preserve** (常操常存),
**never forgetting nor abandoning** (勿忘勿助),
then the **Great Symbol is held** (大象执焉),
and the **Great Dao is present** (大道在焉).

In ancient times, **Confucius told Yan Yuan**:
*"One day’s effort in self-restraint and returning to ritual*
*brings the world to the **Dao of Ren** (归仁)."*
Thus, it is known that **where the Great Dao leads, the world follows** (大道所归, 天下所归).
Whether **returning to humanity** (归人) or **returning to the Dao** (归道),
all is **heartfelt sincerity** (心悦诚服).
How could there be **harm** (何害) in this?

I know that **one qi permeates and transforms all things** (一气相贯通).
All **things transform in silence** (万物皆默化).
**Merging and releasing**, **above and below rest in harmony** (上下相安)
within the **Great Cycle of Heaven** (泰运之天).
This is the **direct return to nature’s essence** (直自然之依归),
not something **felt in an instant** (非一时所感激).
If one merely **adorns the ears and eyes for a moment** (徒饰片时之耳目),
it **will not fail to flow continuously** (未始不源源而来).
But if one **listens to refined music** (世之雅乐) or **enjoys fragrant baits** (香饵),
it is enough to **make a passing traveler pause** (令过客停骖),
**dallying without departing** (流连不去).
Yet such pleasures can **only be temporary** (可暂而不可常).

The **Dao is without taste** (道无味),
not like **rich, fatty, sweet, or crisp** (肥浓甘脆)
that **makes one chew endlessly** (令人咀嚼不已),
**satiating without limit** (餍饫无穷).
Yet **what truly satisfies the palate and delights the heart** (人之爽口悦心者)
**is never wearying** (自不厌焉).

This is the **ultimate taste within the Dao’s plainness** (无味中之至味),
**not to be compared with the world’s overbearing flavors** (非世味之浓所可拟).»

「Though the Dao has no fixed place or form, it is difficult to conceive and even harder to grasp. Hence it is said: *"It cannot be seen, yet cannot be exhausted; it cannot be heard, yet cannot be drained."* Such is its nature—**encompassing heaven and earth without excess**, **conforming to all things without omission**. This is why the Dao is called great.

**A scholar must know where to aspire!**

This speaks of how a person must first **align with the harmonious exchange of heaven and earth** (天地交泰) before they can **fuse and disperse** (融融泄泄) in such a way—**not merely like refined music that lingers in the heart or fragrant baits that tempt the senses**, but rather to evoke **sighs of admiration without end**.

Yet such a realm is not easily attained. If one is in the throes of **private desires raging** (私欲甚炽) and **vital qi fading** (血氣將衰), and does not first **proceed from the realm of intense motion** (极动之处) to **gradually reach the state of stillness** (静地), then **the mundane heart will not perish** (人心不死), **the Dao-heart will not arise** (道心不生), **the ordinary breath will not cease** (凡息不停), and **the true breath will not manifest** (真息不見).

Only when **stillness follows extreme motion** (动极而静之际), must one **avoid letting true intention** (真意) dominate. This intention is **yin in nature**, belonging to **the category of *ji* earth** (己土).

Soon, one will feel **dimly vague and indistinct** (恍恍惚惚), as **yin and yang intermingle** (阴阳交媾). Then, one enters **the boundless realm of profound obscurity** (杳冥之境)—**like a dream yet not a dream**, **like waking yet not fully awake**.

Within this settled stillness, **suddenly a thread of warm qi arises** (一缕热气), **mingling and flowing continuously** (混混续续). As **the qi flows freely and the spirit harmonizes** (气畅神融), the two **converge within the Yellow Palace** (黄房之间)—**on the verge of distinction yet indistinct**, **indistinct yet suddenly distinct**. This is the **manifestation of true *yan* lead** (真铅现象).

Then, **the heart’s blossoms surge forth** (心花怒发), **warm qi fuses** (暖气融融), **the primordial spirit leaps** (元神跃跃)—**not through external provocation**, but **naturally arising**. Thus, **the signs of the Mysterious Pass** (玄关兆象) and **the foundation of the Great Ultimate** (太极开基) are realized.

At this moment, one must **employ a single point of true heart** (一点真心), **issuing true intention** (发真意) to **contain and gather** (收摄之). This intention is **yang in nature**, belonging to **the category of *wu* earth** (戊土).

In essence, it is **one intention**, merely **dividing the foundation of motion and stillness** (动静之基) into **the earths of *wu* and *ji*** (戊己之土).

For when **the Mysterious Gate** (玄牝) remains unopened and **chaos prevails** (混沌之中), **true intention** (真意) as the master is **the intention of boundless wonder** (无欲观妙)—**called *yin* earth**. But when **the Mysterious Gate opens and true essence manifests** (玄牝开而真机现), **one observes desire through its aperture** (有欲以观其窍)—**called *yang* earth**.

**One is the nameless origin of heaven and earth;**
**the other is the named mother of all things.**
**Generating heaven, earth, humans, and beings,**
**all arise from this single point of true intention** (真意), **flowing through them like a thread**.

**A cultivator who can master and govern this true intention** (真意主宰运行) will **not be swayed by sensation into thought**, nor **driven by motion into distraction**. As it is said:
*"The Celestial Pass is under my control;*
*the Earthly Axis is in my heart.*
**The cosmos resides within the body;**
**all transformations spring from the mind**—
**all these are the operations of this moment’s luminous awareness** (灵觉), **to be mastered and sustained**."

Hence it is said:
*"If one lingers too early in the breath,*
**the true essence does not yet appear** (真机未现), **and gathering it is futile** (采之无益);
*if one lingers too late,*
**mundane thoughts have already arisen** (凡念已起), **and gathering it will be mixed with impurities** (夹杂), **unfit as medicine for refining one’s essence** (不堪为我炼功大药).
**This requires great wisdom and great strength**—
only then can one **recognize it clearly and hold it firmly** (认得清、把得定) in this single breath, **as the foundation for attaining immortality and sainthood** (成仙证圣之本).

Though this **Mysterious Pass** (玄关) begins with **soft and delicate qi** (柔脆之气), **one feels only a faint warmth rising from the lower *dan*** (下元), yet over time it **surges through the body** (踊跃周身), **as if unstoppable**. A student must **discern it clearly at its subtlest stage**, **sustaining it with true intention** (以我真意主持) **without the slightest dispersion**. With time, the **qi-essence grows mighty** (气机大有力量), **allowing it to rise naturally, unchecked and unrestrained** (兀兀腾腾). One must **follow its course without added intention or interference** (不加一意，不参一见)—**then it is obtained**.

When the **qi-essence is robust and flourishing** (气机壮旺), **a single moment of stillness triggers the Celestial Essence’s motion** (天机发动), **rapid as lightning**. Even in **the midst of all clamor**, it cannot be restrained.

**One must have a heart of luminous awareness** (灵觉之心) to **master it**—**then there will be no flaw**.

**Chapter Thirty-Six: The State’s Sharpest Tools**
*"When one is about to close them, they must first be fully extended;*
*when one is about to weaken them, they must first be fully fortified;*
*when one is about to discard them, they must first be fully employed;*
*when one is about to seize them, they must first be fully granted."*
This is called **the subtle art of *wei* clarity** (微明).

**Softness overcomes hardness;**
**weakness overcomes strength.**
**A fish cannot survive outside the depths.**
**The state’s sharpest tools must not be displayed to the people.**
**Heaven has its ebb and flow, wax and wane;**
**humans have their lifespans, fortunes, and paths of fulfillment.**
This too is the **ordinary course of qi and fate** (气数之常).

Yet this **applies only to the common masses** (凡夫), **not to the sages** (圣人). For **the sages possess the power to reverse heaven and earth** (挽回天地之能) and **the virtue to invert the *qian* and *kun*** (扭转乾坤之德)—**nothing more than inverting yin and yang** (颠倒阴阳) and **counteracting the workings of *dao* creation** (逆施造化).

For instance, **when autumn arrives**, **all things withdraw**, yet **one struggles to close and weaken** (欲歙弱而难整). The sages **then extend the vital breath of heaven and earth** (张天地之气运) and **strengthen the functional capacity of the vital qi** (强血气之功能).

**When winter comes**, **all things decay**, yet **one seeks to wither and seize** (欲槁夺而难生). The sages **then revive the cosmic essence** (气象之重兴) and **grant the years and months as their own** (岁月之我与者).

This is **both the subtlest and the clearest** (至微而至明), **both the most ordinary and the most extraordinary** (实常而实异)—**a truth only the sages can fathom**.

**To transform danger into safety,**
**to reverse chaos into order,**
**requires not mere mortal courage but divine transformation** (神化).

Yet **the essence of their return-and-restoration technique** (返还之术) is simply **softness** (柔) and **weakness** (弱).
**Only because they are soft do they overcome hardness;**
**only because they are weak do they overcome strength.**

What is their method?
*"If humans lack precision, they perish;*
**if fish lack water, they die.**
**Once a fish is removed from the depths,**
**the waters dry and life’s essence fades** (水涸而生机息矣).

**Humans and fish share the same dependency on water.**
But it is not **the water of heaven’s dew** (天露之水),
nor is it **the water of the Dao’s profound depths** (造道渊深),
**the primordial water of unity** (一元之水).

**It flows ceaselessly** (汩汩乎来), **frequently nourishing** (频相灌溉).
**Zhuangzi once said:**
*"They moisten each other with dew;*
**they breathe on each other with vapor—*
**yet better still is to forget each other in the rivers and lakes."*
This is his meaning.

**Later generations of heterodox schools** (旁门) **mistake tangible, material essence** (有形有质之精) for **the foundation of cultivation and longevity** (修炼长生之本), **utterly failing to grasp the Dao as a substance**—**vigorous, upright, and pure**, **its essence arising from profound obscurity, boundless void, and spontaneous naturalness** (恍惚杳冥、虚无自然而生者).»

The secrets of alchemical fire and the essence of culinary mastery are not imparted to the unworthy—only to the **sage-teacher** (聖師) does one reveal them, only to the **utterly sincere** (至誠) does one approach them, and only to those who have **deeds and virtue**, who **seek the Way with humility** (虚心访道), and who **sincerely pursue a master** (竭誠求師) can the **chance of Daoist attainment** (仙緣) be forged.

For the **heavenly mechanisms** (天機) are profound and hidden, the **weight of heaven and earth** (天地至重) is supreme, and the **ghosts and spirits** (鬼神) hold them in reverence. To recklessly transmit these to the unworthy invites **calamity upon the Nine Ancestors** (殃遺九祖). It is like the **state’s most vital implements** (國家利用之密器)—they cannot be casually revealed to others.

Thus, the **noble person** (君子) keeps them **meticulously concealed** (缜密而不出), and the **seeker** (學者)—does he grasp this truth?

This speaks of those who cultivate the Dao: they truly hold **the cosmos in their grasp** (宇宙在手), and **all transformations arise from their mind** (萬化生心). Yet still, they need only **observe the Dao of Heaven** (天之道) and **grasp its operation** (执天之行), following it to take and opposing it to act—this suffices.

It is not a matter of **harboring vital force within lethal force** (寓生機於殺機之中), but rather what is called **the supreme yin, resplendent** (至陰赫赫), and **the supreme yang, austere** (至陽肃肃). The resplendent arises from Heaven, the austere from Earth. To take the supreme yang from the supreme yin—this is the **seizing of opportunity** (資機).

If a person can **plunder yang from the black mountain’s cavern** (黑山窟), or **extract treasure from the demon’s lair** (鬼窩里), then they are **stealing vital force from lethal force**. Yet all this is done within the **void of heaven and earth**, or **plucked from the stillness of the human body**—this is the **true essence** (真精), not the **common essence of the world** (世之凡精).

If one can **plunder it without losing the moment**, and **apply it with a single effort**, then there will be **progress in proportion to the work**. I urge the seeker to **yield with softness** (以柔), to **weaken with humility** (以弱), to **establish virtue and deeds** (立德立功), so as to **earn the favor of the divine and heavenly** (神天之佑), and thus receive the **transmitted incantations of the immortals** (仙人傳授口訣).

Otherwise, even the greatest matters—those that **stir heaven and earth and move ghosts and spirits**—though even the immortals **rarely transmit them**. For the **heavenly mechanisms are most secret** (天機至密), the **heavenly laws are most strict** (天律最嚴), and they **cannot be defied**.

Zhuangzi said:
*"If the Dao could be offered to others, then all would offer it to their lords.
If the Dao could be advanced to others, then all would advance it to their kin.
If the Dao could be given to others, then all would give it to their siblings.
If the Dao could be transmitted to others, then all would transmit it to their descendants."*
Yet none of these are possible—why? Because **without inner virtue, the Dao cannot stand**, and **without inner mastery, the Dao cannot move**.

When we combine the words of the sages, we see that **the state’s most vital implements cannot be lightly shown to others**.

Later generations of cultivators must not treat the **Great Dao as a public matter**, imparting it indiscriminately to all, lest they **invite heavenly retribution** (天譴) and **regret it forever**. This is the meaning of **"public yet not public, not public yet public"** (有公而不公，不公而公)—a subtlety beyond the grasp of the unenlightened. Let them take heed.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Seven: The Dao Ever Does Without Doing**

The Dao ever does without doing, yet nothing remains undone.
If lords and kings could **guard this**, all things would **transform of themselves**.
When transformation is about to arise, I shall **restrain it with the nameless Primordial** (無名之朴).
The nameless Primordial—**it too will not desire**.
Through **non-desire, stillness** (不欲以靜), the world will **settle of itself**.

Though the Dao is naturally without doing, yet to **fixate on non-doing** becomes the **doctrine of stubborn emptiness** (顽空之學). One must **root oneself in non-doing** (無為植其本) while **beginning action** (有為端其用)—**non-doing within doing**, **doing yet still non-doing**. Thus the **essence stands firm** (體立) and the **Dao moves** (道行), the **Dao is complete** (道全) and **virtue is fulfilled** (德備).

This is the **"ever-responsive yet ever-still"** (常應常靜), the **"ever-quiet yet ever-alert"** (常寂常惺). When released, it **fills all six directions**; when withdrawn, it **hides in the central void**. Even in this **boundless, formless moment** (沖漠無蹤), there is the **probity of seated illumination, leaving nothing unobserved** (坐照無遺之概).

Though it is called **non-doing**, yet **doing is contained within it**; though it is called **doing**, yet **non-doing encompasses it**. Thus the **Great Dao is within me**, the **Great Root ever endures**.

Even the most exalted lords and kings—if they lack this Dao as their foundation, then **all things are severed and difficult to transform**. Only by **holding fast to this Dao** can **all under heaven—people and things—harmonize in their natures**, **resonate in their breath**, and **transform silently and imperceptibly**, until **great peace takes form**.

Yet in times of prolonged tranquility, the **ancient Dao is hard to uphold**. This is the **inevitable course of human inclination** (情所必至) and the **fixed principle of things** (理有固然)—there is no cause for wonder.

When **creation and destruction recur frequently**, and **luxury arises**, then **human hearts grow more treacherous**, and **calamities multiply**. This too is the **fate of heaven and earth**, from which **no one can escape**.

Only the **sage possesses the methods of enduring prosperity** (保泰持盈之法) and the **strategies of lasting governance** (久安長治之謀). In the age when **culture first emerges**, they **refine the nameless, undifferentiated Primordial** (浑然無名之太朴)—**without doing, without action, without thought, without deliberation**—and **apply it to themselves and to others**, **guiding those before and leading those behind**, **purely in accordance with Heaven**, unmixed with human contrivance. Thus they **stabilize the court within** and **pacify the world without**.

The **first line of the *Hexagram of Array*** (屯之初九) says: *"The sun lingers, beneficially residing in the upright"*—this is the **first stabilization of the unenlightened age**.

Just as **stone contains jade and the mountain gleams**, and **water cradles pearls and the river enchants**, even the **primordial and undifferentiated** (凡朴) has such power—how much more so the **nameless Primordial**? To **unify all people and things under its governance**?

If it is not **restored to its original, undivided state** (不歸浑穆), then it is **impossible to make the extremes converge** (會極歸極) or **ensure all attain the ease of the mat and cushion** (咸登衽席之安).

Only by **not knowing yet knowing**, by **following the pattern of the Emperor** (順帝之則), by **forgetting morality and the Dao**, by **not recognizing the unity of Heaven and humanity**—only thus does one **truly attain it**.

Thus it is said:
*"The nameless Primordial—it too will not desire.
Through non-desire, stillness—then the world **sets itself aright** (天下自正)."*
This is the **tranquil and undemanding governance** (恬淡無為), the **natural order without contrivance** (郅治無為), where **the ruler does not know what he transforms**, and **the people do not know what they respond to**. The ruler and the people **both rest in the Dao of non-doing**, and there is **an unknowing yet natural harmony**.

Thus **Shun governed without doing**, and so **he alone was revered through the ages**.

For those who rule over the people, can they not take **non-doing as their foundation**?

This discourse on **governing the age** holds **non-doing as its root**. The **principles of self-cultivation** are no different.

Lords and kings are like **the human body**—**most exalted and noble**. The common saying is:
*"A human body is rare in a kalpa, yet once obtained, it is already a marvel."*

Yet, have you not heard of the **Dharma/True Teaching**—is it not far more splendid?

If one does not cultivate now, **one’s vital essence and spirit will dwindle**, and **life and body will falter**.

In the blink of an eye, **one’s qi is extinguished**, and **one knows not whether one becomes a ghost or a gnome** (為蜮), a beast or a bird.

Through the **six paths of cyclic existence** (輪回六道), one turns and turns again without cease—when will one ever break free?

Now, with the **Dharma’s feast** (法筳) spread wide and the **Great Way** (大道) opened vast,
should one not hasten to practice and preserve it,
lest the years slip by in vain?

All things, like the **human body’s five senses and hundred limbs** (五官百體),
with **vital essence and blood-qi** (血氣),
can uphold this **constant Dao of non-doing** (無為常道),
then **all anxieties naturally subside** (諸虑自息),
and **the body’s framework is in perfect order** (百骸俱理).
The **skin becomes supple and the hair lustrous** (肌肤润泽，毛发晶莹),
not unlike **golden form and jade-like substance** (金相玉質).

If lords and kings can uphold it,
all things will **transform in harmony** (自化).
This is like **the inner light of a single mind** (一心内照),
then **changes resonate with mystic responsiveness** (通灵).

Yet **the fire of cultivation is not yet refined** (火候未纯),
and **one’s qi and essence still linger** (氣質尚在).
At this juncture, **spirit is greatly purified** (精神大整),
and **wisdom arises frequently** (智慧频生).
Some delight in **discourse on past and future** (過去未來),
to **showcase their talents** (以逞其才);
others take pleasure in **speaking of worldly achievements** (建功立業),
to **boast before others** (以夸於世).

All these actions spring from **impure virtue/power** (道德未纯).

The elixir of jade nectar now perfected,
once more the furnace and cauldron are set in order,
the primordial *Bu* of the Nameless is reclaimed,
poured into the Eight Trigrams Furnace.
Within, the divine fire of heaven burns naturally;
without, the common hearth is adjusted in measure.
After prolonged alchemical transformation,
even the Nameless *Bu* is utterly forgotten.

When one is **without knowing, without desiring**,
serene and unadorned,
the mortal form undergoes transformation,
returning naturally to the **primordial One-Qi of Before-Heaven**—
thus the Dao of the Transcendent is fulfilled.

As it is said:
*"By stillness without desire, the world will naturally align itself."*

This is the **single thread** that unifies the **Way of the Sovereign’s governance and self-cultivation**—
the thirty-eighth chapter:
*"The Virtue/Power of the Highest is not virtue/power."*

The virtue/power of the Highest is not virtue/power,
and thus it has virtue/power.
The virtue/power of the Lowest does not lose virtue/power,
and thus it has no virtue/power.

The Highest acts without doing yet accomplishes nothing;
the Lowest acts and yet accomplishes something.

The Highest in benevolence acts yet accomplishes nothing;
the Highest in righteousness acts and yet accomplishes something.

The Highest in ritual acts yet none respond,
so it raises its arm and forces compliance.

Thus, when the Way is lost, virtue/power arises;
when virtue/power is lost, benevolence arises;
when benevolence is lost, righteousness arises;
when righteousness is lost, ritual arises.

Ritual is but the **thinnest veneer of sincerity and trust**,
yet it is the **beginning of chaos**.

Prescience is the **flower of the Way**,
yet it is the **origin of folly**.

Thus, the **true person** abides in substance, not in form;
dwells in reality, not in ornament.
Hence, one discards the latter to take the former.

In the **ancient age**, the winds were vast and untrammelled,
each following its natural course;
the waters deep and boundless,
each resting in its own nature.
Above and below acted without contrivance,
rulers and people shared in joy.
Sincerity and thickness became the custom,
and disputes did not arise.

How profound and complete was the Way in those times!
It was because they abided in the **non-action of heaven**,
following nature’s spontaneous course.

In those days, the old were cherished as old,
the young as young,
the wise as wise,
the kind as kind.
They tilled the land with ease, took joy in their labor,
fed on virtue and drank in harmony,
knowing nothing of the names *virtue/power* or *Dao*,
nor hearing of *benevolence, righteousness, ritual, wisdom*.

Yet they preserved their **primordial purity and integrity**,
allowed their *qi* to move naturally,
and harmonized with the flow of heaven and earth—
as if untaught yet transformed,
achieving without action,
united with virtue/power and the Dao,
without conflict with *benevolence, righteousness, ritual, wisdom*.

When *virtue/power* and *Dao* are spoken together,
*Dao* is the **substance**, *virtue/power* the **function**.
When *virtue/power, benevolence, righteousness, ritual, wisdom* are discussed as a whole,
then *virtue/power* and *Dao* are again the **substance**,
while *benevolence, righteousness, ritual, wisdom* are the **functions**.

Later sages, though establishing these for the transformation of the people,
named *virtue/power* and divided them into *benevolence, righteousness, ritual, wisdom*—
yet within *virtue/power* are *benevolence, righteousness, ritual, wisdom*,
and within *benevolence, righteousness, ritual, wisdom* is *virtue/power*.
There is no separation, no lack.

But in later ages, *virtue/power* was divided.
Further still, *benevolence, righteousness, ritual, wisdom* became fixated on one-sidedness.
This arose from the **shift of cosmic *qi*** and the **deceit of human hearts**.

The Transcendent One wished to return people to their roots,
restore them to their origin,
and thus sighed:
*"The person of Highest virtue/power acts without doing,
following their nature without knowing of virtue/power—
thus their virtue/power endures.
The person of Lowest virtue/power, knowing virtue/power’s excellence,
loves its name and delights in its practice,
fearing to lose it and thus their reputation.
These two minds—one divided, one consumed—
shatter the singular sincerity of the Highest,
where no duality remains, no thought lingers.
From having virtue/power, they return to being without it."*

Moreover, when the Highest acts without doing,
the people of the world align in one Way, one custom,
all abiding in a world of non-action.
When the Lowest acts with doing,
amid the rise of flourishing customs and varied governance,
they take joy in the constant of action.

Is it not so?
Those who forget contrivance **still the world’s machinations**,
while those who delight in affairs **stir the world’s strife**?

Yet when times are exhausted, they return;
when things are exhausted, they transform;
when people are exhausted, they revert.

In this age of many affairs,
when customs grow corrupt and hearts deceive,
the natural purity is long lost.
There must be a ruler who loves benevolence,
governing with virtue/power, purifying the source, rectifying the root,
turning chaos into order, peril into safety.

Though forced to act,
yet even in action, they adapt to the times,
following principle in their conduct—
action remains non-action,
thus the world is governed with ease.

Then there are those who love righteousness,
in times of disorder seeking to restore peace,
taking pride in sweeping away the old,
yet unable to return to stillness.
Though they may briefly pacify the world,
renewing people and things,
yet those above and below emulate them,
and the strife and contention among the people cannot cease.

As for the ruler of Highest Ritual,
the hearts of people grow ever more corrupt.
They practice the etiquette of reciprocity,
discussing the ways of giving and receiving—
some generous in giving yet stingy in return,
others kind in granting favors yet vengeful in response—
and cannot rest in tranquility.
Morning brings changes, customs shift,
yet they cannot harmonize near and far, great and small.
The slightest discord provokes raised arms and conflict,
and war erupts anew.
They cannot dwell together in peace.

Thus it is said:
*"When the Way is lost, virtue/power arises;
when virtue/power is lost, benevolence arises;
when benevolence is lost, righteousness arises;
when righteousness is lost, ritual arises."*

Down to this day,
as people grow more corrupt and affairs more complex,
sincerity and trust have reached their ruin,
and ritual alone is invoked to maintain order.
Yet such ornamentation, lacking inner sincerity,
only multiplies daily disputes and disputes over right and wrong.
Those who rely on ritual to bind the hearts of people
have not considered that outward compliance does not stem from the heart—
it must end in disaster and estrangement,
seeking safety only to invite danger.

Thus it is said:
*"Ritual is the thinnest layer of sincerity and trust,
yet it is the origin of chaos."*

*"As for wisdom, it is not some extraordinary strategy or foresightful scrutiny—it arises from sincerity and clarity, achieved without deliberation, without study, and without effort. It is as naturally luminous as a mirror; how could it compare to deceit or baseless trust?

Yet the superficial trappings of the Way are not the Way itself. Moreover, excessive scrutiny under the guise of clarity will inevitably lead to emptiness, deceit, and falsehood without one’s awareness. In oneself, one may pridefully claim exceptional insight, but this is merely the beginning of folly.

Thus, a true man of virtue possesses steadfast discernment and resolve. He knows that sincerity and depth are the essence of ritual, so he embraces substance over superficiality; he recognizes that empty ornamentation is not true wisdom, so he seeks the real over the illusory. When discernment in selection is proper, the Great Way is not difficult to restore.

This explains why, as morality decays, benevolence and righteousness emerge; when benevolence and righteousness decay, ritual and wisdom take their place—ever descending, a natural consequence of human nature and custom, nothing to marvel at.

As for cultivation, when swallowing saliva and breathing *qi* appeared, the Way shifted; when herbal alchemy and elixir refining emerged, the Way shifted again. Down to today, myriad false paths abound—beyond reckoning! Who still understands that the *xuanguan* (玄关)—that single orifice—is the essence of cultivation?

I now reveal it to people: To perceive this *xuanguan*, one must wait after great toil and great rest before its nature becomes clear. It is also said: *'When thoughts arise, that is the ailment; not clinging is the remedy.'* And again: *'Lay down the butcher’s cleaver, and you become a Buddha in this instant.'* Ultimately, it is about ceasing the tumult of worldly desires and杂念 (*zaonian*), awakening from the chaos—this is the *xuanguan*, the 'bank' one returns to.

It is also said: *'The other shore is not distant; the return of light and reflection is the shore itself.'* Yet fear arises: before the *xuanguan* opens, one may forcefully seek it; after it opens, one may obsessively guard it. Such fixations only stir confusion—like the sky, originally cloudless, yet when the clouds disperse, the vast, wondrous expanse appears. But if, after the clouds vanish, one reintroduces smoke and dust, the once-clear sky becomes constricted, murky, and unrecognizable.

The Buddha said: *'Dwell thus, subdue the mind thus.'* Such profound truths cannot be articulated in words—words only distort them. Nor can they be grasped through thought—thought only errs.

At the moment before the *xuanguan* opens, I simply follow its natural illumination. Once it opens, I rest in its effortless clarity. If thoughts scatter, I withdraw them—that is all. If the mind grows dim, I steady it—that is all. How simple and direct!

Yet people complicate matters by layering bed upon bed, seeking motion in motion, stillness in stillness—only to fall into contrivance. The *xuanguan* lies plain before them, yet they obscure it with later-day intellect.

I reveal a key principle: Let the thoughts churn endlessly, unresolved. The moment you awaken to their nature, they vanish instantly—that *is* the *xuanguan*. Thus, cultivation depends on this awakened awareness, like the unobstructed sun in an endless sky, ever luminous. The slightest shadow plunges one into the darkness of hellish defilement. If you dispel the clouds, the blue sky and white sun stand revealed.

But if worldly thoughts arise, you remain trapped in the ordinary. This is not the foundation of the immortals.

In truth, the Daoists have no secret beyond *mingxin jianxing* (明心见性)—enlightening the mind, realizing true nature—this is the essence of cultivation.

If asked what the elixir is, it is the primordial *qi* of the *dantian* (丹田), the unborn essence. Yet this essence, when united with the innate divine spirit (*yuanshen*), becomes the preheaven *taiji* and *wujji* (太极、无极), the primordial command before parents were born.

If one establishes one’s nature as one’s fate and fulfills one’s fate through nature, then eternal life is attained.

But those who seek the *xuanguan* of the watery realm, desiring to forge the golden elixir, must first harness the preheaven breath (*shenxi*). Without it, they cannot refine, temper, or nurture—preheaven nature and preheaven fate cannot unite, and the five and six energies cannot coalesce into the golden elixir.

Even so, once preheaven nature and fate are attained, without true *taixi* (胎息), it is like a man and woman unable to wed—no union, no offspring. Thus the *Danjing* says: *'True intent is the matchmaker.'* Now it is said: *'True breath is the matchmaker'*—does this not contradict the ancient texts?

The unenlightened mistake *zhanyi* (真意) as the divine essence of elixir refinement and union; *zhanyi* (真息) as the means. Yet both are merely the convergence of spirit and *qi*.

Without true breath, true *qi* cannot ascend and descend, unite, or nurture the formation of the primordial pearl. With true breath but no true intent to command, guide, and seal it, the breath cannot flow freely, advancing and retreating as it will.

Thus, true intent is the key to elixir refinement.

Yet true intent, without the original divine spirit, originates from where? Only when the *xuanguan* first opens does one recognize this true intent. Then, one must hold it fast, as Yanzi (颜子) revered his principles with unwavering devotion—thus it is attained.

Moreover, the divine spirit’s manifestation *is* true intent, *is* a single good deed, *is* the way of attaining all things through one.

If students recognize this clearly, the foundation of the Great Elixir is established.

Chiuzu (邱祖) once said: *'If the breath is even a hair’s breadth undetermined, the fate is not your own.'* I tell students: To seek longevity, first subdue the breath."

「Yet subduing the *qi* has two meanings: one is to suppress and store this *qi* within the inner palace (中宫), remaining still and unmoved; the second is to regulate and govern it strictly, for longevity lies precisely in this mastery of *qi*.

Beyond this, there is no other Dao. Those who practice must adhere to this principle, lest they betray my earnest intentions.

**Chapter Thirty-Nine: Taking Humility as the Foundation**

In antiquity, those who attained *One* (得一者):
The heavens gained *One* and became clear,
The earth gained *One* and became tranquil,
The divine spirit gained *One* and became luminous,
The *gu* (谷, grains) gained *One* and became abundant,
All things gained *One* and thus came to life,
And lords and kings gained *One* to establish their virtuous rule over the world—
This was the singular purpose of *One*.

The heavens without clarity would fear fragmentation,
The earth without tranquility would fear upheaval,
The divine spirit without luminosity would fear extinction,
All things without life would fear annihilation,
And lords and kings without virtue would fear downfall—
Their lofty status would tremble.

Thus, the noble rely on humility as their foundation,
The exalted rely on lowliness as their base.
Hence, lords and kings call themselves *gu* (孤, lone), *gua* (寡, few), and *bu gu* (不谷, unworthy)—
This is their adherence to humility as the foundation, is it not?

Therefore, the multitude of chariots lacks chariots,
And they do not desire to gleam like jade or stand firm like stone.

The Great Dao is none other than *One*.
What is this *One*? (一者何)」

The primordial *hóngmèng* before differentiation—this original *yuánqì*, vast and formless, the boundless *wújí*—gave birth to the *tàijí*, the ultimate principle that generates all things.

In essence, *yuánqì* is formless, thus called *wújí*. All things arise from *wújí* and take shape, truly the root of the world, thus called *tàijí*. This is the Dao.

The sage, unable to name it, yet naming it, thus says *One*.
Without *One*, there are no things; without things, there is no *One*.
To possess it is to live; to lose it is to perish.
From the beginning of *yuánshǐ* onward, those who obtain *One* and manifest form and likeness—endless, unceasing, generating without end—whether vast as the *zhōusha* sands or fine as dust, none can escape it.

As the *Zhōngyōng* says:
*"To see it, you cannot see it;
To hear it, you cannot hear it;
To embody it, you cannot abandon it."*
What else could this be?

Thus, in summary:
The heavens’ clarity comes from obtaining *One*;
The earth’s tranquility comes from obtaining *One*;
The spirit’s luminosity comes from obtaining *One*;
The valley’s resonance comes from obtaining *One*;
All things’ vitality comes from obtaining *One*;
The sovereign’s rectification of self and the world comes from nothing but obtaining *One* to uphold it.

Though great and small differ in form,
Noble and base diverge in path,
Exterior and interior vary in refinement,
Dark and bright divide in kind,
Ghosts and humans span the unseen—
How could anything transcend this *One* as its embrace?

Even the heavens, most exalted, would fear collapse without *One*;
The earth, most vast, would fear upheaval without *One*;
The spirit, most subtle, would lose its luminosity without *One*;
The valley’s echo, full of *qi*, would falter without *One*;
All things, bearing form, would perish without *One*;
The sovereign, most high and noble, without *One* to uphold the world, would face peril in lofty rank and ruin in exalted name—
How could *One* be disregarded?

If one can disperse from *One* to the myriad things—vast, boundless, unfathomably deep—
Then heaven, earth, spirit, valleys, all things, and sovereigns
All rely on this *One* as their sovereign,
Coiling through the heavens, weaving the earth, without gaps or exhaustion, filling all without end…
Thus, its extremity is such:
Nothing is higher than the Dao,
Nothing is nobler than *One*.

Yet, from non-being to being—what is there to exalt?
From subtlety to manifestation—what is there to prize?
Even if nobility surpasses the capital,
