It arises from *qi* emerging from the obscure to the manifest,
Thus it is said: *"The noble relies on humility as its foundation."*
Even if height reaches the boundless,
It arises from *qi* ascending from low to high,
Thus it is said: *"The exalted relies on lowliness as its base."*

As for those in the world—
Though their rank is high like a marquis,
Though their honor is noble like a king,
They know the Dao arises from low to high,
From humble to noble,
Thus they call themselves *"gu"* (lone), *"guǎ"* (few), *"bùgǔ"* (unworthy)—
Is this not relying on humility as the foundation?

If one does not reside in humility,
But places oneself too high,
Then the mind lacks mastery, the Dao cannot stand,
The heart becomes scattered, the spirit cannot coalesce.
To harmonize all things in great centrality and rectitude,
Returning to the Dao—
Few indeed can achieve this.

Even those who drive many chariots cannot govern both center and periphery,
Yet those who drive but one chariot still hold sway within—
Thus, having chariots is like having none;
Greed for many is not as good as embracing *One*.

Like jade, if it gleams and multiplies,
Excess breeds devaluation;
Like stone, if it layers and piles,
Excess breeds peril—
These are what the *Tàishàng* does not desire.

Better to embrace *One* in humility and lowliness,
Only then to ascend to heights beyond reach,
To nobility beyond speech—
This is the path of cultivating the Dao to true perfection,
Where there is only *One*, no second.

Confucius said: *"My Dao is one and penetrates all."*
Mencius said: *"The Dao is but one."*
Yet—what is this *One*?

The ancients spoke of the *hónghóng-mèngmèng* state,
Where there is no thought, no dross—
A single, empty yet luminous, still yet alert essence.
This essence, when vast, contains the *dharma-realm*;
When narrow, not even a speck of dust can stand.
When manifest, it reaches the eight *yí* and eight *huāng* without obstruction;
When hidden, it scrutinizes even the finest dust.
This is the *extreme of wújí*,
The *non-divine divine*,
True beyond naming, beyond imagination.
The Dao of nature and life—this is all.

The *Tàishàng* likens the sovereign to the human heart:
If the heart can constantly hold and preserve *One*,
Without forgetting or aiding it,
Moment by moment reflecting inward,
Ever internally illuminating,
Then it will not lose *One*.
*One* is also *du* (solitary).

Solitary as the realm of *dujue* (solitary awakening),
With awe and vigilance,
This is the originally most high and noble—
Perhaps it can be long preserved.
Yet this is the study of cultivating nature,
Thus *shèn dān dú* (one mind in solitude) suffices.

But cultivating life involves action and effort—
If not beginning from the lowly,
From the humble and impure,
How could the medicine be obtained?
Much less the *jīndān* (elixir)?

The lower refers to the lower *dāntián*;
The humble refers to the filthy lower realm.
The scholar, seeking *yīng lái fù* (the return of *yang*),
With *qi* surging upward,
Must ascend from below to the apex—
How else could one obtain the pure and strong *qi*
As the treasure of eternal life?

If not from the lower *dāntián*, the turbid realm,
With divine fire descending to refine,
How could one forge the supreme *yang qi* as medicine?
The ancients said: *"Seeking yang within yin, stealing treasure from the ghost’s lair"*—
Truly not false!

Above all, one must have a single heart, no dual thoughts—
This is the way of guarding *One*.

When arriving at naturalness,
Forgetting self and others,
Then *One* is obtained.

When the cultivator reaches this state,
All things and matters under heaven
Are handled with ease and appropriateness—
*"Obtaining One, all matters are complete."*
Is this not true?

「If one clings to form and image,
Chasing after them in frenzy,
And is ensnared by the seven emotions and six desires,
By one’s body, family, and spouse—
Reluctant to cease, ever entangled,
Then one is far removed from the Dao.

Do not claim that external things are too manifold to speak of the Dao;
Even matters like nurturing the mind and cultivating the nature,
Refining the Dao and concocting elixirs,
Advancing fire and withdrawing talismans,
Gathering and sealing [the essence]—
All these categories are but empty constructs of their forms,
Established as guidelines for later scholars.

If the cultivator’s heart retains even a thread of impurity,
It becomes an obstacle to the Dao.
Hence the *Tàishàng* says: *"Make ten chariots vanish without chariots"*—
Not wishing to be polished like jade,
Or rigid like stone,
But fluid and natural.

For the Dao is but one Dao;
Why should the scholar seek anything else?

**Chapter 40: The Emergence of Existence from Non-Existence**
The reversal is the Dao’s movement,
The yielding is the Dao’s application.
All things under heaven arise from existence,
Existence from non-existence.
The great Dao is inherent in all—
Whole and complete in each,
So why seek further?

Yet one knows not that since the birth of humanity,
*Qi* is constrained by form,
The mind is veiled by matter,
And desires are stirred by knowledge—
Thus the Dao is drowned and lost in myriad ways.

Unless one expels all external disturbances within,
And disperses all outer agitations without,
Concentrating the spirit and regulating the breath,
Severing thoughts and forgetting artifice,
How could the primordial *yang qi* arise,
And the Dao’s *qi* return?

Hence it is said: *"The reversal is the Dao’s movement."*
This is the foundation of elixir refinement.

When the medicine is returned to the furnace,
And the elixir begins to form,
Mercury and lead merge as one—
Day and night, one must observe internally,
And the golden elixir shall manifest.

From this point onward, with proper gathering,
Continuous and unbroken,
Clear and ever-present,
Using humility to descend,
And gentleness to refine—
This is the marvel of returning the elixir.

Yet it is not only in returning the elixir that this applies;
From the initial practice to the completion of the elixir,
One must always focus the mind inwardly,
And concentrate *qi* to achieve softness.

For the elixir is refined from the *Tàihé*’s singular *qi*;
The cultivator must treat it with humility and harmony.
If even a trace of roughness arises,
The mundane fire will stir,
And harm the Dao.

Hence it is said: *"The yielding is the Dao’s application."*
All matters under heaven,
Though beginning with tangible forms and rules,
Did not originate from existence itself.
The sage, when the great Dao is fulfilled,
Though capable of infinite transformations and completeness,
Must first gather and refine from the utmost emptiness and non-existence,
Then the grand application flows,
And boundless *qi* permeates the two extremes [of *yin* and *yang*].

If one does not refine from non-existence,
How could one achieve such boundless suffusion?
Hence it is said: *"Existence arises from non-existence."*

The scholar’s essential cultivation:
At first, from non-existence to existence,
Refining subtle *yang* from stillness;
Then, from existence to non-existence,
Returning to tranquility from the flourishing within;
And finally, again from non-existence to existence—
A blurred chaos, self and others forgotten—
Over time, refining the three-inch *yang* spirit,
And the six-foot golden body.

Thus, existence and non-existence cycle eternally,
Iterating without end.
The scholar must adhere to this principle,
Or risk deviation.

This speaks of the great Dao of the golden elixir;
It is not something external,
But the true *qi* flowing through the body.
In stillness, it is deep as the abyss;
In motion, it surges like the tide.
Only those who cultivate with purity,
Focus inwardly with clarity,
Can reflect and verify it themselves—
Beyond what words can exhaust.

If one understands that the motive of movement is the source of life’s origin,
Then the elixir of eternal youth—
Could it be anything else?

Moreover, what is shared by all humanity,
Unchanged, without deficiency—
Only when the body moves does the essence not flourish,
When the mind moves does the *qi* not settle.
Thus arise birth, aging, sickness, and death,
Endless cycles of suffering,
Unceasing rounds of rebirth.
To escape all this,
One must first attain stillness in cultivation—
Only then can one grasp the motive of movement,
And restore the original *qi* of life’s beginning.

But this motive of movement is subtle in force,
Tender in *qi*—
A moment’s carelessness,
And the *guǐ* water [of impurity] arises,
Transforming the primordial flow into the coarse, post-heaven form,
Unusable for the Dao.

Hence it is said: *"See it, yet cannot use; use it, yet cannot see."*
From this single movement onward,
If gathering is not lost to time,
Then eternal life has its foundation,
And the great elixir its root.

If one clings to existence and strives forcefully,
Or holds fast to what one favors and guards rigidly,
Though one may obtain the medicine at times,
And await the elixir’s completion,
It cannot compare to the harmonious surge of *qi*—
And because of later improper gathering,
Or flawed refinement,
The singular *Tàihé qi* is injured by hasty, vulgar fire,
The Dao’s supreme *yang* vitality must endure humiliation and softness,
Only then can the elixir’s path be cultivated.

Hence the *Tàishàng* teaches:
*"Dull its sharpness, resolve its chaos,
Harmonize its brilliance, align it with dust."*

Yet though the Dao moves through *qi*,
It remains non-existence giving rise to existence;
If existence is not nurtured with yielding,
It cannot return to the empty non-existence of Heaven,
And how then could the Dao be fulfilled?

One may know that the return of the primordial *yang*
Is the Dao’s motive,
Yet fail to understand that all forms must return to formlessness—
For form is the Dao’s trace,
Formlessness its truth.

Understanding this,
One’s cultivation and refinement will not lack a foundation.

**Chapter 41: Great Vessels Take Time to Form**」

The superior scholar hears the Dao and diligently practices it;
The middle scholar hears the Dao, as if storing and forgetting it;
The inferior scholar hears the Dao and laughs greatly—
One who does not laugh is unworthy of the Dao.

Thus the *Jiàn yán* says:
*"Clarify the Dao as if in darkness,
Advance the Dao as if in retreat,
Smooth the Dao as if in roughness,
Virtue’s greatness as if in a valley,
Supreme brightness as if in disgrace,
Boundless virtue as if in scarcity,
Established virtue as if in theft,
Sincerity as if in deception—
The great square has no corners,
The great vessel takes time to form,
The great sound is rare in tone,
The great form is shapeless."*

The Dao is hidden, nameless;
Only the Dao, when given and fulfilled, is good.
Before Heaven and Earth came into being,
There was a boundless, formless void—
A vast, hazy *qi* of primordial harmony,
Fermenting for ages,
Until the *qi* transformed and filled the cosmos,
And suddenly, with one awakening, the *Tàijí* opened its foundation.

When it moved, it became *yang*—
Light and clear *qi*, ascending to form Heaven;
When it stilled, it became *yin*—
Heavy and turbid *qi*, descending to form Earth.
Heaven and Earth unfolded,
And beings thrived and multiplied.
Among all the myriad folk,
How few can emulate Heaven and Earth’s functions?

Only the Sage, awakening from the chaos,
Refines the Great Elixir.
With this, one governs the body,
And with this, one cultivates the world.
Though I dare not seal my lips in silence,
Nor teach indiscriminately to all,
Yet in accordance with *yuan* and *yì*,
And adapting to things and beings,
This aligns with Heaven and Earth’s boundless, selfless measure.

At times, I meet the superior scholar—
Hearing my Dao, they joyfully pursue it,
Diligently practice without doubt,
Such a person I have long sought to see.

At times, I meet the middle scholar—
My words pass their lips,
Enter their hearts,
Yet this is commonplace,
Nothing extraordinary,
Though I may love and cherish them,
They quickly lose interest.
Alas, the world’s tastes are thick,
While the Dao’s are thin,
Sagely intent is shallow,
While worldly intent is deep—
Some drift, some settle,
As if storing and forgetting,
And they cannot escape this fate.

As for the inferior scholar,
Their habits deepen daily,
Their *qi* and nature grow perverse—
Hearing my Dao,
They doubt it as heretical speech that misleads the world,
Or accuse it of gathering crowds for profit.
How could they know
That the cultivation of the gentleman begins with the union of *yin* and *yang*?
That the Sage’s Dao is none other than *yin* and *yang*?
That harmony yields life,
While obstruction brings transcendence?
Though their methods differ,
Their principle is one—
Yet the vulgar claim that immortality and sainthood are mere illusions.
How could such cultivation
Ever ascend to the Nine Heavens?
Or else, how could one
Align with Heaven and Earth’s vastness,
And the myriad beings’ flourishing,
Yet remain so constrained in nature?
Why are there so few immortals and sages today?

Thus I laugh at their boastful words,
Their false and eccentric practices.
Alas, this Dao can only be spoken of to those who truly understand—
It is difficult to explain to the shallow and unlearned!
The mayfly does not know of dusk and dawn,
The cicada does not know of spring and autumn,
The well-frog does not know of rivers and seas—
How strange that they should laugh?

One who does not laugh is unworthy of seeing
The Dao’s utmost evenness and constancy,
Its utmost divinity and strangeness—
For divinity and strangeness lie within the ordinary.

Moreover, the Dao has no fixed form or sound,
Yet those of the world who cling to rigid virtue,
Guarding themselves like a cornered beast,
Cannot flow freely and naturally.
I, however, am boundless without bounds,
A unified whole,
Never confined by edges—
How could there be any hesitation?

The Great Elixir’s path is slow to manifest,
Yet its truth is swift to fulfill.

All things that are easily attained are not beautiful to behold,
And those rushed to completion are not great vessels.
I can follow step by step, ascending without expecting swift results,
Not reckoning shallow gains—
Dwelling long in the Dao, it shall surely be accomplished—
Why should I regret myself?

In essence, the Dao is rare in speech, naturally effortless,
Its form vague and elusive.
If I can attain ultimate emptiness and profound stillness,
Then without sound, the Great Sound emerges,
Without form, the Great Form takes shape!

Applied to the four seas, it remains ever true,
Transmitted through ten thousand generations, it knows no end—
How could it merely be revered in one age,
And fail to shine brightly across all ages?

The *Odes* say:
*"In that place, there is no evil,
In this place, there is no decay."*
The Dao’s establishment and practice
Truly possess such divine and wondrous qualities.

Who is it that acts for the Dao?
Who is it that receives from the Dao?
It is also said:
*"To practice the Dao is to lend it and see it fulfilled."*
This speaks of those who embrace the Dao in the world—
Their use is never insufficient,
They nourish all things without depletion,
They encompass the dusty realm with abundance,
And yet bring about the Great Transformation—
Truly, they are the highest of scholars.

The Supreme Sage speaks of those in the world
Who do not conceal their light or cultivate obscurity,
Who establish virtue and refine themselves—
Yet when they gain even a little,
They boast and exalt themselves.

The Five Aggregates are not yet emptied,
The Six Dusts remain impure—
Like a house roofed with thatch,
Fire depends on something to burn.

If one only cultivates oneself without seeking others,
And merges indistinctly into the realm of *wuheyou* [non-existence],
The boundless wilderness—
Even if external harm comes,
It is like lighting a fire in empty space—
It shall surely extinguish itself.

Such cultivation of the self is true cultivation.
Only thus can people dwell in peaceful harmony with the Dao’s natural order,
Otherwise, without attaining the Dao,
They invite blame instead.

Confucius said:
*"To have while seeming to have not,
To be full while seeming empty,
To be boundless while seeming constrained."*
It is no wonder they are despised by others.

Those who cultivate the Dao in this way
Can avoid the anxieties of external concerns
And escape the troubles of external harm.

---
**Chapter 42: Loss as Gain**
The Dao gives birth to One,
One gives birth to Two,
Two gives birth to Three,
Three gives birth to all things.

All things carry yin upon their backs and embrace yang,
Their harmonizing *qi* is the essence of balance.
What people detest—
The solitary, the orphaned, the bereaved—
The king and duke consider them worthy of honor.

Thus, some things are benefited by loss,
Others are harmed by gain.
What people revere, I also teach:
*"The arrogant and violent shall not meet a natural end."*
I shall take this as my lesson.

The Daoist path, from beginning to end,
Holds emptiness and nothingness as its essence.
The Primordial Heavenly Sovereign,
Called *Xuwu Ziran* [Emptiness-Naturalness],
Is the very meaning of this.

From emptiness comes reality—
This is called true reality.
From nothingness comes existence—
This is called true existence.

If one does not embrace emptiness and nothingness,
Not only are the Seven Emotions and Six Desires
Stifled, blocking the true spirit’s essence,
Leaving no capacity to respond to all things and transform the yang spirit—
Even in observing emptiness and enlightenment,
If one clings with even a trace of forced restraint,
It is but the mind controlling the mind,
Directly obscuring the original face beyond recognition.

In summary: emptiness and nothingness are the Dao’s substance,
Harmonious balance is the Dao’s function.
If people can attain this, the Dao is nearly realized.

The Supreme Sage says, *"The Dao gives birth to One"*—
What does the Dao possess?
Only emptiness.

Yet within ultimate emptiness,
A breath of *qi* stirs—
And thus heaven and earth are born.
Hence it is said:
*"There was a primal chaos, born before heaven and earth."*

Before the Boundless,
It was indistinct and formless—
Only this emptiness.
When it moves, it becomes yang;
When it stirs, it becomes yin—
Thus, *"The *Yijing* speaks of the Supreme Ultimate,
Which gives rise to the Two Principles."*

This is what is meant by:
*"The Dao gives birth to One,
One gives birth to Two."*

In the human body,
Within the subtle and obscure,
A single awakening splits—
Heaven and earth open and close,
*Qi* flows in cycles—
Stillness and condensation form yin and essence;
Motion and circulation form yang and *qi*.

If there is no true intention to govern them,
Then yin and yang scatter chaotically,
And there is no way to generate life and attain the Dao.
Thus, between yin and yang,
The Primordial Spirit and true intention are vital to their balance—
This is what is meant by:
*"Two gives birth to Three."*

From this,
One yin and one yang,
One motion and one stillness,
*Qi* flows and transforms,
Its mastery remains unchanged—
And thus all things generate endlessly—
This is what is meant by:
*"Three gives birth to all things."*

Some say:
*"Heaven One generates water—metal generates water;
Earth Two generates fire—wood generates fire;
Heaven Three generates wood—water generates wood;
Earth Four generates metal—earth generates metal."*
Using the Five Phases’ generation to explain
The Supreme Sage’s meaning of *"One, Two, Three, all things"*
Is overly strained—
Better to say the Dao is the Boundless,
One is the Supreme Ultimate,
Two is yin and yang,
Heaven One and Earth Two combine to form Three—
This is the clearest explanation.

*"All things carry yin upon their backs and embrace yang,
Their harmonizing *qi* is the essence of balance,"*
Illustrating that the Dao is the Primordial Emptiness-Naturalness *qi*,
Transforming into yin and yang—
All things’ generation comes from yin and yang.

*"Suong"* (harmonizing) means central—
If yin and yang lack this central harmony,
The center has no master, and the master is unsettled.
Even things struggle to be born—
How much more so for cultivating the Dao?

The *Yijing* says:
*"Heaven and earth blend their threads,
All things refine their essence."*
Thus, the three—*jing* (essence), *qi* (breath), *shen* (spirit)—
Must be complete,
So that yin and yang merge with the Supreme Ultimate and cannot be separated.

If yin and yang exist separately
And the Supreme Ultimate vanishes,
Then the creative power is lost,
And the vital force of all things is extinguished.

In general, cultivating the Dao and refining the elixir
Cannot do without true yin and true yang,
But without the Primordial Harmonious *qi*,
The elixir cannot form,
And the Dao cannot be fulfilled—
For the Dao is originally structured by this Harmonious *qi*.

Generating humans, generating immortals—
It is but one principle,
The difference lies only in obedience or defiance.

The Dao is but this Primordial *qi* as substance,
With yin and yang as its functions—
Thus, the Way of Alchemy is attained.

Observe the kings and nobles, high in station,
Noble in rank,
Yet they call themselves *"gū"* (孤, lone),
*"guǎ"* (寡, few),
*"bùgù"* (不榖, unfulfilled).
What is their intent?

For the lofty are prone to peril,
The full are prone to loss—
Like lightning beneath the sun,
Swift thunder follows.

Only he who does not rely upon his height,
Who does not boast of his nobility,
But instead dwells in humility and gentleness—
Such may long preserve his wealth and honor,
And his kin shall not fall into ruin.

Thus, *"gū"*, *"guǎ"*, *"bùgù"*—
What common folk abhor,
Are the very titles by which kings and nobles refer to themselves.

Then, if the Dao is the supreme treasure of Heaven and Earth,
How can those who cultivate it ignore the meaning of humility and gentleness?

*The Shū* says:
*"Fullness invites loss,
Humility invites gain."*

This is without exception.

Gain does not begin with gain,
It must first undergo loss before gain;
Loss does not begin with loss,
It must first undergo gain before loss.

Thus, wealth and poverty,
Indigence and prosperity,
Gain and loss—
When extremes bend, they rise;
When heights rise, they must bend—
This is the Dao’s cyclical flow,
Nature’s immutable course.
Even Heaven and Earth cannot escape it,
How much less can mortals?

Alas, the human Way is such—
What difference is there from the Great Way?

Seekers of the Dao desire a single yang to revive and renew,
Yet first must silence all attachments,
Purely nurturing the Harmonious *qi*,
And severing all restless and grasping thoughts.

If one diminishes again and again,
To the point of nonexistence,
Then within the gathered darkness of all yin,
True yang shall arise,
And the benefit to one’s self shall be great.

But if one boasts of his talent,
Exalts his wisdom,
Then pride shall not be far behind—
None have escaped the error of the discerning spirit,
Or the flames of false fire consuming the body.

To seek gain and instead suffer loss—
This is the general rule of all things under Heaven,
Not merely in the cultivation of the Dao.

As for all worldly affairs,
They are but illusions,
Not worth fixation—
Yet mortals still cling to them as if they were the teachings of the next generation.

Know well: the Great Dao of Alchemy
Is for the present moment,
Yet its consequences last through all ages—
How can it not be taken as law?

The *Tàishàng* (太上) says:
*"What mortals teach, I also teach—*
*Gentleness alone."*

If one does not employ gentleness but instead relies on rigidity,
He shall be like the tyrants of the world,
Who ravage and plunder,
Yet none escape the law’s net
And meet a good end.

Thus, the proud and fierce meet death,
While gentleness and weakness are the path to life—
This is the very essence of Daoist cultivation.

My teaching of others
Emphasizes gentleness and weakness first—
Seekers of the Dao must not neglect this!

*The Wùzhēn* (悟真) says:
*"From emptiness the Dao gives birth to one *qi*,
From one *qi* it produces yin and yang.
Yin and yang thus form the three substances,
And the three substances generate all things in flourishing—*
This is the meaning of:
*'One generates two, two generates three, three generates all things.'*

At the outset of seated meditation,
One must first extinguish all emotional ties,
Sweep away all delusions,
Reach perfect emptiness and stillness,
No different from the dull and the foolish—
Neither fully asleep nor fully awake,
Neither fully asleep nor fully awake.

This is the undifferentiated *qi* of *Hóngmēng* (鸿蒙),
The very image of the Dao.
Suddenly, a spark of awareness stirs,
And the void is pierced by clarity.

After this stirring,
I perceive all phenomena as void,
A single spirit alone in motion—
Embracing the Primordial, guarding the One—
Some call it *true intention*,
Some call it *right mindfulness*,
Some call it *the Buddha’s perfect and equal enlightenment*.

At this moment, there is but one mind,
No duality of thought.

When observing the yang’s emergence and the formation of elixir,
If it thrives and flourishes like the blending of threads,
Then apply the two stages of forward practice:
The first stage is *taking and returning to the palace*,
The second stage is *returning to the furnace and sealing firmly*—
This is the stirring as yang, yang governing ascent;
The stillness as yin, yin governing descent.

Again, examine the strength of the *qi*’s movement:
If it is now fully robust,
Begin the *river vehicle’s* circulation,
The four stages of taking and refining—
Cooking and consuming the elixir,
Drying the self’s mercury.

This is the taking of yang to pair with yin,
Both arising from the One.

As for each inhalation and exhalation,
Each opening and closing—
None are without the One *qi* dividing into two *qi*.
Yet the heart’s essence and kidney’s *qi*,
The heart’s yin and kidney’s yang—
All depend upon the true intention
For their taking, refining, union, and harmonization.

This is the yin and yang two *qi*,
Combined with the true intention as three substances—
All unfolding naturally,
Without contrivance or manipulation.

The key lies in humility and yielding,
The slightest trace of self-exaltation or rigidity—
Small, it may topple the elixir;
Great, it may destroy life itself.

Thus it is said:
*"The rigid and fierce shall not meet their death,
I shall take them as my teachers."*

Learners must know:
Before attaining the elixir,
Rely upon the heart of emptiness and stillness;
After attaining the elixir,
Nurture it with the intention of gentleness and harmony—
Beware of excessive thought and worry,
Of self-aggrandizement and rigidity.

This is the supreme key within the supreme key—
Learners, take heed!

Otherwise, with a heart brimming with delusion,
Where shall the Dao reside?

Such refining is blind refining—
A single piece of rigidity,
Though attained, it shall be lost;
Such cultivation is blind cultivation—
Without elixir or Dao,
Yet rushing to take and refine,
To circulate and transform—
Not only is it wasted effort,
But it shall surely harm the emotions and deplete the essence.

The damage to body and mind is not slight,
Yet they still refuse to blame themselves,
Instead blaming the Great Dao as false,
The Alchemical Elixir as untrustworthy—
Such are they who truly do not know the Dao as the Way!

Only in perfect emptiness and perfect gentleness,
By preserving the heart in stillness,
And nourishing the *qi* in harmony—
Shall the Dao never fail to manifest.

This is to say:
Daoist cultivation, healing disease, prolonging life,
Ascending to immortality, becoming a sage—
It depends upon nothing other than the three treasures: essence, *qi*, and spirit.

Yet essence is not the essence of copulation,
But the Primordial Beginning’s true suchness,
The radiant singular spirit—
As earlier said:
*"Dim and vague, yet within it lies an image."*

From emptiness comes generation—emptiness is the Dao.
*"The Dao gives birth to One"* means emptiness generates essence; essence is nature itself.

Qi is not the breath of inhalation and exhalation, but rather *"the primordial essence of the First Heaven, a single qi diffused"*—as earlier said:
*"Dim and vague, yet within it lies a substance."*

From One comes generation—One is essence.
*"One gives birth to Two"* means essence generates qi; qi is life itself.

Spirit is not the spirit of thought and deliberation, but rather the luminous radiance alone, ever-awake and unobstructed—
as earlier said:
*"Fathomless and dark, yet within it lies essence,
That essence is most true, within it lies trust."*

From Two comes transformation—Two is qi.
*"Two generates Three"* means qi transforms into spirit—
spirit is the true intent of the Primordial Spirit.

All are transformations of the Great Harmony’s singular qi.
Only by nurturing with gentleness and softness shall one attain it.

If one clings to impatience and a hasty heart,
or generates a violent and tyrannical qi,
these are of a different kind—far from the Dao.
Preserving the body is already difficult; how much more to aspire to immortality!
Thus there is the warning against forceful coercion.

The Most High teaches patience and compassion,
and so speaks thus.

Confucius arranged the *Yijing*, thrice emphasizing the *Qian* hexagram,
and through figures like the tilted *Yi* vessel,
he demonstrated his teachings with earnestness—
was this not the same intent?

**Chapter 43: The Benefit of Non-Action**
The utmost softness in the world overcomes the utmost hardness.
There is nothing that enters the unbroken—
thus I know the benefit of non-action.

The teaching of the unsaid, the benefit of non-action—
few in the world attain it.

What is the Dao?
It is the primordial *qi* of Hongmeng.

Before Heaven and Earth opened, this *qi* existed in the void;
after Heaven and Earth split, this *qi* dwelled in Heaven and Earth.
This *qi* existed before Heaven and Earth yet never perished,
and endures after Heaven and Earth yet never ceases.

When Heaven and Earth obtained this *qi*, they became the Dao.
The Dao is Heaven and Earth—
speaking of Heaven and Earth, the Dao is within them.

Only Heaven and Earth could embrace this *qi*,
thus they move endlessly without obstruction.
The *qi* that endures ten thousand years without decay,
the *qi* that flows ceaselessly, nourishing all beings—
this is the same *qi*, interconnected.

The Sage follows Heaven and Earth:
what is sincere within becomes manifest without—
though inner and outer differ, the *qi* is the same;
what is fully realized in oneself becomes accomplished in others—
though self and others differ, the *qi* is one.

What is its nature?
It is emptiness alone.

One thing is connected, and all things are connected;
emptiness is never unclear—
one thing is illuminated, and all things are illuminated.

Confucius said:
*"To govern is like the North Star in its place,
while all stars naturally revolve around it."*

Mencius said:
*"The gentleman transforms through cultivation of spirit,
aligning above and below with the flow of Heaven and Earth."*

This is truly beyond thought or time—
the *qi* naturally permeates, silently and steadily.

Thus it is said:
*"The world’s vastness comes from me, its peace from me.
The multitude of beings comes from me, their nurturing from me.
The distance of past and future comes from me, their connection from me."*

The Sage’s Dao is vast—
truly beyond comparison.

Consider this:
The universe has no end, yet the Dao encompasses it—
how great is it!
Gold and jade are the most rigid, yet the Dao penetrates them—
is it not firm?

Yet it is said in the *Odes*:
*"The mandate of Heaven, solemn and unceasing."*
And again:
*"Heaven’s bearing, without sound or scent."*
This is the utmost softness.

Still, without this *qi*, Heaven and Earth would be inert and useless;
without this *qi*, beings would be dull and lifeless.
With it, they live; without it, they perish.

Thus *"the utmost softness overcomes the utmost hardness"*
—because without *qi*, there would be no things.

Whether vast as the Three Thousand Worlds,
or minute as dust or hair,
all are contained within it.

Not only is it the utmost softness,
but also the utmost nothingness—
is this not what Confucius called:
*"Visible yet unseen, heard yet unheard,
grasped yet intangible?"*

How could there be separation?
Yet when things reach extreme softness, they become useless.
Only the Dao’s utmost softness
can sustain the world’s utmost hardness.

When things reach utter nothingness, what purpose do they serve?
Only the Dao’s utter nothingness
can govern the world’s myriad existences.

This is nothing more than the undivided *qi*,
flowing without obstruction.

Thus the Most High says:
*"I know the great benefit of non-action."*

Moreover, Heaven and Earth act without action yet transform,
the Sage governs without action yet brings order—
how could there be a single being or thing not under its influence?
Is this not because of the *qi*’s all-encompassing and vast reach?

Within the human body, the vast *qi* circulates naturally,
without need for manipulation,
spontaneously irrigating the entire body,
filling every hair—
its benefits are indeed profound.

As for teaching,
the ancients relied on personal example, not words.
The teaching of teaching is not as true
as twice the teaching of non-teaching.

Heaven speaks not, yet the four seasons proceed;
the Sage speaks not, yet the world transforms.
To observe the Sage’s natural ease, his loose robes and composed demeanor—
is this not the same path of non-action leading to natural perfection and self-governance?

Thus it is said:
*"The teaching of the unsaid, the benefit of non-action—
few in the world attain it."*

Alas, how lamentable!

This describes the Dao’s natural non-action,
encompassing Heaven and Earth,
nurturing all beings,
rooted in the Great Harmony’s singular *qi*,
flowing through the cosmos,
penetrating Heaven and humanity—
whether vast or minute, hidden or apparent,
all are complete within it.

Thus the utmost softness nurtures the utmost hardness,
the utmost nothingness contains the utmost existence.

Hence, one connection leads to a hundred connections,
one movement sets all in motion—
like an echo in an empty valley,
each sound responds in harmony.

The divine mystery of the Dao—
none can surpass it!
Who but the Sage could truly align with this?

If one is but a novice,
yet possesses true faith and great resolve,
following the Way and keeping to its path,
progressing from shallow to deep,
from low to high,
starting with exertion yet eventually attaining natural ease,
then alone can one penetrate its essence.

One must endure with patience again and again,
bear with forbearance again and again—
through the twelve times of day,
never letting aversion arise,
never abandoning one’s resolve.
Only when deep understanding is gained,
dwelling in tranquility and nurturing life,
finding abundance on all sides,
does one suddenly grasp the intent of the Supreme Dao,
truly without a shred of falsehood.

As for refining the primordial foundation,
the ancients said:
*"When essence is born, there is the time to prepare the medicine;
when the medicine is produced, there is the time to gather it."*

The primordial *qi* of the Dao gives rise to essence,
essence gives rise to *qi*—
this is the principle by which Heaven and Earth generate life,
in accordance with the Way.
If one follows it naturally,
though able to bear sons and daughters,
essence will be consumed, *qi* will scatter,
and life will be exhausted, ending in death.

The Supreme Dao pities mortal beings,
wandering through birth and death,
endlessly cycling through rebirth,
and thus reveals the method of reverse cultivation:
returning to the root, restoring youth to age,
transforming weakness into strength,
so that one may attain immortality and verify sainthood,
forever free from generation and annihilation.

Initially, it teaches one to cultivate emptiness and foster stillness,
beginning from the state of no knowledge, no awareness,
and seeking the realm of knowledge and awareness.
The *Yijing* speaks:
*"Silent and unmoving, yet responsive and thus in harmony—*
this is it."

The essence of post-heavenly cultivation has form,
while the essence of pre-heavenly cultivation leaves no trace—
in that vague, indistinct state,
there is yet something within.
This is called *the opening of the Mysterious Pass*,
*the foundation of the Great Ultimate*—
from here, one must focus the spirit upon emptiness,
unify the *qi* with the void,
illuminate the mind internally,
and observe the breath of each inhalation and exhalation,
its opening and closing,
its ascent and descent,
its gathering and returning to the central palace,
its bathing and nourishment.

In time, at the moment of profound obscurity,
suddenly a thought arises from the rule,
a *qi* emerges from the void—
this is essence giving rise to *qi*.

This *qi* is not of material form—
if it were of material form,
then it would have beginnings and endings,
fixed quantities.
How could it encompass Heaven and Earth,
enter the finest hairs,
penetrate without trace,
shatter without resistance?

This *qi* is the very source of life for all beings—
obtaining it brings life,
losing it brings death.
Though it is the utmost softness,
it can govern even the Sage;
though it is the utmost nothingness,
it can rule all things.
The ancients likened it to medicine,
for it can cure old age and disease,
nurture the immortal embryo.
Thus it is called *"the Elixir of Longevity"* and *"the Nectar of Rebirth,"*
and also *"the True Person’s Root of Immortality"—*
truly the world’s greatest treasure.

The ancients said:
*"A thousand taels of gold cannot buy a single strand of this."*

If mortals can obtain this *qi*,
then eternal life is within reach.

Yet the method of gathering it
must also conform to the central and the proper,
only then will there be no calamity.
If one has the medicine but fails to blend it well,
to brew and refine it properly,
to take it at the right moment,
to nourish it by the correct method,
and if the fire’s strength—whether fierce or gentle—
the medicine’s balance—whether aged or fresh—
and the dosage—whether heavy or light—
are all mismanaged,
then like the inversion of yin and yang,
or the disruption of cold and heat,
it will occur out of season,
bringing forth natural disasters,
and causing all things to perish.

Students who master the Supreme Dao’s preceding chapters,
putting forth effort with precision,
will never err.

I reveal the function here to this single principle—
truly worth more than ten thousand gold.
If one perceives this principle clearly,
then there is mastery everywhere,
the medicine of eternal life can be obtained,
and the realm of the immortals is without difficulty.

**Chapter Forty-Four: The More You Hoard, the More You Lose**

Which is closer to you—name or body?
Which is more abundant—body or wealth?
Which brings greater suffering—gain or loss?

Thus it is said:
*"The more you cherish, the greater the expense;
the more you hoard, the greater the loss."*

Knowing contentment brings no disgrace,
knowing restraint brings no peril—
this is how one endures long.

Those who love name and wealth
all believe that name can exalt their body,
and wealth can enrich their body—
if the body lacks name, it will be obscured and unnoticed;
if the body lacks wealth, it will be destitute and unbearable.

Thus, the greedy for name
forsake their body without hesitation;
the covetous of wealth
lose their body without regret.
The *Guozi* says:
*"The avaricious man sells himself for riches,
the valiant man sells himself for name."*

Human nature is such,
and this lament has been the same from ancient to modern times.

Yet consider: which is closer—name or body?
Name is external,
body is internal.
People seek name for the sake of their body—
why then, through name, do they lose their body?
Is it that name is truly close,
while the body is truly distant?

Which is more abundant—body or wealth?
The body is precious,
wealth is base.
All seek wealth for the sake of their body—
why then, through wealth, do they lose their body?
Is it that the body is truly less,
while wealth is truly more?

Think deeply on this!

If one has name but loses their nature,
or has body but lacks name,
which has gained, which has lost?

To abandon the body while wealth remains empty,
or to lose wealth while life is preserved—
which endures, which perishes?

From this perspective,
it is better to gain the body and lose name and wealth
than to gain name and wealth while losing the body.

Moreover, those who love name and wealth
damage their spirit and life,
and their excessive love brings great expense;
those who hoard deeply
employ deceitful schemes,
injure their body and mind—
their hoarding thus brings great loss.

To be held in high regard is to be feared by the state,
to accumulate wealth is to be censured by Nature.
From ancient times onward,
how many have been mighty yet met with misfortune,
rich yet faced execution!

All because they failed to restrain themselves,
did not retreat and conceal,
were insatiable in their greed,
thus inviting hatred from the people
and retribution from Heaven.

Only those who know contentment and restraint
walk the middle path,
remaining stable to the end.
Free from disgrace and peril,
they neither falter nor topple,
and thus long preserve their body,
extending even to their descendants.

When **Fan Li** thus escaped the calamity of **Goujian**,
and **Zhang Liang** attained the leisure of **Chisong’s** wanderings—
truly, they were men who grasped the subtle Dao!

Later seekers shall take heed of this sage teaching.
Here, **contentment and restraint** are likened to **stopping the fire to nurture the elixir**,
with **reputation** as **light** and **wealth** as **medicine**.

Those who covet illusory splendor are often ensnared by demons,
those who labor to transport riches rarely escape misfortune and blame.

Before the medicine returns to the furnace,
one must **stir the fire** to refine it;
once the medicine enters the cauldron,
one must **halt the fire** to preserve it.

If the fire burns **excessively** and one fails to stop it,
not only will the elixir be ruined and the cauldron overturned,
but one will invite sickness and disaster—
even death by flames, and great peril.

And yet, the **Great Dao** is empty and boundless,
with no place for grand distinctions among men.
Some indulge in fine wine and delicacies,
in beauty and adornment,
in gold and jade, pearls and gems,
in pavilions and palaces.

Others face **heavenly demons**, **earthly demons**,
**ghostly demons**, and **spiritual demons**,
all manner of trials that test the **Way**—
some boast of becoming **immortals**,
others claim to be **true persons**,
proudly asserting they have ascended to the **Celestial Hall of Precious Treasures**—
yet in a single distracted thought,
their **spirit and life** are shattered,
and many who pursue the **Great Dao** fail to achieve it.

Again, some revere the **manifestation of the Spirit**,
and **San Shi** become their scourge—
they believe there is a body beyond the body,
and in an instant, their **golden elixir**, their greatest treasure,
is stolen and destroyed.

Such cases, and countless others,
all stem from **unrestrained fire**—
the elixir **reverts** when the fire is not halted,
the **refinement** fails when the **nurturing** is neglected—
thus, their **intentions scatter** while their **spirit perishes**,
their **external pursuits disrupt** their **inner essence**.

For those who cultivate the **Way**,
illusions of **reputation**, **form**, **light**, and **appearance**—
all must be **dismissed with a single stroke**,
held with no attachment whatsoever.
If one thus **knows restraint** and **contentment**,
constantly **nurturing the numinous elixir**,
then one attains **the utmost excellence**,
and **never falters or declines**.

**Chapter Forty-Five: The Great Fulfilled as if Deficient**
The great fulfilled appears as if deficient,
yet its efficacy is **unexhausted**.
The great abundant seems as if void,
yet its efficacy is **unending**.
The great upright bends like a bow,
the great skilled feigns clumsiness,
the great eloquent speaks softly.

**Calmness** overcomes **coldness**,
**stillness** surpasses **heat**,
and **serene clarity** is the **true norm** for the world.

The **Dao** is naturally empty and boundless,
moving with the **Heavenly order**,
following **nature’s inclination**,
harmonizing with **Heaven and Earth’s** transformation,
rising and setting with the **sun and moon**—
having nothing yet encompassing all,
doing nothing yet accomplishing all.

When the **Great Dao** is not yet complete or full,
there are still **traces of action**,
some **form and image** may be glimpsed,
and one feels **self-satisfied and content**,
overwhelmed with delight—
until, upon its **great completion**,
it seems as if **deficiencies multiply**,
the **greatly fulfilled** appearing as if **unfulfilled**.

In the **great abundance’s** wake,
it seems **vast and formless**,
the **greatly full** appearing as if **unfull**.

Is this not **the more one studies, the worse one fares**,
or **the more one excels, the more one is censured**?
No.

The **Dao** is the innate perfection of all beings,
pure and empty, still and unmarked—
when **form and spirit** are both wondrously refined,
and one’s **self** merges with **True Reality**,
then **self and Dao** are one—
what **fulfillment** or **abundance** remains?

If **fulfillment** and **abundance** persist,
they are still **dual with the Dao**,
not yet reaching the **domain of divine transformation**.

Thus, those who cultivate the **Way**
**grow in having yet become without**,
**increase yet decrease**,
never perceiving **fulfillment** or **abundance**.

Hence, the **greatly fulfilled** appears as if **deficient**,
and the **greatly abundant** as if **void**.

Therefore, all phenomena are **emptied**,
while **True Reality** stands alone.

**Taking things as conditions**,
**responding to time naturally**—
truly, it **penetrates Heaven and Earth**,
**spans ancient and modern**,
and **applies universally**.

How could its efficacy ever be **exhausted**?
How could its efficacy ever **cease**?

When the mind is empty as the sea,
the spirit still as a mountain,
yet one feels utterly useless—
then suddenly the vast *qi* stretches forth,
most rigid, most great—
how could it be so straight?
Yet in return, one feels the unbearable strain of constraint.

Its marvels are boundless—
how could they be so skillful?
Yet one only feels the stupidity of ignorance.

Words are plain, yet their meaning profound;
speech is concise, yet its logic subtle.
Speaking only when necessary,
uttering only when the time is right—
how could eloquence be so vast?
Yet one only feels stammering,
as if unable to speak at all.

Because of this—
this bending, this clumsiness, this stammering—
the heart aligns with emptiness,
the will aligns with humility,
virtue aligns with breadth,
and deeds grow ever more exalted.

This is truly the Dao’s return to voidness,
learning’s return to spontaneity.
In harmony with Heaven and Earth’s turning,
unaware of the sun and moon’s passage—
thus its greatness endures.

If one follows the *Taiji*’s principle,
aligns with the constant of yin and yang,
and endures prolonged cultivation—
when lead and fire are fully charged,
withstanding nine colds without faltering;
when the elixir is refined and settled,
forgotten after three cycles—
is this not what the *Taishang* meant by:
*"Agitation overcomes cold, stillness overcomes heat"*?

When clarity and purity reside within,
void yet luminous, unencumbered—
returning to the boundless heaven,
embracing the harmonious symbol—
what agitation, what cold, what stillness, what heat remains?

The scholar, possessing a serene and tranquil mind,
transforming the seasons of cold and heat,
concentrates the body’s true *qi*—
thus the true Dao of the world stands firm.
What fear is there of errant paths emerging?

This is the ultimate plainness, the ultimate commonality,
the ultimate emptiness, the ultimate nothingness.
Before one attains the realm of voidness and commonality,
one only perceives fullness, not deficiency;
only perceives advantage, not disadvantage.
Thus the *Taishang* said:
*"The less one has, the more one gains.
The more one has, the more confused one becomes."*
The proverb states:
*"A great bell makes no sound,
a full jar makes no echo."*
Truly not false.

The great virtue is non-virtue—
thus it has virtue.
Great action is non-action—
thus it has action.
Not empty words.

The Dao originates as voidness, one *qi*.
Because of its attainment,
it is without attainment;
because of its lack of attainment,
it is true attainment.
Thus, the higher the Dao,
the lower the heart;
the greater the virtue,
the more humble the will—
this aligns perfectly with the Dao.

If one has any excess,
one boasts arrogantly, swells with pride,
despises others, domineers—
its lack of Dao and virtue is then evident.

Thus the *Taishang* said:
*"In learning, one gains daily;
in the Dao, one loses daily—
lose again, lose again,
until reaching nothingness"—
only then is attainment achieved.

The scholar must not view cultivating the Dao or refining the elixir
like the crafts of a hundred artisans,
deeming progress as personal gain.
If cultivation centers on voidness as its essence,
and reaches the stage of forgetting—
this is progress.
When forgetting is forgotten,
one has entered the realm of transformation.

For the Dao-seeker,
retreat is advancement,
weakness is strength.
Empty becomes full,
nothing becomes something,
reverse becomes upright,
subtraction becomes addition.
Thus, the scholar’s progress or stagnation
depends solely on whether the heart forgets or not.

---
**Chapter 46: When the World Has the Dao**

When the world has the Dao,
warhorses are driven out to manure fields.
When the world lacks the Dao,
warhorses breed in the suburbs.

No crime is greater than desire,
no calamity greater than discontent.
No fault is greater than greed.

Thus, contentment is true contentment.
When the world has the Dao,
rulers and people alike are at ease.
Conquest is unnecessary,
so horses are released to the forests,
fields are plowed and tilled,
merely to fertilize the land.

When the world lacks the Dao,
chaos has already taken root.
Some act out of necessity,
bandits rise in succession,
weapons clash without cease—
how could warhorses not be bred
to meet the state’s demands?

Thus, the horses’ retreat is because of the Dao,
their proliferation is because of its absence,
and their fate is tied to the world’s great affairs.

Alas!
How could we have rulers who rule,
ministers who minister,
fathers who father,
sons who son,
upholding benevolence, discussing righteousness,
reviving poetry, explaining ritual—
a time when the capital embodies the Dao?

Yet prolonged peace and indulgence
breed desires and greed.
Having this, lacking that,
exotic arts and licentious crafts
are arrayed before them.
Fine clothes and delicious food are insufficient—
they then covet palatial chambers.
A thousand-li domain is still too narrow—
they then lust after ten-thousand-li fiefs.

Alas!
Within, they indulge in moral decay;
without, they indulge in bestiality.
Adding to this, they prioritize profit and swift results,
exhaust resources through warfare,
demand excessively, crave endlessly—
internal and external chaos ensues.
What awaits but ruin?

All this stems from a single desire’s origin.
When desire arises, greed follows;
when greed arises, the unobtained is sought;
when obtained, fear of loss takes hold.

If this continues,
moral order will not collapse,
calamities will not arise,
the state will not fall,
the world will not perish—
this will never occur.

Thus it is said:
*"No crime is greater than desire."*
If desire did not exist,
how could greed arise?
If greed does not arise,
then the winds of superficial harmony, superficial completeness, superficial beauty
would not be difficult to restore.

And it is said:
*"No calamity is greater than discontent."*
If one’s desire does not arise,
this will is always satisfied,
this heart always at ease—
neither seeking from the world
nor harming others.
When success comes, one accepts it;
when failure comes, one accepts it—
where would calamity originate?

Again it is said:
*"No fault is greater than greed."*
If one knows contentment,
one naturally guards one’s portion,
accepts one’s fate,
follows the seasons,
listens to Heaven—
without flattery, without arrogance,
without contention, without seizure—
walking the straightforward path,
long basking in the great harmony—
what fault remains?

When much more, the true heart shines within, the true nature solidifies within.
Cultivate oneself in stillness,
ever finding joy within.
Act in one’s natural station,
without conforming to the external.

Thus, those who naturally hold the world
always preserve their world;
those who hold a state
always preserve their state;
those who hold life
always preserve their life.

What they fear is that once desire arises,
it cannot be pruned away,
ultimately leading to extreme extravagance and insatiable craving—
and no remedy exists.

Is there a Dao to seek the world and obtain it?
From ancient times, gain and loss hinge on a single thought.
A thought once turned astray
becomes a great calamity.
This is how reckless desire brings about boundless ruin.

Life and death depend on the slenderest thread.
If that thread is preserved,
great plans will surely unfold.
This is how mastering the thought
creates boundless blessings.

Thus, one knows that desire, though initially slight,
grows vast in the end—
how can one not be cautious?

Hence it is said:
*"Know contentment, know contentment is always contentment."*
Those who do not know contentment
seek more and lose more,
and in losing, seek even more.
Thus they exhaust their strength and weary their spirit,
toiling endlessly—
what benefit is there?

Do they not know that prosperity and adversity,
gain and loss,
are governed by Heaven,
not by human effort?

Why toil ceaselessly and grow ever more incompetent,
when rest and ease would be the wiser choice?

If one knows contentment,
they follow nature effortlessly,
act without striving—
what need for worry or concern?
Without schemes or grasping,
where could they go without thriving?

Let people take heed!

This likens the world to the human body and the horse to the alchemical use of fire to refine elixirs. If a person is in accord with the Dao, their vital essence is full and their qi abundant—why then refine elixirs? To follow and preserve this naturally is sufficient.

If one lacks the Dao, however, their essence wanes and their qi scatters, forcing them to rely on the primal spirit and true breath to cultivate body and mind. Yet at the outset of practice, they must first nourish the external fields, hence the saying: *"Warhorses are born in the wilderness."* Only when yang vitality arises and medicinal herbs flourish may they proceed with the alchemical methods of advancing fire and withdrawing talismans, the tactics of field battles and fortress defense—until all impurities are purged and pure radiance emerges, as when the world is at peace and the state at ease, and the horses are returned to Hua Mountain, hence the saying: *"Drive the horses back to manure."* Yet the chaos of the world and the peril to one’s self all stem from a single thought of desire. If unchecked, it festers unseen, until essence and marrow are exhausted, and life itself cannot be preserved—how tragic is this?

When desire arises in mortals, they must seek its fulfillment to find satisfaction. Even if all wishes are granted, the abyss of desire is unfathomable, greed insatiable—coveting the next hill after gaining one, endlessly turning without rest. Those who possess the world lose it, and those who possess life—how could they not lose both?

The *Odes* say:
*"Without schemes or grasping, where could they go without thriving?"*
Only those who know contentment may dwell in tranquility, ever abiding in the Dao’s realm. No need for meritous deeds to mend deficiencies—only follow nature, and merge with Heaven.

The *Tao Te Ching* warns: *"No fault is greater than the desire for desire"*—these words teach one to cut off encroaching desires at their root, to guard against subtle transgressions, and to discipline the craft of covetousness, just as Confucius spoke of *"caution in solitude"* and the Buddha taught *"right awareness."* All are one in the Dao.

Have scholars ever glimpsed this truth?

**Chapter Forty-Seven**
*"What is not wrought is perfected."*

The Way lies unseen within the home—
yet one may know the world.
No need to peer through windows—
yet one may perceive the Dao.

The farther one roams,
the less one knows.
Thus the Sage acts without striving yet knows,
sees without gazing yet names,
does without doing yet achieves.

The virtuous one possesses all things within,
yet refines the self without stepping beyond the courtyard.
Yet the shifts of the world’s order,
the changes of human hearts,
and the principles of preserving life while averting ruin,
of reversing chaos into harmony—
all are clearly understood within the mind’s breadth.

Is this mere craft or calculation?
Truly, all things share the same source—
to exhaust the logic of one’s self
is to grasp the logic of all under Heaven.
Thus, without leaving home, one knows the world.

Ancients shaped creation through the mind—
no need to conceal light behind windows—
yet the silent Emperor’s mandate,
the unceasing decree of Heaven,
and the mysteries of growth, decline, and transformation,
of yin and yang’s creative work—
all are fully illuminated within the heart.

Is this mere conjecture?
It is because Heaven and humanity are one continuum—
to cultivate one’s own life
is to align with the Emperor’s decree.
Thus, without gazing through windows, one sees the Dao.

If one wanders abroad, inquiring into the world’s affairs,
or consorts with wise friends, probing the subtleties of Heaven’s will—
one may gain some knowledge.
But if one seeks it not within but without,
not in the self but in externals,
even what is learned will be less than what lies near.

The Way is plain before one’s very door—
why abandon the near for the distant?

Mencius said:
*"Speaking near yet pointing far is good speech,
and guarding brevity while applying broadly is the good Way."*
By this reasoning, learning grows ever nearer yet farther,
ever more concise yet expansive.
How can the near and the concise be neglected?

Thus the Sage embraces unity to encompass the three,
observes emptiness to cultivate stillness,
never stepping beyond the door or hut,
never treading the dusty markets,
dwelling among trees and stones,
roaming with deer and swine,
moving not a step,
consorting with no one—
seemingly lonely and secluded.
Yet when the spirit is steadied, wisdom arises—
though not walking, one surpasses those who do;
though lacking knowledge, one surpasses those who know.

Common folk take what they see as their aim,
but the Sage does not seek what is seen—
thus, all day long, in the unseen and unheard,
one rests from thought and discards artifice;
in what is unseen and unmanifest,
one guards against deceit and covetousness.

With only inner knowing,
and no outward perception—
seemingly obscure and void—
yet the boundless gives rise to life.
Though unseen, it is ever more manifest;
though unnamed, it is ever more established.

As for the myriad beings of the world,
the mysteries of hidden spirits and ghosts—
all are wrought by this Way of non-action.

With order and essentials,
beginning and ending are fulfilled.
What is feared is being ensnared by perception,
fixated on names and forms,
boasting of achievements,
and clinging to opinions—
thus the mind is scattered, unable to unite in virtue,
the heart is mixed, not like the Great Void.
Hence the Way fails to manifest, and virtue does not transform.

No wonder one exhausts a lifetime’s spirit
yet gains nothing.

If applied to governing the world,
one cannot follow principle to perfection—
without action, yet all under Heaven aligns of its own accord.
If applied to cultivating the self,
one cannot refine the void and merge with the Way—
without action, yet the self attains true perfection.
What use is mere external seeking?

Thus the virtuous one must be cautious in solitude,
for the essence of humanity and the origin of Heaven’s decree
are known without seeking.

This speaks of the Way’s foundation in non-action,
and its key in caution in solitude—
thus, without action, there is no failure to act;
without knowing, there is no failure to know.

Yet this is not the lifeless non-action of withered wood and ash.
Earlier, I spoke of *"all phenomena emptied, one spirit alone illuminated"*—
this is the true meaning.
Again, *"one awareness moves, one yang arises"*—
this is the primal qi.

Gathering herbs or refining elixirs
is but the cultivation of these two—nature and life.
Without true meaning, where would nature depend?
Without true qi, how could life be refined?
To gather true meaning with true qi,
when the two merge into one,
it returns to the origin of the Great Polarity—
this is called the Elixir.

Thus, within non-action, there must also be action;
within non-knowing, there must also be knowing and awareness—
only then does one not fall into emptiness or cling to form.
When practice deepens, emptiness becomes form,
and form becomes emptiness.
In time, both are forgotten,
and all things merge in harmony—
thus one attains perfect alignment with the Dao.

If one does not know the Way of humanity,
one may still perceive the Way of Heaven.
Confucius said:
*"Heaven speaks not—
the four seasons proceed, and all things are born."*
This is the essence of non-action.

Non-action is Heaven’s nature,
action is Heaven’s decree;
non-knowing is the original spirit,
awareness is the original qi.
Between Heaven and Earth,
without the two, there is no transformation;
without the one, there is no mystery.
Mysterious yet not mysterious,
not mysterious yet mysterious—
thus one attains the marvel of unity within duality,
of mystery within transformation.

This cannot be fully expressed in words.
It lies with you, the cultivator—
nurture the void and the still,
constantly guard the empty and the luminous,
and thus nature and life will endure,
while the Great Way may be fulfilled.

Do not confuse non-action with action,
nor cling to one side—
though it be the true path,
divergent roads will open.
Consider this well.

**Chapter Forty-Eight**
To practice the Way is to daily diminish,
to study is to daily increase.
To practice the Way is to daily diminish—
diminish again and again,
until one reaches non-action.
In non-action, there is no failure to act.

Thus, those who take the world
must act with non-interference.
When interference arises,
it is insufficient to take the world.

Scholars memorize words and chapters,
and artisans seek teachers and friends,
gathering much knowledge and hearing much—
only then do talent and wisdom emerge,
and skill and cleverness arise.

The wider one’s knowledge and the more one acts,
only then can one wield the brush to compose with ease,
or swing the axe to shape with mastery.
Thus it is said: *"To study the Way is to daily increase."*

But to practice the Dao is the opposite.
If one engages in extensive reading and broad familiarity with ancient texts,
yet fails to turn inward and reflect,
to guard the heart with singular focus—
then though one gathers knowledge and multiplies insights,
the mind will scatter and the spirit will falter.
Though learning grows, the Dao recedes,
until at last one is lost in the boundless *Tai Ji*,
drowned without trace.

Thus, those who cultivate the Dao must strip away like peeling a banana leaf,
draw out like unraveling silk—
the more one strips, the less remains;
the more one draws, the more empty it becomes,
until one reaches the realm of *wu-wu* (non-nonbeing).
Only then has one attained it.

When cultivation reaches this stage,
naturally one’s mysteries are unfathomable,
one’s transformations boundless:
When gathered, there is presence;
when dispersed, there is absence.
Desire one thing, and it is one;
desire ten thousand, and it is ten thousand.
The sun, moon, and stars revolve at my command;
the winds, clouds, thunder, and rain obey my designs.

What is its grandeur?
Yet scholars, grasping one principle while discarding another,
like eating sugarcane—
eating it all, then casting it aside,
still return to nothingness.
Thus it is said: *"To practice the Dao is to daily diminish,
diminish again and again,
until one reaches non-action."*
In non-action, there is no failure to act.

Observe those who seek to take the world—
they must not hesitate to raise armies and mobilize troops,
to brandish weapons and clash steel.
How can there be *wu-shì* (non-affairs)?
Yet within the chaos of affairs,
one must return to *wu-shì*,
only then can one unite heart and virtue,
and devise strategies in seclusion.
Then the mind will not scatter,
and wise counsel will emerge.

Thus, commanders issue strict orders:
ears hear nothing external,
eyes see nothing external,
the heart wanders not to external schemes—
only then can one adapt to circumstances,
and take the world as easily as turning the palm.
Otherwise, in endless agitation,
the more affairs, the more disordered the mind;
the more disordered the mind, the more dimmed the spirit.
Before the enemy even arrives, one cannot remain composed,
and at the first clash of arms, the ranks dissolve in chaos.
To hope for victory in such a battle—
how difficult is it not to be?

What of when the eastern barbarians are not yet pacified,
and the western nomads rise anew—
one crisis not yet resolved, another erupts—
if one does not master stillness to govern movement,
or ease to await exhaustion,
few escape defeat and ruin.
In antiquity, those who fled in defeat,
whose cities fell in ruin,
all succumbed to the perils of *you-shì* (affairs).

Thus, military strategy employs tactics of deception and ambush.
When the enemy sees banners filling the horizon,
trees trembling like soldiers,
even facing a great foe, they are struck with dread,
and few dare not flee at first sight of the wind’s warning.

Only by treating affairs as if they were nonexistent,
abandoning all entanglements,
and focusing the heart inward—
like Zhuge Liang amid a million troops,
his hair unbound, fan in hand, effortlessly composed—
could he secure the survival of Shu,
and prolong the Han dynasty in its hour of peril.
But if one’s heart is entangled in affairs,
then the mind is already disordered,
the spirit masterless—
like Xu Shu, who could no longer offer counsel
when his mother returned to Cao Cao,
and thus failed to aid the First Emperor in restoring the realm.
How could such a one ever take the world?

This speaks to those who cultivate the Dao:
if they see only increase, not diminution,
then their mind grows dim and the Dao fails to coalesce.
Thus it is said: *"Virtue is singular;
two or three, and it grows dim."*
Only by refining and forgetting in turn,
forgetting and refining in turn,
does one not become an obstacle to the Dao.
If memory clings without release,
chattering endlessly—
this is indeed the Dao’s greatest concern.
Thus, one must gradually dissolve and extinguish
until there is nothing left at all.
Only then is nature fully realized.

Then, from nonbeing arises being,
and one can move unseen among spirits,
transformations beyond measure.
The scriptures say that *"the world"* symbolizes the Dao,
*"taking the world"* symbolizes cultivating the Dao,
*"affairs and non-affairs"* symbolize *you-wu* (action and non-action).
If one can remain pure and non-acting,
one is purely *xiantiān* (primordial) *qi*—
how difficult is it then to attain the Dao?
This is the essence of taking the world.

But if one clings to forced action,
it is all *hou-tian* (later-heaven) contrivance,
and one falls into a side path.
This is the opposite of taking the world.

Some may ask: *"How can alchemy and elixirs be non-action?"*
Know this: one follows the ascent as it ascends,
not forcing it to rise;
one follows the descent as it descends,
not forcing it to fall.
Even if gathering is endless,
and refining methods are manifold—
it is all pure spontaneity,
without a trace of contrivance.
Though there is action, it remains *wu-wéi* (non-action).

Those who merely swallow saliva and breathe *qi*
can never obtain the elixir or attain the Dao.

---
**Chapter Forty-Nine**
**The Sage Has No Fixed Heart**

The sage has no fixed heart,
but takes the hearts of the people as his own.
To the virtuous, I am virtuous;
to the wicked, I am also virtuous—
thus virtue is complete.
To the trustworthy, I trust them;
to the untrustworthy, I also trust them—
thus virtue is sincere.

The sage dwells in the world,
blending his heart with the people’s.
All eyes and ears are fixed upon him,
yet the sage treats them like children.

The sage’s heart is empty, hollow, clear, and luminous—
nothing is excluded from its perception,
yet nothing is obscured within it—
like a still mirror reflecting the moon,
its radiance shooting in all directions.
It responds to each thing as it is,
without preconceived intent—
how vast is its clarity!
It encompasses the great without exclusion,
embraces the small without omission—
beauty and ugliness, good and evil,
none escape its grasp—
how boundless is its capacity!

Yet what is this heart?
It neither forces nor resists nature—
thus it is said: *"The sage has no fixed heart."*
Meaning the sage does not anticipate before arrival,
nor clings when it has passed—
only now is present, without attachment.
It is nothing but adapting to circumstances,
responding to each moment,
taking the hearts of the people as his own.

And what is the people’s heart?
Only to love the good and hate the evil.

Thus the sage treats the people’s goodness with encouragement and praise,
and the people’s unvirtuousness without failing to guide and lead them toward the good.
Both goodness and unvirtuousness, the sage encompasses with boundless virtue/power.
The good are moved with joy and devotion, ever more striving to be virtuous;
the unvirtuous awaken with resolve, transforming their ways to follow the good.
This is **"virtue/power of goodness"**—
when the ruler cherishes goodness, none dare fail to comply.
The workings of response and transformation are such,
and none can escape their natural unfolding.

The sage also treats the people’s faithfulness with reverence and elevation,
and the people’s disbelief without failing to love and admire them.
Both faith and doubt, the sage unites with sincere, undivided embrace.
The faithful find peace and clarity, deepening their trust;
the doubters rise with vigor, abandoning disbelief to embrace faith.
This is **"virtue/power of sincerity"**—
when the ruler cherishes sincerity, none dare act without genuine feeling.
The principle of giving and receiving is so profound,
that none can doubt its divine effect.
The people’s virtue returns to its natural simplicity—
why should there be any hesitation?

Since all share the same heart,
and all hearts follow the same principle,
the sage views all hearts with one mind,
and aligns all principles with one truth.
Though the world be vast, he contains it with sincerity;
though the people be numerous, he unites them with righteousness.
Even among the wise and deceitful, the loyal and false,
all are treated with equal fairness—
greatly impartial, without self.
With open sincerity and public fairness,
he places his heart in theirs,
blending all under heaven into one principle—
the people daily advance in goodness without knowing it.
The marvel of his transformative influence
is far beyond the later ages’ exhortations to filial piety and loyalty,
or teachings of propriety and righteousness.

Thus it is said: *"He blends the hearts of all under heaven with simplicity."*
He sees all under heaven as one family,
unites the realm as one person—
his benevolence and compassion embrace all,
as one with the people in this way.
Thus the people submit to his virtue/power and cherish his benevolence,
loving him as their parents,
revering him as gods,
looking up to him like teachers and guardians.
Whatever they hear or see,
they always measure it against the sage’s words and bearing—
none know anything beyond this.
Hence it is said: *"The people fix their ears and eyes upon the sage."*

The people’s expectation of the sage is such—
yet what other way does the sage have?
Only to govern the multitude with gentleness,
to lead the people with compassion—
like parents to their children:
whether wise or foolish, clever or dull,
his love is the same;
he lifts them up and protects them,
guiding them with sincerity.
If the world has any who cannot be transformed,
such do not exist.
This is the governance of *wuwei*—
how distant it is from those who rely on words and laws!

In the scriptures, **"the sage"** is a metaphor for the heart,
**"all under heaven"** for the body.
The sage’s cultivation of the self
is nothing but nurturing the fundamental spirit and fundamental qi.
Yet since humans have fundamental spirit, they also have ordinary spirit;
since they have fundamental qi, they also have ordinary qi.
At the outset of practice,
how could one not summon other thoughts,
or fail to stir ordinary breath?
Only those who understand the Dao and cultivate it for long
will see the fundamental spirit arise.
If I treat it with an even mind,
even if other thoughts remain unbanished,
I still treat it with an even mind.
If the fundamental spirit has any who cannot see it,
such do not exist.
Once the fundamental spirit is born,
cultivation of the Dao has a foundation—
one must then quietly guard the elixir field,
and regulate and nourish the fundamental qi.
If, at this time, the fundamental qi moves on its own,
I must treat it with harmonious qi—
even if ordinary qi has not yet ceased,
I must still treat it with harmonious qi.
If the fundamental qi has any who cannot make it arise,
such do not exist.

One must know:
the fundamental spirit is obscured by ordinary spirit,
like a bright mirror long sealed by dust—
if not quickly polished and washed,
how could it suddenly become clear?
The fundamental qi is drowned by ordinary qi,
like white silk stained by oil—
if not well cleansed and rinsed,
how could it return to its original state?

At this point, if one generates a single restless thought,
or stirs a single harmful intention,
it is as if seeking the fundamental spirit as one’s true self,
while the ordinary spirit only grows stronger.
To seek to see one’s true nature—
is this not difficult?
To seek to restore one’s fundamental qi—
is this not a great challenge?

Only the sage’s governance of all under heaven
does not distinguish between goodness and evil, sincerity or falsehood—
he treats all with a heart of benevolence and loyal simplicity:
the good he treats as good,
the unvirtuous he also treats as good;
the faithful he treats as faithful,
the unbelieving he also treats as faithful.
A sphere of pristine simplicity,
blended in his embrace.
This is emptiness—
this is the Dao.
Emptiness naturally gives rise to spirit,
the Dao naturally gives rise to qi.
There is nothing that does not come naturally in response.

Otherwise,
if the heart is not emptied,
there is no Dao from the start—
and if one seeks to conquer the spirit’s obscurity,
or make the Dao’s qi endure,
how could this be attained?

The cultivation of the self and governance of the world
share the same Dao,
and the principles are one without division—
to know how to govern the world is to know how to cultivate the self,
to understand external causes is to understand internal principles.
Thus, to illustrate this principle,
its teaching to the learner is most profound and intimate.

The student must diligently guard the true constant,
and skillfully nourish the emptiness—
then the fundamental spirit and fundamental qi
will naturally return to their original state.
If one generates even a single extraneous thought,
or stirs a single extraneous breath,
one may fear that without cultivation, the Dao cannot be attained,
and the more one cultivates, the further the Dao recedes.
Let the student be cautious and warned!

**Chapter Fifty: The Profound Nurturing of Generation**
Birth and death.
Those who cultivate birth alone number thirteen;
those who cultivate death alone number thirteen;
those who cultivate life—
moving toward death—also number thirteen.
Why is this so?
Because of the profound nurturing of *generation*.

「For those who skillfully govern life,
walking upon land they meet no rhinoceros or tiger,
entering battle they are not struck by armor or weapons.
The rhinoceros finds no place to lodge its horn,
the tiger finds no opportunity to strike with its claws,
and weapons find no opening for their blades.
Why is this so?
Because they dwell in the realm of no death.

The Dao of Heaven and Earth in generating beings,
though countless transformations and changes occur,
without end or limit,
yet their Way does not exceed
the interplay of yin and yang—
swelling and waning, advance and retreat,
preservation and annihilation.
Between these lies nothing beyond
the flow of *Taiji*'s principle and qi.
Life and death are but day and night.
Day and night cycle endlessly,
their motion ceaseless,
just as life and death cycle,
endlessly alternating without cessation.
Yet within this bending and extending,
this coming and going,
the fundamental nature remains
a duality of opposing forces,
with no deviation.

Speaking of those who traverse birth and death,
ten out of thirteen meet yang-qi and live,
and ten out of thirteen encounter yin-qi and die.
Those who do not conform to Heaven and Earth’s constant yin-yang,
who gain yang and live yet remain like others—
after birth, their knowledge unfolds and desires arise,
senses are stirred by material cravings and actions multiply,
thus they exhaust their vital essence and deplete their spirit,
shortening their lifespan and forfeiting their fate—
these are the ones who move toward death’s domain,
also ten out of thirteen.
Thus the number of those who live
does not surpass those who die;
the power of yin
exceeds that of yang.

The principle and qi of *generation* and *transformation*,
do they not fear exhaustion?
Yet the origin of *Taiji* is
soundless and odorless—
when it moves, yang is born;
when it stirs, yin emerges.
It manifests as the Five Phases,
scatters as myriad things,
exhausting all oddities and variations,
beyond speech or name,
yet never lacking.
Thus, when one follows its course,
life flows endlessly;
when one opposes it,
all returns to the root.

When life is extinguished, death takes its place;
when death is still, life stirs anew—
this is the Way of *generation* and *transformation*,
the virtue of utmost sincerity in the numinous,
embedded within it,
from the beginning of all things to this day,
unchanged.

If not so,
all things would have life but no death,
and the teeming multitude would fill the cosmos,
leaving no room for Heaven and Earth,
nor the means to nurture life.
This is why the waxing and waning,
the swelling and emptying,
serve to uphold the principle of life.

If one knows the profound nurturing of Heaven and Earth’s *generation*,
it lies in this waxing and waning, swelling and emptying.
Thus, one observes the Way of Heaven,
follows Heaven’s course—
within the killing mechanism, they seek the life-giving,
and from death’s grasp, they draw vital qi.
They embody the seasons of spring, summer, autumn, and winter,
and contain the functions of growth, flourishing, gathering, and storing.

By guarding in harmony yet acting in opposition,
Heaven and Earth nurture and transform all beings,
and thus maintain their eternal, unobstructed state;
the human body returns to its origin,
