to become a sage of extraordinary virtue,
eternal and unchanging—
all this arises from the same source.
This is not the task of the feeble!

Only those who excel in governing life
employ the inverted methods of yin and yang,
the counteracting ways of *generation* and *transformation*—
descending to ascend,
advancing to retreat;
stilling to observe the natural,
moving to await yang’s emergence;
urgently driving the celestial stem,
leisurely guarding the alchemical furnace;
returning to *Taiji*,
restoring to utmost sincerity;
exiting with entering, nonexiting with exiting—
enduring from ancient times to the present;
aligned with sun and moon,
united with Heaven and Earth…
Thus, even when confronting great trials,
they find no obstacle impassable.
Even fierce as rhinoceros or tiger,
they dissolve into kindred harmony;
even sharp as armor or blades,
they vanish as if never existed.

Why then fear the rhinoceros’s horn,
the tiger’s claws,
or the weapons’ strike,
and calculate life and death,
existence and annihilation,
in a single moment?
Why is this so?
Because they dwell in the realm of no death.»

「Moreover, the Sage refines their nature and establishes their fate over years.
When gathered, they take form;
when dispersed, they become *qi*.
The sun and moon revolve at my command,
the winds and thunders obey their summoning.

Though rhinoceros and tiger rage fiercely,
and weapons may seem formidable,
how can they reach what has no form?
For only the formless can master the formed—
never the formed compel the formless!

Alas, all things with form have life and death,
but the Sage, formless, transcends them.
They govern the source of life and death—
all things see them as life and death,
yet what calamity or harm can they inflict?
This is the tenfold *Dao* of Heaven and Earth’s completeness,
the threefold interplay of *yin* and *yang*.

Humans draw upon the *yang* of *Buqian* (乾) for birth,
and meet the *yin* of *Bunkun* (坤) to die.
This is the fundamental principle of *Taiji*—
the interplay of *yin* and *yang*,
flexing and extending,
cycling endlessly in cause and effect.

Without *yin*, *yang* cannot manifest;
without death, life has no meaning.
Thus, retreat and repose are but ways
to nurture the profound *virtue/power* of life’s continuity.

Yet in their abandon to passion and desire,
where reason perishes and humanity is lost,
their death differs from others.
For those who follow *yin* and *yang* in life and death
are truly embraced by *Taiji*’s boundless essence,
their *true qi* flowing in harmony with the two principles.

But those who defy Nature’s *yin-yang* cycle
have lost their original *yuan qi* (元气),
and are overwhelmed by excessive *yin* and malevolent forces.

Hence, they are said to be *“ten with three”* (十有三).
The ten represents completeness—*Dao* encompassing Heaven and Earth;
the three comes from Heaven’s *One* (天一) generating Water,
Earth’s *Two* (地二) generating Fire,
and the union of Heaven’s *One* and Earth’s *Two* forming Water and Fire’s threefold harmony.

Moreover, Heaven’s *One* generates Water,
and Metal also generates Water;
Earth’s *Two* generates Fire,
and Wood also generates Fire—
thus the four symbols are complete.

The Earth has no fixed position,
wandering through the four symbols,
and in its purest, most refined essence—
the *Dao*’s ultimate substance—
it governs *yin-yang qi*,
drives the mechanisms of creation,
and in Heaven and Earth becomes *Wujiji* (无极).

Yet *Taiji*’s principle in the human body:
stillness brings forth the *yuan shen* (元神) of soundless purity,
unity without duality;
movement manifests *liang zhi liang neng* (良知良能) in timely action—
*true intent*.
Together, they form the Five Phases.

This is the shared *virtue/power* of life’s continuity
for Heaven, Earth, and all beings—
present in all things,
or returning to the boundless *Taixu* (太虚),
unchanged by life and death’s rise and fall.

Thus, those who skillfully cultivate life
enter their chambers in quietude,
observing the *One yang*’s return and renewal,
and drawing it upward, downward,
or back to the alchemical furnace for nourishment.
With ninefold transformations complete,
the fire’s essence is fully refined.

This is the *Dao*’s height—
where dragons and tigers submit,
and the *virtue/power* is so profound
that even ghosts and spirits yield.
What harm then from rhinoceros, tiger, or blade?

Consider the ancients:
dwelling in secluded mountains,
with rhinoceros and tigers in throngs,
and wolves as companions,
yet willingly tamed,
content in their own boundless freedom—
many such examples exist.

Or single-riding warriors
who command the loyalty of warlords,
who abandon armor and weapons,
revered as divine,
loved as kin—
such devotion arises from *Dao*’s profound *virtue/power*.

And when filial piety moves Heaven,
or bandits surrender in sincerity;
when chastity and righteousness stand firm,
and deceitful hearts are stilled—
all these stem from the *virtue/power* of utmost sincerity.

Observe again:
the rhinoceros cannot strike with its horn,
the tiger cannot grasp with its claws,
the weapons cannot find purchase—
truly, this is no falsehood.

Essentially, the *One yuan*’s *li qi* (理气)
is not the *yin-yang* of creation.
I can remain serene and bright,
formless yet luminous—
what then of life and death?

Those who know life and death
are bound by its traces,
but I can erase those traces,
returning to the undivided *ming* (命),
the *Taihe* (太和) of Heaven.
Though traces of life and death may appear,
the principle endures unobstructed—
why then rejoice in life,
or dread death?

The ancient Sages chose *yi* (义) over life,
sacrificed their bodies for *ren* (仁).
They saw knives and saws as commonplace,
and boiling cauldrons as trifles—
why was this so?
Because they found *Dao* within,
and knew no fear without.

Hence it is said: *“the realm of no death.”*

And the sevenfold completion of Water,
united with the tenfold, makes thirteen.

**Chapter 51: Reverence for the Dao and Esteem for Virtue/Power**
The *Dao* gives birth,
*virtue/power* sustains it;
things take form,
and forces complete them.
Thus, all things reverence the *Dao* and esteem *virtue/power*.

The *Dao*’s reverence,
the *virtue/power*’s nobility—
none command them, yet they endure naturally.
The *Dao* gives birth,
*virtue/power* sustains it,
nurtures and matures it,
refines and perfects it,
covers and protects it.
It births without possession,
acts without reliance,
nourishes without domination—
this is *xuan de* (玄德), the profound *virtue/power*.

The *Dao* has no name,
and without a name, it is *Wujiji* (无极).
This is the pure *qi* of emptiness—
the foundation of life’s continuity
for Heaven, Earth, and all beings.
For it is neither presence nor absence,
neither vast nor minute—
it encompasses and covers all things without distinction,
hence called *“Great Virtue/Power”* (大德).

The *Dao* is the shared *Taiji* of all things;
the *virtue/power* is each thing’s individual *Taiji*.
Thus, all things draw upon life’s source,
rooted in *Taixu*’s principle,
and the *One yuan*’s *qi* spreads boundlessly.

No matter their size or subtlety,
all rely on the *Dao* for life,
and entrust their essence to fate’s decree.
Within them, *yin-yang* harmonize,
and the sun and moon revolve—
like grasses and trees:
resting in day and night,
nourished by dew and rain,
and thus their roots are cultivated.

This is the *Dao* giving birth,
and *virtue/power* sustaining them.
All things, receiving this nurturing,
emit a fragrant influence—
first as *qi*, then as form.
By this, they grow unseen yet steadily,
day by day, month by month,
until humans become fully human,
and things attain their own perfection without flaw.

Thus, all things reverence the *Dao* and esteem *virtue/power*.
For the *Dao* is the principle of life’s creation—
without it, life cannot be sustained.

*Virtue/power* is the origin of nurturing all things—
without it, there is no way to store and cultivate.
How exalted is the *Dao*, how noble is *virtue/power*?
Yet both are granted from Heaven,
and take things as their occasion.
No need for forced action—
naturally aligned with the *Dao*.
Without contrivance, *virtue/power* is spontaneously fulfilled.
No one commands it, no one ordains it,
yet ever thus—
nothing coerced, all returns to nature.

What *Dao* is this?
The grand central path of Heaven and Earth,
the essential key for sages to attain immortality and enlightenment.
If one seeks to cultivate the Golden Immortal,
how else but through the *Dao*?

Thus, one concentrates the spirit in emptiness,
unites the *qi* in boundlessness.
At the threshold of void and stillness,
a single *yuan*’s true *qi* emerges—
this is the birth of the *Dao*.

Once the *Dao* arises,
one then nourishes it in stillness,
draws from it in motion.
Hold fast to the One,
and all phenomena dissolve into emptiness.
Ever maintain, never forget, never assist—
then *virtue/power* has its foundation.

Follow the *Dao* in its unfolding,
and the *Dao* will grow daily.
Nurture *virtue/power* in its essence,
and *virtue/power* will flourish daily.
To grow and nurture—
like things thriving in abundance,
until autumn arrives and ripeness is assured.

When the *Dao* is thus complete and ripe,
its cultivation and preservation—
what differs from humanity?
It is but returning to the unformed origin,
restoring the undivided truth.

Ultimately, what is this "generation"?
Its generation is the self-moving *qi* of void.
Why claim it as mine to possess?

Even when the signs of *yang*’s birth appear—
internally circulating the Heavenly Canopy,
externally pushing the Dipper’s handle—
it seems like action,
yet purely entrusted to nature,
without the slightest contrivance—
not acting, yet not relying on action.

Thus, as *virtue/power* advances daily and the *Dao* lengthens,
the transformations of *wen* and *wu* unfold,
and the bath of sealing solidifies—
all governed by the original spirit.
This is having a master yet no master,
no ruler yet a ruler present.

Such cultivation—
how deep and distant the *Dao* becomes!
Thus it is called *"Xuan De"* [Mysterious Virtue].

**Chapter 52: The Origin of the World**
The world has its origin,
which becomes the mother of the world.
Having obtained its mother,
one knows its child;
having known its child,
one returns to guard its mother—
thus, no danger in life’s end.
Close the *xuè* [mouth], seal the gate,
and labor ceases for life’s span.
Open and return to clarity—
no harm befalls the body—
this is called *"clinging to constancy."*

The essence of the Golden Elixir—
what else is it?
Only the primordial *yuan*’s true *qi*,
which the ancients likened to true lead,
to golden blossoms,
to white snow,
to the *White Tiger*’s first crescent *qi*—
all metaphors for the same:
when *Kàn* [Heaven] and *Kūn* [Earth] unite,
*Kàn* loses a *yang* and descends into *Kūn*’s palace,
while *Kūn* receives this *yang*’s true golden nature,
thus solidifying into *Kǎn* [water].
Hence, the elixir is called "golden"—
it falls from *Kàn*’s domain,
known in the body as *yang* essence.
Though dwelling in the water palace,
it is the primordial *qi*,
the mother for elixir cultivation.

The adept, standing before the furnace,
must press the *yang* lead from the water palace
to serve as the elixir’s mother.
Thus it is said:
*"All the body’s blood is pure yin,
The body’s yang essence, none recognize."*
This essence—
neither within nor without,
not entering the six senses’ gates,
not among the six dusts—
hidden in the form’s mountain,
seen yet unseen, heard yet unheard,
yet ever generating—
the true seed, the great root of the body.

If the *yin* essence lacks this primordial *yang* lead as its mother,
the *yin* essence scatters easily,
with no way to congeal into the elixir.

The *qi* thus is the same as that of all sentient beings—
boiled and refined in the cauldron and furnace;
then the primordial *Dao*’s *yang* essence and the ultimate *yin* essence,
emerge from the void’s extreme stillness and profound obscurity—
this is the elixir’s mother, also the mother *qi*.

With *yang* fire pressing it,
it ascends in flight to the *níhuáng* (泥丸),
mingling with long-accumulated *yin* karma and dissolving,
descending to the upper palate,
transforming into sweet dew—
this is the *yin* essence, also called *ziqi* (子气).

From this point, it descends to the *Chónglóu* (重楼),
pouring into the divine chamber,
to be ingested and swallowed,
nurturing the gentle divine fire,
adjusting and nourishing this primordial *Dao*’s *yang* essence and ultimate *yin* essence.
Thus, the *Tàishàng* (太上) says:
*"Once the mother is obtained, the child is known;
Once the child is known, return to guard the mother."*
At first, the mother clings to the child and comes,
then the child clings to the mother and dwells,
and finally, the child and mother unite in harmony and mutually nurture,
as *yin* and *yang* reverse and merge in oneness—
though the body perishes, no harm befalls.

From this moment, firmly adhere to the central rule:
one soul internally contained,
ensuring that internal thoughts do not stray outward,
external thoughts do not intrude inward,
keeping silent and sealing the six gates,
softly and subtly, unchanging and unceasing,
neither helping nor forgetting,
acting or not acting as if diligent or not.
Thus throughout life, the *Jīnxiān* (金仙) attainment is realized.

Otherwise, if one seeks external gains,
one has already lost the internal treasure.
As it is said:
*"When the mouth opens, the divine *qi* scatters;
When the mind is disordered, the fire’s power wanes."*
Heeding the external while neglecting the internal—
the life’s essence is already spent,
where then is the life’s root?
Thus, it is unsalvageable throughout one’s life.

If a person can close the *duì* (兑) and seal the gates,
preserving the essence and abundance of *qi*,
merging the mother *qi* and child *qi* into the elixir,
the ancients said:
*"The Primordial Emperor of the *Yuánshǐ* (元始) suspended a single millet-seed pearl in the void,
seemingly present yet not present,
seemingly empty yet not empty—
only those who silently recognize and merge their hearts can perceive it.
None are smaller than this elixir;
those who can see it are truly wise."*

When the *yang* essence arises,
the entire body softens like silk—
this is the utmost gentleness.
If one can guard this gentlest *qi*,
without mingling a single thought,
without adding a single perception,
over time, it will naturally give rise to vast, surging *qi*,
piercing the heavens and passing through the sun.
Thus, *"Guarding softness is called strength."*

However, at the outset,
the divine light descends to illuminate the *qìhǎi* (气海),
then the fire steams and the water boils,
the golden essence radiantly manifests,
like tides, like flames,
like mist, like smoke—
then I must withdraw my sight and return my hearing,
protecting its brilliance,
guiding it back to the earthen cauldron,
restoring it to my primordial *yīqì* (一气).
If small, it repels illness and prolongs life;
if great, it achieves immortality and sainthood—
what harm can the body suffer then?
Otherwise, old age, sickness, and death,
come swiftly—
how can one not grieve?
All this is self-made;
the heavens do not predestine such limits.

If a person does not cherish the *Dao*,
do they not at least cherish their body?
Do not abandon your body to calamity—
regret will come too late.

This is the *Dao* of True Constancy,
only the Sage can follow its constancy,
without defying its way.
Thus, day by day, month by month,
they reach the divine and boundless,
the ever-changing and unfathomable.
The saying goes:
*"Steadfastness is the foundation for sainthood;
a vacant heart is the vessel for the *Dao*."
How can one neglect this?*

This refers to the true *yang* essence,
which originates from the moment one receives *qi* and forms the body.
Life arises from *qi*;
how can *qi* be trifled with?
Consider before birth—
was there not already this *qi*?
After death—
this *qi* is not necessarily extinguished.
The primordial *yīqì* (一气) hangs suspended in the great void,
wherever there is form, *qi* is within it,
wherever there is no form, *qi* returns to the great void.
All things within heaven and earth,
with tangible manifestations,
have birth and death to speak of,
but this *qi* neither generates nor perishes,
neither defiles nor purifies,
neither increases nor decreases—
empty yet not empty,
not empty yet empty,
most divine and most wondrous—
thus it is the initial *qi* for all things under heaven to sustain life ceaselessly.
If a practitioner of the *Dao* knows this initial *qi*,
then the way of longevity can be attained,
and the station of the Immortals can be realized.

The Immortals have no other marvel,
merely clinging to the *yang* essence and nurturing the *yin* essence,
refining them endlessly—
then the *yin* essence transforms into *yang* essence,
and the *yang* essence returns to *yang* spirit,
as it is said:
*"This body is not the body of a mortal,
but the body of an Immortal from the *Dàluó* (大罗) Heaven."*

If one were to cultivate only one aspect,
how could one attain the marvel of both form and spirit,
united with the True *Dao*,
reaching the utmost extremes of transformation,
and achieving the most saintly and enlightened state?
Thus, when the fire’s time arrives,
the golden elixir manifests,
naturally forgetting speech,
forgetting taste,
forgetting smell,
seeing yet not seeing,
hearing yet not hearing—
as it is said:
*"The elixir field holds treasure,
naturally forgetting all emotions when confronted with phenomena."*

This is the principle of valuing the internal over the external,
guarding the internal while forgetting the external—
an immutable law.
Yet before obtaining the elixir,
one must still close the *duì* (兑) and seal the gates,
to accumulate essence and *qi*.
Know that the small elixir is the mark of wisdom,
and guarding the *Tàihé* (太和) brings natural strength.

Entering *qi* with the spirit,
storing the spirit within *qi*—
suddenly, a single millet-seed pearl,
its light illuminating the *dharma* realm—
this is the golden elixir’s radiance,
the moment when the Great *Dao* is about to manifest.
At first, the spirit descends to await the *qi*,
then the *qi* arises,
and again the spirit presses it upward,
drives it back to its source—
thus the elixir of longevity is obtained,
and what harm can the body suffer?

This is achieved by constant vigilance,
unceasingly flowing forth.
---
**Chapter 53: Walking the Great *Dao***

*"Though I have some knowledge,*
*walking the Great *Dao*,
*only reverence is my fear."*

The Great *Dao* is most level,
yet people prefer narrow paths.
The morning is most clear,
yet the fields grow overgrown,
the granaries empty;

「Wearing fine silks and adorned with sharp blades,
disdaining sustenance yet hoarding wealth—
this is called the *thief of the Dao’s staff*.
How far from the *Dao*!

The *Dao* of the gentleman begins with husband and wife;
the *Dao* of the sage does not stray beyond yin and yang.
If one can move in accordance with Heaven,
and act naturally in alignment with one’s nature—
to cultivate oneself is *ren* (benevolence),
to harmonize with things is *zhi* (wisdom).
Uniting inner and outer to achieve singular accord,
thus all things find their place and all actions are fitting.
What need is there for contrivance or artifice?
Yet the *Dao* is fundamentally level and plain,
without hidden strangeness or monstrous deviation.
In the latter ages, men loathe moderation,
preferring the bizarre and novel,
and so they stray to crooked paths,
unaware of their true course.

Take the court: its laws and institutions
are indeed the means to transform the people,
yet those in ignorance fail to recognize them,
and instead seek only to overturn them.
They delight in new arrivals and despise old ministers,
favor constant upheaval and change the state’s governance.
Ancestral precedents are discarded,
as though a man’s vital *qi* were wounded and lost.
Morning policies are abandoned,
the countryside lacks proper models.
Thus, the people neglect farming for ease,
fields and lands grow overgrown,
grasses and herbs are left untended,
and the sources of wealth are exhausted.
Daily waste increases,
granaries are depleted,
legumes and grains vanish,
and stored wealth is spent.
Is this not like the depletion of a man’s *jing* (essence) and *qi*,
consumed and worn away until none remains?

They do not seek inner substance,
only outward display.
Thus, garments must gleam with utmost splendor,
blades and swords must vie in sharpness and brilliance,
feasts must overflow with rare delicacies,
and treasures must be piled high—
unmindful of the scarcity of their roots,
only competing in the flourishing of their branches.
Yet to desire endless taking,
unexhausted use,
and seamless execution—
is this not sheer folly?

All this stems from neglecting the natural *Dao* of Heaven,
and abandoning the unadorned *Dao* of daily practice.
It is as though a thief steals goods,
hiding his head while exposing his tail—
like a staff standing upright,
its shadow seen but its form concealed.
This is a metaphor for those who practice the *Dao*
but confuse falsehood for truth.

The Great *Dao*—how lofty it is!
This fleeting comprehension is but sudden knowledge,
unplanned, unforced—
no need for contrivance or artificiality.»

「Vast and boundless, the primordial *qi* of Heaven and Earth at their first opening—
this is the original *ancestral qi* of the Preheaven.

It is like Mencius’ sudden perception when seeing a child fall into a well,
that moment of shock and compassionate distress.
My *Dao* speaks of knowledge arising from utter ignorance—
this is true *liángzhī* (良知, innate true-knowing).

The stirrings of such true-knowing are not difficult to recognize,
but to act upon them and sustain them eternally—
this is no simple task.

When it moves, it is right before one’s eyes,
yet in an instant, the lure of external things arises,
desires well up, emotions take shape—
unaware, it slips into the private knowledge of Postheaven.

This is why it must be tempered and applied,
and why it is so formidable.

Only when the eyes hold the *smart pearl* (智珠),
and the chest wields the *wisdom blade* (慧剑),
can one pierce through demons,
sever the threads of desire—
from refining elixirs to attaining the *still point* (还丹),
all are true-knowing unfolding into true abilities,
a straightforward path with no oddity—
this is why the Great *Dao* is so level and easy.

Alas, the Great *Dao* is plain and ordinary,
yet those who rush forward in haste to seek results
often fail to follow the broad and straight way,
straying into narrow, twisted paths,
mistaking themselves for those who have attained the *Dao*,
yet never awakening in their entire lives—
how lamentable this is!

*"Zhōu yù"* (朝喻) is the body;
the body wishes to be refined, not destroyed,
so one must suppress evil and preserve sincerity—
only then can human desires begin to be purified,
and Heavenly principle fully realized.

With time, the luminous light shines forth,
how could the field of the heart ever be overgrown?
With spirit unified, how could the storehouse ever be empty?
Without embroidered adornment, one’s honor is self-fulfilled;
without ointments or grains, one’s hunger is satisfied—
why cling to outward embellishments,
or weary of food and drink?

The *wisdom blade* is sharp indeed—
external weapons are of no use.
The Three Treasures fully attained—
internal wealth is inexhaustible.

If one attains this,
they truly steal Heaven and Earth’s luminous *yáng* (灵阳) *qi* to make the elixir.

Yet today’s practitioners,
not following *Zhōngyōng* (中庸, the Middle Way),
daily incline toward perverse paths—
a body encrusted with dust,
its filth too vast to cleanse.
Moreover, they recklessly provoke misfortune,
losing the primordial *yáng* (元阳) *qi*.

Thus, distractions come thick and fast,
leaving no trace of an empty, transcendent spirit.
When desolation reaches its peak,
are not the orifices fully sealed?
When strength is exhausted and spirit weary,
what sign of fullness remains?
Poverty is cause for sighing—
has not the vital *qi* been fully drained?

They boast of outward brilliance,
yet their adornments are but superficial.
They praise the blade’s edge,
yet their belts are mere symbols of status—
this is already the end!

Yet they persist: disdaining food to indulge in rare delicacies,
coveting wealth to seek abundance—
why not ponder how practitioners cleverly manipulate techniques,
stealing back primordial *qi*,
seeking what lies within rather than without?
The *Yì* (《易》, *Book of Changes*) says:
*“The one who composes the *Yì* knows the art of thievery.”*
This is exactly what it means.

If one abandons this to seek other paths,
how fragmented can it be—
dare one call it the Great *Dao*?

Others comment that the phrase *“Jièrán”* (介然)
and the following sentences speak of a fleeting moment
when a single thread of light appears—
how could this not be knowledge?
Yet when tested in actual practice,
one often falters in execution,
so it is called formidable.
This reveals the difficulty of understanding the Great *Dao*.

The next section discusses scholars
who fail to grasp the fundamental source,
merely priding themselves on outward refinement,
who do not seek true traces
but only chase empty fame—
like standing a staff and seeing its shadow,
grasping its likeness but not its truth,
so they are called *thieves of the staff* (盗竿).

This critique is also apt.
From ancient times, all who practiced the *Dao*,
their skin supple and radiant,
their hair lustrous and bright—
these effects, though common to all,
cannot yet be taken as definitive proof.

What of refining the *jīng* (精, essence) and *qì* (气, *qi*)?
When the *yáng* (阳) light touches,
how could the *yīn* (阴) gloom endure?
Like frost and snow melting in the sun.

Thus, even chronic ailments and old diseases
transform into festering sores and pus,
discharged through the large and small intestines—
not only novices suffer this,
even at the stage of the *Great Elixir* (大丹) attainment,
changes still occur.
The Three Corpses and Six Bandits (三尸六贼),
bleeding and oozing pus,
their stench unbearable—
only those whose mind is settled and intent
can truly trust in the principles,
and deeply verify the scriptures.

One must know that suppressing desire and preserving sincerity,
removing turbidity and retaining clarity,
each layer reveals *yīn* *qi* dispelled
and *yáng* *qi* subtly growing—
practitioners of the *Dao* must not overlook these inner transformations.

Do not fixate on a radiant body,
or swift, light steps—
these are not reliable signs.
Nor trust in levitating through clouds,
appearing and vanishing like ghosts and spirits,
instantaneous transformations of *yáng* *qi*,
or traversing ten thousand miles in a moment—
these too are not to be taken as the *Dao*.

For marvels and oddities,
heterodox and perverse teachings,
must lead one astray,
creating deviant forms.
What a pity that one’s entire life’s energy
is wasted in left-handed paths and false gates!
To seek transcendence yet fall into the Three Paths and Six Realms (三途六道),
is this not deeply regrettable?

The essence of this chapter from the *Tàishàng* (太上, Supreme Venerable)
teaches that only by recognizing true-knowing
can one avoid error.

Yet today’s practitioners of the *Dao*,
only seek delicate complexions,
and robust physiques—
they do not realize that external labor
wears the mind,
while the inner fields lie fallow,
the treasure-houses empty.
Many are harmed by Heaven’s *qi* before they even begin.

Postheavenly treasures, though present,
are of what use?
Truly, when the Three Treasures unite,
the outer form naturally glows.
Those who chase external effects,
boasting of food and clothing—
why not seek within,
and first enrich the inner wealth?
When the inner wealth is abundant,
the outer wealth will follow naturally.
Is this not unlike thieves,
who steal not Heaven and Earth’s luminous *yáng* *qi*,
but only the fame of sages’ cultivation?

**Chapter 54: Cultivating the Body**
Those who build well do not topple,
those who embrace well do not fall away—
their descendants’ sacrifices never cease.
Cultivating the body,
its *virtue* (德) is complete;
Cultivating the family,
its *virtue* is abundant;
Cultivating the village,
its *virtue* grows long;
Cultivating the state,
its *virtue* becomes plentiful.」

「Cultivating the Dao in the world,
its *virtue* becomes universal.
Thus, one observes the body through the body,
the family through the family,
the village through the village,
the state through the state,
the world through the world.
How do I know the world is thus?
By this.

Heaven and Earth, in giving birth to humanity,
endow them with *qi* to establish their fate,
and with *principle* (理) to complete their nature.
*Principle* and *qi* originally are one,
nature and fate are never separate.
All arise from the pure, empty *qi*,
swirling through Heaven and Earth,
filling and emptying in ebb and flow,
purely natural, as creation unfolds,
across countless ages.
Though humanity may differ in refinement,
and the Creator’s gifts may vary in measure,
this *qi* is the same,
this *principle* is the same—
never altered, never changed.

The Sage stands at the center, erect and unshaken,
independent yet not leaning,
upright yet not faltering.
Though prosperity and adversity, gain and loss,
joy and sorrow, life and death
differ in myriad ways,
this *principle* and this *qi*
flow within a single body,
filling the two great realms—
never diminished, not even slightly.
Is this not what is meant by *“those who build well do not topple”*?
Otherwise, though solid and substantial—
even towering Tai Mountain, lofty and vast,
still faces ruin and collapse.
For what is shaped, though firm, cannot endure.
Only the formless *principle* and *qi*,
unchanging with things, unmoved by numbers,
enduring through all ages, ever new.

This Dao established within oneself,
transforming and harmonizing all people,
naturally brings deep benevolence and abundant grace,
penetrating to the marrow and flesh,
so the people love and revere them,
clinging in devotion without separation—
not even for a breath’s parting.
Though bound like glue and lacquer, it may be firm;
though mingled like water and milk, it may be harmonious—
yet gathering and scattering are transient,
change and transformation swift,
and in a moment, discord appears.
But benevolent heart and benevolent reputation—
those touched by its grace love without forgetting,
those who hear its wind cherish without abandoning.
How different from children depending on their parents,
or limbs obeying the heart—
no separation, no estrangement.
Is this not *“those who embrace well do not fall away”*?

From this, the virtuous cherish their own and their kin,
the petty delight in their own pleasures and seek their own gains—
yet all govern under the same banner,
revering the rest of the effortless.
Though the Sage may not always remain,
their *virtue* and grace, deeply rooted in the heart,
endure through all ages without ceasing.
The *Odes* say:
*“Generation after generation, descendants,
do not let it fade away.”*
Is this not what is meant?

Once, Confucius praised Shun’s great filial piety:
*“The ancestral temple enjoys him,
his descendants preserve him.”*
This shows that *virtue* is boundless,
and descendants’ sacrifices
are never interrupted through the ages,
renewed at every ancestral feast.
It is said:
*“He who has *virtue* for ten generations
shall have descendants to preserve it for ten generations.
He who has *virtue* for a hundred generations
shall have descendants to preserve it for a hundred generations.”*
And for those whose *virtue* endures eternally—
though billions of years pass,
their descendants, generation upon generation,
grow ever more numerous and flourishing.
Such is the unchanging truth.

All this comes from the Dao of effortless naturalness,
cultivated within oneself yet never lost,
extended to others yet never forgotten—
thus Heaven’s body thrives eternally,
and the world enjoys boundless blessings.

When a person cultivates this Dao in their body,
their form and spirit both attain perfection,
merging with the True Dao.
Dao is body, body is Dao—
the two are one, without division.
What then is *virtue* but truth?

Moreover, when the Dao is cultivated in a village,
the village unites as one body;
when the Dao is cultivated in a state,
the state regards itself as one person.
How much greater then is its *virtue*’s length and abundance?

If one can remain still and govern without action,
serene and desireless,
naturally the winds of the four directions will stir,
the world will turn toward benevolence,
the people will cherish its *virtue* without end—
how universal is its *virtue*!
This is not achieved by coercion or enticement with gain.
It stems from the innate goodness within,
cultivating the true heart in freedom—
like a wandering child longing for home,
or an old man rejoicing in reunion.
Joy comes unbidden yet arrives naturally.

Though people differ in form, their hearts are not unlike,
their fates the same, their natures aligned.
Thus, manifesting *virtue* renews the people,
and calming them begins with self-cultivation—
no difference exists.

Moreover, a village is the accumulation of a family,
a state the expansion of a village.
The world, vast and populous,
is nothing but the gradual extension of one family,
one village, one state.
To know the Dao of one person is to know the Dao of family, state, and Heaven as one.
To cultivate oneself is to cultivate family, state, and world.
Seeking within, one must extend outward—
thus, silently transforming, effortlessly changing customs,
the people naturally rejoice together in harmony.
Therefore it is said:
*“Only when *virtue* transforms does humanity arise,
and only when humanity arises do customs form.”*
Its Dao originates in the body,
its *virtue* extends to the family,
and its transformation spreads like grass bending before the wind—
reaching no distance it does not encompass,
spreading through village and state to the world.

Ah! How do I know the world is thus?
By this.

The *Classic of Changes* says:
*“Great is the Primordial of Heaven!
Firm and strong, moderate and correct,
pure and complete, precise and unceasing!”*
This is the Dao as preheavenal *Yin* gold,
most firm, most vigorous,
erect between Heaven and Earth,
flowing through all things within,
embracing all without remainder,
its sincerity never exhausted.
Its power ever extends yet never bends,
straight yet unyielding—
supporting Heaven, sustaining Earth,
reaching the Han, piercing the sky,
the state never even slightly toppled.
Yet it is the utmost of the boundless,
the most divine of the unmanifest,
thus standing unshaken to this day.»

The human being, if able to establish their foundation in the boundless (*wújí*), erect their primordial spirit (*yuánshén*), then as the ancients said: *"harvest the great medicine from the stillness within"* and *"conduct the fire-alchemy within the realm of non-action."* Dwelling at the center and establishing the pole, the vast *qi* arises—*qi* dependent upon spirit, spirit and *qi* mutually resonating, knotted as one cluster, thus returning to the root and restoring the command—*Dao* abides forever.

Now, the birth of humanity: spirit and *qi* unite; in death, spirit and *qi* separate. If one harmonizes nature and life, blending spirit and *qi*, then they embrace the Primordial and preserve the One. My life is determined by me, not by Heaven—what escape is there? Thus, spirit depends upon spirit, *qi* upon *qi*, a single pulse transmitted, a single truth permeating—one can then undergo myriad transformations, vanishing as ghosts and emerging as gods, manifesting countless, boundless forms, enjoying countless, boundless years. How could it be otherwise than: *"a child begets a grandson, who again begets a child, child upon child, grandson upon grandson—roots deep and leaves flourishing, source distant and flow enduring, with unbroken ancestral rites across ten thousand generations?"*

Yet it is no more than using primordial *qi* as medicine and primordial spirit as the alchemical fire. For primordial *qi* is no-*qi*, primordial spirit is no-spirit. Refining *qi* with spirit to attain the *Dao* is like refining medicine with fire to forge the elixir. Common elixirs may succeed or fail, but the divine elixir knows no end nor beginning—thus it is said: *"The Great Elixir Dao endures ten thousand years without erosion."* It is nothing but cultivating one’s own virtue and perfecting one’s own body, not relying on later acquisitions or external sources—thus its virtue is true.

Though Heaven and Earth give birth to humanity, clean and turbid differing, the wise and unwise varied, yet when the august and sacred descend—from the family to the world—each possesses an inherent constancy. Thus, a single heart can permeate myriad households, a single virtue can inspire myriad people. Cultivating the self to harmonize the family leaves virtue in abundance; cultivating the self to transform the village extends virtue’s reach. As for governing the state and pacifying the world, it is nothing but *"sitting in ease while the world transforms"*—there is no external cultivation of the self that can universally attain such success. This is the constant *Dao* of the age.

Conversely, if one cultivates the self, how could it differ? Discussing the state and the world, it is fundamentally a matter of progressing from near to far, layer by layer. When the three—refined essence (*jīng*), *qi*, and spirit—are all present, it is not a step-by-step process. Speaking of initial practice: *"refining essence,"* yet *qi* and spirit are also involved. The second step: *"refining qi,"* yet spirit and essence are also present. The third step: *"refining spirit,"* yet essence and *qi* are also there. Returning to emptiness and merging with the *Dao*, the *Dao* merging with nature—from beginning to end, they never depart. To depart is to lose the *Dao*.

The body is like essence, but essence is not the essence of mutual resonance—it is the primordial essence received from the void’s five elements at birth. Cultivating the body is refining essence into *qi*. The practitioner, at the outset, obtains this essence to live and grow with it. Cultivating with essence is no different from cultivating the body itself—how true is this! Speaking of *qi*, essence is closer to the body, while *qi* is slightly more distant. *"Cultivating the family, its virtue is abundant"*—for gathering external true yang *qi* to refine internal true yin essence is like harmonizing the self to govern the family. What surplus does one not possess from what is inherently one’s own?

The village, when viewed from the body, is even more distant, yet closer than the family—just as spirit is. Spirit, like fire, belongs to *qi* in its heat and to spirit in its light—thus, two yet one. Cultivating the village is refining spirit back to emptiness. Hence it is said: *"Its virtue is enduring,"* for longevity and vast duration. As for the state, compared to the village it is nearer, yet more distant than the body—its breadth and width cannot be seen at a glance. The state is like the void; cultivating the state is refining the void to merge with the *Dao*. When refining reaches the void, it becomes one with pure emptiness, illuminating the great thousand—how much more so the world! Thus it is said: *"Its virtue is universal."*

As for observing the body through the body, the family through the family, the village through the village, the state through the state, and the world through the world—it is nothing but using one’s own self and family as the model for the world’s self and family, taking the highest as an analogy. The intent is profound, the meaning subtle. The Great *Dao* has no other path—refined to its utmost, reaching the natural void. Exhausted!

Those who study the Great *Dao* have no other way than *"reducing again and again, until non-action is natural."* Non-action yet nothing remains undone—exhausted!

Yet internal elixirs and external elixirs, internal *dan* and external *dan*—filling the *kàn* with *lì*, extracting lead and adding mercury—various metaphors and names, ultimately no more than cultivating the essence, *qi*, and spirit inherent in the body, received from Heaven and Earth at birth. Since they come with life, they are inherently possessed, yet they become enmeshed in the fleshly shell—thus they are called *"yin essence, yin qi, yin spirit."* Since they come with life, they are called *"internal medicine."*

When the practitioner begins their work, they must first close the senses and concentrate within, regulating their breath, merging the three primals into one cauldron, and refining the three primals within that single cauldron—named *"refining essence,"* yet in truth, spirit and *qi* all return to one orifice. Only when spirit is harmonized and *qi* flows freely, blending into one, does the *qi*-mechanism activate, steaming and floating—this is called *"qi-transformation,"* or *"gold born from water’s depths,"* or *"the common parents unite to produce the medicine."* This is the way of the world’s men and women, requiring the method of procreation. If one does not know the reverse cultivation, the essence will instantly transform into tangible, post-heavenly *qi*, leaking through the kidney passage. Hence: *"Consolidate the *qi*, preserve the essence—this is the decisive path to longevity."*

If one seeks longevity, the transformation of essence into *qi* is the wondrous elixir of life.

「When conflicts arise, one must urgently summon the *Tian Gang* (天罡) to subdue within, thrust the *Dou Bing* (斗柄) outward, advance and retreat the *He Che* (河车), and draw back the *Zhong Gong* (中宫) to forge anew. This is the refining of the inner elixir, where essence, *qi*, and spirit merge into one—how could it be merely *qi-transformation*?

The interplay of inner and outer, of *Kan* (坎) and *Li* (离), begins with the body’s inherent capacities, converging in the *Huang Fang* (黄房), nurturing for a moment, and thus *qi* is born—this is the infusion of spirit into *qi*, whereby the essence within the body refines the *Ling Yang* (灵阳, "heavenly earthly *qi*") drawn from the cosmos, achieving *essence-refining into *qi-transformation***.

Next, this *qi* is drawn and ascended, guided and descended, returning to the *Tu Fu* (土釜, "earthly cauldron") for repeated steaming and refining—this is the method of *"leads smelting mercury"* (铅制汞), where the *yang qi* submits to the *yin essence*. The essence is originally what the body inherently possesses, hence it is called *"Li ji yin jing"* (离己阴精, "separate from oneself *yin essence*"*). The *qi*, born from essence-transformation, is thus termed *"Kan wu yang qi"* (坎戊阳气, "Kan’s *wu* phase *yang qi*"*).

Not all *qi* belongs to the heart, nor is it generated within the kidneys. From *Yong Quan* (涌泉) to *Qi Hai* (气海) all belong to *yang*—*yang* is *Kan*. From *Ni Wan* (泥丸) to *Xuan Guan* (玄关) all belong to *yin*—*yin* is *Li*. Thus, the *qi* of water and fire is *Kan* and *Li*, not the heart and kidneys themselves, which is now clear.

Again, when *Kan* contains *qi*, it is called *Di Po* (地魄)—this is the *Bai Hu* (白虎, "White Tiger") of external elixir, and the *Jin Dan* (金丹, "Golden Elixir") of the inner. This elixir arises from extracting *lead* (铅) and adding *mercury* (汞), merging into one—both belong to the *Shui Fu* (水府, "Water Palace") *Xuan Zhu* (玄珠, "Mysterious Pearl").

The distinction between inner and outer is but layer upon layer, not truly separate. When the *Li Gong* (离宫, "Abode of *Li*") contains essence, it is called *Tian Hun* (天魂, "Heavenly Soul")—this is the *Qing Long* (青龙, "Green Dragon") of external elixir, but the true essence of oneself in the inner. *"Jin born from water"* (水中金生) is the *qi* transforming within essence—this is the *Bai Hu* (白虎) *initial crescent qi* (初弦之气) of external elixir, and the *silver within lead* (铅中之银) of the inner.

It is also said: *"Jin Dan* (金丹) is the supreme elixir of longevity." Only when this *Qian Yuan* (乾元, "Primordial *Qian*") *qi* sinks into the human body, and is not smelted by the *Shen Huo* (神火, "spiritual fire"), will it remain submerged and inert. And even when it moves and inclines, this is like the oil in a lamp—without oil, the lamp extinguishes; without *qi*, the person perishes. Life arises from this, hence it is the supreme elixir of longevity.

For it originally falls into *Kan* (坎) from *Qian* (乾), yet now returns from *Kan* to *Qian*—this is the origin of the *Jin Dan* doctrine.

If one seeks longevity, apart from this *Shui Xiang* (水乡, "Water Domain") *lead* (铅) essence, there is no other path. Though this *Jin Dan* is said to be inherent in all, without the *Shen Huo* (神火) to temper and refine it, there is no way to produce it.

Once the true *gold* (真金) is born, one must again subdue the *Bai Hu* (白虎) and tame the *Long* (龙), causing the *Qing Long* (青龙) to submit to the *Hu* (虎). The *Long* (龙) and *Hu* (虎) *qi* reconverge in the *Huang Fang* (黄房), their *qi* devouring and consuming each other to form *Jin Dan*.

When the *qi* returns to the *Tu Fu* (土釜) and meets with true essence, it is nurtured by the *Shen Huo* (神火) to form the *Sheng Tai* (圣胎, "Sacred Embryo"). Once the embryo is formed, internally one employs the natural spiritual fire, softly permeating the *Shen Fang* (神房, "Spiritual Chamber"), while externally adding the drawn and tempered mundane fire, flowing throughout the body. This is the daily practice of *"packaging one’s own mercury to consolidate true essence"* (运己汞包固真精); over time, the embryo dissolves and one transcends.

Ascending to the *Ni Wan* (泥丸), refining all voids, one strives to return to the original, natural state. It is neither the three treasures of essence, *qi*, and spirit being distinct, nor the inner and outer elixirs being separate. If one does not sit until the meditation cushion is worn through, nor grind until the knees are calloused, enduring self-suffering and self-refinement—how could one ever penetrate the profound truth?

I now briefly record the general principles, merely pointing the way for later students. The Dao is inherently ordinary, nothing extraordinary—yet the more one refines, the more ordinary it becomes.

Other transformations are unfathomable; in the eyes of the world, they seem unattainable heights, mysteries beyond comprehension. Yet, as the *Taoist Canon of the Supreme Dao* (太上道德一经) reveals, even the Three Pure Ones (三清) and the Supreme Venerable (太上) are but ordinary mortals transformed.

For the mortal clings to life and death as joys and sorrows, while the sage views them as day and night. Life and death are like waking and sleeping—one must follow them naturally, without disturbance. Those who say longevity and immortality are the delight of the sage are mistaken.

The mortal takes pride in longevity, but the sage finds joy in *Xu Li* (须理, "following the natural order"). Even if one sacrifices life for virtue (*Sha Sheng Cheng Ren* 杀生成仁) or discards life for righteousness (*She Sheng Qu Yi* 舍生取义), it is something the sage has always willingly embraced. Otherwise, who would not fear the horrors of the guillotine and the saw?

From ancient times, noble and virtuous souls have often regarded the cauldron and the furnace as joyous realms, and death as a peaceful path—because they have seen the truth clearly and believed in the fate of life. Their life and death are but the operation of this heart. To live in unease is worse than to die swiftly, just as to cling to pain while awake is worse than to rest in eternal slumber.

So long as spirit remains and principle is complete, what is life to be honored, or death to be shamed? One who listens to the operations of *Zao Hua* (造化, "Creation and Transformation") will never cling to life in the mortal world.

If one loves life and fears death, they are but vulgar and common folk, not the study of sages who follow the natural order in all things. Otherwise, why did Confucius die at seventy, and Yanzi at thirty? They acted according to Heaven’s will, daring not to defy it.

This is something no ordinary mortal could ever comprehend.»

「I secretly hope that scholars seek only what is within, without concern for what is without, and view adversity, ease, life, and death all with equal indifference.

What breadth, what tranquility this heart possesses! The saying goes: *“Recognize the Dao, and let fate unfold as Heaven arranges it.”*

Thus attaining ease in life and serenity in death, forever becoming a transcendent sage beyond the world—is this not superior to those who cling to life and fear death, now and then rejoicing within, now and then burdened with sorrow, their hearts never finding peace?

Moreover, those of lofty Dao, whom Heaven wishes to retain as models to guide the common folk—I do not reject them, nor do I pursue them; I simply listen to Heaven’s will. How could my heart ever be troubled by such matters?

For life and death are both the Dao. To live fully in the Dao and die fully in the Dao—what need then for love or aversion?

All who harbor attachments within their hearts will see their spirit disrupted and their nature lost, unworthy of being called sages.

**Chapter 55: The Depth of Virtue/Power**

The depth of virtue/power is like that of a newborn babe.
Poisonous insects do not sting,
ferocious beasts do not seize,
predatory birds do not snatch.

Though bones are frail and sinews soft, their grasp is firm;
before knowing the union of male and female, yet already formed—this is the utmost refinement of essence.
To cry all day yet never hoarse—this is the utmost harmony.

To know harmony is to know constancy;
to know constancy is to know clarity.
To nurture life is auspicious.
The heart directing *qi* is strength.

When things flourish, they age and decay—this is called un-Dao;
the un-Dao perishes swiftly.

The *Yijing* says: *“Heaven and Earth blend like the finest silk, and all things are transformed into purity. The union of male and female *qi* brings forth all things.”*
From the beginning of creation, they are not far from Heaven—
their *qi* is tender and supple.
If one follows its momentum and guides it,
welcomes its opportunity and nourishes it,
one may yet reach the realm of pristine refinement,
the ultimate harmony of Heaven.

Mencius said: *“The noble sage does not lose the heart of a newborn babe.”*
For when a babe first emits its cry upon the earth,
though it possesses its own world of *Yin-Yang*,
and initiates its own Creation and Transformation,
still it is pure and unadulterated—
a single mass of Heaven’s true nature in its embrace.
Without knowledge, without thought,
static yet harmonious, moving yet in accord with Heaven.

The ancient sages and transcendent ones contained vast light and boundless virtue/power,
comparable in depth to that of a newborn babe.

In the moment of their parents’ embrace,
when nurtured and cared for,
their feet cannot walk,
their hands cannot act.

Though poisonous insects are present,
they cannot sting them;
though ferocious beasts are near,
they cannot seize them.」

「Though fierce raptors may be present,
they have no way to strike—
for when in motion, they do not know where to go,
and when they walk, they do not know where to tread.
Thus, they fear no poisonous insects,
and the insects cannot sting them;
they fear no ferocious beasts,
and the beasts cannot seize them.

Yet when they lie dangerously on a bed,
or rest peacefully in a couch,
though raptors may lurk nearby,
the raptors cannot help but strike them.
Once youthful vigor arrives,
and movement becomes effortless,
though there may be joy in wandering,
they cannot attain the tranquility of a secluded chamber,
and few can escape the calamities of malevolent forces.

Consider the newborn babe,
yet unaware of the union of *Yin* and *Yang* in copulation,
its vitality ever flourishing.
It is evident that the original *qi* is pure and unbounded,
with life’s essence daily unfolding.

If one can focus their *qi* and cultivate softness akin to an infant’s first breath,
then they possess the essence that can be refined.
Observe how the babe’s cries,
endless and unceasing,
though they wail all day,
never harm the throat or larynx—
this is not artificial but natural,
a spontaneous utterance, unrestrained yet effortless.
All is *Heaven’s mechanism* in motion,
*Heaven’s resonance* in sound,
without manipulation, without contrivance—
harmony in its utmost.

Understanding this *original harmony* within,
one aligns with the *eternal Dao*,
requiring no artificial effort to correct or force,
but preserving its true nature,
settling into its fixed essence.

The *Odes* say:
*“Both wise and sagely,
to safeguard their very being,”*
—could this be its meaning?

If not for the cultivation of *harmonious soft qi*,
nourishing the body and mind,
how could one be born and grow ever more vital?
How could one’s *Heavenly body* be sustained without decay,
and this be the greatest of human blessings?

Once strength and vigor arise,
*Heaven’s heart* is disturbed by *human heart*,
and the depletion of spirit becomes frequent.
Now, if we take *original harmony* as the Dao,
ultimate stillness enables ultimate motion,
and supreme softness overcomes supreme hardness.

Thus, one must make the *qi* serve the mind,
ensuring this *qi* returns to the state of the newborn babe,
and not let the *qi* disturb the mind,
lest the mind stray from *original harmony*.
Only then can one achieve harmony without dissipation—
strong indeed, and far from artificial.
This is not unique to the newborn babe.

Observe all things:
their beginning is soft and tender,
their end, hard and strong.
Softness and tenderness are signs of life’s vitality,
hardness and strength, portents of death’s approach.

Thus, when things grow strong, they age,
not as when things remain young, they thrive.
Life’s Dao endures,
while death’s Dao perishes.
Hence it is said:
*“Aging is not the Dao—
the Dao is not to age at all.”*
Why not? Because aging is merely the decay of vitality.
Yet if measured by years,
even at eighty or ninety,
one may still cultivate the Dao of eternal life,
for so long as a breath remains,
this *original harmony’s qi* suffices within the body,
unfailing and complete.

For when the *qi* is first cultivated in softness,
it is like all things bending branches and sprouting buds.
Nurture and foster it here,
and it shall grow daily, monthly,
until it returns to the root of life,
yielding the fruit of longevity.

But when the time of *San-Yang* (late twilight) arrives,
things have aged and are nearing decline—
their essence is no longer fit to refine into medicine.
Yet aging is not merely old age in years,
but the decay of the medicine’s vitality.
If judged by age alone,
even at eighty or nine years,
one may still attain the Dao of unending life,
for so long as a breath remains,
this *original harmony’s qi* suffices within the body,
unfailing and complete.

This is the method of cultivating the self,
taking the newborn babe as its model.

Zhuangzi said:
*“A babe moves without knowing what to do,
walks without knowing where to go,
its body like a withered tree,
its mind as cold as dead ashes—
misfortune does not come,
fortune does not arrive.”*
If misfortune and fortune alike do not manifest,
how could there be human or natural calamities?
The lines *“poisonous insects”* and following convey this very meaning.

Later, it speaks of gathering herbs and refining elixirs,
requiring the *newly sprouted water* of *Heaven’s One* (the first drop of dew).
This water is the very source of human vitality.
Like a wheel of crimson sun,
rising at midnight’s beginning,
its light clear and bright,
illuminating the vast sea;
or like a crescent autumn moon,
emerging in the *Geng-Zhen* direction—
this is when the cultivator’s *mystic pass* opens,
their senses lost in boundless void,
and such a state is attained.

For when the *qi* is first cultivated in softness,
it is like all things bending branches and sprouting buds.
At this stage, nurture and foster it,
and it shall grow daily, monthly,
until it returns to the root of life,
yielding the fruit of hardy vitality.

If one waits until the *San-Yang* phase,
then vitality has aged and is declining—
its essence is no longer fit to refine into medicine.
Yet aging is not merely old age in years,
but the decay of the medicine’s vitality.
If judged by age alone,
even at eighty or ninety,
one may still cultivate the Dao of eternal life,
for so long as a breath remains,
this *original harmony’s qi* suffices within the body,
unfailing and complete.»

The true adept alone can pierce the *watery heaven* within—
when the two currents of inner and outer *qi* merge,
how many would otherwise pass by unknowing.

The scholar who seeks the Great Dao of the Golden Elixir
must empty the heart to seek the Way,
accumulate virtue to turn back the heavens—
only then will the true master manifest in response,
and the *White Tiger’s Classic* be found.
Otherwise, even if one enters the Dao in youth,
they shall grow old without attainment.

Worse still, those who follow false teachers,
straying onto false paths—
many a lifetime’s vital essence wasted in the realm of beasts.
Let the scholar take heed!

**Chapter 56: The Most Noble of the World**
The wise do not speak; those who speak do not know.
Close the mouth, shut the door,
dull the sharpness, resolve the tangled,
harmonize the light, blend with the dust—
this is called *Mysterious Unity*.

Thus, one cannot be too close nor too distant,
cannot be too beneficial nor too harmful,
cannot be too noble nor too base.
Therefore, one is the most noble of the world.

Common men, when they hear a good word or see a good deed,
immediately chatter and boast to stir others—
pleasing for a moment, yet never turning inward to self-examination.
Confucius said, *“Hearsay and gossip are the abandonment of virtue,”*
and truly, he spoke no falsehood.

But the true knower of the Great Dao,
when he has even a scrap of understanding,
is too busy morning and evening with reverent caution—
how could he have leisure to speak?
Even if he reviews the old and gains new insights,
and finds a moment of intuitive understanding,
it is his own and self-attained—
like one sated with food and drink,
drunk and full, knowing its flavor,
yet unable to convey it to others.

Those who love to speak of it
must surely lack something within themselves.
And since virtue is one’s own virtue,
and cultivation one’s own cultivation—
if one truly knows, the more one conceals it,
the more one fears that a single word spoken lightly
might cause even a breath to stray,
and thus the loss of the Way in one’s heart.
This is why the wise do not speak.

If one does speak, it is because they lack virtue within themselves,
truly knowing nothing of the Dao.
If they did know, why would they so casually reveal it?
Thus, those who speak prove themselves even more ignorant.

Moreover, what is unspeakable is the essence,
and what is spoken of is but dross.
The wise do not fail to speak—
they simply cannot speak.
Those who speak do not fail to know—
they only grasp the surface.

As the saying goes, *“When you’ve grasped it, close your mouth.”*
Indeed, knowing the Dao is no easy feat—
why waste one’s *qi* in speech,
or tarnish one’s spirit with idle chatter,
boasting of talent and ability to stand out?
Such actions only invite contempt from the vulgar.

Thus, the noble Daoist seals the mouth, shuts the door,
nurturing *qi* within;
dulls the sharpness, resolves confusion,
stabilizing the spirit;
harmonizes the light, blends with the dust,
adapting to the times, rising and falling with the world,
as if foolish, as if drunk,
as if slow of speech, as if mad—
when the multitude is confused, I too am confused,
neither flaunting nor opposing,
neither clashing with myself nor defying the world.

Should even a thread of pretense or ambition arise,
one cannot help but speak grandly to satisfy a moment’s pride.
Such actions seek either fame or gain.
Has one not heard the *Taoist Canon*’s warning to Confucius?
*“Those who can be fed with wine and meat,
I will whip and beat.
Those who can be honored with rank and wealth,
I will insult and humiliate.”*

Only by secluding oneself to cultivate in silence,
embracing the *original unity*, guarding the *One*,
keeping the spirit silent and the *qi* obscure,
remaining tranquil and desireless,
acting without action, doing without doing—
one cannot be too close nor too distant,
too beneficial nor too harmful,
too noble nor too base.
This is to seek within oneself, not without,
not seeking from others,
fulfilling one’s nature and returning to one’s life—
thus, one is the most noble of the world.

Within the Three Realms, only the Dao is supreme.
I cultivate my Dao, and thus my Dao is noble—
nothing in the world surpasses this.
The *Taoist Canon* says: *“Few understand me, thus I am noble.”*
Do scholars understand this as well?

The man of the Way must not speak lightly,
for few in the world truly know.
If one indulges in idle talk,
not only does it harm oneself internally,
but it also invites external harm to others.
The *I Ching* says: *“If secrets are not kept, disaster follows.”*
Many a cultivator has, through careless disclosure,
invited calamity and misfortune.

Thus, the sage keeps secrets and does not speak.
Even if one finds a worthy listener,
one must proceed with utmost caution and testing—
only after repeated trials,
swearing by heaven and earth,
ascending the platform to teach,
does one dare speak without exaggeration or excess.
This shows the ancient masters’ practice of concealing brilliance
and revering the Dao and heaven.

Those who speak lightly know nothing.
If one truly knows, one is too busy with self-cultivation and verification
to have leisure for idle talk.
Those who speak recklessly
are those who, even in nobility, invite their own degradation.

With my Dao and my nobility,
I can endure from one age to ten thousand ages—
who in the world can surpass this?
Let the scholar cultivate what lies within,
moment by moment, observing inwardly,
lest the wind of idle speculation arise.

---
**Chapter 57: Governing the World with Rectitude**
Govern the world with rectitude,
employ strategy in warfare,
and take the world without conflict.
How do I know this to be true?
Because of this:

The world is filled with taboos and prohibitions,
yet the people grow ever poorer;

「The people abound in greed and flattery,
and the state grows ever more corrupt;
The people abound in cunning and artifice,
and strange things multiply;
Laws and decrees become ever more explicit,
and thieves and bandits flourish.

Thus the sage says:
*"I act without interference, yet the people transform themselves.
I love tranquility, yet the people align with rectitude.
I take no action, yet the people grow rich.
I desire nothing, yet the people return to simplicity."*

Confucius said:
*"My Dao is but one thread that runs through all things."*
This means that through the Dao,
all affairs and things of the world
are not without its penetration and manifestation.
As the saying goes:
*"One root scatters into myriad branches,
yet myriad branches all return to one root."*
To govern oneself and the world—
this is the greatest principle.
The Dao of governing the world
does not surpass
the four occupations—*scholar, farmer, artisan, merchant*—
each finding their place in life,
and filial piety and fraternal respect—
each following their inherent moral nature.
These are the daily, constant practices,
the great precepts of the world,
the eternal laws of ages past and present—
indeed, the common Dao,
also the Dao of rectitude.
What every person ought to fulfill
is what every person inherently possesses as virtue.

For those who lead the people,
they must practice frugality in person,
exhaust their filial kindness and benevolence for the world,
and act with solemn dignity and measured presence.
They must guide with gentleness,
restrain with stillness,
avoid frivolous matters to strengthen their resolve,
and let all things fall into order without effort—
one act of rectitude suffices to bring harmony,
and naturally the winds will follow,
the grass will bow.
Swift as the influence of the stars,
this is the way.

Mencius said:
*"Rectify the ruler, and the kingdom will be steadied."*
And again:
*"The world’s existence has long endured."*
Order and chaos follow in cycles,
as they have since ancient times—
no deviation has ever escaped this truth.
Though in times of order, *rites and music* are employed,
and in times of chaos, *arms and warfare* are used,
when two armies face each other in battle,
and great enemies clash,
the fate of the state—its safety or peril—
and the lives of the people—birth or death—
rest upon a single commander.
How could this not be of utmost importance?

Though the arts of *strategy and calculation*,
and the cunning of *clever schemes and tactical shifts*,
are not what the *noble* esteem,
yet to fulfill the mandate of heaven in punishing rebels,
and to uphold great justice in consoling the people,
does not bar the use of *unexpected victories and decisive tactics*.

Thus the *art of war* has:
*concealed raids and covert assaults*,
*exploiting fatigue and exhaustion*,
*separating alliances and counter-espionage*,
*feigning weakness to deceive*,
*strategies as elusive as gods and spirits*—
these marvelous schemes.
To employ the *Dao of the Unexpected* in warfare,
is to never lose a battle,
never fail in an assault,
to spare the lives of the people,
to not drain their wealth,
and for all to long enjoy the blessings of peace—
is this not the very essence of *taking the world without conflict*?

Yet to unite the mountains and rivers under one rule,
and to gather the heavens and earth under one person—
how could this not involve myriad affairs?
But if one lets the myriad things rush in,
while maintaining a composed and tranquil heart,
naturally one will harmonize with the heavens,
and the heavenly will favor them;
below, the people will revere them,
and all will follow in devotion—
thus the world can be taken without effort.

Hence it is said:
*"The Tang and Yu* [1] *offered three cups of wine in humble deference,
while the Shang and Wu* [2] *conquered with a single move of the chessboard."*
The world was not seen as strange or wondrous,
nor was its conquest taken lightly—
thus the heart was free of burdens,
and its capacity matched that of the heavens and earth.
Hence, when they presided over China
and soothed the four barbarians,
it came naturally and without premeditation.
This is the Dao of governing the world.

How do I know this to be true?
Because the Dao of governing the world
does not go beyond governing oneself—
the body is like a state.
Sight, hearing, speech, and action
must all conform to *rites*;
thoughts, intentions, and wisdom
must be steadied by *emotion/feeling*.
Inner reflections do not stray outward,
external distractions do not intrude inward—
when nature is settled,
the body attains rectitude.
With prolonged cultivation of stillness,
the natural faculties of the heart spontaneously arise.

To apply the *ordinary Dao of life*
as the *elixir method of reversal*,
approaching the furnace with fire—
this is fraught with danger.
The highest method is likened to warfare:
one must act in accordance with the times,
seize opportunities as they arise,
harvest at the proper season,
refine with precision in place,
camp in the field at the right moment,
and defend the city with the proper strategy—
not failing to await the right time and favorable conditions,
and to deploy with unexpected schemes.

As for:
*the fire extinguished by sufficient medicine*,
*the elixir perfected to warm the furnace*,
*elevating the spirit of the sun* [3] *to the void realm*,
*nurturing the immortal embryo in the unbroken chamber*—
one must still cultivate in the *spiritual chamber*,
attending to nothing with complete detachment,
and only then can the elixir be refined,
the immortality achieved.
This is the Dao of governing oneself,
and it mirrors the method of governing the world.
Thus I know the Dao of governing the world
through this very Dao of governing oneself.

He who takes the world
must do so through *non-action*,
yet he who holds the world
must not rely on *excessive action*.
Otherwise, if he enacts too many regulations,
issues too many decrees,
makes the people tremble at every step,
they will inevitably resort to deceit and fraud,
and affairs will fall into disarray.
The world abounds in prohibitions,
yet the people grow ever poorer.

Gold, jade, pearls, and jewels,
carriages, horses, fine garments—
the useful tools of the people multiply.
But when greed arises,
and desires cannot be satisfied,
how could the spirit not grow dim,
and the *qi* not grow turbid?

Honesty and simplicity are forgotten,
while deceit and cunning are esteemed.
When those with skill appear,
people love and admire them,
and all vie to imitate their ways—
thus strange and bizarre things
are all drawn to the fore.

Ah! In the flourishing times of the *Three Dynasties*,
the rulers were all *divine and sage*,
and the people were all pure and virtuous.
Laws were established yet not enforced,
precepts were set yet not applied—
thus they were called *flourishing ages*.

Now, however,
the legal nets are stretched high and thick,
like a fine mesh;
the *five grades of punishment* are severe,
and offenders are not spared;
the *three codes of law* are strict,
and violators are executed.
Yet why do crimes multiply as laws grow stricter,
and thieves increase as decrees become more numerous?
It is because *virtue/power* is insufficient to win the hearts of the people,
and the laws are not enough to awe their resolve.
From ancient times, the people’s unrest has always stemmed from
these very *punishments and coercive measures*—
this is evident since the Qin and Han.

The ancient sages said:
*"Heaven is honored through non-action,
while man is burdened by it."*
If I uphold solemnity and simplicity,
do not overwhelm the people with excessive regulations,
nor confuse the state with constant changes,
but instead maintain composure at the highest echelons,
and dwell in tranquil seclusion within the deepest palaces—
then the people will daily improve in virtue,
yet remain unaware of the effort.»

---
[1] *Tang and Yu*: Refers to the legendary sage-kings *Emperor Shun (Yu)* and *Emperor Yao (Tang)* of the Xia Dynasty, known for their humility and virtuous rule.
[2] *Shang and Wu*: Refers to *King Tang of Shang* and *King Wu of Zhou*, who overthrew the tyrannical *Xia Dynasty* and *Shang Dynasty* respectively through military conquest.
[3] *Spirit of the sun*: Refers to the *yang* spirit or *sun essence* (日精) in Daoist alchemy, symbolizing the refined, luminous energy cultivated within.

「Yet with a serene and tranquil mind, one preserves profound silence and self-cultivation—
solely attending to sincerity in intent and rectifying the heart—
yet who would know that rectifying oneself rectifies the court,
rectifying officials rectifies the people,
all arising from this stillness and composure?

When the people are thus rectified,
each reveres their kin and honors their elders,
none transgress their proper station,
and households endure in harmony.
Then they till the fields for sustenance,
dig wells for drink,
rise with the sun and rest with the night,
their granaries overflowing,
their lives free of want—
and the people grow rich without knowing how.

For this arises when the ruler follows nature,
acts without contrivance,
and lets things unfold of their own accord.

How much more so when one maintains stillness,
guards emptiness,
remains undisturbed and does without action,
settling into the true *hun-hun wū-wū* [1],
that the people, moved and transformed by it,
will not fail to cultivate loyalty and filial piety,
and adopt a plain, unadorned way of life.

The *Shū* says:
*“One man of perfect virtue,
and the myriad states stand upright.”*
Its mechanism lies hidden in subtlety,
its effects manifest as vast as heaven and earth.

I wish those who govern to take rectifying the sovereign’s heart as their priority,
and those who cultivate themselves to first nourish the *Tiān Jūn* [2].

This principle is already clear—
no need for further elaboration.

I envision the moment of seated meditation:
At first, the *yang* qi sinks to the depths of the sea,
like the waning of winter’s end,
all around in silent stillness.
With the divine light descending,
it is as though at the winter solstice, *yang* begins to stir—
yet the void remains soundless,
the walls stark and desolate,
unchanged as before.

From here, the vital forces gradually revolve,
unaware, the Three *Yangs* unfold,
and all things return to spring—
flowers crimson, leaves verdant,
waters bright, mountains clear—
the peak of *yang* is already manifest.

Heaven’s way is thus—
how much more so the human body?

Only when the head tilts slightly downward,
the gaze softly lowered,
does *yin* reach its extreme and *yang* emerge anew.

This stage differs from the earlier practice—
no longer clinging to a fixed intent,
counting breaths with deliberate effort.
One must abandon external fire,
halt the internal forge,
letting nature take its course without interference—
lest one fall back into old habits unknowingly.

This is the art of preserving the aware mind
to nurture the *wuwei* nature.

When enlightenment lingers long,
and righteousness and benevolence mature—
what need remains for preservation or cultivation?
Only the natural order suffices.

Otherwise, at the outset, without exertion,
the mind may rage like a wild beast or untamed steed,
and taming it may prove difficult at first.

Yet when the beast and steed return of their own accord,
as Mencius said of releasing the pig into the marshes,
one must not pursue and bind it—
lest the lively and free become constrained and uneasy.

What then is the method?
The *Yì* says:
*“Heaven and Earth in their weaving,
transform all things into purity.”* [3]»

---
[1] *Hun-hun wū-wū* (浑浑噩噩): A Daoist term describing the primordial, undifferentiated state of natural spontaneity, often linked to the *Zhōu Yì* (周易) concept of *Zhēn* (真, "the Real").
[2] *Tiān Jūn* (天君): The "Celestial Sovereign" or "Heavenly Ruler," a Daoist alchemical term referring to the refined spiritual essence governing the body.
[3] *Yì* (易): The *Yì Jīng* (易经, *Classic of Changes*), here invoking the transformative power of heaven and earth.

This *embroidering-and-fuling* qi, within the human body, is like the inner fire stopping and the outer form remaining still—undisturbed, allowing the breath to flow naturally. For those whose practice is refined, at such times, one need not consciously direct the mind; if one has not yet reached this stage, the mind of awareness must not be forgotten. If forgotten, one risks slipping into unconsciousness, where thoughts arise and vanish in endless cycles, causing the original, pure nature to be submerged beneath the waves of birth and death.

The ancient Buddhas said:
*“To know the source of arising is to know the source of cessation.”*
Thus, through such cultivation, over time one perceives the beginning of arising and cessation, and again, over time, the beginning of non-thought—then it is attained.

As for the teachings of the *Huángtíng* [Yellow Court], they lie in neither existence nor non-existence, neither within nor without; yet they are also within the colored body, yet not within the colored body. Where, then, is this wondrous secret?

It is what the ancients called *“concentrating the spirit in the void, merging the qi in the boundless.”*
To concentrate the spirit in the void and merge the qi in the boundless is like dwelling in the *dantian*, yet not fixating the gaze inward to stare, nor forcing the spirit-qi to cluster within. Only by concentrating the spirit at the *qìhài* [navel region], not far from the flesh and skin—this is the meaning of *“neither within nor without.”*

With intent focused here, one feels the breath at the nostrils cease, while the *dantian*’s qi rolls and churns at the juncture of inner and outer, knotting into a mass. One then sees the *embroidering-and-fuling*, the *hun-hun wū-wū*—the boundless, lively, and unrestrained movement, as it ebbs and flows, truly merging with Heaven’s primordial qi in seamless unity. Life’s essence, life’s qi, and life’s spirit all arise here, not far from Heaven.

This is the meaning of *“merging the qi in the boundless.”*
The ancients called it *“primordial qi,”* *“fetal breathing,”* *“the True Person’s breath reaching the heels.”*—what else could it be?

This *primordial qi* is the undifferentiated, formless, nameless, and boundless *hun-hun wū-wū*—a single, pure breath. As for *fetal breathing*, when a person first receives breath in the womb, the body is nourished there before the mouth and nostrils open—how else could breath be taken? Only this *qìhài*’s qi, connecting with the mother’s navel-wheel, allows growth to unfold. When the child emerges with a cry, this qi then circulates through the nostrils, each establishing its own *qián-kūn* [heaven-earth]. My demonstration shows the *qìhài*’s qi interfacing with the external Heavenly breath—neither within nor without, *embroidering-and-fuling* in fusion, blending into one—this is returning to the initial moment of receiving breath, reconnecting with the mother’s qi. This is *fetal breathing*.

As for *“the True Person’s breath reaching the heels,”* it means the True Person’s breath is stored deeply and extended subtly—unseen, unheard, untouchable, profound and sealed, like breath reaching the soles of the feet. Though the breath of the nostrils can be used, it must follow its natural course; one must not rely solely on it for inhalation and exhalation. If one uses only this breath without concentrating the spirit at the *qìhài*—one inch and three *fēn* below the navel—failing to find this void within, to draw Heaven’s breath into the boundless, the clear and turbid will remain intermixed, and the elixir cannot be forged.

The ancients said:
*“Heaven’s single true breath gives birth to man—
this breath is neither mouth nor nostril,
nor the knowing, moving soul.”*
They also said:
*“The Mysterious Female’s gate few know—
do not wastefully act through mouth and nostrils.
Though you practice inhalation and exhalation for a thousand years,
how will you grasp the Golden Rooster and the Hare?”*
From these words alone, it is clear: *“Exit through the Mysterious, enter through the Female”*—this lies between the *dantian* and the flesh, one inch and three *fēn* below the navel, where the vapors condense into a unified whole.

Those who study the Dao—whether during tea or meals, in speech or social exchange—should subtly focus a little intent on this void within, naturally merging the qi in the boundless. One will then perceive the true qi stirring, in a wonder beyond naming. Yet in this breath-work, awareness does not enter inward, and the *kǎn* [water] naturally clarifies.

This is the unspoken secret of generations of immortals and sages. I now reveal it plainly—later scholars must not dismiss it as mere words.

---
**Chapter Fifty-Eight: Calamity and Fortune Intertwined**

When governance is *mèn-mèn* [dim and indistinct],
the people are *chún-chún* [pure and sincere].
When governance is *chá-chá* [sharp and scrutinizing],
the people are *quē-quē* [deficient and weary].

Calamity and fortune lean upon each other;
fortune and calamity lie hidden within.
Who knows their ultimate end?
Perhaps there is no fixed principle.

What is upright becomes strange;
what is good becomes ominous.
The people’s confusion has long endured.
