is that they excel in humility,
and thus become the sovereign of all valleys.

Therefore, the Sage who wishes to surpass others
must first speak in a way that places himself below them;
who wishes to lead others
must first position himself behind them.

Thus, the Sage, though in the highest place,
does not inspire awe,
and though he moves forward,
he does not harm others.
Hence, all under heaven willingly follow without exhaustion.

Because he does not contend,
none can contend with him.

All desire others to submit to them,
yet some, though not submitting, are submitted to,
while others, though eager to submit, are ever more resisted—
this is because they exalt themselves,
unwilling to lower their hearts or humble their *qi*.

Observe how rivers and seas become the gathering place of all valleys—
it is because they receive them from below,
and thus become their sovereign.
If rivers and seas were to place themselves above the valleys,
though the valleys might still flow toward them,
where would be their capacity to contain them?

Thus, the Sage perceives this early:
to surpass others, he must speak from a lower place,
as Yao consulted the Four Mountains,
and Shun inquired of the Four Gates—
all teachings, commands, and matters
were sought from ministers and neighbors,
never exalting his own wisdom.
This is why the more he humbles himself,
the more others elevate him.

To lead others, he must place himself behind them,
as Yu, Gao, and Yi did—
though they were enlightened first,
they always yielded precedence to others,
placing themselves last.
This is why the more he retreats,
the more others advance.

Because he positions himself below and behind,
even as emperor, he lacks the terror of authority,
and thus is not feared.
Though he governs the divine and human,
he lacks the harshness of domination,
and thus does not harm others.
Therefore, all under heaven willingly follow him as their leader,
with no resentment.

The *Odes* say:
*"There is no blame in him,
nor in me any fault.
May we thus, night and day,
perpetuate our good name."*

What else is this but this?
Because he does not contend for superiority or precedence,
others naturally place him above and before him.
They submit to his teachings, revere his spirit,
and endlessly receive his grace and virtue—
how could they then dare to contend for superiority or precedence,
and rival the Sage in competition?

This is the analogy for elixir cultivation:
first, the divine fire descends into the elixir field,
then the fire steams and the water boils,
and from the depths of the water, gold is born—
only then does the elixir of longevity begin to manifest.

Humans receive the central *qi* of Heaven and Earth to live,
originally complete and whole.
Yet after birth, *qi* and constitution constrain them,
and desires obscure them,
so that the original *qi* of Heaven
is scattered and dissipated throughout the body’s stale *qi*,
unable to gather in one place for long.

Now, to converge the Five Phases,
harmonize the Four Symbols,
unite them in the central palace,
and return them to the mysterious orifice—
one must first release all entanglements,
allow no private thoughts to arise,
close the eyes and seal the mouth,
so that the eyes see through the nose,
the nose faces the navel,
and the heart-fire descends into the elixir field—
in but a moment, the mysterious pass opens,
one *yang* revives,
and the *qi* of the whole body
naturally gathers in the elixir field,
fusing and flowing freely,
a joy beyond words.

Yet at the outset of observation,
if the fire is not intense, the gold will not emerge from the ore;
if the fire is too fierce, it will scorch the essence and blood,
stifling the vital spark.
Only when it is neither clinging nor detached,
neither present nor absent,
does the *qi* of the elixir field leap with restless energy.

This is like the rivers and seas receiving all valleys—
thus the valleys gather toward them.
The Sage receives all under heaven—
thus all hearts turn to him.

In a single body,
the heart is the most noble and exalted,
while all other limbs are small and lowly.

---

The **Taoist Sage** thus likens the heart to the vastness of the rivers and seas,
and all other limbs to the smallness of the valleys and the lowliness of the common folk—
this is the **elixir field** of the body.

Those who cultivate the **Dao/Way** must likewise take the lowly as their foundation,
the humble as their root,
yet not place themselves above the noble and exalted,
so that they may descend to the common and ordinary—
then their attainment will be effortless.

For the ordinary man,
the spirit (*shen*) is the master,
while *qi* follows its movements, rising and falling,
thus giving birth to sons and daughters,
and life and death.

The **Perfect Sage** (*Zhiren*) takes *qi* as the master,
while the spirit listens and moves at its command.
As the *Wuzhen* says: *"Though others act as master, I am the guest."*

The great adept,
when *qi* moves,
inverts the workings of **Nature** (*Zao*) and **Transformation** (*Hua*),
upsets the **Heaven-Earth** (*Qiankun*) polarity,
and simply follows its ascending and descending flow—
returning to the furnace to seal and refine,
then awaiting the **True Essence** (*Zhenxin*) to manifest,
cycling and revolving in harmony,
never relying on the spirit for guidance.

Yet when one observes the **True Qi** (*Zhenqi*) surging and blending,
inverting and reversing its course,
and completes the ninefold transformation,
the **elixir** (*dantian*) matures and the **Pearl** (*zhuzhu*) awakens—
is this not a lofty state,
exalted above all,
the object of reverence for ten thousand men,
the spectacle of all under heaven?

Only when one abides in the lowly and follows behind in this manner,
does the spirit of **lively detachment** (*huodantian*) arise—
a heart of humility and modesty,
thus avoiding the calamities of **tilting the elixir field** (*qīdān dǎodìng*),
**mercury fleeing** (*gǒngzǒu*),
and **lead scattering** (*qiānfēi*).

Hence, when one remains in the lowly without striving,
all under heaven cannot contend with him.

**Chapter Sixty-Seventh**
I possess the **Three Treasures** (*Sān Bǎo*),
and all under heaven call me great—
yet this seems unworthy.

For only the truly great can appear unworthy;
if one seems worthy,
it is long since one has been small.

I possess the **Three Treasures**,
and cling to them in safeguarding:
the first is **compassion** (*cí*),
the second is **frugality** (*jiǎn*),
the third is **not daring to lead all under heaven** (*bù gǎn wéi tiānxià xiān*).

For **compassion** brings forth **courage** (*yǒng*);
**frugality** brings forth **breadth** (*guǎng*);
and **not daring to lead all under heaven**,
thus one can fulfill one’s role and endure.

Now, if one abandons **compassion** for **courage**,
**frugality** for **breadth**,
and **not daring to lead** for **leading ahead**,
then one is already dead.

For **compassion**—
when wielded in battle, it wins;
when used in defense, it holds firm.
When **Heaven** intends to save a man,
it shields him with **compassion**.

The **Dao/Way** is fundamentally **Limitless** (*Wújí*), yet it is also the **Great Ultimate** (*Tàijí*).
Neither great nor small,
neither great nor small—
it is both great and small at once.

There are indeed words that no thought or speculation can exhaust.
If one insists on calling it "great,"
then it is *"the boundless and mighty **Harmonious Qi** (*Hàorán qì*),*
*filling the space between Heaven and Earth."*

If one seeks to describe it as "small,"
then it is *"the nameless **embryo** (*pǔ*),*
*most subtle and minute,*
*hidden in the boundless expanse."*

In man,
its possession means life,
its loss means death.

All arise from the **Limitless** yet take form,
from the **invisible** yet manifest.
Truly, the small is its origin,
while the great is its unfolding.

It is better to speak of the small to reveal the **Dao/Way**,
than to speak of the great to explain it.

Thus the **Taoist Sage** says:
*"All under heaven call me great."*

For **"I"** is the **Dao/Way** itself.
The **Dao/Way** has no fixed form or body;
yet when called "great,"
it seems to have a form and body that can be likened—
this does not truly resemble the **Dao/Way**.

If one wishes to embody **Heaven’s Dao/Way**,
and resemble the Sage’s form,
from the opening of **Heaven** to the present day,
those who have established **Heaven’s polarity** (*Lìjí*) and expounded the **Dao/Way** and its teachings—
for ages now,
they have all relied on the **Limitless** as the **Ultimate**,
the **unmanifest** as the **manifest**,
the most subtle and minute as the **Dao/Way**.

Yet how can one practice the **Dao/Way** when it is **soundless and odorless** (*wúshēng wúchòu*),
flickering and indistinct?
How can the student begin?

The **Taoist Sage** says:
*"I possess the **Three Treasures**,
and cling to them in safeguarding."*

If one holds them steadfastly without loss,
treasures them as precious,
never forgetting them in each thought,
then one may return to the origin
and restore the **Primordial Sovereign** (*Wéihuáng*)’s descent.

What are the **Three Treasures**?
The first is **compassion** (*cí*), which is **benevolence** (*rén*) itself.
**Benevolence** and **compassion**, gentle and pervasive,
are the **elemental principle** (*yuán*) of **Heaven**.
The gentleman embodies **benevolence**,
and this suffices to nurture all men.

Moreover, it encompasses the **Four Beginnings** (*Sì Duān*),
and includes all **virtues** (*shàn*).
**Benevolence** is within it—
then the **Dao/Way** is within it.
Filling and exhausting it,
it can embrace **Heaven and Earth**,
and permeate **ancient and modern**.

This is the **foundation of the Golden Elixir** (*Jīndān zhī běn*),
and the adept must cherish and remember it.

Yet its **Dao/Way** extends to the utmost greatness,
while its **root** (*míng*) ascends from the utmost subtlety.
If one does not know to **forget all thoughts** (*wànniàn jū wàng*),
and **illuminate the inner essence** (*yīlíng nèizhào*),
but instead merely pursues breadth and becomes dissipated,
seeks vastness and wanders aimlessly,
then one will gain nothing of **benevolence**,
and nothing of the **Dao/Way**.

Only when one **inverts and seeks within oneself** (*fǎnqiú*),
and **steadfastly guards the heart** (*dǔshǒu zhū xīn*),
does one establish oneself while also establishing others,
and reach fulfillment while also fulfilling others.
Guarding the narrow yet applying the broad—
this is what the ancients called **"obtaining one, all things are accomplished"** (*dé yī wànshì bì*),
is it not the essence of **frugality** (*jiǎn*)?

For **frugality** is the means to seek **benevolence**,
and the key to cultivating the **Dao/Way**.
The student, having known **compassion**,
must further nourish it with **frugality**,
then alone may he align with the **Dao/Way**.

Yet if one exalts himself in pride,
and magnifies his own greatness,
without the measure of humility and modesty,
then within he knows only himself,
and without he scorns all others.
He boasts of his cleverness,
and arrogantly proclaims his wisdom,
truly believing he is the first to awaken and harmonize—
often he regards all under heaven as incapable of surpassing him.

In the end,
his nature is not **serene and tranquil** (*tiánjìng*),
his *qi* is not **harmonious and peaceful** (*héping*),
and yet he hopes to complete the **Nine Turns of the Elixir** (*jiǔ zhuǎn dānchéng*),
and reach the **Nine Heavens of the Dao/Way** (*jiǔ tiān dǎojí*)—
this is indeed difficult.

The ancients said:
*"The essential key to refining the elixir*
*is to take **awakened awareness** (*língjué*) as the **Dao/Way’s body**,*
*and **harmonious blending** (*chōnghé*) as the **Dao/Way’s function**—*
*then, in all things and places,*
*one must not dare to lead all under heaven."*

Moreover, **compassion** (*cí*) is the **innate capacity** (*liángnéng*) of man’s heart.
Exhausting one’s own heart to establish the **fate** (*mìng*) of all things,
what a vast **vow** (*shìyuàn*) this is!
Nurturing the **sincere and upright** (*cùnzhōng*) nature within,
to seek the **peace** (*ān*) of all things,
what a mighty **precision** (*jīnglì*) and **strength** (*liàng*) this is!

Thus the man who guards **compassion** is the same as the man who nourishes **courage**.
**Zengzi** told **Zixiang**:
*"When reflecting upon oneself without shrinking,*
*even if one is clad in coarse garments and broadcloth,*
*I would not fear."*

「If one reflects upon oneself and shrinks back,
even if there are a thousand people,
I would still go forward.

*Is it not the man who casts aside all private thoughts of self-interest,*
*who can possess such great courage?*

**Courage** (*yǒng*) must be rooted in restraint (*yuē*) before it can be expressed broadly (*bó*).
If one does not restrain, how can one be broad?

Only when one can **prudently** (*shèn*) govern one’s actions,
**sparingly** (*shěng*) consider one’s thoughts,
**dedicate** (*zhì*) a single mind to the **rectifying field** (*fāngcùn*),
**gather** (*shōu*) all the **hundreds of limbs** (*bǎitiǎn*) to the **elixir field** (*dāntián*),
**unceasingly** (*miánmiánmìmì*) and **unflaggingly** (*bù'èrbùxī*), **continuously** (*jìjì*) and **purely** (*chúnchún*),
**without slackness** (*wúdài*) or **neglect** (*wúhuāng*),
then **naturally** (*zìrán*) one’s **self-cultivation** (*xiūshēn*) will be complete,
and **all under heaven** (*tiānxià*) will find **peace** (*píng*).

*How else could one achieve such breadth if not through frugality?*

As for **not daring to lead all under heaven** (*bùgǎnwéitiānxiàxiān*),
it is the **proper humility** (*qiānzūn*) and **radiant virtue** (*guāng*) of **steadfastness** (*ānzhēn*).
**Gentleness** (*róushùn*) and **yielding** (*róushùn*) to all under heaven,
while none under heaven dare to contend with him—
this is to **align with** (*shùnchéng*) the **Dao/Way’s unfolding** (*tiāndào*),
and the **Dao/Way** will **silently** (*mò*) grant its completion (*chéng*).

*Without the **virtue/power** (*dé*) of **harmonious blending** (*chōnghé*),*
*how could one dare not to lead all under heaven,*
*and still achieve a **great instrument’s** (*dàqì*) late fulfillment (*wǎnchéng*) like this?*

Thus, **compassion** (*cí*), **frugality** (*jiǎn*), and **yielding** (*hòu*)
are all the **foundations** (*běnshǐ*) of seeking the **Dao/Way**.
**Courage** (*yǒng*), **breadth** (*guǎng*), and **leading** (*xiān*)
are all the **ultimate expressions** (*mòxiào*) of following the **Dao/Way**.

Yet today’s scholars are otherwise:
**Discarding compassion** (*shěcí*) yet **pursuing courage** (*qǐyǒng*), they will **nurture a cruel heart** (*shēngrényīnxīn*);
**Discarding frugality** (*shějiǎn*) yet **seeking breadth** (*qǐguǎng*), they will **harbor greedy desires** (*xīn huái tānniàn*);
**Discarding yielding** (*shěhòu*) yet **striving to lead** (*qǐxiān*), they will **engage in rivalry** (*bǔyǒuchéng*—*contesting competition*).

*All are paths that lead to death.*
Even if they **chance to survive** (*xìngcún*),
they are but **living corpses** (*xíngshī*), **wandering flesh** (*zǒuròu*),
**mingling indiscriminately** (*làncè*) among the masses (*rénqún*),
*what difference is there between them and the dead?*

In summary, **compassion** is the **natural principle** (*shēnglǐ*) of man,
a **shared essence** (*xìng suǒ tóngrán*) within all.
Only when one **guards it with restraint** (*shǒuzhīyǐyuē*) and **expresses it in harmony** (*chūzhīyǐhé*),
will **compassionate benevolence** (*cíhuì*) and **tender concern** (*cèdá*) flow forth from **true sincerity** (*zìchū zhēnchéng*),
and **none under heaven** (*tiānxià*) will fail to **bow in submission** (*xīnzhé*) and **yield** (*qūshì*).

*When benevolence suffices to benefit others,*
*a **compassionate man** (*rénzhě*) is **unmatched** (*wúdí*),
*how could there ever be defeat in battle,*
*or failure in defense,*
*bringing shame upon all under heaven?*

The *Book of Documents* (*Shū*) says:
*"Only the **Heavenly** (*wéitiān*) **obscures** (*yīn*) and **nourishes** (*bì*) the people below,
**harmonizing** (*xiángxié*) their dwelling in **cooperation** (*xiángxié*)."*
*Granting them life and fulfillment* (*yǐshēngyǐsuì*),
they **endure** (*yǒngxiǎng*) the **eternal peace** (*wúshì*) of **Heaven** (*tiān*),
*this is what is called the **Heaven’s intent to save** (*tiān jiāng jiùzhī*) them.*

The *Book of Odes* (*Shījīng*) says:
*"The **Heaven’s command** (*wéitiānzhīmìng*) is **profoundly harmonious** (*yúmù*) and **unceasing** (*bùyǐ*)."*
*It reveals the **clear and boundless qi** (*qīngkōngyīqì*),*
**flowing and unending** (*liúxíngbùxī*), **nurturing and limitless** (*fāyùwújiāng*),
*and it is said that **compassion alone** (*cíwèizhī*) suffices to **guard** (*wèi*) it.*

The **Dao/Way** is called the **Great Dao/Way** (*Dàdào*),
*its essence is **boundless** (*wújí*) yet the **Supreme Ultimate** (*Tàijí*).*
Yet if one does not **trace its origin from the boundless** (*cóngwújízhīshǐ*),
**searching** (*zhǐ*) for a **foothold** (*jīnyá*) in the **chaotic indistinctness** (*húnhúndùn*),
how could one **fathom** (*cè*) the **root of the Supreme Ultimate** (*Tàijízhīgēn*)?
How could one **measure** (*mì*) its **beginning and end** (*qǐzhǐ*)?

Scholars must first **clarify the Dao’s source** (*Dàoyuán*).
In the **unseen and unheard** (*bùshòubùwén*),
they must **seek out** (*zhǐ*) the **most subtle and profound essence** (*zhìyǐnzhìwēizhītǐ*),
*this is what is called the **void yet responsive** (*xūérlíng*).*

Yet its **subtlety is already extreme** (*guǎnqíxiàyǐshèn*),
*like a single grain of millet* (*shǔzhūyīlì*)—
*and there is something that can be **imagined** (*xiàng*).*

In totality, it is **form without form** (*wúxíngzhīxíng*),
**state without state** (*wúzhuàngzhīzhuàng*).
**Approach it and one does not see its head** (*yíngzhībùjiànqíshǒu*),
**follow it and one does not see its tail** (*suízhībùjiànqíhòu*).
*This is the **warm and luminous compassion** (*ǎirán yīpiàn cíhuì*) within man’s heart.*

Even the **most stubborn and base** (*suǒwéizhuānzuì*) of men,
*does not **extinguish** (*mǐn*) the **compassion** (*cízhīxìng*) within them.*
Confucius said:
*"If I seek **virtue/power** (*rén*), then **virtue/power** (*rén*) will **immediately** (*sī*) come to me."*

The cultivation of the elixir—what else is it?
It is nothing but nurturing this compassion.

What is compassion?
When the King of Qi sees a cow trembling and cannot bear it,
When a villager sees a weak child fall into a well and feels distress—
These are the stirrings of the heart of virtue,
The return of the Heavenly heart.

Reflecting on this, such impulses and thoughts arise without ceasing—
Only fear that people fail to perceive them.

The scholar, from the spontaneous movement of true nature,
Expands and acts, thereby establishing a foundation for elixir cultivation.
Yet one must not seek breadth at the cost of neglect,
But rather adhere to restraint and subtlety.

With single-minded devotion, wholehearted sincerity—
The ancients said:
*"The heart must dwell within the chest,
Lest thoughts stray through the gate of totality."*

Thus, the more restrained, the broader;
The more subtle, the more manifest.
The more precise the restraint,
The wider the unfolding.

How can the scholar not take frugality as the root?
Though frugality is a virtue of the mind,
It is indeed a fine method to avoid excess through restraint.
Yet if not tempered with humility and gentleness,
One risks the violent nature igniting and harming the elixir.
Thus, restraint must be paired with utmost harmony—
In all things, humility and self-effacement,
Never presuming to lead—
This is the way of emptying oneself for others.

When the heart of virtue endures,
The Dao-qi endures as well.

If one values courage over compassion,
Seeks abundance over frugality,
And places oneself before others,
Then the heart is merely ordinary.
How could one expect to hold the Three Treasures without loss?

Moreover, when a person possesses compassion,
It is most effective in winning others’ favor—
In battle, victory is assured;
In defense, the city stands firm.
This is like tending the furnace with fire,
Burning away the Three Vermin;
Or guarding the city with ritual purity,
Thus preserving the embryo-spirit and original essence.

This gentle and harmonious heart
Is the key to elixir cultivation and Dao-nurturing.

Heaven, in creating man,
Endows him with life and, without fail, with compassion.
If one can constantly reflect and return to virtue,
The elixir of eternal life is already within reach.

**Chapter 68: The Virtue of Non-Contention**
A true warrior does not rely on brute force,
A skilled fighter does not rage,
One who triumphs over enemies does not contend,
And one who wields people places himself beneath them.

This is called the virtue of non-contention,
This is called harnessing others’ strength,
This is called aligning with Heaven—
The ultimate of the ancients.

*"Shì"* (士) means teacher of the people.
A teacher of the people wields armies,
Originally valuing martial prowess.
The *Yijing* says:
*"Firm yet yielding, advancing boldly yet following order,
Mighty yet without slaughter."*
This is wielding force without force—
The mark of a true teacher of the people.

When facing a great enemy, armies clash,
Two forces stand arrayed—
One cannot avoid deploying troops,
Brandishing weapons, charging like rivers,
Their roar shaking the heavens.

Yet in truth, with few soldiers,
A sudden onslaught by a mighty foe—
It is no difficulty to play music and drive back the invaders,
Or compose melodies to lift the siege.
This is the way of achieving might without anger.

When the troops return in triumph, proclaiming victory,
While others boast of their deeds,
He alone humbly withdraws—
Such a general can stand without shame.

Even if honors are heaped upon him,
He remains modest, his face flushed—
This is how one who conquers without contention behaves.

The *Shujing* says:
*"You alone do not contend,
And none in the world will contend with your ability."*
Is this not what is meant?

Such men arise from promoting the old while establishing the new,
Gathering wisdom and broadening benefits,
Not relying on their own talents,
But skillfully using others’ talents as their own;
Not boasting of their own wisdom,
But skillfully using others’ wisdom as their own.

This is the way of humbling oneself to elevate others—
If not for heeding wise counsel,
Consulting the good path,
Maintaining an open heart like a valley,
And practicing humble reverence—
How could one achieve such mastery of war and victory,
Turning the world as easily as flipping one’s palm?

This is the virtue of non-contention,
By which one serves the people.
This is harnessing others’ strength,
By which one commands the world.
This is aligning with the people below,
And harmonizing with Heaven above—
By which one serves the people’s hearts.
This is harnessing others’ strength,
By which one commands the world.
This is aligning with the people below,
And harmonizing with Heaven above—
Standing with Heaven and Earth,
And establishing the ultimate of all ages.

Alas! Without the saint’s utmost sincerity and fulfillment of nature,
How could one, amid the chaos of war,
Conceal the winds of peace and courtesy,
Wield force without force,
Act in anger without anger,
Contend without contention—
As described here?

Moreover, ruling with broad tolerance,
Planning with deference to others,
Placing the worthy in office,
And the capable in their posts—
Then all under Heaven’s scholars
Will offer loyalty and sincerity,
Eager to perform deeds and fulfill commands,
As if Heaven’s seasons flow without speech,
And all things present their offerings—
This is how one aligns with Heaven and Earth,
And establishes the ultimate.

The *Shijing* says:
*"Reflect on the virtuous Hou Ji,
Who aligned with Heaven—
None but you could achieve this ultimate."*
Without such a man, to whom shall we turn?

This speaks of refining the medicine with fire—
Though there are fierce methods of rapid distillation,
Yet one must act according to the time,
Follow the natural course,
Wield force without force—
Thus avoiding the peril of overturning the elixir or toppling the cauldron.

Even when the vital breath moves,
And true yang arises—
Most vast, most strong,
Filling the two great vessels—
What differs from a warrior’s fierce, overwhelming rage,
Sweeping all before him?

Moreover, when gathering and refining the fire,
It is only because the浩然 (*hàorán*) breath expands—
Not for the sake of strength itself.
Thus, after purification,
Ordinary bones become jade bones,
And the mortal body transforms into a golden body.

It is said: *"Taking the Golden Elixir in the palm,
Or seizing the world as one picks up a feather."*
Only when the spirit is both concentrated and effortless,
And breathing is both regulated and unrestrained,
Does one purely follow Heaven,
Without human interference.

Even when Heaven and man contend,
And reason and desire clash—
One must still uphold reason to restrain desire,
And exhaust human effort to merge with Heaven.

When cultivation reaches its utmost refinement—
Principles profound, benevolence mature—
No trace remains of selfish ambition,
No striving for merit or prowess—
Thus the benevolent stand unmatched beneath Heaven.

Such is the way of humility and yielding,
Of emptiness and submission to others,
Of attuning to the ebb and flow of *qi*,
And letting it guide one’s transformation.

Therefore, the elixir’s completion
Has its unseen yet manifest,
Its motionless yet ever-changing,
Its effortless yet inevitable fulfillment.
What differs from a master’s skill in wielding others?

In cultivating the human *Dao*,
If one remains ever at peace,
Ever yielding and compliant,
Desire needing no suppression to suppress itself,
Reason needing no invocation to arise naturally—
This is called the *virtue* of non-contention.

With a heart of non-contention,
Following principle in action,
Adapting to circumstances in movement—
It is as though using others’ strength
To achieve one’s own success,
Yet encompassing Heaven and Earth’s transformations
Without overreaching.

Confucius said:
*"What does Heaven speak?
The four seasons proceed therein,
All things take life therein."*

The Sage merges with the *Dao* in truth,
Standing firm as the very threads of Heaven and Earth,
And establishing the ultimate measure for all ages.

**Chapter 69: The Victorious Through Compassion**
In warfare, it is said:
*"I dare not be the aggressor while the other defends,
Nor advance an inch only to retreat a foot."*
This is called:
Moving without movement,
Pushing without arms,
Conquering without force,
Seizing without weapons.

No greater calamity than underestimating the enemy—
Underestimating the enemy risks losing what is most precious.

Thus, when armies clash,
The compassionate prevail.

Ancient strategists spoke of battle tactics:
*"I dare not be the aggressor while the other defends."*
The aggressor is like a sovereign—
A sovereign’s decrees command absolute authority;
The defender is like a minister—
A minister obeys the sovereign’s will,
As in *"Let the other be the host, I the guest."*

Thus, if I am the aggressor,
It means my post-Heavenly human-heart takes command,
While my pre-Heavenly *Dao*-heart retreats in submission.
How dare I let the post-Heavenly human-heart dominate,
And treat it as the guest while relying on it utterly?

*"I dare not advance an inch only to retreat a foot"*—
This means:
When victorious, even an inch gained must be firmly held;
When defeated, not even a single step may be conceded.
If one advances with an inch’s gain
Yet retreats by a foot’s measure,
It is to gain little and lose much,
Difficult to succeed and easy to fail.

In warfare, this is the mark of an incompetent general;
In cultivating the *Dao*, this is the fate of one who achieves nothing.
How dare I?

Only by yielding to the other as host,
And submitting myself as guest,
Does the enemy have no advantage to exploit,
And I no vulnerability to attack—
Thus, controlling others without being controlled,
Avoiding humiliation entirely.

Advance where opportunity allows,
Retreat where difficulty arises.
In advancing, one must rouse the spirit of boundless momentum;
In retreating, one must not present an assailable posture.
Such caution, such vigilance—
Always yielding precedence to others,
While placing oneself last.

Thus, in marching armies,
It appears as though others can move while I seem unable;
Yet when I press forward with resolve,
Stretching forth my arms,
It seems as though others have arms while I have none.
When facing the enemy in battle,
With armies locked in struggle,
It seems as though others can resist while I cannot.

Though drums thunder and flags billow,
The enemy wields weapons—
How could I alone be weaponless?

Thus, advancing without recklessness,
Retreating without haste—
Truly knowing that the fate of the state,
The rise or fall of the nation,
Hangs upon this single battle—
How could the enemy be treated lightly?

Observe the ancients who treated the enemy with caution—
They often succeeded;
Those who underestimated the enemy—
They invariably failed.
As seen in Guan Zi’s campaign against the Yanrong,
Or Zi Yu’s battle at Chengpu.

Moreover, the prosperity or decline of the court
Depends on the success or failure of its generals.
If one does not approach matters with utmost prudence,
Boasting of talent or relying on cunning,
And claiming none can surpass them—
Like Meng Ming’s reckless advance,
Or Gao Gu’s overconfident charge—
There has never been one who did not bring ruin to the state,
Destruction to the family,
And extinction to the ancestral line.

The Sage’s greatest treasure is *trust*—
Underestimating the enemy betrays the sovereign’s faith.

When two armies confront,
Two forces clash—
Yet one remains weak and yielding,
Most compassionate, most benevolent.
Always fearing that the *qi* of slaughter
Disrupts the harmony of Heaven and Earth;
Never rejoicing in the might of arms
To expand territory through conquest.

Warfare on the battlefield
Is but a last resort.
Though no weeping follows the army’s retreat,
The heart of compassion and pity,
The anguish and urgency of grief,
Already permeate the encamped troops,
And the three armies are nourished by it,
While the common people respond with gratitude.

Thus, the virtuous either do not war,
Or, if they do, they prevail.
Is this not because the heart of compassion
Extends to others,
And enters their very souls?

This illustrates the birth of true *yang*,
The flourishing of *qi*—
Only then may the fire be refined and the work proceed.

If one does not quietly await the *yin* essence’s movement,
And leisurely regulates the ascent and descent,
The advance and retreat,
The cyclical circulation of spirit—
There will be none who do not suffer from errant fire,
And face great humiliation.

At the four critical junctures,
One must await the *kan* *qi*’s spontaneous motion,
Lest *li* presume to dominate.
Thus it is said:
*"I dare not be the aggressor while the other defends."*

In the *Dao* of cultivation,
Advancing brings stability,
Retreating brings disaster.
Like Heaven’s ceaseless motion,
Like water’s unending flow—
Only then does one ascend daily without halt.
If one proceeds intermittently,
Now pushing, now neglecting—
One step forward, ten steps back—
Gaining little, losing much—
Success will never be attained in a lifetime.

This arises from ignorance of the *Dao* of returning to the root,
Of restoring one’s true nature—
A path of daily practice,
Not to be seized by clever schemes,
Nor achieved through contrived actions.

「Indeed, those who cultivate the *Dao* of the elixir in reverse—
Forcing the *Dao* against its natural flow—
Study as though they do not study,
And labor though their efforts yield no fruit,
Yet follow the *Dao*’s unfolding,
Proceeding with ease and without hindrance,
Taking nothing in hand—
It is like the Great Yu taming the waters,
Acting where there is no need to act.

Should one instead advance fire and invoke talismans,
Lightly rushing forward or retreating,
It is like a soldier who scorns his enemy—
None have escaped fire rising to harm the elixir,
Or the furnace crumbling while the cauldron fails,
Thus causing lead and mercury to scatter in ruin.

Ah! To trust nature purely,
And to hold oneself in reverent caution—
This alone ensures the radiant brilliance of success.

But if one recklessly fancies cleverness,
The treasure of longevity will be slain by this very *post-heaven* thief of the corpse,
And its harm will be without end—
How then can one hope for an elixir that is both great and enduring?

Only with boundless compassion,
And profound sorrow for all things,
Does one then manifest in peace,
And act with gentle compliance—
Thus does nature yield in all directions,
And every battle is won without struggle.

Students of the *Dao* must be cautious:
Do not let the *post-heaven* mind of knowledge dominate,
While the *pre-heaven* primordial *qi* retreats in submission—
Then perhaps will there be no deviation.

**Chapter Seventy: The Humble Clothes the Precious Jade**

My words are very easy to know,
Very easy to practice.
Yet none under Heaven can know them,
None can practice them.

Words have a *root* (*宗*),
Affairs have a *master* (*君*).
It is only because of ignorance,
That none recognize me.

Few know me—
Thus I am held in esteem.

Therefore, the Sage wears coarse raiment,
Yet harbors the most precious jade.

The Great *Dao* is the inherent goodness of human nature,
A matter of daily practice,
Ever near and close—
One cannot separate from it for even a moment.

To separate is to lose the *Dao*,
To lose the *Dao* is to lose humanity—
Then what words are there?

Even if my teachings were piled in millions,
Filling chests and boxes beyond count,
Yet all are intimately tied to human nature,
Close to daily practice—
None are difficult to know or practice.

So why is it that none under Heaven can know or practice them?
Is it because my words are not easy to know or practice?

No—it is because words have a *root*,
And affairs have a *master*—
The *root* being the innate knowledge one does not need to study,
The *master* being the innate ability one does not need to learn.

If one knows the *root* of words,
Then one draws near from within oneself,
And all words become virtuous speech.

If one knows the *master* of affairs,
Then one silently observes their hidden essence,
And all actions become virtuous deeds.

The *Dao* is like a great road—
How could it be difficult to know or practice?

To reflect inwardly and be sincere—
No greater joy is there.

If one does not know that words have a *root*,
Or that affairs have a *master*,
And instead seeks them in distant and lofty places,
Or in broad and remote lands—
Then one indulges in trifles and loses one’s resolve,
Pursues the vast and strays from the essential,
Makes one’s form a burden,
And one’s nature a chain of *qi*—
Thus does the original virtue of Heaven’s *Dao* grow dim?

Even so,
Their knowing me adds nothing to me,
Their ignorance harms me not at all.
For my being is my own—
It does not depend on others’ knowing or ignorance.

Few know me—
Thus my value remains unchanged to me.

Therefore, the Sage dons the most humble rags,
Yet within holds the most noble jade—
He obscures his presence in the mountains and forests,
Hides himself in rocky caves—
Only to align with the principles of nature and life,
To harmonize with Heaven and Earth’s *Dao*,
And to cultivate what lies within himself.
Whether others recognize or deny him,
He cares not in the least.

This is why the wise grow ever wiser,
And the foolish grow ever more foolish.

The words of the Supreme Sage (*太上*) are *Dao* in every line,
And every character speaks to the self.

To speak of *Dao* is to speak of humanity,
To speak of humanity is to speak of the *Dao*—
Nothing could be easier than this.

So why are there those who find it difficult to know or practice?

It is because the ignorant seek the *Dao* afar,
Yet it lies near—
They seek what is easy as difficult,
And what is simple as complex.

They do not attend to the true and constant *Dao*,
But instead chase after dross and remnants—
Like the study of rhetoric, memorization, legal codes, or divination—
The more they learn, the more their minds stray,
The more affairs they handle, the more their nature deteriorates.
No wonder the words of the Supreme Sage
Are understood by all in their time,
Yet only the Supreme Sage understands them in later ages.

It is because they fail to grasp
That words have a *root*,
And affairs have a *master*.

This *root* and *master*—
They are the “**middle**” (*中*) of humanity itself.

When Yao and Shun passed on the *Dao*,
It was nothing but “**Hold fast to this middle**” (*允执厥中*).

Later, the teachings of Confucius on “**purity and unity**” (*纯一*),
Of the *Zhongyong* on “**solitary vigilance**” (*慎独*),
And of the *Kō* School on “**innate knowledge**” (*良知*),
Are all but variations of the human “**middle**.”

This single character “**middle**” (*中*)
Encompasses much.

Within humanity, it is twofold:
One guards the *middle* of form—
Zhu Xi says: “**Guard the middle to regulate the outside.**”
To guard the *middle* is to turn light inward,
To focus attention on what is central.
At the point one inch below the navel,
Neither clinging nor rejecting—this is it.

One guards the *middle* of the formless—
The *Zhongyong* says: *“Before joy, anger, sorrow, or delight arise, this is the **middle**.”*
Luó Cóngyàn taught Lǐ Yánpíng:
“Observe the *middle* in stillness,
And the phenomena of joy, anger, sorrow, or delight—
In this unarisen state,
Neither heard nor seen,
With reverent caution and fear—
Then nature settles and the mind clears,
And one sees the original face.
Then human desires grow pure,
And Heaven’s principle shines bright.
From ancient times, sages, immortals, Buddhas,
All took this as the first step of practice.
But one must first guard the *middle* with strenuous effort,
And finally attain the *middle* in pure nature.”

The three sages have different names,
Yet all take the “**middle**” (*中*) as their *root*,
As their *master*.

Even as I teach,
Concentrating the mind and harmonizing the breath—
Then one turns inward to observe the original orifice,
The mind devoid of its own mind,
The *qi* devoid of its own *qi*—
Only then does one achieve peace of mind and harmony of breath.
Peace of mind brings concentration,
Harmony of breath brings regulation.
The essence lies only in the two words: *peace of mind*.

When the mind does not stir—
This is *peace*.
To hold fast to the *middle*—
This is *peace*.
*Peace* is within this *middle*.」

The heart abides here—this is the **mysterious orifice** (*玄關*) of the **elixir scriptures** (*丹經*).
When **Dao/Way** (*神*) and **qi** (*氣*) become interdependent,
The essence of the **mysterious orifice** is established,
This is the *root* of the **Great Dao** (*大道*), the *origin* of the **golden elixir** (*金丹*).

As in **refining fire** (*進火*) and **withdrawing talismans** (*退符*),
**Transporting and refining** (*搬運*) the **river vehicle** (*河車*),
All **actions** (*有為*) and **endeavors** (*有作*)
Ultimately value **modesty** (*謙*), **gentleness** (*和*), **softness** (*柔*), and **docility** (*順*).
**Compose** (*以整*) and **rest** (*以暇*), neither **aiding** nor **forgetting**.
**Essential matters** (*有要*) return to the **non-dual** (*無*),
Yet the **non-dual** (*無*) again gives rise to **duality** (*有*).
When **duality** and **non-duality** cease to stand,
Only then does one align with the **natural Dao** (*天然道體*).

This is the attainment of **oneness** (*一*)—
All matters complete—
The **Way** of my teaching: **"One penetrates all"** (*一以貫之*).

If scholars understand this,
They may solve the **Laozi’s** (*太上*) **lightness** (*輕*),
And avoid being misled by **heterodox paths** (*旁門左道*).

If one does not know the **root** (*有宗*) of speech,
The **master** (*有君*) of practice,
One may not be permitted to enter the hall—
Yet be lost in other pursuits.

When one knows this and acts accordingly,
Naturally there will be attainment within,
No longing for the external.
Like the **sage** (*聖人*) **wearing coarse robes yet harboring jade** (*被褐怀玉*),
Yet **radiant and unrestrained** (*融融泄泄*) without end.

**Chapter Seventy-One: The Superiority of Knowing the Unknown**
*"Knowing the unknown"* (*知不知*), superior;

「Not knowing what one knows is an affliction.
Only by recognizing this affliction—
Does one truly escape it.

The sage is free from affliction,
For he recognizes his affliction—
Thus, he is free from it.

His wisdom shines like a dustless mirror,
Still waters without ripples—
All things reflected, none evading its light.

This divine clarity is innate,
Naturally knowing through phenomena,
As the heart perceives them.
Not conjecturing to claim insight,
Nor guessing to feign knowledge.

His knowing arises from the self-luminous nature,
Not from preordained foresight,
Yet he discerns what others cannot.

This is the wisdom of the supreme sage—
Beyond the reach of common men.

Ordinary minds cannot penetrate truth,
Nor illuminate phenomena.
They speculate on others’ sincerity,
Anticipate the rise and fall of affairs—
If by chance they guess correctly,
Men praise their mirror-like clarity,
And they boast of their divine discernment.

Such contrived knowledge is not divine illumination,
But forcing ignorance into knowledge:
Though they claim understanding,
Their weary minds and labored thoughts
Are already deeply afflicted—
Self-made cleverness,
Self-draining vital essence.

Only by recognizing forced knowledge as affliction,
Does one cure what ails them.
Thus, they investigate truth to fulfill their nature,
Cultivate their essence to await Heaven’s timing.
Wise yet unassuming,
Intelligent yet appearing foolish,
Their minds become empty as water,
Their nature clear as ice,
A single luminous spirit,
Piercing through the Three Thousand Worlds—
Why endure the toil of scheming,
The labor of artifice, as burdens?

Thus, they are free from affliction.

The sage’s insight penetrates all affairs,
His wisdom encompasses all things.
He possesses innate enlightenment,
Lacks no conjectural guesswork.

Common men are afflicted,
But the sage is not—
For he afflicts his ignorance,
Afflicts his obscurity,
And thus, with wholehearted devotion,
He gathers all within the Mysterious Pass.
With time, his luminous spirit blazes forth,
Illuminating without remainder—
Ever present, ever advantageous,
Nowhere hindered.

Because he afflicts his affliction,
He is free from affliction.

This is the knowing of enlightened wisdom—
The highest attainment.
Yet contrived knowledge is a grave peril.
Only by recognizing forced knowing as affliction,
Does one escape affliction entirely.

The sage’s freedom from peril lies in
Treating this peril as peril—
Thus, he knows no peril.

The essence is now clear—
No further elaboration is needed.»

Now let me unfold the subtleties of the Dao more fully:

In general, when seated in meditation, one must first depart from the mundane to cultivate *zhì* (定), striking a single resonant note before self-examining and self-verifying. If indeed there is nothing at all—empty and void—then the mind is clarified and nature is seen.

Thus I have often said: At the outset of seated stillness, this mind hangs suspended in the boundless void. Only when body and mind are settled, and intention and *qi* harmonized, may one gradually gather and reflect inward upon the original palace.

When nothing remains within the chest, and one awakens to this luminous awareness, then the ten directions and three realms are all encompassed within this awakening perception.

Yet the nature of awakening neither arises nor perishes—it is simply clear and self-illuminating, as it ever was.

If one seeks the Mysterious through this, the source becomes utterly pure, and may serve as the foundation for my elixir’s formation.

In an instant, the innate light naturally manifests.

How does one recognize this? Within that former state of vague and indistinct perception—when suddenly awakening and moving, this marks the essential beginning of Dao cultivation.

Yet to govern emotions with nature—if one does not first uncover the true essence of nature itself, and instead plunges blindly into the watery depths seeking the Mysterious, how can one govern emotions when nature is already lost?

For there exists a primal, vibrant vitality of the spirit.

Know that *Lí* (離) does not belong to the mind: when one concentrates auditory resonance, contains visual radiance, restrains the senses of scent, taste, and touch—these are all the workings of divine fire, hence it is called *Lí*.

*Kǎn* (坎) is not *qi*: wherever vital essence resides, that is *Kǎn*.

Even if divine essence enters the blood, and fire heats the waters, this does not necessarily stir *qi*—one must lift from left and right, gathering the waves from the abyssal depths, guiding them upward to the elixir field. Through prolonged refinement, when the fire is sufficient, suddenly the celestial mechanism activates, and the body leaps in ecstasy, from fingertips to every limb, trembling ceaselessly.

The body stands rigid as a wall, the mind cold as ashes. When the elixir field’s *qi* grows warm, this is the balanced state—neither too hot nor too cool—of the fire’s union.

If this phenomenon does not appear, the fire is still too weak and must not be applied. If one persists in this state without igniting the fire, the *qi* will already have dissipated by the time one attempts it—this is the peril of aged, useless medicine. Students must discern this carefully.

Yet when the subtle yang first stirs, it may not yet produce such vigorous *qi*. As long as the mind is tranquil and intention at ease, and breathing harmonized, one may still practice the *Zi-Wu* (子午) River Vehicle.

For the body’s formed and substantial blood, unrefined by fire, remains murky and stagnant—a mass of lifeless sludge. Only when divine fire illuminates does a single point of true *qi* emerge within the blood.

This is as the Buddha said: *"In this defiled and impure age, I cultivated and attained the fruit of the Dao."*

The ancients also spoke of *"taking treasure from the ghost’s nest, seeking lead beneath the black mountain"*—all referring to the same process: within the turbid essence and decayed blood, divine fire refines this single point of true *qi*.

Once *qi* moves, yang arises. One must then know the laws of *Zi* advancing yang and *Wu* retreating yin, along with the methods of *Mǎo-Yǒu* (卯酉) bathing, to extract this true yang and circulate it freely. Internally, it dispels the hidden yin of the viscera; externally, it attracts the celestial true yang’s vital *qi*.

With prolonged practice, even the substance of *qi* transforms.

This River Vehicle method holds boundless profundity.

The ancients said, *"When the *qi* is clear, the Zi-Wu River is drawn and added"*—drawing refers to extracting lead from the watery depths, adding refers to supplementing mercury from the *Lí* palace.

Mercury is the luminous fluid of the mind—the later heaven within the earlier heaven. From the colored body’s turbid essence and decayed blood, divine fire refines it into sweet dew—this is lead, the *qi* within the blood.

This *qi* is what the ancients called *"gold within the water"*—it belongs to the later heaven within the earlier heaven, sufficient only to stabilize the mundane, not to generate the Dharma body.

This is the medicine produced by the union of *Kǎn* and *Lí*, yet it is still not the true elixir.

One must govern emotions with nature, return nature to nature, and when nature and emotions harmonize, refine them together in the *Kūn* (坤) furnace. Suddenly, true yang erupts—this is the union of *Qián* (乾) and *Kūn*, and only then does one forge the divine elixir into a true immortal.

In summary, there is no other method of cultivation—only the ceaseless turning of the River Vehicle.

At the lower pass, the River must be forced;
At the middle pass, the River Vehicle arises from both human and celestial;
At the upper pass, the River Vehicle flows purely of its own accord, never failing in its timing.

As for the methods of *Mǎo-Yǒu* bathing, these are only for beginners lest their minds grow restless and the fire flare unchecked. When one reaches mastery, no methods are needed.

It all depends on the student’s divine illumination.

Chapter Seventy-Two: The People Do Not Fear the **Way**’s **威**

The people do not fear the **威**—the **威** has reached its peak.

Do not confine their dwelling, do not weary of their birth.

For it is only through not wearying that one does not weary.

Thus, the sage knows himself but does not display himself, loves himself but does not exalt himself.

Hence, one discards that and embraces this.

The **威** spoken of is the grand framework of **纲常名教** (social norms and moral teachings), the most difficult transgression of **天理** (Heavenly Principle).

Let them know to be cautious in solitude, even in bed and shadow,
To fear the **威** of Heaven in its hidden subtleties—
Then naturally, Heaven’s blessings will be pure and abundant,
And their longevity will know no bounds.

The *Shijing* (Book of Odes) says:
*"Fear the **威** of Heaven,
And it will preserve you in due time."*

If the **威** of Heaven looms near like a foot’s length,
Yet one does not remain vigilant and clear each morning,
It will lead to the collapse of propriety and the extinction of rites—
Then Heaven’s **天良** (natural conscience) will vanish,
Heaven’s **天罚** (retribution) will not be spared,
And disasters will not be avoided,
Life will not be preserved.

Thus, the people do not fear the **威**—the **威** has reached its peak.

If such is the case,
It is because they do not know that **知仁** (knowing virtue) is their secure abode,
Yet they abandon this abode and do not dwell within it;
That **义** (righteousness) is the source of **生气** (life force),
Yet they forsake this life force and bring ruin upon themselves.

Alas! Those people are unfortunate,
Born not into the age of **太古** (Primordial Antiquity),
Where **德威** (virtue and awe) are feared,
And **德明** (virtue and clarity) are held in reverence—
Thus they act with external transgressions and ignorance,
Heaven’s signs are neglected,
And drought, flood, pestilence, and famine arise—
Disasters of all kinds flourish.

Only the ruler above should guide them with the vast dwelling of **天下** (the world),
And let them wander in the realm of **太和** (Great Harmony),
Not confining them in narrowness,
Yet daily treading upon the brink of peril and annihilation;
Should draw them with **浩然** (boundless) **正气** (right qi),
And let them nourish it in the **清虚** (pure and empty) **天** (Heaven),
Not wearying of their birth,
Yet risking to fall into ruin and cessation.

It is only through cherishing the principle of their own life,
And preserving Heaven’s **天良** (natural conscience),
Without the slightest weariness or exhaustion,
That one attains the *Shijing*’s words:
*"Revere Heaven’s wrath,
And do not dare trifle with it;
Revere Heaven’s decrees,
And do not dare neglect them."*

Heaven observes their **德** (virtue),
And grants them brilliance and prosperity,
Longevity and preservation—
Indeed, there exists one who shares in Heaven and Earth’s eternal duration.
Thus, it is through not wearying that this is achieved.

Who but the sage could accomplish this?

The ancient emperors cultivated humility and non-action,
Illuminated great **德** (virtue/power),
Daily refining and monthly perfecting,
Purifying their hearts and clearing their thoughts,
Penetrating the marvels of **造化** (creation and transformation),
Piercing the clarity of **本来之天** (the original Heaven),
Only knowing themselves in truth.

As for **德业文章** (virtuous deeds and literary attainments),
The external marks they bear—
The sage never uses them to display or reveal himself to others.

Moreover, they revered the **乾** (Qian) at dawn and the **坤** (Kun) at dusk,
Heavily upheld the **Dao/Way** and guarded their bodies,
Never for a breath departing from **仁** (benevolence),
And the world could not add anything to them.

How did they love themselves?—
As for fame, rank, reputation, and glory—
The things others hold in high regard,
The sage never considered them worthy of exaltation.

Though the sage’s own self-knowledge and self-love
Are the same as what all people know and love—
It is only that common people, knowing it, must make it manifest,
Loving it, must hold it in high esteem;
The sage knows himself but does not display himself,
Loves himself but does not exalt himself.
Their caution in solitude is profound,
Yet they do not indulge in ostentation before the world;
They uphold self-nourishment,
Yet they do not dare to boast of it.

How could they be compared with common people?

It is said that by discarding desire and embracing principle,
By fulfilling humanity’s potential in harmony with Heaven,
One ascends to transcend the mortal and attain sainthood,
To sever resemblance and depart from the crowd,
And become an immortal enduring millions of years—
All this arises from knowing the vast dwelling’s security,
Cherishing the eternal life’s joy,
Focusing solely on this and not on that,
And the people naturally move toward goodness, unaware of their own doing.

Abandon this, how could such goodness be achieved?

These words mean:
Do not confine their dwelling—then their dwelling will be grand.
Do not weary of their birth—then their birth will be long.

However, in the cultivation process,
The **元神** (Original Spirit) and **识神** (Perceptive Spirit) must be understood.

When a person first receives **气** (qi),
At the moment of union between father and mother,
A single **黍珠** (millet-sized pearl) is formed—
At this time, it is **絪絪缊缊** (unformed and indistinct),
A mere cluster of **太和** (Great Harmony) **气** (qi),
With no trace of knowledge.

Yet, it is the most divine and wondrous essence,
Exhausting all transformations and producing endless worldly affairs—
All from this single **含灵** (life-containing) **气** (qi) **神** (spirit),
From **无知无识** (no knowledge, no perception) to **有知有识** (knowledge and perception),
From **无作无为** (no action, no doing) to **有作有为** (action and doing),
None of which are not initiated here.

At this stage, **天人一理** (Heaven and humanity are one principle),
**物我同源** (things and self share the same origin),
**体用兼赅** (substance and function are inseparable),
And **显微无间** (manifest and subtle are continuous)—
Thus, it is called the **元神** (Original Spirit).

This is what Heaven bestows.

When the blood and flesh take form,
Ten months within the womb,
A cry at birth—
The infant emerges, and the **识神** (Perceptive Spirit) begins to manifest.

The **元神** (Original Spirit) is the **元气** (Primordial Qi) of **先天** (pre-heaven),
Shared by Heaven, Earth, and all beings,
Hidden within the **太虚** (Great Void).
Once it enters the human body,
It is concealed in the **虚无窟子** (void-like cavity) within the person.
This is Heaven’s gift.

Those who cultivate seek not to grasp at emptiness or color in their pursuit.
Only through **无所知** (no knowledge) and **无所有** (no possession),
Sweeping away all dust and turbidity,
Does the inner essence reveal itself,
And the divine mystery arise.

As for the **识神** (Perceptive Spirit),
It is the **精灵** (refined essence) **鬼** (spirit) of the human body,
The seed of endless **轮回** (reincarnations).
It requires the five senses and the complete formation of the ten thousand limbs,
Only when the person is about to be born and descend to earth,
Does the refined essence’s **魂魄** (soul and vital force) find attachment.

The ancients called this the **后天识神** (post-heaven Perceptive Spirit),
Because it arises from the formed **魄** (vital force).

This is the fundamental distinction between **元神** (Original Spirit) and **识神** (Perceptive Spirit).

Yet after birth,
The two spirits—**元神** (Original Spirit) and **识神** (Perceptive Spirit)—
Merge into one.

Sometimes the **元神** (Original Spirit) is active,
And the **识神** (Perceptive Spirit) recedes in silence—
Though the **后天** (post-heaven) **意气** (intentions and emotions) may stir,
They all arise from **仁义礼智** (benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom),
And manifest as **喜怒哀乐** (joy, anger, sorrow, delight).
The **识神** (Perceptive Spirit) also transforms into the **元神** (Original Spirit).

Sometimes the **识神** (Perceptive Spirit) is active,
And the **元神** (Original Spirit) is concealed—
Though traces of **仁义礼智** (benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom) appear,
They all become **私恩、私爱、私憎、私嫌** (private favors, private affections, private hatreds, private suspicions).
The **元神** (Original Spirit) also transforms into the **识神** (Perceptive Spirit).

The **Perceptive Spirit** alone governs the body and senses—
Once it takes charge, how could there be purity and rectitude
To undertake deeds worthy of Heaven and Earth,
Or confront the spirits of the numinous?

Only in the boundless void, when the Spirit stirs at a single impulse,
Does Heavenly Principle remain untainted—
No trace of post-heaven perception mingles within.
If one steadies the footing here and proceeds with unwavering resolve,
Then one is purely Heavenly Principle,
With not a thread of human desire’s private schemes.

Thus I teach: when in ignorance and unawareness,
Seek the single aperture of the **Mystic Pass**—
For then one is one with Heaven and Earth,
And harmonized with the boundless void.

If one seizes this and presses forward,
Then the generative flow of Heaven and Earth
Need not be borrowed—it arises from within.
The transformations of the void
Need not be borrowed—its mysteries unfold from within.
This awakening is truly the root of Heaven, Earth, and Man.

If cultivators do not begin here,
Where else shall they ascend to sagehood and transcendence?

In sum:
What emerges without thought or deliberation—**Original Spirit**.
What arises with schemes, judgments, and from the colored form—**Perceptive Spirit**.
The **Original Spirit** is formless; the **Perceptive Spirit** leaves traces.
One originates from the void; one emerges from the colored body—
These two are utterly dissimilar.

Once it is clear that the **Original Spirit** arises from the void,
And the **Perceptive Spirit** from the colored form,
Then I return to the root and clear the source,
Striving to seal the inner and outer **Three Treasures**,
Not permitting a single perception or thought
To emerge from form, image, deliberation, or awareness.

If one cultivates thus, if one nurtures thus,
Over time, even the soul of the corpse will transmute into pure **Original Spirit**,
And the eighty-four thousand hairs will become protective **Spiritual Warriors**.
This is what is meant by
*"Transforming Perception into Originality,
Converting Yin into Yang."*

This lies in anchoring oneself in the void,
Not allowing the colored body to dictate perception.

---
**Chapter Seventy-Three: Unsummoned, It Comes**

*"Bold in daring brings death;
Bold in restraint brings life."*
These two—sometimes advantageous, sometimes harmful.
What Heaven abhors, who can fathom its reason?
Thus even the Sage finds it difficult.

The **Dao of Heaven** conquers without striving,
Responds without speaking,
Arrives without being summoned—
Open and yet masterfully plotting.
*"The Heavenly Net is vast, wide yet not missing a thread."*
The *Odes* say:
*"The Mandate of Heaven—
Ah, its solemnity never ceases!"*
He who steals Heaven and Earth’s **qi** to forge the Elixir
Steals from the solemn, unceasing Mandate of Heaven.

This Mandate, in Heaven, is the pure and void **qi**;
In Man, it is the **Great Harmony** of **Heavenly Principle**.
Only by direct cultivation from dawn’s first light,
Until the **Overflowing Virtue** fills the two extremes,
Does one return to the root and restore the Mandate—
Ascending and descending, flowing with Heaven and Earth alike.

How does one cultivate?
First, in **killing false thoughts**—
False thoughts must be firm,
Unyielding to external things,
So that **Right Qi** may always expand.
Second, in **nurturing true thoughts**—
True thoughts must be yielding,
For yielding can delight all hearts,
So that **Overflowing Virtue** may always congeal.

These two—
The relentless, daring force of *"killing"* is the **mechanism of death**.
The hesitant, weak submission of *"not daring"* is the **vitality of life**.
*"Bold in daring brings death"*—this is the foundation of retreat as advance,
Of defeat as the root of victory.
The *Classic of Changes* says:
*"At high noon, the sun declines;
When the moon is full, it wanes."*
Heaven and Earth’s fullness and emptiness move with the times—
Sometimes beneficial, sometimes harmful,
Often contrary to the world’s expectations.
Thus what Man rejoices in, Heaven may abhor.

Moreover, what does Heaven truly abhor?
The **Heavenly One’s** heart cherishes life,
Yet sometimes employs death;
The **Supreme Deity’s** intent upholds virtue,
Yet sometimes enforces punishment.
This is life within death, death within life—
Its meaning profound, beyond mortal comprehension.
What Heaven abhors, who can know its reason?

Thus the Sage, cultivating the **Dao**,
Knows that fortune is the foundation of calamity,
That yielding is the substance of firmness,
And balances extremes while holding to the mean.
Abandoning self-interest, they guard the **Right Way**,
Not contending with the vulgar over gain or loss,
But discerning only their own compliance or defiance.

As for bestowing calamity or blessing,
Granting good fortune or misfortune—
Even the Sage finds it difficult to divine,
Let alone those beneath.

The **Dao of the Sage** is the **Dao of Heaven**.
The Sage acts purely in accordance with Nature,
While advancement, retreat, ascent, and descent
Flow within the body’s transformations.
Heaven’s **Dao** acts without action, in accordance with Nature,
While generation, growth, withdrawal, and storage
Ever circulate beyond the void—
Thus it does not contend with all things for dominance,
Yet life and death hover ever near,
And none can defy its reach—
This is conquering without striving.

It does not speak of principle to the common folk,
Yet its blessings and calamities extend everywhere—
No place escapes its reach—
This is responding without speaking.

Though its timing may vary—
Swift or slow, heavy or light—
There is an unseen balance,
A power beyond mortal design.
Thus it is said:
*"Unsummoned, it comes;
Open, yet masterfully plotting."*

No matter how brilliant one’s talent,
Or how cunning one’s schemes,
If the heart is exposed, all pretense is futile.
*"The Heavenly Net is vast, wide yet not missing a thread."*
This is no falsehood.

To suppress desire requires decisiveness—
Without decisiveness, the heart runs wild,
And human desires cling tenaciously—
Thus *"bold in daring brings death"*—
This is the killing of the human heart.

To preserve Principle requires yielding—
Without yielding, vulgar **qi** becomes violent,
And **Original Qi** falters—
Thus *"bold in restraint brings life"*—
This is the vitality of the **Original Spirit**.

Yet the **mechanism of death** and the **vitality of life** mutually aid each other.
The human heart is easy to kill;
The **Dao-heart** is easy to nurture—
Yet within lies the **Dao of Heaven**.

The **Heavenly Dao** bestows both punishment and virtue, life and death in common use.
Though some may grasp this, few comprehend how life’s potential lies within death’s grasp,
Or vitality’s hidden within the **mechanism of death**—
The world struggles to perceive such subtleties.

Who truly knows what Heaven abhors?
The Sage aligns with Heaven and Earth,
Understanding that what Heaven abhors must be what it loves—
For *"without abhorrence, there can be no true love."*
This cyclic interplay of wondrous function,
Even the Sage finds difficult to fathom.

Yet the Sage’s **Dao** is but Heaven’s **Dao**.
Heaven does not dispute right and wrong with the common folk,
But nurtures all things—
None escape its warmth and favor,
Nor stray against its will.

Heaven does not speak of **sensing and responding**,
Yet yin and yang alternate in motion—
None fail to harmonize in silent accord,
Nor stray against its will.

Truly, **Heaven and humanity share the same Dao**—
Motionless yet responsive,
Transformative in its divine mystery!
All things spring from the same source,
Boundless and impartial—
When things come, it responds;
How could actions be more precise?

The **Perfect One** with **unthinking, unworried sincerity**,
Silently wields **divine function** at the crossroads of life and death,
Stealthily seizes **Heaven’s mechanism** within the **Palace of Creation**,
Descending into the **Watery Realm**,
Shaping the **Golden Land**,
Following the **Hidden and the Strange**,
Severing sight and sound—
Death becomes life, life becomes death;
At first, none know how they intermingle and respond,
Like mother and child, husband and wife—
They come without summons, unite without scheme.

Is this not the swiftness of **sensing and responding**,
And the divine mystery?

Even calamities and blessings are bestowed or taken away,
Fortune and misfortune are fixed or corrupt—
*"The Heavenly Net is vast, wide yet not missing a thread,"*
Truly, none can escape or evade it.
For **emptiness and space are the Dao**,
And **Dao is Heaven**—
One cannot evade emptiness,
Nor escape the **Heavenly Net**.

If humanity does not defy the **Dao**, it does not defy Heaven—
Then how could Heaven cease to nourish them?

**Chapter Seventy-Four: The People Do Not Fear Death**
If the people do not fear death,
How can one frighten them with death?

If I made the people always fear death as something extraordinary,
I could seize and kill them—
Who would dare?

There are always those who execute punishment and kill.
To substitute for those who execute punishment and kill,
Is like substituting for the **Great Carpenter** to hew—
To substitute for the **Great Carpenter** to hew,
Few would not harm their own hands.

In ancient times, those who governed the world
Must have followed what easily moved the people’s emotions,
And preemptively guarded against it.
They did not follow the ruler’s joys or sorrows,
But only observed the people’s likes and dislikes,
Guiding them along their natural inclinations—
Thus their teachings succeeded without severity,
And their governance thrived without excessive measures.

If the people abandoned moral norms and violated propriety,
Daring to transgress the established order,
Yet held no fear of death,
Then even with the **Five Punishments** displayed on the **Torture Stakes**,
And proclaimed on auspicious days,
It would be in vain to establish them—
Of no use to the state or the people—
Would this not be a waste of effort?

Only by leveraging the people’s love of life and fear of death,
And daring to act deceitfully or maliciously,
Would I then follow and kill them—
This is what is meant by *"strike one to warn a hundred,"*
And *"inflict few punishments yet inspire great sincerity."*

Thus the people, from father to son, from elder to younger brother,
Would not dare to act as stepping stones to chaos—
They would fear self-destruction and embrace death.

Yet though the killing is done by those above,
The reason for killing depends on the person.
Only the **most virtuous** kill the **most unvirtuous**,
And the people themselves kill the unvirtuous without mercy,
Finding death sweet.

Mencius said: *"Only Heaven’s officers can be killed"*—
For **Heaven’s officers** are those who may kill others.

If one is not a **Heaven’s officer**,
Yet kills with violent retribution,
This is to govern chaos with chaos—
Not only does the people’s chaos worsen,
But to substitute for those who execute punishment and kill,
Is like a mere craftsman wielding the axe to shape the wind,
Or swinging the blade to carve wheels—
Could such a one master the divine artistry and achieve perfection?

Observing craftsmen and scholars of past and present,
If they cannot equal the **Great Carpenter**,
Yet substitute to hew,
Who has not harmed their own hands?

Alas, the people are unfortunate—
Not born in an age of **Dao**,
Thus bandits and thieves, deceitful and treacherous,
Have no restraint.
And even more unfortunate,
They do not encounter those who execute punishment—
Then where would they find guidance?
If the thin become even thinner,
And the thick also thin,
Is this not deeply lamentable?

「Thus, using the fear of death to illustrate the caution of solitude—
A person’s diligence in solitude is profound,
Then the distinction between Heaven and man becomes clear,
And the separation of desires and principles is manifest.
Though desires are few and faults not yet committed,
And one strives to avoid transgressions yet cannot—
In this, one is ever vigilant and earnest,
Dreading the slightest lapse,
Striving to suppress falsehood and deceit,
It is not difficult to purify desires and refine principles,
And instantly reveal the **original face of nature**.

If, in places unseen and unheard,
One lacks daily cultivation of moral restraint and self-nurturing,
Yet at moments when desires surge and emotions overwhelm,
One attempts to uproot evil and sudden sees **True Nature**,
Then it is certain that evil will not be uprooted but will multiply,
And the more one strives to cleanse the heart, the more it will be disturbed.

The **Supreme Sage** said:
*“If the people do not fear death, how can one frighten them with death?”*
Reason and circumstance are thus interdependent—
Only by guarding against temptation in seclusion,
Having known the wrongness of desires,
Can one restrain them in the moment,
And thus return to the **natural order of the mind**.

When one thought is swept away,
And one thought is purified,
Evil will not sprout anew from that moment.
This is why the people commonly fear death,
Yet those who act strangely—
If I seize them and execute them,
Who would dare to rebel or disrespect,
Or violate the **proper order of relationships**?

Indeed, the **Dao/Way** drives out the lack of **Dao/Way**,
Just as a ruler soothes and governs all subjects,
Commanding the common folk,
Using supreme mercy to kill those without mercy,
And great righteousness to punish the unrighteous—
Naturally, none who submit will remain secure,
Near ones will be pleased while distant ones will come,
And none will turn their weapons against their rulers,
Or strike back in rebellion,
To become enemies or cause harm.

Scholars who wish to discard falsehood and preserve sincerity,
To return to their roots and restore their origin,
Must suppress evil **gradually**,
And maintain constancy **steadfastly**,
So that when one opening is illuminated,
All openings will be **vital and responsive**,
And the **original spirit** will be at peace,
Obedient to its natural commands.
As it is said:
*“If a person can remain ever pure and clear,
Then all of Heaven and Earth will naturally return to them”*;
And again:
*“If a person can correct their spirit in one moment,
Then all evils will not dare to intrude.”*

This is akin to how those who execute punishment
Can kill without resentment,
And the condemned will meet their fate with acceptance—
The same **natural Dao/Way**,
A rare and wondrous effect.

**Chapter Seventy-Five: The Virtuous Excel in Not Valuing Life**
The people’s hunger arises from their rulers’ excessive taxation,
Thus they hunger.
The people’s difficulty in governance stems from their rulers’ meddling,
Thus they are difficult to govern.
The people’s indifference to death comes from their rulers’ obsession with prolonging life,
Thus they treat death lightly.

Only those who do not make life their pursuit
Are truly virtuous in excelling beyond valuing life.
From ancient times, the people are the foundation of the state,
And food is the **Heavenly necessity** for the people.
A state without people is a state without support—
Who will uphold it?
The people without food cannot survive—
How will they live?
Thus, for those who rule over others,
There must be those who open fields and cultivate land,
Deepening the **roots of abundance** before the people have need;
And those who establish rites and regulate changes,
Controlling the **flow of excess** after the people have eaten;
As well as those who reduce labor to fill shortages,
And curtail waste to aid insufficiency—
Then the people will naturally be well-fed and content,
Without the **cries of the starving** in the ninth month,
Or the **lamentations of famine**.

Even if droughts persist and famines recur,
Yet if the granaries are filled,
There will be no fear of calamity.

But in this world, rulers are often arrogant and licentious,
Their extravagance growing daily:
Some indulge in rare delicacies and curiosities for amusement,
Others build jade towers and palaces to gratify their desires—
Often the granaries are emptied,
Yet expenditures never decrease.
Even basic provisions are lacking,
And they impose additional levies:
First, they add taxes and increase tolls,
Yet still the people fear their **oppressive burdens**;
Next, they plunder and extort without restraint,
Ignoring even **Heaven’s command**.

Their love for music and beauty is paramount,
Their luxury and excess ever escalating.
They drain the **vital essence** of all the people,
To satiate their own **licentiousness**.
Even when the state’s treasury is exhausted,
Their demands for tribute never slacken.

Heaven and Earth produce wealth,
Only in these limited amounts.
If such **harsh extortion** continues without end,
And the people are taken without measure—
Even if there are frequent **abundant harvests**,
And years of **prosperity and blessing**,
How can one expect the people to not hunger?

In the age of **Zhuāng Lóng**,
Clothing was worn and food was eaten,
Houses were dwelt in and fields were tilled,
Each cherishing their own kin,
Each nurturing their own elders.
The **noble ones** had no need to inquire about rites and propriety,
Yet naturally dwelled in **Ren** and followed **Yì**;
The **common folk** had no fondness for loyalty and sincerity,
Yet naturally found joy in their work and peace in their homes.
Indeed, the rulers governed through **non-action**,
And the people transformed themselves through **non-action**.
Customs were not artificially made pure, yet they were pure,
And the **folkways** did not seek antiquity, yet they were ancient.
How harmonious and flourishing was that era!

But afterward, as **laws and regulations** multiplied,
And the **folkways** grew ever more **deviant and subtle**,
As **statutes and decrees** were frequently proclaimed,
And **bandits and thieves** became ever more rampant—
Not only under tyrannical rulers,
But even among **wise and enlightened kings**,
Who strove with vigor to govern,
Exerted their utmost in statecraft,
And whose policies grew ever more complex—
The more elaborate the laws, the more deceit arose,
The stricter the decrees, the more cunning flourished.
Is this not because **qi and techniques** cannot be reversed,
Or **Heaven’s will** cannot be altered?
Or because they **fail to grasp the roots and foundational principles**,
Yet seek only superficial results?

Not only does this fail to benefit the people’s livelihood,
But it **burdens the Way of the world**.

The people’s hearts are naturally at ease,
Yet rulers disturb them with **policies and commands**.
The people’s emotions are naturally without excess,
Yet rulers disrupt them with **rules and systems**.
Every additional **edict** from the court
Multiplies **deceit** among the common folk,
And every new **regulation**
Encourages **clever evasion** among them.

No wonder the **Way of the world** becomes ever more **perverse**,
And the people’s **moral inclinations** grow ever more **erratic**,
Making governance ever more difficult!

Only when the ruler sits **upright and still**,
And extends their **virtuous influence** without action,
Will the people below **naturally settle into order and obedience**.
The ruler and the people will be at peace in **Heaven’s non-action**—
Is this not joy indeed?

Moreover, those who **overvalue life** within
Will labor their hearts ever more,
And exhaust their **vital essence** ever further—
Their **spirit and qi** will dissipate,
And their **life and fate** will perish,
The more they cling to life, the faster they die.

Thus, the more one **obsesses over life**,
The more lightly one treats death.
Only those who do not **honor life**,
Nor **prioritize its preservation**,
Who dress and eat according to **natural circumstances**,
And whose expenditures **do not contend with the world’s resources**,
Will find their hearts at ease and their bodies secure,
Their **original nature** restored and their **fate prolonged**,
And **endless blessings** forever enjoyed.»

Nurture the **Great Harmony** within,
And invite **Heaven’s favor** upon yourself—
How much more virtuous than those who prize **artificial action**?
The **spirit** is the ruler, the **vital essence** the people.
Follow the **constant Dao** in its natural course,
With **spirit** as sovereign, and **vital essence** obeying its command.
Thus, when the **spirit** wanders, the **vital essence** leaks away.
The depletion of **vital essence** stems from the **spirit’s** flight—
Like the people’s hunger arising from excessive taxation by the ruler.
Though their forms differ, their principles are one.

The **heart** is the sovereign of the body;
When the **Heavenly Sovereign** is at ease,
All limbs follow its decree.
But if the **Heavenly Sovereign** is disturbed,
Then the **vital essence and qi** of the whole body are drained—
How much more so when the fields are laid waste!

Thus, **Immortal Adepts** possess the art of **returning to the origin**,
Taking **qi** as sovereign, while the **spirit** obeys its command—
Just as a ruler follows the people’s desires and yields to their sentiments,
So that **qi** is abundant, the **spirit** complete,
And the people at peace, the nation prosperous.
This is the way of **serving above by nourishing below**,
Of **abundance above** supplementing **deficiency below**—
To govern the world as one’s own affair,
Rather than making oneself the world’s burden.

Though the **Alchemical Dao** speaks of **action**,
It must first arise from **non-action**,
Then return to **non-action** once more—
Only then does one grasp the **original Heavenly spirit and qi**,
And ascend to **sainthood**, transcending the mundane.
If one clings entirely to **action**,
The **spirit** grows restless, and the **qi** weakens—
Inevitably, the more one refines, the more the **qi** scatters,
The more one labors, the more the **qi** disperses.
Only by guiding its momentum and acting in accordance with the times,
In governing oneself and the people alike,
Should one hold such a view.

If one fears wealth will fail and life will perish,
Then one exhausts **vital essence** and **spirit** in vain,
Hoping to preserve **postnatal life**,
Day and night in torment, restless in sleep—
Many a **spirit and qi** is thus consumed.
Yet **life and fate** are Heaven’s decree, not man’s to seek.
A ruler who fears for those who cling to life
Only hastens them toward death’s embrace.
Only by not valuing the **limited postnatal life**,
And instead preserving the **boundless prenatal fate**,
Can one safeguard **original nature** and **fate**,
And thus honor the body truly.

**Chapter LXXVI: The Soft and Weak Take Preeminence**
When a man is whole, he is soft and weak;
When he dies, he becomes rigid and strong.
Grasses and trees, when alive, are tender and pliable;
When dead, they wither and decay.
Thus, the rigid and strong are companions of death,
While the soft and weak are companions of life.
Hence, the strong in arms do not conquer,
And the rigid in wood bends and breaks.
