The strong and great must yield to the lowly,
While the soft and weak ascend above.

When man receives the **Yang Harmony qi**, he lives;
When he encounters the **Yin Cold qi**, he dies.
When the **Yang Harmony qi** gathers,
The four limbs grow supple, the body soft as silk,
And life’s vitality flows unceasing.
But when the **Yin Cold qi** congeals,
The flesh grows dry and parched, the skin withers and falls away—
And the **qi of death** approaches.

Observe the pot and steamer:
When steaming, the **Yang qi** rises in mist,
And all things dissolve and transform.
But in freezing cold, they harden and stiffen.
Observe the earth in spring and summer:
When **Yang qi** thrives, all things flourish and grow—
None fail to bloom and thrive.
But in autumn and winter, when **Yin qi** prevails,
All things wither and fall—
None escape decay.

Man and all things share a single source;
There is no division between them.
Thus, in all things under Heaven,
The rigid and strong are companions of death,
While the soft and weak are companions of life.

Take warfare as an example:
Often the strong are defeated, the weak victorious—
As when **Ziyou**’s overbearing strength led to ruin,
And **Bibi**’s yielding tactics triumphed at **Rand**.
Why is this?
Because the strong grow exhausted in their vigor,
While the weak harbor the seeds of revival—
Thus, they cannot prevail.

Observe the trees:
Though wood is strong, when **Yin qi** dominates and **Yang qi** wanes,
It is bound and broken—
No longer capable of growth.
The strong and great exhaust their **life qi**,
And invite the **qi of death**—
Truly the lowest of all things.
The soft and weak, however, see **Yin qi** dissipate and **Yang qi** flourish—
The highest of all things.

How can man not embrace weakness and reject strength,
Nor submit to lowliness while aspiring to preeminence?

The **Dao of Cultivation** most reveres the **Mysterious Pass**—
The **original qi** of Heaven, Earth, and all things.
This **qi** is both supremely soft and hard,
Supremely weak and strong,
Yet neither soft nor hard, neither weak nor strong can be seen.
In the dim and hazy void,
Suddenly, **Yin** contains **Yang**, and **killing** gives birth to **life**—
Seemingly present, yet absent;
Seemingly real, yet empty.
This is the **silent, odorless**,
The **Heavenly mechanism** of **original beginning**.
If man can **steal this primordial void-qi**,
Then the **original life-giving source** is obtained,
And **ascension to Heavenly Immortality** is no longer difficult.

Even if one steals the **original qi of the Mysterious Pass**
As the **precious elixir**,
Yet when gathering herbs in the **two critical seasons**,
One must still focus the **qi** to achieve softness—
Like an infant, whose bones are soft, body weak, yet grip is firm—
Only then can the **original qi** be secured as the **elixir’s foundation**.

In the **four seasons of alchemical fire-refining**,
One must know the body’s softness, like silk, most exquisite—
This is the **original prenatal vitality**, bursting with life,
The **harmonious and lively** symbol.
With this **Yang qi**, one can forge the **Immortal Elixir**.

Then, at the **retreat-and-seal stage**,
When returning to the **furnace** for final refinement,
One must still be **soft and dense**,
**Clear and lucid**, without slackness or neglect—
Like one drunk or mad, the **spirit** lazy in thought,
The tongue lazy in speech—
As the saying goes:
*"Under Heaven, spring clouds are as lazy as I,
Who knows I am even lazier than spring?"*

Only such **softness and weakness** preserve the **original Yang qi**,
Enduring without decay.
In summary, ten months in the womb,
Three years at the breast,
Nine years in seclusion—
All are the **original soft and weak qi**,
Which, when perfected, yields the **elixir** and **Immortal attainment**.

The **Cultivator** must seek this **soft and tender qi**,
Lest he burn and refine in vain,
Wasting **spirit and vital essence**.

**Chapter LXXVII: Act Without Relying**

The **Dao** of Heaven—is it not like drawing a bow?
The high, it presses down; the low, it lifts up.
The excessive, it diminishes; the insufficient, it supplements.
The **Dao** of Heaven diminishes the excessive to supplement the insufficient,
But the **Dao** of humankind does otherwise—it diminishes the insufficient to serve the excessive.
Who can serve the excessive to benefit the world?
Only the one who holds the **Dao**.

Thus, the Sage acts but does not rely,
Accomplishes but does not dwell—
Does this not mean he does not desire to be seen as virtuous?
The **Dao** of Heaven flows and unfolds,
Nurturing all things,
With naught but **yin** and **yang**,
Coming and going, alternating in motion,
Great, central, and most upright,
Without favor or bias.

Hence, **yin** at its peak gives rise to **yang**,
And **yang** at its peak gives rise to **yin**.
When **yin** is excessive and **yang** declines,
It suppresses **yin** and aids **yang**;
When **yang** is excessive and **yin** declines,
It suppresses **yang** and aids **yin**.
Ebb and flow, full and empty,
Move in harmony with time,
Thus all things are born and transformed,
Achieving effortless spontaneity,
A Heaven enduring ten thousand years without decay.
How different is this from the archer’s art?
Grasp the bow with steady resolve,
The inner will upright, the outer form aligned,
Ensure the front and back arms,
Balanced and smooth—
The high, it presses down; the low, it lifts up.
The excessive, it diminishes; the insufficient, it supplements.
Then, in accord with the hand, it releases,
Following the mark and striking true,
Without fear of missing.

Yet it does not compare to the **Dao** of Heaven—
Taking the people’s **fat and marrow**,
Filling its own **sacks of greed**,
Often diminishing the insufficient to serve the excessive.
Who can take the **excess of the sovereign**,
And serve the **insufficient of the world**?
Only the **Dao**-holding Sage,
Who follows the **Dao** of Heaven and aligns with human nature,
Diminishes what is excessive, supplements what is insufficient,
So that the common people do not sigh in resentment.
Though acting for oneself,
It is still in accordance with the **Dao** of Heaven—
Never boasting of what is done;
Though achievements come from sincerity,
It is but the utmost sincerity fulfilling one’s nature—
Never claiming credit for them.

Such a Sage,
Nearly in harmony with Heaven’s effortless transformation,
Not bound by natural course,
Not desiring to show traces of action.
The **power** of nurturing things,
Brilliant and evident to all,
Is it not because the virtuous do not wish to be seen as virtuous?
This is why Heaven has no limits,
And only the Sage unites Heaven and Earth.

At the beginning of life,
One is purely **yin** or purely **yang**,
Most balanced and upright,
Without victory or defeat, without disparity.
Thus, the sun advances by day, the moon by night,
With tender bones and weak body yet flourishing.
But once life emerges,
Fire dwells above, water below,
Water and fire not mingling—
Hence, **yin** is always excessive,
And **yang** always insufficient.
As **yang** water is scorched by **yin** fire,
Human desires grow ever more,
Vulgar **qi** ever more fervent,
While Heaven’s **true heart** is daily obscured,
And **true qi** gradually fades,
The **mechanism of life** left without trace.

If the excessive **yin** fire were to descend,
Supplementing the insufficient **yang** water;
And once the **yang** water’s insufficiency is mended,
Still restraining the excessive **yin** fire—
Like the archer’s art,
Diminishing the excessive, supplementing the insufficient,
Then **yin** and **yang** are balanced.
This is all through water’s and fire’s own movement,
**Yin** and **yang**’s own interplay,
And Heaven itself does not know who does this.

The **Dao** of humankind is burdened by action,
The **Dao** of Heaven is honored by non-action.
What else is cultivation but this?
To restore the **post-heaven yin and yang** to the **pre-heaven yin and yang**,
Flowing without ceasing,
Achieving effortless spontaneity.

**Chapter LXXVIII: Receive the Filth of the Realm**
Under Heaven, none are as soft and weak as water,
Yet none can overcome the firm and strong as water does.
There is nothing to replace it.

Thus, the soft overcomes the strong,
The weak overcomes the rigid—
All know this, yet few can practice it.
Hence, the Sage says:
*"To receive the filth of the realm,
Is to be its lord of soil and grain.
To bear the realm’s misfortunes,
Is to be its king under Heaven."*

Words that seem reversed.
In the **Supreme Chapter**, it was said that the soft and weak are the followers of life,
The firm and strong are the followers of death—
Thus, the soft and weak occupy the high,
The firm and strong occupy the low,
And it can be known that the utmost softness is the utmost rigidity,
The utmost weakness is the utmost strength.

Daily, nightly, in all things,
The soft and weak are beneficial to all yet do not contend,
Ever in the humble low yet do not weary.
Though but a drop’s worth,
People may scorn it.
Though but a ladle’s measure,
People may slight it.
Yet when it accumulates into the deep,
Gathers into the sea,
Then vast and boundless,
It can bear boats yet also overturn them;
It can nurture things yet also destroy them.
Not only does it overcome all under Heaven,
Even those skilled at attacking the firm and strong—
No firmness remains unbroken,
No strength remains unconquered—
Yet none can stand against it.

Thus, it is known:
The utmost softness under Heaven,
Can govern the utmost rigidity under Heaven;
The utmost weakness under Heaven,
Can repel the utmost strength under Heaven.
Water, water!
How soft and weak it is,
Yet how strong and firm it becomes!

Moreover, under Heaven, there is no task easier than attacking water,
Yet the firm and strong are ultimately unable to prevail.
Why do people not embrace softness while also embracing rigidity,
Not adopt weakness while also adopting strength—
Everywhere it is seen?
Do they not know that softness overcomes rigidity,
That weakness overcomes strength?

It is because Heaven’s **true heart** moves,
Yet none know it,
And once moved,
It is instantly stained by desires,
Deflected by customs—
Thus, they bristle in arrogance,
Unwilling to rest in softness and weakness.
Hence, as cleverness grows,
**Virtue and principle** are lost;
As preferences become fixed,
**True nature** is extinguished.
So that **Dao-heart** departs,
Human desires arise;
**Guest-qi** flourishes,
**True-qi** diminishes.
Life’s **mechanism** gone,
Life’s **mechanism** extinguished—
To make life flourish without end is difficult indeed.

The Sage says, *"To receive the filth of the realm,
Is to be its lord of soil and grain,"*
As **Emperor Tang** of **Shang** spoke:
*"If my person has guilt,
It does not affect the myriad lands;
If the myriad lands have guilt,
The guilt lies upon my person."*

Stepping back is stepping forward—
Thus receiving the Mandate of Heaven from the boundless.

*"To bear the realm’s misfortunes,
Is to become the sovereign of all under Heaven."*
As King Wu declared:
*"I received this victory—
Not because my father, King Wen, had guilt,
But because I, his unworthy son, lacked virtue."*

What follows is what precedes—
Thus bearing Heaven’s blessings without fail.

How could it compare to later ages’
Sleeping on wood, tasting gall,
Enduring filth and accepting defilement?
Such efforts are mere speculation!

This is the eternal, unchanging principle,
The eternal, ungrounded scripture—
The true words for all under Heaven.
Yet the Sage seeks only within himself,
Never demanding such of others.

Water is the metaphor:
When one *yang* first stirs, true essence begins to form.
Its mechanism is most frail,
Its force most yielding.
Yet gradually it gathers, condenses,
Day by day, month by month,
Until it becomes the vast *qi*,
Most great, most strong—
Filling the two poles,
Encompassing all things,
And nothing is firm it cannot penetrate,
Nothing strong it cannot shatter.

*The Awakening to Reality* says:
*"The White Tiger’s Head Scripture is supreme treasure,
The Hua Pool Divine Water Scripture is true scripture.
The highest goodness is like water—
Its source profound, unlike common medicines."*

Though *qi* is soft and weak,
Like water:
Yet in its softness lies inherent strength,
In its weakness lies ultimate power—
It bears the weight of heaven and earth,
Holds up the sun and moon,
Summons wind and rain,
Drives thunder and lightning.
This is the firmest strength under Heaven.

Though called "vast *qi*,"
Its essence is true essence.
It must be nourished with the utmost softness and weakness,
And with *wuwei* as action,
With non-doing as doing—
Only then may one attain it.

When it says,
*"To bear the realm’s filth,
Is to become the lord of soil and grain;
To bear the realm’s misfortunes,
Is to become the sovereign of all under Heaven,"*
It means:
The ancients turned inward to blame themselves,
Saying, *"Truly, I am without virtue—
What guilt could the people have?"*

The scholar who seeks virtue in others—
Why not first cultivate within oneself?

**Chapter Seventy-Nine**
*Constantly do good to others,*
*And great grudges will have lingering grudges—*
How can this be called good?

Thus the Sage holds the left contract,
Yet does not blame others.
Therefore, the virtuous govern contracts,
The unvirtuous govern disputes.

Heaven’s Way has no favorites—
It always sides with the good.
The Way of cultivating oneself:
Only goodness is the treasure.
The Way of doing good:
Self-governance comes first.

For the Way lies within, not without;
Cultivation is in oneself, not in others.
Only by observing inwardly at all times,
Reflecting inwardly at all moments,
Correcting faults when they arise,
And striving harder in goodness—
May one truly manifest goodness in body and mind,
And forever be the perfect child of Heaven and Earth,
The complete Sage,
Without any deficiency.

It is clear that doing good
Only requires self-examination,
Not others’ compliance.
If one blames others but not oneself,
Observes outward but not inward,
Even if one resolves a momentary anger,
And though grudges may be dispersed,
They will not become enemies—
Yet without inward self-reproach,
Without restraining anger and desire,
Though one may resolve it outwardly,
One cannot release it in the hidden depths.
How can one then be pure and unstained,
Free and joyful,
And return to the original, naturally good nature of Heaven and Earth?

Thus, reconciling great grudges will leave lingering grudges—
How can this be called good?

Only the Sage, in conduct and interaction,
Prioritizes self-cultivation,
And does not weigh others’ praise or blame, likes or dislikes.
Like a contract split in two, each party holding one half,
Forever as evidence—
Clear that there is no deceit from me,
No deceit from you.

The Sage holds virtue as he holds the left contract—
Only cultivating within, never blaming others.
This is why he aligns with Heaven and Earth in greatness.

This is called *"the virtuous govern contracts."*
The unvirtuous, however,
Value the external over the internal,
And take scrutiny as clarity,
While weighing others’ grievances and grudges—
This is *"the unvirtuous govern disputes."*

Those who *"govern disputes"*:
Take examining others as their task,
Never reflecting on themselves,
Yet the people do not follow.
How can they compare to those who govern contracts,
Who weigh their own faults heavily,
And others’ lightly—
Yet all trust them in sincerity!

It is clear:
Those who blame others lighten their own burden—
Their goodness cannot be complete.
Those who blame themselves lighten others’ burden—
Their goodness is restored.

When people turn to goodness,
Heaven’s heart favors them—
For countless eons, without fail.

*The Book of Documents* says:
*"Heaven has no favorites—
It aids only virtue."*
Is this not the same as *"The Supreme Way has no favorites—
It always sides with the good"*?

The Sage’s learning:
Only lies in purifying the heart,
Retreating and hiding in secrecy.
For outward goodness or evil,
Praise or blame, compliance or defiance—
All are disregarded.

Thus, filtering thoughts, deepening reflection,
Concentrating spirit, silent illumination—
Until the mind is clear, nature revealed,
Desires purified, principles refined—
Above, aligning with Heaven in virtue,
Enduring through all ages without erosion.

Its practice begins with guarding the center,
Its completion lies in fetal breathing.
Do you understand this inwardly?

The ancients spoke of fetal breathing—
Not to be taken as external breath or *qi*.
When breath is suspended,
The true *yin* and true *yang* in the *dantian*,
The original spirit and original *qi*,
Merge as one, blending into a single *qi*—
Misty and vast, thriving and vigorous,
Opening and closing, appearing and vanishing.
Visible yet unseen, heard yet unheard—
Imagined yet leaving traces,
Faint yet taking form—
This is perhaps the original breath of life.

The heart attains it and has substance,
Nature attains it and has use.
Without this *qi*, life cannot exist.
To become a high-grade immortal,
One cannot do without this *qi* as master.

The ancients said:
*"The beginning of life—
Because of principle, there is *qi*;
Because of *qi*, there is form.
This is the natural order of Heaven and Earth in giving birth to man."*

The practitioner of reverse cultivation indeed slowly stirs the *yang* fire and *yin* talisman from the myriad forms and colors, drawing them back into the Five Bright Palaces. With the primordial *Taiyi* ancestral *qi* and the natural divine fire, one refines them—thus transforming the form into a single *qi*. Further refining this *qi* allows it to condense into spirit.

If one steadfastly guards the void, nurturing the numinous *yang*, without returning to the origin of the *Wuji*, one may then manifest form when emerging and leave no trace when retreating. What difference is there between this and ordinary humanity?

In essence, this prenatal breath is the return to the moment when one’s parents’ mingled *qi* and blood first coalesced. If one cultivates this prenatal breath, day and night with the true intent of making the nonexistent into the existent, the formless into the formed, and the thoughtless into the thoughtful, preserving and gathering it, it will then form a spiritual embryo as the original spirit.

When the ten months have passed and the form is complete, it sheds its shell and ascends through the crown, piercing the heavens, soaring among the *Luanhe* and *Xinghan*, riding the *luan* and *he* cranes, traversing the celestial realms, and ascending to the Jade Capital—all in the blink of an eye.

Yet this prenatal breath, though refined from the mortal’s colored form, is not the ordinary essence, *qi*, or spirit congealed. Though the alchemist cannot do without the postnatal, formed, and colored essence and *qi* as their foundation, they do not rely on them entirely. For postnatal essence and *qi* are all material and substantial, bound by fate—subject to birth and death, the wheel of rebirth inevitable. Moreover, coarse essence and coarse *qi* are all dull and sluggish things—how could they be numinous?

Ultimately, one borrows the crude matter within the mortal’s colored form, refining it through countless forging and purifying processes, extracting that spotless, pure, and supremely numinous essence, *qi*, and spirit to become the true *Yi* breath, returning it to oneself to form the immortal embryo and spiritual elixir. The saying of "extracting lead and adding mercury" is but this.

All else that clings to form and color is not the true Dao.

As the ancients said:
*"The embryo forms from the latent breath,
The breath rests within the embryo."*

Thus, to forge the spiritual elixir and achieve an ageless body, one cannot do without nurturing the prenatal breath. To gather the prenatal breath in the void within the *dantian*, one must first form the embryo—otherwise, it will not return.

If one remains purely still and active in harmony, with not a trace of mundane *qi* mingling or the mundane spirit straying outward, then the spirit will not scatter, and the *qi* will not dissipate—it will not disperse like a child from its mother or a husband from his wife. Do you understand?

---
**Chapter 80: The Small State with Few People**

A small state with few people—
Let there be ten or a hundred households’ worth of tools, yet unused.
Let the people hold death in reverence and not wander far.
Though they have boats and carriages, they do not ride them.
Though they have armor and weapons, they do not display them.
Let the people return to using knotted cords for record-keeping.
Let their food be sweet, their clothing fine, their dwellings secure, their customs joyous.
Neighboring states are visible, the sounds of chickens and dogs heard—
Yet the people, from birth to death, do not visit one another.

A small state with few people, remote and sparse—
To become a flourishing, grand realm,
And cultivate the refined customs of antiquity,
Seems indeed difficult.

Yet if one reduces wasteful expenditures,
Trims superfluous formalities,
And lets the tools of ten or a hundred households lie unused,
Then waste diminishes while goods increase—
The wealth of the state can thus be achieved.

Moreover, if one does not indulge desires lightly nor court death through private schemes,
If one does not flee to distant lands or dwell in foreign realms,
But clings only to parents and cherishes siblings,
Holding death in reverence and not wandering far—
Then an era of tranquility, harmony, and kinship can be attained.

Thus, *"Charming is our sovereign, cherishing this land,"*
Though they have boats and carriages,
They do not venture far to strange lands,
Leaving their ancestral homeland.

When the court’s benevolence penetrates deeply,
Soaking into the marrow and flesh,
With affection like that of parents and siblings,
Then even rebellious and stubborn folk
Transform into the virtuous.
Though they have armor and weapons,
They have no need to display them.

Thus, with the ruler serene above and the people joyous below,
The people at ease and the state prosperous—
If one restores the governance of knotted cords,
Enjoys the harmony of the *Taihe*,
Honors kin and elders,
Dwells in proper abodes and tends proper fields,
Finds delight in food and clothing,
And takes joy in settled customs,
With sincerity fostering tradition—
What fear is there that the state is small or the people few,
That it cannot achieve grandeur and abundance,
Or that benevolence cannot prevail?

When the people love their ruler as parents,
And the ruler views them as children,
With loyal hearts and deep affection—
Even a moment’s separation is unbearable.

Though neighboring states are near,
Visible at a glance and their chickens and dogs heard within earshot,
The people, from youth to old age, from birth to death,
Do not once visit one another.

This is because the people, moved by gratitude and transformed by virtue,
Are deeply bound to their ruler.
Thus, they rest in the governance of *Wuwei*,
Enjoy the Dao’s celestial harmony,
And do not dare to stray even a step.

In this way, how could the state fear being small,
Or the people fear being few?

Necessarily, their influence spreads to the four quarters,
Their reputation carried far—
And the world submits to virtue,
All nations aligning in unity.

This is the method for those aged and weakened by declining essence.

When a person grows old,
Their essence and *qi* scatter,
Lead and mercury diminish—
To cultivate the Great Elixir’s Dao seems indeed difficult.

Yet the matter of the Golden Elixir
Is not of postnatal essence and *qi*,
But of the primordial lead and mercury.
If one attains the supreme unity’s method,
Extracting and taking it,
Ingesting and consuming it—
Whether old or young,
One may obtain the medicine in an instant,
Achieving success in a thousand years or three months.

The only misfortune is failing to hear of the primordial *Yi* breath,
And instead relying solely on the postnatal, formed essence—
Not only will the aged achieve nothing,
But even the young and vigorous
Will ultimately gain nothing.

The very first step must be endured with effort—
keeping the hands from straying,
the feet from moving lightly,
the eyes from gazing outward,
the ears from listening to extraneous sounds,
the mouth from speaking,
the mind from idle thoughts—
from dawn till dusk,
cleansing the mind of anxieties,
nurturing the inner while restraining the outer,
retiring into seclusion,
so that not a single thread entangles,
nor the slightest burden lingers.
With time, sincerity and clarity arise naturally—
the gaze turns inward, concentrated;
the tongue’s essence is held within;
the mind remains pure and preserved;
the limbs relax effortlessly,
and every movement aligns with the Dao.

This is the meaning of *"a hundred-bushel vessel unused"*—
then, when used, it lacks nothing.
*"The people"* are likened to the body.

As one grows old,
who does not fear death with utmost dread,
or cherish life with profound longing?
Yet to fear death without seeking life
is merely futile dread.

Only through careful solitude,
constant inward observation,
and ceaseless self-reflection—
never straying from the mind’s core—
will one, over time, attain harmony within.
Then even the heavens and earth may be ordered,
and all things nurtured.
How much more so for what lies within oneself,
yet remains unordered and uncultivated?

This is the way of establishing the Mysterious Transformation,
nurturing the Spirit of the Valley:
enduring like a thread unbroken,
using it without strain;
ever-present like the waking mind,
guarding it without failure;
still yet ever luminous,
luminous yet ever still—
*(that is, ever responsive yet ever tranquil,
without force or artifice).*

This is what is meant by
*"moving yet unrestrained, still yet perfectly nourished."*

It does not involve the alchemical furnace’s revolutions,
nor the celestial stem-and-branch’s shifts.
Nor does it require
suppressing chaos with martial might—
though warfare may befit the field,
defense suits the city,
and ritual purity is paramount—
taking analogy from the alchemist’s fire,
the strategist’s victory over foes.

This is the method of pure cultivation,
not the craft of yin-yang supplementation.
Not only can the aged, through practice,
obtain the elixir and return to youth,
but even the young,
if they adhere to this discipline,
may persist unceasingly, without division or pause,
harmonizing with the serene and reverent Heaven.

Yet hasty progress yields no swift results,
and impatience begets no great attainment.
Only through slow, leisurely cultivation—
like water’s gradual seepage,
fire’s steady permeation—
will virtue mature and benevolence ripen,
and the Dao be fulfilled.

Thus:
*"Though there be chariots and armor,
there is no use for them;
Though there be arms and troops,
there is no place to deploy them."*

The movements of advance and retreat, ascent and descent—
the methods of morning *tūn* and evening *méng*—
the Supreme Sage has already spoken of them:
*"War is the last resort; it will surely bring years of calamity."*

This shows that the alchemical practice of refining at the furnace,
gathering elixir through fire,
is a method established for those whose post-natal *qi*
is bound and obstructed by material entanglements—
if one does not follow this path,
then common *qi* cannot transform,
and true gold cannot be restored.

If one can make stillness and nourishment their practice,
without resorting to the arts of boiling and frying,
merely abiding within the void and the tranquil,
then unknowingly and unawares,
without action or thought,
one naturally becomes whole and undivided,
pure and upright,
silently harmonizing with Heaven,
not awaiting words or deliberation,
yet flowing seamlessly with Heaven and Earth.

This is the method of *"making the people return to using knotted cords while governing them."*
No reliance on script or spoken exposition,
yet *"the Sage above does not need calculations or plans."*
*"Let them enjoy their food and adorn their clothing,"*
meaning their essence permeates within,
their *qi* circulates without.
Sweetness within and beauty without—
there is that which cannot be named or spoken of.

*"Let them dwell in peace, delight in their customs,"*
then their hearts are settled in virtue and benevolence,
and wherever they go,
all is fitting.

When cultivation reaches this stage,
clarity is ever-present,
suchness is effortless,
one can meet all things with no mind—
how could they be stained by external objects?
Even when there are sights and sounds,
they are as if unseen and unheard,
never stirred by color or sound.

Thus it is said:
*"Neighboring lands may gaze upon each other,
yet they do not visit or communicate."*

This is the supreme and highest attainment,
the lowest and lowest is nonexistent—
the profoundest of profundities,
the most wondrous of wonders.
Ah!

When cultivation reaches this stage, one finds great harmony with the Dao.

Speaking of Daoist practice, there have been two ancient methods: those who cultivate through purity, and those who cultivate through yin-yang supplementation.

Cultivation through purity means refining the void and unity—no need to refine essence or *qi*. Yet this is not for the ordinary practitioner; only those of the highest root and capacity can speak of it. If one’s innate nature is truly extraordinary, proceeding from this step onward follows the natural Way of Heaven and Earth, unlike the teachings of my master today, which still involve contrivance.

Within the human body, there exist the mechanisms of yin-yang (坎 *kǎn* and 离 *lí*), the opening and closing of Heaven and Earth (乾 *qián* and 坤 *kūn*), the movements of sun and moon, water and fire, ascent and descent, advance and retreat—just as the heavens move in their courses, all naturally, without need of artificial manipulation. One need only rectify the original spirit, letting it act without thought or deliberation, without striving or planning. Then, the pure and empty *qi* flows boundlessly, naturally inhaling and exhaling, rising and falling like the opening and closing of Heaven and Earth, the coming and going of sun and moon, the ascent of water and descent of fire, the ebb and flow of yin and yang—advancing and retreating thusly.

Though there are stages of fire and heat, they amount to nothing more than calming the mind, reducing desires, and maintaining inner stillness, allowing true *qi* to circulate without ceasing. Though there are movements of ascent and descent, they are merely the constant rising of true water and the constant descending of true fire. Even in the practice of Daoist bathing, it is only a matter of restraining anger, suppressing desires, purifying thoughts, and washing the heart, so that the Great Harmony may be embraced.

Though there are elixirs and pills, they are but this: taking spirit as father and *qi* as mother, the two entwined into one, merging without separation, giving birth to that primordial point of true soul—what is called the true essence of the Palace of Separation (離宮), or the true mercury of the human body. By refining one’s spirit-*qi* into this true mercury, a fetus is formed, and later, a sun-body emerges—organs, bones, blood vessels, the five viscera and six bowels, hair, flesh, and all awareness, none of which differ from the human form. Divided, it can manifest as myriad bodies; united, it returns to one *qi*. All stems from the union of spirit-father and *qi*-mother, their fusion producing this true mercury’s essence, which becomes the sun-body.

Yet this true mercury requires the condition of birth. The heart governs the *qi* of the five viscera; when the central *qi* ascends, the *qi* of the five viscera ascends with it; when the central *qi* descends, the *qi* of the five viscera descends with it. Its motion arises from the movement of true mercury; its stillness arises from its repose. In short, motion and stillness, ascent and descent—all belong to the natural Way, and one must only follow its natural operation.

Yet few ancient immortals began their practice from this step. What is meant by yin-yang supplementation?

First, one must recognize the foundation of the Supreme Ultimate (太極), the emergence of primordial yang, and then invest this point of true yang-*qi* into the dantian, just as parents unite—precision and blood merging into one within the womb. This is the primordial true seed, planted in the Palace of Mutual Sensation (交感宮), where lead and mercury are daily refined, gradually growing until, one day, it emerges from the shell, and the immortal of the naked embryo is born. Without this true seed, one’s practice is empty. Though there may be gains, they will only preserve the physical form and cannot produce the transcendent body.

Do you understand? With this point of true yang-*qi* planted in the womb, one may then apply the illumination of spirit-light, and over time, the true yang will stir. It is permissible to draw the water of *kǎn* upward and guide the fire of *lí* downward, refining all the turbid yin-dregs and corpse-*qi* of the physical body, extracting only the pure *qi*, blending it with the soul-yang, and forming it into an elixir to nourish the spirit—thus achieving flight and transformation. Yet even this is the natural Way.

Ordinary people, having fallen into the post-heavenly realm, have depleted their spirit and *qi*, and their years have aged them. Like travelers far from home, they must return from afar, and thus must exert effort.

Since spirit and *qi* are divided and not yet unified, during seated practice, one must use a portion of intention and *qi* to gather them both into the dantian, ensuring not a single thread escapes outward, not a breath leaves or enters. One need only follow the rhythm of inhalation and exhalation—from one to ten, from ten to a hundred, from a hundred to a thousand, to ten thousand—without constraint. Then, having tightly sealed the great gate, one stores the spirit at the dantian’s bolt, practicing thus for a while, before halting external breath and allowing true breath to move. One then nourishes it again, and soon the true yang-*qi* surges forth like a gale or billowing clouds. Quickly, one opens the pass and guides it upward.

In its ascent, one relies on spirit, not *qi*—only concentrating the spirit at the tailbone’s end is sufficient. When the true *qi* rushes forth, one nourishes it for a moment before descending. In summary, when true yang first stirs, one must exert some effort before ascent or descent becomes possible. For the vulgar body’s turbid *qi* is too heavy, and it must be vigorously agitated to dispel its filth, revealing the pure and clear spirit-*qi* that will serve as the foundation for the elixir. Hence the ancients said: At first, one gathers the medicine, but without fierce, martial fire and urgent refining, true gold cannot be extracted from the ore—this is why martial fire is called "wild battle."

Once ascent and descent are complete, when the dantian is full and the heart and spirit are at ease, one may then employ the method of refining the void, following the *qi*’s natural movements to complete the work.

「This is indeed a strenuous effort, yet it is the natural way of things. One must follow this path in practice.

**Chapter Eighty-One: Acting Without Striving**
*"True words are not beautiful; beautiful words are not true."*
*"The virtuous do not argue; those who argue are not virtuous."*
*"The wise are not learned; the learned are not wise."*
*"The sage does not hoard.*

When one acts for others, the more one gives, the more one possesses.
When one gives to others, the more one shares, the more one’s virtue grows.
The Dao of Heaven benefits without harming;
The Dao of the sage acts without striving.

This chapter concludes the entire text, showing that all sayings of the *Dàodé Jīng* are sincere and without deceit. One must not dismiss this scripture as unpolished in language and shelve it without discussion or reflection.

Know that the Dao has no name—it is only called *Dao* by force.
The Dao has no words—all words are obstructions.
Yet, to teach and transform beings, one must establish provisional names,
as a bridge for later learners.

Once words are spoken, they must come from the heart,
and their expression must be measured and true.
This is indeed a profound and genuine discourse,
harmonizing with the ancient yet never erroneous,
conforming to the present yet universally applicable.
Without embellishment or falsehood,
it requires no ornate rhetoric—what need for excessive complexity?
Simple transmission and direct pointing reveal the very nature of *xìngtiān* [性天, inherent nature of Heaven].

True words need not be beautiful.
If one speaks of ornate literature and elegant prose,
that is the speech of scholars and literati,
who adorn themselves with superficial brilliance to please the world—
such words are not to be trusted.

If their words are true yet virtuous, and they do not seek to deceive through contrivance,
what need for argumentation?
Those who argue are like the deaf and blind,
pretending to hear and see to fit in with others,
covering their ears while stealing the bell—
such behavior is not virtuous.

Virtue lies within oneself; wisdom lies within one’s heart—
why must it be excessive?
Confucius said, *"My Way is one, and it runs through all things."*
Mencius said, *"The Way is one and only one."*
What need for vast learning?

Those who seek vastness are like those who do not grasp the essence of the Dao,
whose words lack proper foundation.
They wander through the teachings of a hundred schools,
mastering only the recitation of phrases and literary flourishes—
this is not the sage’s learning, which is profound and guided by the utmost harmony and rectitude.

In truth, the Dao is such that nothing exists outside of it.
It can be taken without restriction,
used without exhaustion.

The sage is empty yet not empty,
has yet not has.
Like a clear mirror suspended high,
reflecting all without storing any—
how can there be accumulation?

If there were accumulation,
then the mirror would bear the stains of dust,
the water would be tainted by sand—
this is not the empty, luminous essence of the sage,
which is vast and impartial.

Only when the sage’s nature is thus empty and luminous,
does he respond to circumstances and follow the *yuan* [缘, conditions],
though the myriad people may be in chaos,
virtue cannot be universally applied—
yet even one person wronged will be held as a burden of guilt,
and his heart will always ache.

If the entire realm remains untouched by benevolence,
his heart will bear constant pain.
In giving to others, what need for excessive restraint?
Thus it is said:
When one acts for others, his own merit increases;
When one gives to others, his own virtue grows.

It is like the light of a mirror—
when objects come, they are reflected;
when objects depart, the reflection ceases—
there is no premeditation in between.

The sage’s heart is thus:
The sage’s heart is Heaven’s heart;
The sage’s Dao is Heaven’s Dao.

Heaven acts through silent transformation,
though there may be waxing and waning, fullness and emptiness—
all belong to the mechanism of nurturing life,
benefiting without harming.

The sage creates through effortless spontaneity,
though there may be loss and gain, granting and taking—
all belong to the response of benevolence,
also acting without striving.

If Heaven and Earth were to benefit or harm,
then Heaven and Earth would also be partial and not impartial—
how could they endure for ten thousand years unchanged?

If the sage were to act with striving or contention,
then the sage would also hoard without dispersing—
how could his utmost sincerity persist without end?

Alas!
Heaven and Earth are vast;
The sage is vast.
Though there are true words,
they arise from the heart’s intent—
no borrowing, no pretense.

Based on self and verified by the common people,
it is the innate virtue of Heaven,
the shared essence of human hearts.

To act for others is to act for oneself;
To give to others is to give to oneself.
This is the unity of self and other,
the oneness of Heaven and humanity—
here lies the sage’s harmony with Heaven, revealed in his breadth.

This commentary concludes.
The teacher then calls his disciples and tells them:
*"The Great Dao today is as precarious as a pile of eggs.
It depends on you, those who study the Dao,
to uphold Heaven and Earth,
and rectify the balance of *qiánkūn* [乾坤, the cosmos].

Even if you speak of wicked and rebellious men,
there may be conflict with arms—
yet Heaven has its arrangements,
and it will never leave you with unforeseen calamity.

Only fear that you may hold virtue too narrowly,
trust the Dao too lightly,
waver in resolve—
sometimes acting, sometimes abandoning it—
thus severing your connection with Heaven.

Unable to align above with Heaven,
even if Heaven has tenfold benevolence,
desiring to nurture you in a realm of rest and tranquility,
how can you receive Heaven’s grace if you cannot embrace it?

You have now seen the Dao clearly,
grasped its essence with strength—
your own foundation is firm.
Only in each word and action,
breath by breath, must you remain in harmony with Heaven—
then Heaven will cherish you,
protect you, and never forsake you.

For the Dao is Heaven, and to honor the Dao is to honor Heaven;
To love the Dao is to love Heaven.

Such silent accord and profound trust
will naturally lead to auspicious blessings.
Heaven’s Dao is originally one with humanity’s Dao—
examine the ancients and the moderns:
There have been none who defied the Dao and escaped Heaven’s disdain,
nor any who followed the Dao and failed to receive Heaven’s favor.
You may now awaken to this truth.

In summary, each must follow what is right,
exhaust what is sincere—
as for external judgments of right and wrong, misfortune and blessing,
all are governed by Heaven’s will.
You must not fret over taking another’s place—
leave such concerns to Heaven."*

The essence of the Great Dao lies solely in the two aspects of **shen** (神) and **qi** (氣)—
yet there is a distinction between **pre-Heaven** (先天) and **post-Heaven** (後天).
Cultivators must never fail to grasp this.

The ancient scriptures state:
Pre-Heaven **yuan shen** (元神), the body;
Post-Heaven **shi shen** (識神), the function.
Without pre-Heaven **yuan shen**, the Great Dao has no master;
Without post-Heaven **shi shen**, the Great Dao has no use.

You must diligently refine your practice—
when in the **hunhun dun dun** (混混沌沌, profound chaos),
the state of **wuzhi wujue** (無知無覺, no awareness),
nurture the pre-Heaven **yuan shen** to rule as sovereign.
Then, with a sudden start and awakening,
the post-Heaven **shi shen** emerges.

This **shi shen** is not the fleeting, wandering thoughts of your ordinary mind,
but the **zhengdeng zhengjue** (正等正覺, true and pure awakened spirit),
which, when activated, brings forth knowledge—
thus it is called **shi shen**.

Yet once this **shi shen** arises,
it may swirl with chaotic, deluded thoughts,
endless distractions and desires—
and you shall fall into private streams and sensual cravings,
unable to forge the Elixir.

Only when the mind is **yīxīn liǎozhào** (一心了照, singularly illuminated)
and steadfastly resolves to nothing else—
when the will is unscattered,
and the spirit is condensed—
then breath may be regulated,
and the Elixir will form.

Hence it is said:
*"The mind fixed solely on the silken thread,
no sight of white reeds or red banks beyond.*
Even though called **shi shen**,
its essence is the pre-Heaven **yuan shen**."

Thus the ancients proclaimed:
*"Heaven’s heart is the master, the **yuan shen** the function.
Skillfully employ the thief’s trick,
and return to the workings of Creation."*
What need then to fear failing to ascend to the sacred?

You must also understand this, surely.
When Heaven’s heart stirs,
constantly stabilize and contain it—
no wandering thought,
no breath of impurity—
so that Heaven’s heart remains,
and the Dao-heart is ever condensed.
Even with **shi shen**, it is as though without.

For those who seek **true cultivation** (修真),
at the very outset,
the precious task is to subdue the **mang zhì** (妄識, deluded perceptions)
of the two eyes, ears, mouth, body, and mind.
Then gather the spirit at the **Dan xióng** (丹扃, Elixir Gate),
regulate the breath at the **Dan tián** (丹田, Elixir Field),
and ensure that all ordinary breaths are extinguished—
only then does the **yuan qì** (元氣, Original Qi) begin to return in submission.

Once the **yuan qì** has gathered,
**yīnyīn huópó** (氤氳活泼, vibrant and lively),
**wǎnzhuǎn yōuyáng** (宛转悠扬, gently undulating),
like a living dragon in motion,
fully invigorated—
this **yuan qì**’s vigor
can drive the **Hé chē** (河车, River Vehicle).

Yet if the **qi jī** (氣機, Qi mechanism) is too violently stirred,
and the **Hé chē**’s work of **huà jīng wéi qì** (化精為氣, transforming essence into Qi)
and **huà qì wéi shén** (化氣為神, transforming Qi into spirit)
is not properly condensed at the **Dan dǐng** (丹鼎, Elixir Cauldron),
then the unrefined **yīn jīng** (陰精, yin essence) cannot be fixed,
and will not long remain in the post-Heaven **Dan dǐng**.
At the slightest provocation of ordinary fire,
it will stir the **yín gēn** (淫根, lustful roots),
and spawn **yín shì** (淫事, lustful deeds)—
and all will be lost.

Even if you forcefully suppress it,
not letting it move—
yet the post-Heaven **qì jīng** (精氣, essence and Qi)
are all of pure **yīn** (陰),
unrefined, and without constraint,
they must leak—
even if constrained, they must leak.

With this method alone,
you can **duó tiān dì guǐ shén zhī quán** (奪天地鬼神之權, seize the authority of Heaven, Earth, and spirits),
and participate in the **zào huà yīn yáng zhī fǎ** (造化陰陽之法, principles of Creation’s yin and yang),
to **zì zhǔ zì duó** (自主自奪, command and restrain yourself):
*"My life is mine alone—
not Heaven’s to control."*
Truly, you become an **immortal of eternal youth** (長生不老之仙),
the very **Yánluó lǎozi** (閻羅老子, Lord Yama) himself
has no power over you.

Thus the method must not be revealed to the unworthy,
lest Heaven’s spirits lose their authority over **shàn è bào yìng** (善惡報應, reward and retribution).

You, who know the Dao,
must surely grasp its meaning.
Tightly seal your lips and tongue,
do not recklessly spill Heaven’s secrets,
its hidden keys.

Once the **yuan qì** has gathered in the **Dan tián**,
and you practice the **Hé chē** method,
you must further rely on post-Heaven ordinary **qì** (氣) as **yáng huǒ yīn fú** (陽火陰符, yang fire and yin talisman),
urging and prompting it—
making it rise and fall, come and go without end,
stirring and refining it,
so that it transforms the ordinary into the true,
its changes unfathomable.

Though the **yuan qì** of the Elixir is rooted in pre-Heaven **yuan qì**,
post-Heaven ordinary **qì** is still indispensable for its function.

As for the Elixir itself,
its success depends entirely on the **huǒ hòu** (火候, fire timing).
The ancients, standing before the furnace,
were supremely cautious—
lest even a slight deviation in breath
disturb the **zào huà yīn yáng** (造化陰陽, workings of Creation’s yin and yang).

Thus it is said:
*"Advancing fire and employing talismans
is like cooking rice—
too gentle, and it remains raw;
too hasty, and it burns.
Hence the value of **chāngchāng** (惺惺, alertness) ever present,
and **miánmián** (綿綿, gradual continuity) without cease."*

From these two phrases,
you may discern the subtleties of fire use.

When the **ground thunder rumbles** (地下雷鳴),
and the fire presses the **jin** (金, metal),
at that moment, if not for **wǔ huǒ** (武火, martial fire),
how could the **jin qì** (金氣, metal Qi) ascend?

Yet martial fire must be wielded skillfully—
let its flames surge ten thousand feet,
its radiance shoot in all directions,
but I shall douse it with a single drop of cool water,
spread across the ten directions—
this is the **qi zhàng ér xīn xiǎng** (氣壯而心享之道, power of Qi and the joy of the heart),
the **qīngjìng tiándàn** (清净恬淡為本之妙術, wondrous art of purity and tranquility).

Hence it is said:
*"Dragon and tiger meet on the battlefield—
in a flash, their fate is sealed.
I urge you: when evil arises, act with virtue,
For strength gained through contention brings only harm."*

Indeed, the power is to be feared,
the moment is perilous—
yet the heart must never falter in its vigilance before the furnace.

Once you grasp this meaning,
you will never again face the threat of collapse.

Still, the **Hé chē** method must be practiced as such.
If applied indiscriminately to **shǒu zhōng** (守中, Central Retention),
before the **qi jī** is fully open,
and the heart-spirit is at peace—
if you simply follow the **zìrán** (自然, natural) method—
how can the spirit and Qi merge into one,
or any **yào wù** (藥物, medicinal substance) be gathered?

This must be done
when the **Xuān guān** (玄關, Mysterious Gate) first appears—
**shèn qì** (腎氣, kidney Qi) ascending,
**xīn yè** (心液, heart fluid) descending—
using the **shù qì** (數息, breath-counting) martial fire,
no thought allowed to stray,
no breath to rush.

Thus, with slow and steady urging,
stimulating the **tǔolì jīguān** (橐龠機關, bellows mechanism),
only then do ordinary breaths cease,
and **true breaths** (真息) emerge,
the human heart die,
and the Dao-heart live.

Else, let alone speak of *zìrán* (naturalness),
there will be no *zìrán* at all.

Hence it is said:
Even the *shēng zhī* (生知, innate-knowing sage)
must still descend to *xià kùn zhī* (下困知, acquired knowledge through hardship),
and only through diligent practice can mastery be attained.

The ancients spoke:
*"The Western Mountain’s White Tiger rages unchecked,
The Eastern Sea’s Green Dragon cannot be withstood.
With both hands seized, they battle to the death—
Forged into a single mass of purple gold frost."*

Again it is said:
*"To subdue the Dragon, one’s will must be as vast as heaven,
To tame the Tiger, one’s spirit must be fierce as smoke.
Even the dullest fool who grasps this truth—
Shall surely become a transcendent sage upon the earth."*

This *wǔhuǒ* (武火, martial fire) method
may be applied when the Dragon and Tiger do not yet meet,
when Water and Fire have not yet harmonized—
but if practicing the *hé chē* (河车, River Vehicle),
when already the Dragon roars and the Tiger howls,
when yin and yang respond in perfect accord,
to still employ this method here
would only risk driving the true *qi* into chaos and dispersion.
Mencius warned:
*"Like chasing a released pig—*
*once it has entered the thicket,*
*to then call it back is a grave mistake."*

I shall now reveal the complete technique—
you must strive with all your might to cultivate it,
to fulfill the hopes of your teacher.

**Do not recklessly disclose this—**
lest you invite calamity upon yourself.
